# Burn Rate

# Chapter 1

The farmer's shin was broken in two places. Vel could see the bone through the split skin above his left ankle, and the infection had already started — a hot redness climbing the calf, the wound edges tight with fluid that smelled sweet and wrong. Three days, his wife had said. A wall collapse in the grain shed. He'd been pinned under the rubble for six hours before they dug him out, and nobody in the settlement had anything to offer beyond boiled rags and willow-bark paste.

Vel knelt on the packed-earth floor beside the cot and pressed her palms flat against his leg, one hand above the break and one below. The farmer — Hael, forty or so, hands calloused from decades of soil and timber work, the nails cracked and dark — made a sound through his teeth but didn't pull away.

"Still," she said. "Don't move the leg."

She reached for the ability. The Ledger shifted. She felt it the way she always felt it: not pain, not sound, but a subtraction — something behind the sternum changing weight, the number at the back of her mind moving the way a clock hand moves, noticed only when it clicks. Thirty-three became thirty-two.

Warmth moved through her palms and into the leg. She could feel the shape of the damage now — the two fracture lines running oblique through the tibia, loose splinters floating in the swollen tissue, and under all of it the infection threading deeper than she'd estimated. Not surface rot. The bacteria had gotten into the marrow, settled into the exposed bone. Osteomyelitis. She'd need to push further than she'd planned.

"Shit," she said, quietly.

"What?" Hael's wife was by the window. Maret. She'd been wringing the same cloth for twenty minutes, her knuckles white against the fabric. She was thin in the way of someone who'd missed meals for months — not starving but diminished, the body quietly consuming its own reserves. Her dress had been let out and taken in again, the stitching visible where the seams had been reworked twice. When Vel had arrived that morning, Maret had offered her bread, and the bread had been the best thing in the house.

"Infection's deeper than it looked. I'm going to push the healing again."

"Does that cost you more?"

Vel didn't answer. She pushed the ability into the bone.

The second level went. Thirty-two became thirty-one.

The infection fought back in the way infections do — not with intelligence but with the blind persistence of something that had been growing for three days and didn't want to stop. She drove the healing through it, felt the bacteria die in waves, felt the swelling deflate and the redness retreat down the calf like a tide going out. The fracture lines knit. The splinters pulled back to the bone's surface and fused. The tissue reorganized itself around the repair, and Hael's breathing went from shallow and controlled to deep and surprised.

Vel sat back on her heels. Her hands were shaking — the specific fatigue that follows ability use, the body registering that something permanent has been taken. Not the tremor of exhaustion. The tremor of subtraction. She wiped her palms on her trousers.

The leg was functional. The bone was set, the infection cleared. A wide pale scar where the skin had split, stiffness for a week or two, and he'd need to keep weight off it for a few days. But he'd walk. He'd work the fields. He'd be able to stand at the well and haul water for his wife, and that was the kind of thing this settlement couldn't afford to lose.

"That's it?" Hael said. He was staring at his leg with the expression of someone who'd been in pain for three days and couldn't yet trust that the pain had stopped.

"That's it. Stay off it today. Tomorrow you can move around, but nothing heavy for a week."

He touched the scar on his shin, pressing with his thumb. Vel watched him press too hard and wince and then press again, as if checking. His eyes were brown and tired and grateful in a way that made her want to leave the room. She'd never liked the gratitude. The work was the work; the thanks turned it into something she'd have to carry.

Maret put the cloth down. She crossed the room and stood over her husband's cot and put her hand on his forehead, feeling for the fever that had been climbing since yesterday. Her own hand was still trembling from the wringing. She noticed Vel watching and tucked it behind her back.

"What do we owe you?" Maret asked.

"Whatever you can manage."

Maret went to a tin on the shelf above the stove. She counted the marks with the careful fingers of someone who knew exactly how many were in the tin before she opened it. The sound was thin — a small number in a small container. She came back with seven marks and a jar of preserved cherries, the glass cloudy with age but the fruit inside still a deep, livid red.

"The cherries are good," Maret said. "I put them up last autumn, before the —" She stopped. "They're good."

Before the fever, Vel thought. Before eleven people died and the Hoarded sent no one. Before the grain contract was renegotiated and the payments cut and the settlement started eating itself from the edges in. Maret didn't finish the sentence because she didn't need to. Everyone here knew what last autumn meant.

"Thank you," Vel said. She took the marks and the jar.

She picked up her pack from where she'd set it by the door — canvas worn soft with decades of use, the straps repaired with cord and leather scraps, the buckles tarnished. She checked the main compartment out of a habit forty years deep and stepped outside.

The afternoon sun was warm. Late spring, the air carrying turned earth and the smell of something flowering in the hedgerows along the settlement's southern boundary. Twenty or so houses clustered around a well with a crumbling stone lip and a winch mechanism held together with rope and a bent nail. Beyond the houses, the fields stretched south toward a tree line gone shaggy and untended. Wheat and barley in alternating rows, but patchy — gaps where the soil hadn't responded to the last planting, or where nobody had been strong enough to work the ground after the fever took three of the settlement's best field hands.

Vel walked the settlement's single road. Her knees complained about the packed earth — fifty-eight years of kneeling on bad surfaces, and she'd stopped noticing the stiffness years ago and then started noticing it again recently, which meant it was getting worse. She passed the grain shed where Hael had been hurt — the south wall collapsed inward, the roof sagging, timber beams cracked along the grain. Nobody had the levels to Shape it back together, and nobody had the labor to rebuild it by hand. So the grain was being stored in a lean-to behind the meeting hall, open to weather and rats, and that was how things went in a settlement the Hoarded had decided wasn't worth maintaining.

Two children were playing near the well, throwing a rope back and forth with the listless energy of kids who'd run out of games an hour ago. One of them watched Vel pass and said nothing. The other waved. She waved back. They were thin in the shoulders, the way Maret was thin — not starving, just less than they should have been. A woman she hadn't met was mending a fence post at the far end of the road, hammering with a flat stone because the settlement's only real hammer had broken a month ago and nobody could afford the marks for a new one from a trader.

This place had been functional once. The Hoarded had run a grain program through here: Fertility Charms applied to the soil each season, yields shipped north to Vault storehouses, the settlement paid just enough to keep planting. Standard arrangement. Then the rust-fever swept through two years ago, took eleven people in six weeks, and the Hoarded did nothing. Sent no healers. Spent no levels. Adjusted the contract terms to account for reduced labor capacity and let the settlement shrink. The arithmetic of abandonment — Vel had seen it a dozen times in northern settlements, the same slow calculus: if the output doesn't justify the investment, cut the investment. The people inside the equation were incidental.

She sat on the low stone wall by the well and ate one of the cherries. It was good — tart, preserved in something that might have been brandy, with a sweetness that had deepened over the winter months. Maret had put these up before everything went wrong, and she'd given them to a stranger for healing her husband, and that was the kind of trade this world ran on when the powerful looked away.

Thirty-one.

The number sat behind her sternum the way it always did. Not pressure, not sound — a persistent awareness, like a stone she'd swallowed years ago that had never dissolved. She'd learned to live with it the way you learn to live with tinnitus or a bad joint. It was just there. At three hundred, she hadn't noticed it. At two hundred, it had been background noise. At one hundred, she'd started counting. Below fifty, she'd stopped being able to not count.

Thirty-one levels. Forty-one years of spending. Every ability she'd ever used — healing the sick, fighting when she had to, building shelters, mending bones, pulling people from collapsed buildings and flooded canals and burning rooms — a withdrawal from a total that never went up. She'd watched the number drop from three hundred to this, one ability at a time, each one a trade she'd chosen to make. And she'd make the same trades again, every one of them. That wasn't the problem. The problem was that the account was almost empty and the biggest withdrawal was still ahead.

She'd budgeted one level for this stop. Standard bone-set, infection clear, tissue repair. One level, then back on the road. You budget everything when you're at thirty-three. You plan each spend the way you plan each meal when the food's running low — what you can afford, what you can't, what it costs to be generous.

The infection had been deeper than she'd judged. So she'd spent the second level, and now the plan was off by one, and one was a lot when you were at thirty-one.

She finished the cherry and wiped the juice on her trousers. Her hands were still shaking. They'd stop in an hour. They always did.

---

The settlement had given her a room — a storeroom at the back of the meeting hall, cleared of grain sacks and furnished with a cot that smelled of burlap and old dust. She sat on the cot and spread her supplies on the floor. The light came through a window that didn't close all the way, and she could hear the well pulley creaking as someone drew water outside.

She took stock. Not of the pack — she knew what was in the pack down to the weight. She took stock of what was left of her.

Thirty-one levels. She ran through her remaining abilities the way she'd done every night for the last eight years, the way a miser counts coins, touching each one, confirming it was still there. Healing: she could manage three, maybe four standard applications before the ability degraded below useful output. Below level twenty, it wouldn't have enough force to do more than close a shallow cut. Combat: she had a handful of defensive options — a Redirect that could turn an incoming strike, a Perception boost that sharpened her awareness of threats, a short-range Conceal that could buy her thirty seconds of not-being-seen. Nothing that would stop a real fighter. Nothing that would win a confrontation she couldn't avoid. Utility: an Endurance enhancement that could stretch a day's march into two, a Nightsight, a Weatherskin that blunted rain and cold. Minor abilities she'd relied on for decades, each one now carrying a price she had to weigh against everything else she might need.

This was what thirty-one looked like. Not helpless, but thin. A toolkit with most of the tools removed, and the ones remaining too valuable to use casually.

She'd been planning the route to zero for eight years. Mapping the path, cross-referencing landmarks, confirming distances, working out the mechanism — how the threshold opened, what it required, what she'd need to make it work. She knew the destination: a place in the far south called the Scour, where the land dropped into a vast erosion basin and the earth's bones showed through the surface. She knew the route, more or less. She knew what zero was — not death, not an ending, but a door to a place where people lived without the Ledger, without levels, without the constant arithmetic of what you could afford and what you couldn't.

She'd seen the door open once, eight years ago. The sight had rewritten the architecture of her life.

The problem was the math. She'd studied it, tested what she could test, thought about it until thinking looped back on itself. A single person reaching zero at the threshold could trigger the crossing — she'd seen that. But the crossing had been unstable. The door had flickered, had held for seconds, had barely worked. One person's descent to zero wasn't enough energy to keep it open cleanly. She needed more. She needed a sustained cascade — two people spending down together, the combined output stabilizing the threshold, holding the door long enough for a clean crossing. And for the cascade to generate enough energy, she needed someone with a long way to fall. Someone starting high. Someone with levels to burn — a lot of levels — who was willing to burn them all the way to nothing.

She didn't have a lot of levels. She had thirty-one.

Which meant she needed a partner. Someone high-level, someone willing to spend their way to zero alongside her. And that was the gap in her plan that she'd been unable to close for eight years, because the kind of person who had a high level was the kind of person who'd never deliberately burn it down, and the rare few who might were almost always too depleted to matter.

She'd looked. She'd spent years looking. Traveled through Spent Towns and settlements and the edges of the Margins, asking careful questions. Had anyone heard of a Hoarded defector? Anyone at 250 or above who'd walked away? Anyone with the levels and the willingness to descend? The answers had always been no. The Hoarded didn't leave. That was the whole point of being Hoarded — you stayed, you preserved, you held your three hundred intact until the Stillness hollowed you out from inside and your children pretended it was dignity.

She couldn't keep looking. Each trip cost levels she didn't have. Each healing job cost levels. Each fight, each emergency, each time someone needed help and she was the only one around — levels gone, levels she'd meant to save for the journey. At thirty-three she'd still had room to maneuver. At thirty-one the margin was thin enough to see through. If she dropped below twenty, the cascade wouldn't have enough energy from her side to work. If she kept working, kept traveling, kept spending on other people's emergencies, she'd cross that line within a year. Maybe sooner.

Now. It had to be now.

She packed her supplies. Checked the water skin, the dried food, the knife she'd carried since she was thirty. She was leaving in the morning. She'd head north — there was a trading post two days up the road where she could resupply and listen for news. She was out of time for waiting. She'd find a partner or she'd cross alone and hope the threshold held.

She sat with that for a moment. Eight years of planning, and it came down to leaving a storeroom in a dying settlement with thirty-one levels and a route she still wasn't sure would work.

Outside, the well pulley stopped. The light through the cracked window turned gold.

---

The news found her that evening.

She was in the meeting hall with four of the settlement's remaining adults, eating a meal of boiled grain and root vegetables that tasted like everything else she'd eaten in the northern settlements — adequate, filling, stretched thin. The hall was the largest building in the place, a timber-frame structure with a repaired roof that leaked in two spots and a stone hearth that drew well. Dried herbs hung from the ceiling beams, and the room smelled of rosemary and woodsmoke and the sour tang of grain that had been stored too long.

A trader named Sorren had arrived an hour before sunset, leading a cart pulled by a mule that looked as tired as its owner. Sorren was a compact man in his fifties with a weathered face and the particular lean of someone who'd spent decades walking beside a loaded cart — one shoulder slightly higher than the other from holding the lead rope year after year. He had dust in the creases of his neck and a habit of tapping his spoon against the rim of his bowl between bites, a small percussion that filled the gaps in conversation. He'd been running the northern route for twenty years, he said. Dry goods, tools, once in a while a bolt of fabric or a crate of lamp oil. The settlements needed him and paid him poorly and he came back anyway because the route was his and he didn't know how to be anyone else.

The conversation was the usual. Crop reports. Road conditions. Which settlements had been hit by the rust-fever and which had scraped through. Whether the Hoarded grain contracts would be renewed for the coming season or whether the terms would tighten again. Vel half-listened. She'd heard versions of this talk in a hundred settlement halls. The northerners discussed the Hoarded the way you discuss weather — a force you couldn't control and mostly just endured, with occasional flickers of anger that never quite became anything louder.

Olla, the oldest of the settlement's adults, did most of the talking on the local side. She was white-haired, her hands palsied, but she managed the field rotation for the entire settlement and remembered every planting decision going back thirty years. She had opinions about soil drainage and kept them at conversational volume. She ate her grain in precise, small spoonfuls, and between bites she asked Sorren questions with the patience of someone who'd been extracting useful information from traders since before Vel was born.

"The eastern road still passable?" Olla asked.

"Passable. Muddy past the fork. They had rain last week."

"And Cairn Hill?"

"Standing. They got through the winter better than most. Good leadership there." Sorren tapped his spoon. "Actually — heard a strange one at Cairn Hill. Two days back." He paused in the way people pause when they have a story they've been carrying and have been waiting for the right table to set it on. "A Hoarded lord walked out of the Vault."

The room went quiet. Not the quiet of shock — the quiet of people in a settlement the Hoarded had abandoned, hearing something they didn't know how to place.

"Walked out," Olla said. "What does that mean, walked out?"

"Means what it sounds like. Used an ability to open the gates — first ability the man had ever used in his life, the way the story goes. Some kind of shield or force thing, blew half the gate hall apart. Then he just walked through it. Out into the open. Hasn't come back." Sorren tapped his spoon twice. "House Aldraste. The son."

"The preservation house," Olla said. She set her spoon down with the deliberate motion of someone organizing her reaction. "Three hundred for six generations."

"Until now." Sorren shrugged. "Could be road talk. You know how stories get by the time they've passed through three trading posts. But I heard it from Breck's people at Cairn Hill, and they got it from a Vault courier who'd actually been in the building when it happened. Said the boy dropped ten, twelve levels in the first hour. Using abilities he'd never touched. Burning through them like he'd just discovered his hands worked."

Vel had been eating steadily through the conversation, her face neutral. She'd trained herself out of visible reactions decades ago — a useful skill when you spent your life in settlements where strangers were assessed quickly and remembered long. But she was listening now with an attention that had nothing to do with the food.

"How long ago?" she asked.

Sorren looked at her. He'd been polite to her all evening — the considered politeness you showed a healer who'd just saved a man's leg — but she could see him recalculating. The question had been too quick. Too specific.

"Five, six days," he said. "Give or take. Courier was moving fast."

"And do they know where he went after?"

"North, is what I heard. Or maybe just — out. Away. The Hoarded don't exactly build people who know how to navigate." He tapped the spoon, looked at it, set it down. "They'll be sending people after him, I'd think. Can't have a lord's son burning down in public. Makes the whole philosophy look like what it is."

"Mm," Vel said.

"Why the interest?"

"Just curious. Not often you hear about a Hoarded walking out."

"Not often," Sorren agreed. "Not ever, really. I've been on this route twenty years and that's the first one I've heard of that wasn't secondhand rumor from two centuries back." He picked up his spoon and resumed tapping. "Probably won't last, though. Kid's never been outside walls. He'll burn through half his levels trying to boil water and they'll collect him inside a week."

The conversation moved on. Olla asked about the price of lamp oil. Someone else talked about the well mechanism and whether it could hold through summer. Vel ate her meal and let the talk wash over her.

A Hoarded lord. House Aldraste. Level three hundred — or he had been, before he started burning. If the story was five or six days old, he could be at two-eighty by now. Two-seventy. Depending on how fast he was spending and how reckless he was about it. And he'd be reckless, because the Hoarded never learned efficiency. They went from zero experience to full power and had no concept of calibration. Every defector she'd ever heard of — in the old stories, the settlement legends — had burned levels like they were infinite, because when you'd never spent a single point, you had no understanding of what a point was worth.

But if he was real. If the rumor was solid.

He had what she needed. The levels. A long, long way to fall. And if she could find him, and if she could reach him before the Hoarded collected him, the gap in her plan closed.

She finished her meal. She thanked Olla for the food and the rosemary, which was genuinely good — Olla kept a small herb garden behind the meeting hall, a careful plot she tended with the same attention she gave the field rotations, and the rosemary had made the boiled grain taste like something a person might actually choose to eat. She thanked Sorren for the news and the company. She stepped outside into the cooling evening, the sun below the tree line now, the sky darkening from gold to the blue-gray of late spring twilight.

In the storeroom, she sat on the cot and pulled the journal from her pack.

It was battered. The leather cover had gone soft from years of rain and handling, the edges worn round, the binding cracked along the spine and restitched with heavy thread that didn't match the original. The pages inside were thick — the kind of paper that held up to weather — and crowded with writing. Two handwritings filled the margins and overflowed into every gap the text allowed. The first was small and precise, the ink faded to brown, the annotations methodical: distances measured in day-counts, terrain descriptions, water sources, settlement names with populations and average levels noted in careful parentheses. The second handwriting was looser and faster, a different color ink — additions and corrections and questions written over and around the first, sometimes arguing with it. On a page mapping a southern route, the first hand had written *passable in dry season* and the second had added *barely — took us two days longer than this says, and the creek crossing is worse than it looks.*

The southern routes were the most detailed sections. Page after page — paths through territory Vel had been studying for years, landmarks, warnings, notes about which settlements might shelter travelers and which wouldn't. The first handwriting had mapped these routes with the thoroughness of someone who expected to walk them again. The second handwriting had filled in what the first had missed.

Vel turned the pages slowly. She handled them with a care that didn't match how she handled anything else in her life — her pack, her tools, her own body, all of these she treated with the rough efficiency of long use. The journal was different. She turned each page as if the paper might not survive carelessness.

The route she needed was here. South through the Margins to the Scour. She'd cross-referenced it against every other source she'd found over eight years, and this journal was still the most detailed map she had. The annotations were old now, and the landmarks might have shifted — settlements moved, roads washed out, terrain changed by weather and the slow collapse of overused land. But the bones of the route were sound.

She looked at a page near the back where the two handwritings overlapped most densely, the annotations crowding each other, the ink from different seasons layering into a small record of two people who'd traveled the same ground at different times and paid attention to different things. She let her thumb rest on the margin where the second handwriting had corrected a distance calculation — *not 4 days, closer to 5 with full packs* — and held it there for a moment longer than the information required.

Then she closed the journal. Wrapped it in the square of oilcloth she kept it in. Fit it back into her pack against the flat of her back where it rode best.

A Hoarded lord at three hundred, burning down. Five or six days out of the Vault. The Hoarded would send people after him — that was certain. A lord's son spending levels in public was a political crisis, a repudiation of everything the great families built their authority on. They'd want him back before the story spread, before the Spent Towns heard about it, before anyone started asking what it meant that a preservation house had produced a defector.

She'd need to reach him first. Or reach the area he was in and work from there, because a man who'd never been outside walls wouldn't leave a clean trail — he'd leave a mess of wasted levels and confused settlements and burned-out abilities pointed in every direction.

North. He'd gone north, or maybe not — but the Vault was roughly northeast of here, and a man walking away from the only home he'd ever known would walk in whatever direction felt like away. She'd head north toward the Vault's region and listen. A Hoarded lord dropping levels wasn't subtle. People would notice. People would talk. She'd find the trail.

She didn't know if she could reach him in time. She didn't know if he'd still be free when she did — the Hoarded moved slowly on most things, but a defecting lord was the kind of emergency that cut through institutional inertia. She didn't know if he'd listen to a fifty-eight-year-old woman at thirty-one who wanted to walk him to the bottom of everything he'd ever been.

But she was out of time, and he was the first real possibility in eight years, and thirty-one was already too low for the luxury of waiting for a better one.

She lay back on the cot. The storeroom was dark now, the window a blue rectangle. She could hear Sorren's mule chewing in the yard, the slow grinding of an animal that didn't know or care about Hoarded lords or thresholds or the number thirty-one. The settlement was winding down — a door somewhere, footsteps on packed earth, the well pulley one more time. The child who'd been singing earlier in the afternoon had stopped.

Vel closed her eyes. The number occupied the space behind her sternum, precise and permanent. She thought about the journal in her pack and the route south that it described. She thought about the door she'd seen open once, eight years ago — the flash of somewhere else, buildings and light and people, a world without this world's arithmetic — and the specific ache that lived in the gap between that moment and now. An ache she didn't name, because naming it would make it a thing she carried openly instead of a thing she could fold small and keep inside the practical architecture of planning and movement and what needed to be done next.

She'd leave at first light. North.

The last thought before she slept was subtraction: thirty-one minus whatever the road would cost, minus whatever finding the Aldraste boy would cost, minus whatever it took to convince him. The answer was a number she didn't know yet, and not knowing was what passed for hope at this end of the Ledger.


# Chapter 2

The Conservatory Rite began, as it always did, with the presentation of objects.

This week the Aldraste household had supplied the table: a cracked ceramic bowl that a single Mending Charm could make whole, a bundle of cut flowers already browning at their edges, an unlit oil lamp with a full reservoir, and a small mechanical clock whose mainspring had wound down three hours ago. The objects were arranged on a stone pedestal at the center of the Conservatory hall — four mundane invitations to act, positioned under excellent lighting so every guest could appreciate them not being addressed.

Kem Aldraste sat in the third row of the Aldraste section and watched a petal fall from the browning bouquet.

Around him, sixty-three Hoarded lords and ladies sat in the same practiced stillness they had brought to the Conservatory every week for as long as there had been a Conservatory. The hall was built for this — high ceilings that stretched sound thin, stone walls that absorbed noise, benches cut from the same quarry so no seat creaked differently from another. Even the air felt managed, still and temperate, neither warm enough to invite fidgeting nor cool enough to prompt a shiver. The architecture of inaction.

The Rite had no officiant. No one spoke. That was the point. For two hours every week, the great families of the Vault sat in proximity to objects that could be improved, repaired, revived, or corrected with a single ability use, and demonstrated their collective commitment to not doing so. The cracked bowl stayed cracked. The lamp stayed dark. The flowers wilted. The clock was silent. The Hoarded watched these small failures accumulate and called the watching a discipline, and the discipline a philosophy, and the philosophy a civilization.

Two weeks ago the Ventrathi family had contributed a wounded songbird, which had chirped weakly for the first ninety minutes and then gone quiet in a way that was distinct from sleep. The Ventrathi matriarch had accepted compliments for the difficulty of her selection for days afterward. The bird, Kem gathered, had represented an exceptional test of resolve. A healing charm for a bird that size would have cost a single level — one three-hundredth of the capacity any person in this room carried — and sixty-odd people had sat in cushioned silence while it died.

Kem had attended the Conservatory Rite every week for twenty-six years. He had missed perhaps a dozen sessions — illness twice, a diplomatic reception once, and once when he was nine and had hidden in the kitchens until a servant found him and carried him back. The servant had been docked a level for the exertion of carrying him. She'd been at two hundred and ninety-eight, doing acceptable work. After that she was at two hundred and ninety-seven, which was not a meaningful difference in any functional sense but mattered enormously in the Vault's social accounting, where the gap between three hundred and anything less than three hundred was the gap between person and instrument.

Kem's mother sat in the row ahead, at the end nearest the aisle. Lady Aldraste's posture was precise without being rigid — a calibration she had maintained for decades, her shoulders in a line that communicated authority rather than effort. Her hair was dark and pinned with small silver clasps in the formal Hoarded style, and she wore a robe the color of good iron that had been pressed that morning by servants who did not have the option of being wrinkled. She was watching the pedestal, or appeared to be watching the pedestal. Kem had understood since he was a teenager that his mother watched everything — the other families' composure, the politics of who fidgeted and who didn't, the slow rearrangement of social capital that happened in this room under the cover of stillness. She missed very little and mentioned less.

There was also the thing she did with Lord Aldraste's collar. Every week, at some point during the first half hour, she reached across without looking and adjusted the fabric at his neck — a fold that had gone wrong, or a clasp that had shifted, something small and particular. She did it with the unhurried precision of a person who had been tending this man's appearance for thirty years, maintaining the surface of something whose interior she could not repair. Kem did not know whether the other families noticed. He noticed every time.

His father sat at the head of the Aldraste section. Lord Aldraste's posture was perfect — better than perfect, the kind of composure that had outlasted the person who had cultivated it, running now on memory alone. His hands rested on his thighs in a symmetry that looked arranged because it had been. His robes were immaculate, the collar recently adjusted by his wife, his face set in an expression that had once been dignity and was now the shape his face held when no one was directing it.

His eyes were open and they were not looking at anything — not closed, not focused on some distant consideration, not deliberately unseeing, just empty. The eyes of a man whose attention had been thinning for years, like cloth held too long in sun, until what remained was the structure without the thread. Lord Aldraste at sixty-one had the same three hundred levels he'd been born with — every point of potential intact, a full reservoir that had never been tapped for any purpose — and the man carrying that reservoir was dissolving by increments so small that most of the family had stopped registering the change. His speech had slowed, not thoughtfully, the way a careful man weighs his words, but mechanically, as if the apparatus that assembled sentences was losing components. He paused mid-phrase now, and what came after the pause did not always connect to what had come before. Servants guided him to meals. His valet dressed him and arranged his limbs in the morning. In the Conservatory, he sat with the flawless composure of a man who had spent six decades perfecting the posture and would likely maintain it for the remaining years of his body's function, regardless of whether the mind behind it continued to attend.

The Hoarded called this Stillness. They said the word with the reverence other cultures reserved for devotion or mastery. Lord Aldraste has achieved remarkable Stillness — a credit to the House. The highest form of the Hoarded discipline: a man so committed to preservation that he had preserved himself into absence.

Kem watched the clock on the pedestal not tick.

He had been watching his father disappear for a long time. It had not always been like this. There were years — Kem could not have named the exact year it started, because the onset had been gradual, a slow erasure of small things before the large things followed — when his father had been present, sharp even. Lord Aldraste had run the House's affairs, negotiated alliances, spoken with a precision the other families respected. He had laughed, rarely and specifically, at things that were genuinely funny. He had put his hand on Kem's shoulder one afternoon when Kem was twelve and said something about patience that Kem could no longer remember the content of but still remembered the weight of — the hand, the pressure, the sense that the person behind the hand was fully there.

That person was not here. Kem watched his father's face for some sign of interior life — a reaction to the falling petal, a flicker toward the unlit lamp, anything suggesting the machinery was still running behind the blank composure — and found nothing. The Ledger read three hundred. The man read empty.

The second petal fell.

Kem stood up.

He did not decide to stand. His legs straightened and his weight shifted and then he was vertical, and the sound of his bench creaking under the redistribution was, in the Conservatory's silence, an event. Sixty-three faces turned toward him. The Ventrathi matriarch's lips parted. The Drassic family's eldest son went rigid in his seat. A minor-house lord in the back rows flinched, actually flinched, as if Kem had thrown something.

His mother said his name.

"Kem."

Not a question, not a command, not an appeal. His name in her voice, held steady by the same composure that held everything else in place, and underneath it something she was not going to show this room — something compressed into that single syllable and kept there by thirty years of Hoarded discipline. She did not stand. She did not reach for him. She looked at him with eyes that were, unlike her husband's, entirely present, and she said his name once, and she let the Conservatory's silence seal around it.

He walked out.

His footsteps on the stone floor were the loudest sound the Conservatory had heard in weeks. Each step was a small detonation of decorum — heel striking stone, the rustle of his robe, the whisper of displaced air. He walked past the Drassic section and the Ventrathi section and the minor families in the back rows who were, he understood, already rehearsing how they would describe this moment to the people who hadn't been here. He kept walking.

The Conservatory doors were shut. They were large, ornamental, carved with patterns that represented the founding of the Vault — a history of deliberate inaction rendered in hardwood by artisans who had been paid in marks and spent levels they would never recover to produce a monument to people who spent nothing. The doors were designed to be opened from the outside, by servants, because the Hoarded did not open their own doors.

Kem did not know how to open a door, and this was not a figure of speech. The doors had handles — brass, polished weekly — and in principle the mechanism was simple. Pull or push, depending on the hinge direction, and the door would move. But Kem had never done it. Twenty-six years in the Vault and every door had been opened for him, closed behind him, held at the appropriate angle for the appropriate duration by people whose job it was to manage doors so that Kem and people like Kem never had to think about them.

He thought about them now. He reached for the handle and what came instead was something from deeper in his mind, a reflex he hadn't known he possessed, an instinct that reached for the three hundred levels sitting untouched at the edge of his awareness — the background hum of the Ledger, the number always there, always full.

A Projection Shield deployed in the corridor with a sound like the air itself splitting. The force wall erupted outward from Kem's extended hand, rippling, crackling with energy so far in excess of the task that the excess was itself a kind of statement — not one he had intended, but the room heard it regardless. The Shield hit the Conservatory doors and blew them both off their hinges. The left door slammed sideways into the corridor wall and cratered the plaster. The right door went further, tumbling end over end down the hallway and coming to rest against a decorative column twenty meters away with a sound that suggested the column had opinions about the impact.

Stone dust drifted in the sudden stillness.

Kem stared at the wreckage. The brass handles were still attached to both doors. One of them had bent.

He checked his Ledger — the number that was always there, the background awareness he had never thought about because it had never changed, the quiet hum of capacity sitting full and ready at the edge of his mind.

Two hundred and ninety-seven.

Three levels. He had spent three levels to open a door that a brass handle and a human arm could have managed for free.

Behind him, the Conservatory was silent, but it was a different silence now — not the practiced silence of discipline but the shocked silence of sixty-three people who had just watched an ability deployed in a room where abilities had not been used in living memory. He could feel the weight of their attention on his back. He stepped through the ruined doorway, his shoes crunching on stone debris, and walked into the Vault's corridors without a plan.

---

The corridors were built for people who did not hurry. Wide, polished stone, lit by ability-sustained lamps that servants maintained at a cost of one level per lamp per year. Portraits of Hoarded lords along the walls, painted by Spent Town artists because painting required effort and the Hoarded would not spend on their own likenesses. A carpet runner, deep blue, that absorbed his footsteps and gave the impression of walking through a building after hours. The Vault was beautiful. Kem had never thought about whether it was beautiful because he had never had another point of comparison, but in the clarity of whatever was happening to him — adrenaline, perhaps, or whatever happened inside a man who had never acted and had suddenly acted — the beauty registered, and it registered as expensive.

His legs were already complaining. The longest walk he normally took inside the Vault was the route from his chambers to the Conservatory, perhaps eighty meters, with a pause halfway in the gallery where a bench invited rest. He'd walked past that distance and the burn in his calves had progressed from complaint to argument. His right shoe had started to rub at the heel. The shoes were handmade, commissioned from a Spent Town cobbler who'd spent two levels on the fitting, and they were perfect for marble floors and wrong for everything else.

He took a wrong turn near the eastern residential wing. Then another. The Vault contained several hundred rooms arranged across multiple wings and levels, connected by corridors that all looked essentially identical — polished stone, blue carpet, portraits, lamps. He had lived here for twenty-six years and he could not find the way out, because he had never needed to find it. Servants appeared when he needed to be somewhere. They walked ahead. He followed. The idea that the building had a layout requiring personal comprehension had never entered his planning, which was consistent with the larger fact that he had never planned anything.

He turned a corner and the corridor changed.

The stone here was a shade darker, the walls slightly thicker, and the doors — there were two of them, heavy, unmarked — sat flush with the wall in a way that suggested reinforcement. No portraits on this stretch. No carpet runner. The lamps were maintained to the same brightness as everywhere else, but the light seemed to illuminate less. Kem had known this wing existed the way he knew the kitchens existed — as a fact without detail, a section of the Vault he had been redirected away from so smoothly as a child that he hadn't realized the redirecting was deliberate until years later. No one discussed it. Not in the way the Hoarded didn't discuss Stillness — with euphemism and careful avoidance — but genuinely didn't discuss it, as if the corridor and its sealed doors occupied a gap in the family's collective attention.

The silence behind those doors had a different quality from the Vault's usual quiet. The Vault's silence was the absence of action — a held breath, a negative space. This was a silence with density, a silence that suggested containment rather than emptiness. The silence of people doing something and being quiet about it.

Kem did not stop. He was aware, dimly, that if he started investigating things he did not understand, the momentum carrying him toward the gate would fail, and momentum was the only resource he had that didn't cost levels.

He found the gate hall by accident — a wrong turn he'd taken out of frustration that deposited him in a wide ceremonial space he half-recognized from childhood memory. High ceiling, vaulted, the Vault's main entrance, rarely used because the Hoarded rarely left. Two guards stood near the gate in the Vault's understated uniform, neither armed. Hoarded guards observed and reported. They did not intervene physically, because physical intervention was action, and action was spending. Actual enforcement, when enforcement was needed, was handled by — Kem did not know who handled actual enforcement. The question had never come up in twenty-six years of never needing to enforce anything.

The guards watched him approach. The older one, a man with the expression of someone who had trained himself to see without reacting, held his position and said nothing. The younger one — a woman, perhaps thirty, with a crease between her eyebrows that suggested she had not expected today to contain anything worth creasing over — opened her mouth and then shut it.

"I'm leaving," Kem said. He heard it come out flat and declarative, the grammar of a person who had practiced formal speech his whole life but had never had to say anything that mattered.

"My lord." The younger guard's eyes flicked to the older guard, who did not look back. "The gate is — I'd need to send word to Lady Aldraste before —"

"Open it, please."

"Protocol requires notification of the household before any —"

The Projection Shield came again. Smaller this time — his mind, apparently, retained some memory of the last deployment and had attempted calibration — but still too much, still a rippling wall of force that crackled with surplus energy. The latch mechanism shattered. The gate groaned and swung outward on its hinges, not violently but firmly, and warm late-spring air flooded into the hall with a complexity of smell and temperature that hit Kem like a change in altitude. The air smelled of soil and growing things and something he couldn't identify — something open and directionless that turned out, he would learn later, to be the smell of distance itself.

Two hundred and ninety-five.

By the gate, recessed into the wall on the left, stood a row of ceremonial pedestals — one per major House. Each held a House signet, a heavy ring of worked metal bearing the family crest, displayed in the gate hall as a symbol of the families' commitment to the Vault. The signets stayed because the families stayed. Nobody wore them. They were polished weekly by servants and admired on formal occasions and otherwise left alone.

The Aldraste signet was silver-dark, heavy, the crest a closed circle. Kem picked it up. The metal was warm from the lamp-heated alcove, heavier than it looked. He put it on his finger, where it fit exactly, because it had been sized for him at birth — because the Hoarded planned generations ahead for eventualities they would never act on.

He did not take it because he wanted it. He took it because leaving it would mean he'd paused to consider whether to take it, and pausing to consider would mean this was a decision, and if this was a decision he would have to weigh it against the consequences, and if he started weighing consequences he would sit back down.

He walked through the gate.

---

The world was louder than the Vault.

This was not an observation but a physical event — sound arriving from multiple directions at once, layered and unmanaged and alive. Birds, several varieties producing several kinds of noise, none of them curated or dampened by architecture. Wind moving through trees that were actual trees, not the Vault's manicured garden specimens but full-grown pines that creaked and shed things and produced a rushing sound when the breeze moved through their crowns. Insects. The distant suggestion of running water. And underneath all of it an ambient texture of sound that was simply the landscape existing without anyone's oversight.

The ground outside the Vault was not level. The gate opened onto a path that descended a gentle slope toward a tree line several hundred meters away, and the path was dirt — packed dirt with loose stones and exposed roots and ruts where rain had channeled. Kem's shoes, designed for polished stone by a cobbler who had reasonably assumed the shoes would never leave a building, made contact with the dirt and communicated immediately that they were not going to be helpful.

He walked. His calves, already burning from the longest interior walk of his life, discovered that downhill was a different kind of work from flat, and that neither set of muscles had been meaningfully developed by twenty-six years of sitting in ceremonial silence. His heel blister announced its presence with each step. A stone worked its way under the ball of his right foot through the thin sole. The sun — the actual sun, unfiltered by walls or ceilings, arriving with a directness he had not prepared for — landed on the back of his neck and began, with the impersonal patience of natural processes, to burn him.

He reached the tree line in what felt like twenty minutes and was probably closer to forty. His legs were trembling. His shirt was damp across the back. The pines gave shade, which was an improvement, and the ground beneath them was cushioned with needles — a surface that gave slightly under his weight, that smelled like something alive and decomposing, that concealed things he could not see. A small brown animal darted across the path ahead of him and he startled so badly he stumbled sideways into a tree trunk. His shoulder hit bark and the bark was not smooth.

He stood in the pine forest, breathing hard, rubbing his shoulder, and took stock.

He was Kem Aldraste, level two hundred and ninety-five. Heir to one of the five major Hoarded Houses, a title that he suspected had been functionally revoked the moment he'd blown the Conservatory doors off their hinges. He was wearing robes designed for sitting and shoes designed for marble. He carried no food, no water, no pack, no map, and no money — he was not entirely certain money existed as a physical object, because the Hoarded didn't handle currency, and the servants who managed the household accounts had never explained the financial system to someone who was never expected to interact with it. He had his clothes, his House signet on his finger, and approximately two hundred and ninety-five levels of raw ability that he had no training in using and that he had so far deployed exclusively for the purpose of destroying doors.

His practical skills included reading, several languages, all theoretical. History, comprehensive, all theoretical. Governance philosophy, extensive, all theoretical. And the ability to sit motionless for two hours while flowers died.

He was hungry.

Not the anticipatory appetite that preceded Vault meals — meals that arrived at precise intervals, prepared by servants, served on plates he had never washed and could not have identified the washing process for. This was hunger as a physical condition: the hollow pull below his sternum, a shakiness in his hands that he hadn't connected to his stomach until his stomach growled loud enough to startle a bird off a low branch. He had walked further in the last hour than he normally walked in a week, and his body had burned through whatever reserves it carried and was now presenting him with a demand he had no manual process for addressing.

He knew the theory. Sustenance Charms were a basic utility ability — one of the first things children in the Spent Towns learned to calibrate, or so his tutors had told him. The principle was straightforward: draw on the Ledger to generate food and water from ambient energy, scaled to need. A single person's meal might cost one or two levels. Efficient, practical, the most common ability use in the world outside the Vault. Something any person at level one hundred and fifty did daily without thinking about it.

Kem had never used a Sustenance Charm. He had never used any ability before this morning. He knew it the way he knew the historical causes of the Spent Town migration: as information, arranged in his mind by tutors, disconnected from the physical reality it described.

He activated the charm.

His intention was modest. Something to eat. Bread, maybe. A portion of food appropriate for one person sitting on a log in a pine forest.

What he got was bread — eight loaves of it, golden-crusted, steaming, materializing on the ground around the log in a rough semicircle. Following the bread came a roast, enormous, glistening, radiating heat and the smell of rendered fat, the size of something you would present to a banquet table on a wheeled cart. Then fruit — apples and pears and things he did not recognize, piled in mounds. Wheels of cheese. A cask of something that smelled alcoholic. Smaller dishes appeared in a widening ring: pastries in stacked rows, cured meats fanned out on wooden boards that had materialized along with the meats, pickled vegetables in ceramic jars, bowls of stew that steamed gently in the cool pine-filtered air.

The food kept coming. Kem stared at it. He had no idea how to make it stop.

A final surge produced a three-tiered cake — elaborately iced, studded with what appeared to be candied fruit — which materialized on a tree stump roughly two meters from the log. The stump was not level. The cake began to slide. Kem lunged for it and caught the bottom tier with both hands. The icing was warm and got on his fingers and his sleeves and the signet ring.

He held the cake and looked at the food. It occupied an area of approximately four meters in every direction, arrayed across the forest floor with the abundance of a banquet prepared for a lord's household — which, in a sense, it had been. The Charm had drawn from his own experience of meals, and the only meals Kem had ever experienced were the ones served in the Vault, where a gathering of thirty was a modest dinner party and the kitchen staff spent levels freely to produce food that matched the family's expectations.

He checked his Ledger.

Two hundred and eighty-eight.

Seven levels. He had spent seven levels on lunch.

He set the cake down on the flattest piece of ground he could find, which was not flat enough. The cake settled at an angle. A line of ants discovered the icing within seconds and began a purposeful advance.

He sat on the log and ate a piece of bread. The bread was excellent — the interior soft, the crust shattering properly, the flavor precisely what he was used to because it had been generated from his palate's only reference point. He ate it and watched a bird land on the roast and begin eating with a directness he found briefly instructive. Then a second bird arrived, and then something larger came out of the trees — a fox, rust-colored and cautious, which approached the roast with its head low and its eyes on Kem and then committed to the meat with the single-mindedness of an animal who had decided to deal with consequences later.

Kem ate bread and watched the fox eat roast and thought about the arithmetic.

Twelve levels gone in the first hour. The Projection Shield in the Conservatory had cost three. The second Shield at the gate, two more. The Sustenance Charm, seven. At this rate — he tried to calculate how long his levels would last and realized the calculation required knowing what daily survival cost, and he did not know what daily survival cost, because he had never survived a day without institutional support. He did not know how to start a fire. He did not know how to find water that was not conjured by ability. He did not know which direction led to anything, or what anything would look like when he arrived, or how far his legs could carry him before they stopped working, which based on the evidence so far was not very far at all.

The fox looked up from the roast, muzzle dark and wet. It regarded Kem for a moment, then returned to eating.

He stood. His calves protested. His blister had soaked through his sock and he could feel the wet fabric sliding against raw skin with each step. He left the banquet where it sat — the bread and the roast and the cheese and the three-tiered cake with its column of ants and its growing audience of birds — and he walked south along the path because the path went south and he did not yet have the experience to know that the direction a path goes is not necessarily the direction you should go.

The pine forest thinned. The trees gave way to scrubby grassland and the shade gave way to full sun and the sun fell on him with a direct, impersonal heat he had not prepared for, because he had not prepared for anything. His neck was already pink. His face felt tight. The air tasted different out here — drier, carrying dust and pollen, nothing like the Vault's managed atmosphere. He could feel the Ledger humming at the edge of his awareness, quieter now by twelve points. It was not a sensation he could have described to anyone who hadn't felt it — a fullness slightly less full, like a room with a few books pulled from the shelf. He barely noticed it.

He walked south. His body did what it could, which was less than he would have liked and more than he had any right to expect from a body that had spent twenty-six years doing nothing. His feet bled. His shoulders ached from holding his arms in the unfamiliar rhythm of walking. The signet ring on his finger caught the sun and flashed, and he looked at it — the Aldraste crest, the closed circle, the symbol of a House built on the principle that the best use of power was no use at all — and he kept walking, because he had started walking, and stopping would require a decision, and the only decision he had made today was to stand up, and everything after that had been momentum.

Behind him the food cooled in the pine forest. The fox ate until it couldn't move and lay on its side near the roast. The ants disassembled the cake tier by tier. The stew thickened in its bowls and the bread went stale and the Vault's gate remained open because no one inside had yet convened a committee to determine whether it should be closed.


# Chapter 3

She found him by the trail he'd left.

Not footprints — those were everywhere, and half the north road carried traffic this time of year. What Vel followed was the residue. Two days north of the settlement where she'd healed the farmer, she picked up the first sign: a scorch mark in a roadside clearing where someone had used a Flame Charm on a pile of dry kindling. The kindling itself was still there, blackened to powder, the heat signature too uniform and too thorough for any natural fire. Someone who didn't know what a campfire was supposed to look like had pointed an ability at it and held on until everything burned.

A mile further, the food. She smelled it before she saw it — the sick-sweet reek of Sustenance Charm output decomposing in late-spring warmth. She stepped off the road and found a trampled patch of meadow where enough food for forty people had been conjured and left. Bread, fruit, some kind of stew in a conjured basin that had half-collapsed as the ability's residual structure faded. Most of it was three days old and turning to gray mush in the sun. Insects had found it. A fox had dragged something meaty into the brush.

Forty portions. One person. She'd heard the stories already — a Hoarded lord who'd walked out of the Vault, House Aldraste's son, apparently sound of mind and unsound of everything else. The settlements between the Vault and the highlands were mentioning it the way they mentioned anything the Hoarded did: with the flat attention of people who knew it didn't concern them. Vel had been walking hard for two days because it concerned her very much.

She kept going. A stream crossing showed the shimmer of a Purification ability used on water that was already clean — she cupped a handful from the same stream and drank it, tasting nothing but cold and the faint mineral edge of snowmelt. Another clearing, this one with the grass pressed flat in a precise rectangle where a Shelter Projection had been maintained overnight. The rectangle was absurdly large for one person. Temperature-regulated, too, from the way the grass had grown differently inside the projection's footprint — an ability designed for a military bivouac, used as a mattress on a warm spring night.

She did the arithmetic as she walked. The Sustenance Charm — that was the big one, eight to ten levels for a full-company output. The Flame Charm, one level. Shelter Projection, two to three depending on duration. Purification, one. The Projection Shield he'd used to leave the Vault — she'd heard that part of the story, the door blown open in the middle of a Conservatory Rite — was three levels minimum, probably more if he'd overdriven it the way he seemed to overdrive everything. Add whatever she couldn't see, the small spends that left no visible trace, and he was somewhere around —

She stopped on the road and pressed the heels of her hands into her eyes.

Around 286. In three days.

Vel had been 286 once. She'd been twenty years old and already three years into a pattern of spending that would define her life, and at 286 she'd felt the first real awareness that the number went in one direction. She'd spent one level that day — a Binding to secure a collapsed beam in a settlement the Hoarded had abandoned to rain damage — and stood afterward feeling the absence like a chip taken from a tooth. Subtle. Permanent. The beginning of an arithmetic that never stopped.

This man had burned fourteen levels on food and fire and clean water and a warm place to sleep.

She found him in the early afternoon, four hundred meters past the last clearing. He was sitting on a fallen log beside the road with his boots off, examining his feet with the pained concentration of someone encountering a problem he genuinely didn't understand. The boots sat beside him — soft-soled, pale leather, the kind designed for the Vault's polished stone corridors. They were scuffed and dirt-stained and one had split along the sole where road gravel had worked through material that had never been meant to touch ground.

Vel stopped at the edge of the tree line and watched him.

He was tall. Young — mid-twenties, the unlined face and full color of someone whose body had never been asked to do anything. Pale skin going pink at the nose and ears from three days of sun he'd never been exposed to. Vault silks, once cream and gray, now snagged by branches and darkened with sweat and ground-in dirt. His hair was dark and needed cutting, or more likely had always been maintained by servants who no longer existed in his life. On his right hand, catching the afternoon light, a heavy signet ring: the House Aldraste crest.

As she watched, he produced a small shimmer of light — a Glow, the most basic illumination ability — and held it near his foot to examine a blister on the ball of his heel. He was sitting in partial shade. The sun, which was directly available two feet to his right, would have done the same job for free.

One level. To look at his own foot.

Vel stepped out of the trees.

He looked up sharply, and she saw several things move across his face at once: the quick contraction of surprise, then wariness, then — under both of those — a relief he didn't fully succeed in hiding. He'd been alone for three days. He didn't know how to be alone.

"Hello," he said. He dismissed the Glow with a flick of his hand that suggested long habit — abilities as casual as breathing. "I don't suppose you might be able to — that is, I've been trying to determine whether this road leads to the Spent Towns, but the route has been somewhat unclear. The crossroads back there had what I believe was a cairn, or possibly just accumulated rocks, and I wasn't entirely certain which branch it was indicating, and now I suspect I may have —"

"It goes to the highlands," Vel said. "Spent Towns are another week south of those."

"A week." He looked at his feet. The blisters were raw and layered — new ones forming on top of old ones, the kind of damage that came from pushing through pain you didn't understand because you'd never experienced it before. "Right. A week. On these."

"On those, no. You won't make three more days."

He nodded slowly, as if she'd confirmed something he'd been trying not to think about. "I'd considered — I do have a Mending ability. For the skin, I mean. But I suspect you're going to tell me that's not the right approach."

"I'm not going to tell you anything yet." She came closer. She was looking at his hands now. Soft. No calluses, no scars, no evidence of grip or impact or labor. The hands of someone who had held ceremonial objects and nothing else for twenty-six years. "How many levels have you spent since you left?"

The question landed differently than she'd expected. He didn't flinch or deflect. He just went still for a moment, that slight inward focus of someone checking their Ledger — and the casualness of it, the way he consulted the number the way she might check the position of the sun, confirmed something about where he'd come from. At 300, the Ledger was background noise. You didn't ration what you'd never had to count.

"I'm at two hundred and eighty-six," he said. Then, with careful precision: "That is rather more than I intended to spend. I'm aware of that."

Fourteen levels. Vel had spent fourteen levels in the last four months, and each one had been budgeted and planned and weighed against the journey ahead.

"Tell me what you spent them on," she said.

"You already know, don't you." He gestured vaguely north. "The trail. I wasn't — it didn't occur to me that the abilities would leave traces. I suppose that's obvious in retrospect."

"Tell me anyway."

He was quiet for a beat. Then he started listing them, and the formality of his speech — the Hoarded habit of complete, qualified, subordinated sentences — made the inventory worse. Each item arrived fully grammatical and absurd.

"There was the Projection Shield at the Rite, which I will not pretend was calibrated appropriately — it was my first ability use and I was not prepared for the output. Then outside the Vault walls, I produced sustenance, though the quantity was — I'm aware that I made food for approximately forty people. I was attempting a single portion. The interface between intention and result is not as intuitive as I'd been led to believe." He paused. "And then it was dark, and the road at night is genuinely quite dark — I had not expected that — so I used a Light ability. And a fire, because the instructions in the books all say to build fires at camp and I couldn't make the wood — I couldn't get it to catch, so I used a Charm. And the water in the stream had things floating in it —"

"Leaves."

"— which I could not identify at the time, so I cleaned it. And the ground is very hard and very cold at night, so there was a shelter of some kind, which I think was oversized because I didn't know how to — I didn't know the parameters." He stopped. Looked at her. "That's not all of it. There were smaller things. A light when I dropped something in the dark. A warming when the morning was cold. I'm not — I don't have a complete inventory because I wasn't tracking them at the time."

He said this last part with something that might have been embarrassment, though the Hoarded register made it hard to tell — it came out sounding like a formal admission of accounting irregularities rather than a man confessing he'd burned levels on things a child could do with a match and a blanket.

Vel sat down on the log beside him. Not because she wanted his company. Because she'd walked twenty miles in two days to find this man and her legs were telling her to sit or they'd stop cooperating.

"The leaves," she said. "In the water."

"Yes?"

"They were leaves."

"I understand that now."

A bird called from the tree line. The afternoon was warm and still, the road empty in both directions, and the faint stink of rotting conjured food drifted from the north on a breeze that didn't care about either of them.

"You know what a level costs," Vel said. It wasn't a question.

"In the abstract. The Hoarded teach the mechanics. Each use is a permanent reduction. No restoration, no recovery. It's — we learn this. We learn it the way you learn that fire is hot. A principle to be respected and avoided."

"Not the abstract. What it actually costs. What it means to go from 286 to 285 and know you're never getting that back."

He considered this with more seriousness than she'd expected. "I don't think I do. Not yet. At 286 the loss still feels — theoretical. There's so much left. It's like spending from a full purse. You know the coins are gone but the weight hasn't changed."

That was honest. It was also exactly what she'd expected, and it made the disgust worse because it wasn't stupidity — it was the specific ignorance of someone who'd never had to count. He understood the principle. He couldn't feel the arithmetic. At 286, you couldn't. At 31, the arithmetic was the loudest thing in any room.

"Why did you leave," she said.

He looked at the road. At his ruined feet. At the signet ring on his hand, which he turned once, unconsciously, before answering.

"My father is level three hundred. He has been three hundred for sixty-one years. He sits in the Conservatory every week with perfect discipline and perfect composure, and he is —" Kem stopped. When he started again, the formal register cracked just slightly, a simpler sentence pushing through the qualifications. "He's dying. Not — his body is fine. His Ledger is untouched. But he's empty. He's been emptying for years and no one will say it because his number is still perfect. The Hoarded call it discipline. It's not discipline. It's — I don't know what it is. But I watched it happen and I couldn't watch anymore."

"Stillness," Vel said.

"You know the word."

"Everyone outside the Vault does. They just don't care, because it only eats the people who chose to do nothing."

He absorbed the sharpness of that without flinching, which surprised her. "That's fair," he said. "I think that's fair."

They sat. The bird called again. A cart passed on the road — a farmer heading north with a load of early vegetables, who glanced at them with the mild disinterest of someone who saw strangers on roads every day and would continue to see them long after these two were gone.

"I want to reach zero," he said. Not grandly, not as a declaration. He said it the way someone might say they wanted to get home before dark — a practical statement about where they were headed. "I don't know what zero is. I don't know if it's — whether it's an end or a beginning or just nothing. But I'd rather be at nothing than at three hundred and whatever he is."

Zero. He said it the way everyone said it who didn't know what it meant. The end of the count. The bottom. He didn't know it was a door, that she'd seen someone step through it and vanish into somewhere real, that she'd been trying to follow for eight years. He just knew he didn't want to be his father.

Vel looked at him — the ruined boots, the signet, the blisters, the sun-pink face of a man who'd been indoors for twenty-six years. Everything wrong with the Hoarded wrapped in expensive silk and sitting on a log unable to identify a leaf. He was waste made flesh — not the waste of spending, but the waste of everything the Hoarded represented. A system that preserved people until they rotted from the inside out, and a boy who'd walked away from it burning levels on campfires.

He was at 286.

The number was a physical thing in her chest. Two hundred and eighty-six. She'd been searching for this — for someone like this — for years. The mechanism required a sustained cascade, a high-output descent from a spender with enough levels to amplify the threshold's energy as they burned down. One person crossing alone was unstable — she'd seen that. She'd seen what unstable looked like, the threshold flickering, the terrifying half-second when it might not hold. She needed volume. She needed someone who could fall from a great height.

Two hundred and eighty-six. Even after the waste. Even after the food and the fire and the clean water and the warm bed and the light to look at his own goddamn feet. He was still at a number that could fuel the kind of descent the crossing required.

She let the satisfaction settle somewhere underneath the disgust, a quiet warmth she didn't examine. He solved a problem she'd been carrying since the day she'd watched Maren disappear.

---

"I'm heading south," Vel said. "To zero. I know the route, I know the territory, and I know what's there when you arrive."

The shift was immediate — he sat straighter, and the Hoarded formality returned like a reflex, the careful speech of someone trained to evaluate propositions. "You know what zero is?"

"I know what it is."

"And you would — what are you proposing, precisely?"

"That we travel together. I guide you south. I teach you how to survive without burning through your Ledger on every problem you can solve with your hands. We both reach zero."

He was quiet for long enough that she thought he might refuse. Then: "What do you need from me?"

"Your levels. I'm at thirty-one. The route south gets harder and the people in it get less friendly. I can't afford to spend on everything the road's going to throw at us. I need someone with levels to handle what I can't."

That was true. It was also not all of it. The mechanism, the cascade, the specific way his high-level descent would stabilize the threshold — that was the part she kept folded and locked behind practical language. He didn't need to know yet. He didn't need to know until they were deep enough that the alternative to continuing was dying on a road, and by then it would be too late to care about the terms.

She was aware of what she was doing. She was aware that it was the kind of calculation the Hoarded made every day — evaluating a person's usefulness and structuring an arrangement to extract it. She didn't like the parallel. She filed it where she filed most things she didn't like and kept talking.

"What happens at zero?" he said. "You said you know. You said something's there."

"I'll tell you when you need to know."

"That's — when will I need to know?"

"When we're closer."

He leaned forward, and the formal hedging thinned into something more direct. "I'm going to be spending my levels on this journey. Every level I spend is permanent. I think I have a right to know what I'm spending them toward."

"You'd be spending them anyway. You just told me you want to reach zero."

"Wanting to reach zero and agreeing to reach it under your direction with no understanding of what zero contains are — those are not the same commitment."

She waited. He waited. They watched each other across a gap that was wider than the two feet of log between them.

"There's something at zero," she said. "It's not death. It's not the Stillness. It's a real place. I've seen it." She held the rest of it closed — the threshold, Maren, the cascade, the years of searching, all of it — and gave him one true thing. "If you reach zero the right way, in the right place, you don't end. You go somewhere."

"Somewhere."

"Somewhere that isn't this." She gestured at the road, the trees, the general condition of a world governed by a number that only went down. "That's what I'll tell you now. The rest comes later."

He processed this the way he processed everything — aloud, working through it in real time, as if thought only became real when it was spoken. "So you're asking me to trust you with the destination, trust you with the pacing, trust you with my levels —"

"I'm asking you to walk south with someone who knows the road when you don't know what a leaf looks like."

"I know what a leaf looks like."

"You purified a stream because of one."

"That's not —" He stopped. Started again. "All right. Fine. That's a valid point."

"Terms," Vel said. "I guide, you follow. I decide when you spend and on what. I decide the route, the pace, and the stops. I tell you what you need to know when you need to know it."

"And my role in this arrangement is —"

"You walk. You learn. You don't use a single ability without asking me first."

"That's. Those aren't terms. Those are orders."

"Yes."

"You understand that I'm at two hundred and eighty-six and you're at thirty-one. I don't mean that as — I'm not threatening anything, but in any standard assessment of bargaining position —"

"You can't start a fire."

He opened his mouth. Closed it.

"You can't identify clean water," Vel said. "You can't walk six miles without destroying your feet. You can't navigate a road with a cairn on it. You've been outside for three days and you've spent fourteen levels on problems that don't require abilities. At your current rate you'll be at zero in two months and you'll be standing on a road in the middle of nowhere with nothing around you and no one to tell you whether you just ran out or arrived somewhere. Do you want that to be how it ends?"

"No."

"Then my terms are the terms. Because my thirty-one can get us both where we need to go, and your two hundred and eighty-six can't get you to the Spent Towns."

He sat with that. She watched him sit with it — the internal negotiation of a man who'd been raised to command now encountering the specific humiliation of needing someone with a number that wouldn't register as a rounding error in the Vault's accounting.

"I'd like to propose a modification," he said.

Vel waited.

"To the information condition. I accept that you have context I don't have, and I accept that there may be things I shouldn't know yet — I'm not naive enough to think that information shared at the wrong time is the same as information shared at the right time. But I'd like your word that you will tell me. Not never. Not when it's convenient for you. When I genuinely need to know, by your judgment, but with the understanding that you'll tell me you're holding something back. I don't need to know what it is. I need to know there's something I don't know."

That was smart. It was the best thing he'd said, and it was exactly the kind of condition she couldn't fully honor — because the mechanism's specifics, the fact that she'd spent years looking for a high-level defector willing to burn down, the fact that he was a solution to a problem rather than a person she'd happened upon — all of that was information that would make any sane man walk away. And she needed him to keep walking.

"I'll tell you what I can, when I can," she said. "If I'm holding something back, I'll tell you there's something I'm not sharing."

"But not what."

"Not what. Not until you're ready for it."

"'Ready' being determined by —"

"Me."

He exhaled. A long, deliberate breath that she recognized — the Hoarded substitute for the profanity they'd trained out of their children. His hands rested on his knees, the signet ring catching light, and she could see him weighing it. The road south, alone, in split boots, burning levels on everything. Or the road south with a woman who wouldn't tell him where they were going but knew how to get there.

"Fine," he said. "Your terms."

"Good."

"I want to — I'd like to say something."

"Say it."

"I don't trust you."

"Good."

"I want to, though. At some point."

Vel looked at him. Twenty-six years old. Three days free. Blistered, sunburned, sitting on a log in the ruins of his only life with a ring he couldn't stop touching and a number that meant nothing to him yet.
