He was everything she'd spent four decades opposing — a Hoarded aristocrat, a monument to inaction, a walking argument for the system that had ground down everyone she'd ever cared about. And he'd looked at his father's empty eyes and walked out of a door that only opened once, because he'd rather be at zero than at three hundred and gone.

She almost liked him for that. She pushed it aside.

"First thing," she said.

"Already?"

"Take off those boots."

He looked down. "They are off."

"I mean get rid of them. Throw them in the ditch."

"These are —" He stopped himself. "Yes. Fine. They're terrible for this. I know. But what do I walk in?"

"There's a trading post six miles south. We'll buy you real boots."

"With what? I didn't bring — I have no money. The Vault doesn't use money internally."

"What's the signet worth?"

His hand went to the ring immediately, covering it. The movement was involuntary — deep, reflexive, the kind of gesture that came from the body's memory of what an object meant rather than from a conscious decision to protect it.

"It's not — that's my House seal. It isn't a trade good."

Vel looked at him.

"You're serious," he said.

"You've got two split-soled shoes and bleeding feet. That ring could buy you boots, socks, a pack, and enough dried food for a week."

"I —" He looked at the ring. Turned it on his finger. "I took this from the gate stand when I left. I didn't even think about it. I just — it was there, and I was leaving, and I took it. I'm not sure what that means."

"It means you're still deciding whether you left."

He stared at her. She'd hit something — she could see it land, a specific impact behind his eyes.

"I left," he said. Quieter. The Hoarded hedging stripped back to something flat. "I left."

"Then trade the ring."

A long moment. The bird had stopped calling. The afternoon was tilting toward evening, the light going amber through the trees, and the road stretched south between them and whatever came next.

"I'll keep it," he said. "Not because — it's not attachment. Or it is, but I'm not ready to — I'm not going to pretend I'm finished with it yet." He reached into the neck of his shirt and pulled out a flat piece of metalwork — a clasp, heavy, ornamental, the kind used to fasten formal Vault robes. "This. This is just a fastener. Would it work?"

"Probably."

"And I have a few other — formal pieces, from the Rite. Things I was wearing when I left. They're in my bag." He gestured at a small satchel on the ground — Vault-made, embroidered, absurd for road travel. "Would those buy boots?"

"They'd buy boots and leave change."

"Then that." He looked almost relieved. "Boots. From a trading post. With money I earned by — well. By having been born into the right family and wearing expensive things to a ceremony." The corner of his mouth moved. "I suppose that's a kind of spending too."

Vel stood up. Her back was stiff and her calves were humming from two hard days, and the man on the log was looking at her the way people looked at the first fixed point they found in a storm — not with trust, not yet, but with the specific hope that direction might exist.

"Six miles," she said. "Barefoot, on this road, that's about three hours. Your feet are going to hurt."

"They already hurt."

"They're going to hurt more."

He stood. She watched him put weight on the blisters and saw the wince he tried to control — not performing toughness, just the automatic reflex of someone who didn't want to seem weak in front of a person whose opinion had suddenly started to matter.

"Vel?"

"What."

"Thank you. For offering this. I understand it's not — I realize the arrangement is not ideal for either of us, and I don't want you to think I'm not aware of —"

"Walk."

He picked up his satchel, left the ruined boots on the log, and stepped onto the gravel road. The sharp stones pressed into his blisters immediately — she could see it in the way he shifted his weight, looking for a stride that distributed the pain.

"Don't spend anything," she said.

"Right. No. I know."

They walked.

Vel matched his pace, which was slow and uneven — he favored his right foot where the blisters were worst, and every few steps he made a small involuntary sound, a half-breath that wasn't quite a gasp. He didn't complain. She noted that. Didn't file it yet as trust or character — just observed it.

The road went south through mixed forest, the trees thick with new spring growth, the air warm and smelling of mud and green things coming alive. He walked beside her with the signet ring on his hand and gravel under his bleeding feet, and neither of them spoke for a long time.

The arithmetic ran in Vel's head the way it always ran, the way it had run for forty years — constant, habitual, the background hum of a life lived by subtraction. Him at 286. Her at 31. The route south: highlands first, then the Spent Towns, then the harder country beyond. She'd need to budget his spending — ration it, control it, keep him from draining himself on trivial comforts. She'd need to teach him to live in his body instead of his Ledger. She'd need to get him south with enough levels to matter when they arrived.

And she'd need to not tell him why.

The ugliness of that sat beside the satisfaction, and she held both. He solved a problem she'd been carrying for eight years — the gap in her plan, the variable she couldn't fill alone. A high-level spender willing to burn down. She'd asked about defectors in six settlements. She'd chased three rumors that came to nothing. And here he was, real and inconvenient, walking barefoot because she'd told him to and bleeding on a road he couldn't navigate.

He was going to be furious when he found out. She knew that the way she knew her own level — as a number that sat at the edge of consciousness, constant and unavoidable.

But that was later. Right now he was walking south, and his feet were bleeding, and his Ledger read 286, and it was enough. It had to be enough.

"Vel?"

"What."

"Is it always this far between places?"

"Yes."

"Oh."

He kept walking. She kept walking. The road went south and the sun went west and neither of them looked back.


# Chapter 4

The ridge trail had been climbing since dawn, and Kem's calves were filing complaints he had no language for.

In the Vault, the longest walk was sixty meters — Conservatory to the east dining hall, always on flat stone, always in soft-soled shoes designed to make no sound. Nobody ran in the Vault. Nobody climbed. The body was a thing you maintained through inaction, like a vase, and it performed accordingly. Now his body was being asked to carry its own weight uphill through tangled forest for six consecutive hours, and it was responding with specific muscular opinions he'd never known it had.

Vel was thirty paces ahead, moving through the undergrowth with the kind of ease that came from having done this since before he was born. She didn't look back. She hadn't looked back since they'd broken camp that morning, and Kem had stopped deciding whether this was trust or indifference because both options were equally dispiriting.

The highland forest closed overhead. The trees were old — Kem didn't know what kind, couldn't tell one from another, an omission in his education he hadn't known existed until two days ago. He'd asked Vel what a particular tree was called and she'd looked at him as if he'd asked her what walking was. So he'd stopped asking about trees. The light filtered through the canopy in shifting columns, warm where it landed, cool where it didn't. The air carried the smell of earth, of something resinous and sharp, of leaves that had fallen last year and were slowly becoming soil. His pack straps had worn through the linen of his shirt and were working on the skin beneath, which would blister by afternoon. He knew this because it had blistered yesterday.

His Ledger read 286, a number he was aware of the way he'd once been aware of the Vault's walls — always there, always at the edge of thought, a quiet hum of remaining capacity. In the Vault that hum had been a deep, steady presence at 300, the fullness of a reservoir never tapped. Now it was slightly thinner. The fourteen levels he'd burned through since leaving — the Projection Shield, the Sustenance Charm, the absurd incidental wastes that Vel catalogued with the precision of someone counting coins she couldn't afford to lose — had changed the hum's quality in a way he was only beginning to understand. Not emptier. Just different. A glass poured from, for the first time.

Vel stopped.

She said nothing. Just stopped, one hand against a tree trunk, her head angled in a way that meant she was listening with forty years of forest behind her ears. Kem stopped too. The pack settled. His calves expressed themselves.

He listened. Wind in the upper canopy. The click-rustle of small things in the undergrowth. A bird somewhere east, making a stuttering call that might have been a warning or a mating display — he had no way to distinguish these. He heard nothing unusual, which he'd learned was often the problem.

"Company," Vel said, still facing the ridge behind them. "Multiple. Coming down our backtrail."

"How far?"

"Close enough that the birds quit about three minutes ago and haven't come back. And there's a spot about two hundred meters behind us where the mud shows boot treads that aren't ours." She was already moving off the trail, angling down the slope toward denser cover. "Stay behind me. Step where I step."

Kem followed. Quiet was a thing he'd been trained for, in a different context — the Vault's code of controlled movement, the ceremonial discipline of footfall that demonstrated conservation of energy. But the Vault's floors were marble and stone. This ground was made of things that cracked and shifted and gave way, and his boots were stiff-soled, heavy, purchased from a trader near the Vault gates who'd seen Kem's clothes and charged accordingly.

They descended through a drainage gully where the ground was slick with accumulated damp. Vel navigated it without breaking stride. Kem navigated it by sitting down involuntarily and sliding three meters on his back, collecting a streak of mud and a gash across his right palm from a sharp stone. He bit the inside of his cheek and kept moving.

Vel led them along a shelf of exposed rock — no tracks on stone — then through a stand of young trees growing so close together that his pack caught on every third trunk. Twenty minutes of careful movement through terrain that was actively trying to undress him.

Then Vel stopped again. Her mouth compressed into a line he was learning to read as arithmetic she didn't like.

"They've adjusted bearing," she said. Quietly, but not whispering — Vel didn't whisper. "They have a tracker. Someone spent a level on a Perception ability."

"They spent a level to find me?"

"That's the least interesting thing about what I just said." She looked at him. "They spent a level. The Hoarded authorized an enforcement agent to spend. That means Council directive. That means this isn't your family sending someone to talk sense."

Kem felt something settle in his stomach — not cold exactly, more like weight, the specific gravity of understanding that people had been dispatched. That somewhere in the Vault, in a room he could picture precisely because he'd spent years passing its doors, an authorization had been signed and sealed and handed to someone whose job it now was to bring him back.

Vel assessed the terrain. Looked uphill, downhill, checked sight lines.

"We can't outrun a Perception tracker in forest this thick," she said. "Not at the pace you walk."

"I could use a Speed—"

"No."

"You don't even know what I was going to—"

"You were going to suggest a Speed enhancement or a Concealment overlay, and either one would cost you eight to twelve levels because you don't know how to calibrate, and I'm not losing a week's budget because you had an idea." She was scanning the slope above them. "We go to ground. See what they bring."

She found a position — a natural depression behind a deadfall, the massive trunk of a tree that had come down seasons ago and was slowly returning to the soil. Good sight lines upslope. An escape route downhill through a ravine. Tactically sound, Kem supposed, though his understanding of tactics was entirely theoretical, sourced from books he'd found abstractly interesting in the Vault's library.

They waited. Three minutes. Five. The forest sounds around them were ordinary, but behind and above, the bird calls hadn't resumed.

Then they came through the trees.

Seven figures in the gray livery of Hoarded enforcement — not soldiers, because the Hoarded didn't have soldiers, which was central to the philosophy's elaborate fiction. These were enforcement agents: young men and women from minor houses, each allocated five levels of combat training, authorized to operate outside the Vault under specific Council directives. They moved in a loose formation, spaced apart, covering approaches. Their posture held the rigid bearing of Hoarded discipline, but their eyes were moving too fast, scanning the unfamiliar forest with the jittery attention of people who had trained for this scenario in a courtyard.

The lead agent was a woman about Kem's age, dark-haired, a minor-house crest stitched on her collar. She stopped twenty meters away and held up one hand, palm forward — the formal signal of Hoarded authority that Kem had seen a thousand times in the Vault's administrative corridors.

"Kem Aldraste." Her voice carried the precise, clipped diction of someone who had rehearsed this. "By directive of the Hoarded Council, under recovery authority granted to the enforcement branch, you are ordered to submit to escort and return to the Vault for—"

"Move," Vel said.

Kem moved.

***

Vel went right, low, fast. Not the explosive speed of an ability — just the efficient movement of someone whose body had been doing this work for four decades. She covered the distance to the nearest agent in the time it took him to register that the woman behind the deadfall was no longer there.

Her first ability looked small. That was the thing Kem would remember afterward — how small it looked. A precise application of force, no wider than two fingers, directed at the soft tissue below the agent's ear. The man's knees gave and he sat down hard in the ferns, his expression shifting from alertness to confusion to nothing. He folded sideways and lay still.

One level. One agent.

The second outrider — a broad-shouldered woman positioned to the first's left — was already activating her shield. It bloomed in front of her, a disc of translucent force roughly a meter across. Textbook deployment, correct technique, exactly as she'd trained. Vel stepped to her unshielded right and pressed two fingers to the woman's neck with a touch so light Kem almost missed it. Whatever the ability did, it was fast — the agent's face went slack, her knees buckled, and her shield dissolved into the air as her concentration went with her balance.

Two levels. Two agents.

The lead agent — the one who'd been delivering the formal retrieval speech — fired her Combat Pulse. It was the standard enforcement variant: a concussive burst aimed to stun and disorient, well-directed, technically sound. It hit the space Vel had occupied a half-second earlier. Vel had read the woman's shoulders before she'd committed to a direction and was already elsewhere — two steps left, pivot, close. She touched the agent's sternum with the heel of her hand, and the woman's voluntary motor control shut down from the ribs out. She collapsed in stages — shoulders, hips, knees — wearing the bewildered expression of someone whose body had just stopped taking orders.

Three levels. Three agents. The whole thing had taken maybe twenty seconds.

Vel straightened. She wasn't breathing hard. She looked at the four remaining outriders, who were standing in a semicircle in the highland forest with the specific look of people for whom the distance between training and reality had just become measurable.

"Walk away," she said.

One of them did. A young man — barely twenty, his enforcement livery still creased from storage — turned and pushed back through the undergrowth without a word. His footsteps crashed through the forest for a long time. Kem watched him go and did not blame him.

Three remained. Something passed between them — not courage exactly, but the stubbornness of people who'd been given five levels and told those five levels made them agents of the most powerful institution in the world. The man nearest Kem dropped into a combat stance and raised his hands, energy already gathering around his fingers. The woman beside him was pulling power for another Pulse. The third — a lean, quiet figure who'd been hanging back, the tracker — shifted position to cover the flank.

Kem was still standing behind the deadfall.

He was standing there watching Vel dismantle three trained enforcement agents the way she did everything — without wasted motion, without excess force, without theatre. He was standing there with his pack still on and his hand still bleeding where the rock had cut it and his heart going so hard he could feel it in his wrists, in his palms, in the pulse of blood against the gash on his right hand. His fingers were tingling. The Ledger was buzzing at the edge of his awareness — not the steady hum he'd carried for twenty-six years but something awake, something that wanted out, the way a held breath pushes against the teeth.

The man fired his Combat Pulse.

It was aimed at Kem — the target, the retrieval objective, the person the Council had authorized spending to bring home. The pulse crossed the distance between them as a fist-sized knot of concussive force, and Kem watched it come with the peculiar clarity of someone experiencing danger for the first time, each second presenting itself for examination.

He didn't decide to use the Combat Pulse. That was not how it happened. His body decided — the same way a body jerks a hand from heat, before thought, before intent. The ability rose through him like a wall of water breaking: up from whatever reservoir the Ledger measured, through his gut, expanding into his chest, flooding his arms, and out through his open hands toward the agent who'd fired.

He'd been trying to match what Vel had done. A small, targeted burst. A precise application.

What came out of his hands was not that.

The concussive wave expanded as it left him — not a thread of force but a wall, a hemisphere of compressed air and raw energy that blew outward in every direction. The outrider's incoming pulse was swallowed by it like a candle flame in a gale. The agent himself was caught by the leading edge and hurled backward off his feet. He traveled six meters through the air and hit a tree trunk with a sound that was part crack, part wet thud, and slid down to the ground trailing bark and a smear of something Kem didn't want to look at.

The woman beside him was caught by the lateral expansion and thrown sideways across the forest floor, rolling through ferns and deadfall. The tracker stumbled back but kept his footing, shielded by a boulder.

And the hillside behind them — the slope they'd been climbing since dawn — came apart. The wave hit the earth at an angle and the ground detonated. Soil, rock, root systems — a section of slope twenty meters across sheared loose and slid downhill in a roaring cascade of debris. Trees that had been growing there for decades tilted, groaned, and fell in a series of impacts that shook the ground hard enough for Kem to feel it through the soles of his expensive, impractical boots. Dust billowed. Stones bounced and cracked against other stones. A tree landed across the trail they'd walked that morning and splintered down its length.

The noise was enormous. It filled the forest, echoed off the opposite ridge, and came back diminished, as if the landscape were exhaling.

Then quiet. The particular quiet of a forest that has just been hit.

Kem looked at his hands. They were shaking. Not from fear — from something that was not fear. The ability had moved through him with a force nothing like the Projection Shield or the Sustenance Charm. Those had been mistakes of calibration, embarrassing excess. This had moved through his body like a river through a split dam, and the physical aftermath was still running: arms buzzing, chest tight, a warm electric prickling in his fingers that wanted more.

His Ledger had dropped. He felt the shift like a step missed on a staircase — a lurch in the background hum, a number that had been one thing and was now another. He checked.

Two hundred and seventy-one.

Fifteen levels. One Combat Pulse. He'd been trying to spend one.

The woman he'd knocked sideways was getting up. She was bleeding from her hairline and her left arm hung at an angle that meant something structural had failed inside it, but she was getting up, which meant five levels of enforcement training included something about continuing. The tracker was emerging from behind his boulder, hands raised, energy gathering.

Two of the three agents Kem was supposed to handle were still functional. And his allies in this fight consisted of one fifty-eight-year-old woman at level 28 who was currently looking at the destroyed hillside with an expression he couldn't read.

He reached for Speed.

The world blurred. The colors of the forest smeared sideways, the trees stretched into vertical lines, the ground turned into a single flowing surface beneath feet that were suddenly moving faster than any feet had business moving. He'd wanted to close the distance to the injured woman before she could fire. He'd wanted to be quick.

He was not quick. He was a projectile.

He crossed twenty meters of forest floor in less than a second, the air concussing around his body, the leaves he passed through stripped from their branches by windshear. He saw the woman's eyes widen — a slow-motion expansion in his accelerated perception, her mouth opening, the beginning of a shout he heard as a bass moan — and then the tree was there.

It was a thick-trunked highland tree, solidly rooted, patiently doing its work in the exact spot where Kem's trajectory met the physical world. He hit it chest-first at a speed that drove every molecule of air from his lungs. His ribs compressed against the trunk. The tree cracked at the point of impact and a slab of bark the size of a table sheared off and fell on his head.

He bounced. Literally bounced — his speed-enhanced body carried enough momentum that the impact redirected rather than stopped him. He spun sideways, hit the ground with his left shoulder, slid through a meter of loam and leaf litter, and came to rest against a boulder with his face in the dirt and a ringing in his ears that sounded like someone had struck a bell inside his skull.

Eight levels. The Speed enhancement had cost him eight levels and delivered him face-first into the oldest tree in the immediate vicinity.

He lay there for two seconds. The dirt tasted like iron and rot. His left ribs were reporting something he was going to have to deal with later. Above him, the canopy was a kaleidoscope of green and light, spinning gently.

He got up. The ground was unsteady, or he was. His Ledger hummed at 263 and the number felt strange — not low, not by any measure, but changed. Different from what it had been five minutes ago. His body had done things in the last five minutes that it had never done in twenty-six years.

The tracker was moving toward him, careful, smart, staying behind cover. The injured woman had managed to raise her good hand and was pulling energy for something — a Pulse, a Binding, whatever she had left in her five trained techniques. Two opponents, both functional, and Kem was on his feet with dirt in his eyes and a cracked rib and the residual momentum of the Speed ability still jangling through his nervous system.

He tried a shield.

He knew what a shield was supposed to look like — he'd deployed the Projection Shield in the Vault, a mistake meant for a doorframe that had spanned a corridor. He was trying for something small. Personal. A defensive screen, maybe two meters across, enough to block whatever was coming.

What he produced was a dome.

It erupted from the ground centered on his body and expanded outward at speed — a hemisphere of shimmering translucent force that grew and grew and did not stop growing. Five meters. Ten. Fifteen. It caught the injured woman and the tracker and shoved them backward like dry leaves before a broom, tumbling them across the forest floor. It caught Vel — she said "Shit" and threw herself behind a boulder as the dome's leading edge swept past, dragging a broken branch across her forearm. It caught the undergrowth and the smaller trees bent under it. It caught the air itself, and the displaced volume produced a pressure wave that stirred the canopy forty meters overhead.

Ten levels. A shield that could have covered a village square.

The dome held for three seconds and collapsed, because Kem didn't know how to sustain it and the energy dissipated as fast as it had bloomed. In its wake, the forest was rearranged. The two agents were on the ground again, farther away than before. The tracker was face-down in a thicket, not moving. The injured woman was caught in a tangle of fallen branches, struggling, bleeding from fresh cuts, her broken arm pinned beneath her at an angle that made Kem's stomach turn.

His Ledger read 253. Thirty-three levels gone in three attempts.

His chest was heaving. The cracked rib sang every time he breathed in. His hands were still tingling and the tingling was not fading — it was growing, as if each use of power primed the body for more, as if the reservoir wanted to empty itself, as if spending were an appetite that fed on its own satisfaction.

The woman was pulling herself free of the branches. Her face was set, grim, the face of someone operating on five levels of training and whatever conviction had gotten her out of the Vault that morning. She got one knee under her. Then the other. She raised her good hand and Kem saw the energy gather around her fingers, dim and flickering because she was hurt and exhausted but still present, still dangerous.

He didn't try for precision this time. He didn't try to match Vel's compact, surgical technique. He reached for the thing his body was already reaching for — whatever the Ledger offered first, whatever was closest to the surface — and shoved it forward.

A blast of force and light left his right hand. Not a Combat Pulse — something cruder, less defined, shaped only by the desperate intention behind it. It hit the ground three meters in front of the woman and the earth erupted. Dirt, stone, a root system that came up like a hand opening. The concussion threw her clear of the branches and deposited her fifteen meters away in a heap. She did not get up.

The tracker, who had been stirring in his thicket, lifted his head just enough to see the crater and the woman in the heap and the man standing at the center of the destruction. He put his hands flat on the earth in front of him. It might have been surrender. It might have been the only position his body could hold.

Eight more levels. Kem's Ledger read 245.

Forty-one levels. The fight had lasted maybe four minutes.

The forest around them looked like something had come through it sideways. The hillside was a scar of raw earth and displaced stone. A crater marked where his last ability had struck. Trees were snapped, leaning, or simply gone. The undergrowth was flattened in concentric rings from the dome's expansion. Dust hung in the filtered afternoon light, turning the air opaque and golden.

Vel emerged from behind her boulder. She had a scratch along her forearm where the dome's edge had whipped the branch past her. She looked at the destroyed clearing. She looked at the collapsed hillside. She looked at Kem.

Her face was the flattest he had ever seen it.

He was grinning.

***

The grin was not something he could help. He stood in the wreckage of the highland forest with dirt in his hair and a bruised rib and a Ledger that had dropped forty-one levels in four minutes, and his face was doing something it had never done before — not the polite arrangement of features the Vault demanded, but an involuntary physical response to the most intense experience of his life.

His body had worked.

That was the thing underneath everything else — the waste, the destruction, the danger. His body, the soft, untested, decorative body that had spent its entire existence walking between rooms and sitting in ceremonial stillness, had done things. Real things. His hands had shaped force and thrown it. His legs had moved at speed. His chest had taken an impact and kept operating. He was bruised and scraped and filthy and his rib hurt when he breathed and there was blood in his mouth from where he'd bitten his tongue hitting the tree, and he was more physically present in this moment than he had been in all twenty-six years before it. The Vault had preserved him in amber. This forest had cracked it open.

Vel was walking among the downed agents, checking each one with efficient, impersonal attention — pulse, breathing, level of consciousness. She did this the way she did everything: quickly, with minimal wasted motion, as if even attention were a resource she rationed.

"They'll live," she said, straightening from the tracker. "Bruised, concussed. Her arm's broken. He's got cracked ribs." A pause. "Same side as yours."

"Good," Kem said. "I mean — not good. I didn't — I wasn't trying to—"

"Which part?" Vel's voice was level in the way that a still lake is level — pressure beneath the surface, invisible, total. "The part where you leveled thirty meters of hillside with a pulse meant to stun? The part where you ran into a tree? Or the part where you threw a shield that nearly put me through a rock?"

"I didn't — the shield was supposed to be small. I was trying—"

"I know what you were trying." She looked at him. The afternoon light came through the disrupted canopy and lit the dust around her, and in that light she looked like what she was: a fifty-eight-year-old woman who had been doing this for four decades, who had spent forty years learning to do the most with the least, watching him do the least with the most. "I spent three levels. One per agent. Targeted, calibrated, controlled. Total cost: three. Three agents out, no collateral damage, no environmental destruction, no broken bones on my own side." She paused. "You spent forty-one."

He'd known the number. He'd felt each drop, each lurch in the Ledger's hum, the background frequency of his life shifting and shifting again. But hearing her say it was different.

"Forty-one levels on three agents — and that's generous, because the tracker just stopped because you cratered the ground near him. Forty-one levels, a collapsed hillside, a hundred meters of wrecked forest, and a dome that nearly killed your only ally." Vel's voice was quiet. She didn't get louder when things were bad; she got quieter. The quieter she got, the worse the arithmetic. "You walked into this at 286. You're at 245. That's more than most people in the Spent Towns spend in a year."

The grin faded. Not entirely — his body still hummed with residual charge, still felt electric and awake in ways the Vault had never hinted were possible — but the edges of it softened.

"I know."

"You don't." She squatted beside the lead agent — the woman who'd been delivering the retrieval speech — and unfastened a document case from the woman's belt. Opened it. Inside were papers bearing the Hoarded Council's formal seal: heavy stock, precise script, the language of institutional authority that Kem had grown up hearing and had never, until this moment, been aimed at him.

"Recovery directive," Vel said, scanning the pages. "Council-authorized. Your mother's name is on it. Lady Aldraste, requesting enforcement retrieval." She turned a page. "Standard terms. Six enforcement agents allocated. Five training levels per agent. Thirty levels of authorized spending — just on the retrieval team. That doesn't count the tracker's Perception spend."

Vel folded the documents and tucked them into her own pack. Not for keepsake — for information. Kem was beginning to understand that Vel kept everything that might be useful and discarded everything that wasn't, and that this principle applied to objects, conversations, and people.

"This is institutional," she said. "The Council weighed your departure against the philosophy of inaction and decided that bringing you back was worth the violation. They authorized spending to retrieve you." She looked at him. "That doesn't happen. The whole system runs on the principle that spending is never worth it. They've just admitted it sometimes is."

Kem heard this and understood. Not abstractly — he understood it in the Vault's specific language of cost and precedent, the way he'd been trained to think since childhood. The Council had spent political capital to authorize physical capital to retrieve one defected lord. Each level those agents had been permitted to spend was a crack in the philosophy that held the Hoarded world together. They'd sent seven people into the forest to bring back one, and the math of that decision — the public cost of admitting that action was sometimes necessary — told Kem more about his departure's impact than any message from home could have.

"They'll send more," Vel said. She shouldered her pack. "Now that these seven have failed, they'll send better. More training, more authorization, more spending. The cost of leaving you out here goes up every day you keep burning."

Kem looked at the wreckage. The collapsed hillside. The shattered trees. The craters. The felled trunk that had been a living tree that morning and was now a splintered ruin because he'd pushed force into the world without knowing how much was too much. He looked at his hands. They were dirty, scraped, trembling faintly with residual energy, and they had twenty minutes ago done things he still could not fully comprehend.

"I should have — I couldn't control it," he said. His voice caught on the Hoarded formality that wanted to frame this as a measured self-assessment, and he let it go. "The pulse was supposed to be small. I was trying to do what you did."

"I've been doing this since I was seventeen."

"I know that."

"Then know this too. What you just did — forty-one levels of uncontrolled output — that's not recklessness. It's not stupidity. It's ignorance, and there's a difference, but the difference doesn't help us. You've never been taught how much force a situation requires, because the Vault taught you that no force was ever required. So when you reach for it, you get everything. No filter. No calibration. No muscle memory." She started walking. "And I can't teach you calibration in a week. Which means you're going to do this again, and every time you do, we lose levels I've budgeted for things that'll actually keep us alive."

She was already moving south, through the destroyed clearing and past the last of the downed agents, heading downslope toward denser cover where the pursuit would be slower and the terrain would work for them instead of against them. Kem followed. He was sore in ways he hadn't known he could be sore — the impact with the tree was asserting itself across his entire left side, a deep hot bruise that would darken overnight. His tongue throbbed where he'd bitten it. The pack was heavy on shoulders already raw from the straps.

But his body was alive.

He could not explain this and didn't try. The pain, the exhaustion, the cost — forty-one levels, forty-one irreversible, permanent subtractions from the total of what he was — all of it sat alongside something he had no framework for. His muscles had engaged with the world. His cardiovascular system had performed under stress. His hands had shaped force and sent it somewhere. For twenty-six years he'd occupied a body the way one occupies a waiting room — patiently, without investment, until the real thing happens elsewhere. And now, with dirt in his teeth and blood on his tongue and a cracked rib reporting with every breath, he understood that the body was not a waiting room. It was a thing you used, and using it — even badly, even wastefully, even with a Combat Pulse that cost fifteen levels instead of one — produced something the Vault had never offered: the specific, irreducible knowledge of being physically real.

He was terrified of what he'd done. The woman with the broken arm, the man who'd hit the tree, the tracker face-down in the thicket. He had done that. His hands, his power, his ignorance. People were hurt behind him who would not have been hurt if he'd been capable of precision, or of restraint, or of anything other than raw detonation.

And underneath the terror, in some part of himself that the Vault's philosophy had never reached, something was singing. Quietly. Without permission. A bodily joy he did not know how to refuse.

Vel was thirty paces ahead and not looking back. The destroyed forest fell behind them. Dust was settling on the collapsed hillside. Somewhere in the wreckage, seven enforcement agents were in various states of consciousness in a landscape that no longer resembled the one they'd tracked their quarry through, and somewhere to the north, the Hoarded Council was waiting for a report that would not arrive, and the absence of that report would tell them more than its contents ever could.

The pursuit was real. The Hoarded had sent people to bring him home, and those people had found him, and now they were lying in a demolished forest watching a collapsed hillside settle. The next group would be better. More trained, more authorized, more willing to spend. The cost of Kem's freedom was rising, measured in the levels the Council was willing to burn to deny him his.

He shifted the pack on his raw shoulders and kept walking south. His rib ached. The gash on his palm had crusted and pulled every time he flexed his hand. The Ledger hummed at 245 — a number that a week ago would have been meaningless to him and now sat in his awareness like a stone in a shoe, present with every step. He'd lost forty-one levels in four minutes of uncontrolled combat, and the woman walking ahead of him had spent three to accomplish the same thing, and the disparity between those numbers contained a lesson he could feel without yet being able to articulate.

But his hands still tingled. And the grin kept trying to come back, pushing up from somewhere below his ribs, and he had to keep telling his face to stop, and his face was not listening.


# Chapter 5

Two days since the outriders and Vel hadn't spent a point. She tracked this the way she tracked everything — as arithmetic, not virtue. Twenty-eight on the Ledger, the number sitting at the back of her awareness like a tooth she kept tonguing. Twenty-eight could get them to the Spent Towns if nothing went wrong. If Kem stopped hemorrhaging levels on problems a lit candle could solve. If the Hoarded were slow to send the next wave.

A lot of ifs. She was used to ifs.

Behind her, Kem Aldraste, level two hundred and forty-something, was losing a fight with the trail.

The forested highlands rolled south in long ridgelines under a canopy of beech and hornbeam, late-spring light coming through in shafts that shifted with the wind. Good cover. Hard walking — the ground climbed and fell in short steep pitches, roots lacing the path, the soil still soft from rain two nights ago. Vel moved through it without thinking, her pack riding high against her shoulders in grooves worn over decades. Kem moved through it like a man who had been personally insulted by the concept of elevation.

"How much further today?" he asked. He'd been asking this every two hours.

"However far we get."

"Right." A pause. "That's — yeah. Right."

She could hear his breathing. Heavy, effortful, with the quality of someone who'd never sustained exertion for more than an hour at a stretch. His boots — good leather, Vault-made, stitched for ceremonial corridors — were doing things to his feet that she could track by the way his stride had shortened since morning. His hands were worse. She'd been watching them for two days. The blisters had come in on his palms first, where the pack straps rubbed, and then his right thumb from the knife she'd lent him to cut their path through a deadfall section yesterday. He'd gripped the knife too tight, the way people hold things when they're afraid of dropping them. She hadn't corrected him. The calluses would teach him faster than she could.

A root caught his boot. He stumbled, grabbed a low branch that bent under his weight and dumped bark dust into his hair, and stood there for a moment looking at the branch like he was going to apologize to it.

"The roots," he said. "Are they always this — "

"Yes."

"Fine."

They walked. The canopy thickened as they dropped into a shallow valley where a stream cut through exposed rock, and the air went cool and green and damp. Late afternoon. Two more hours of useful light. She checked the terrain, the water source, the sightlines through the trees, and stopped.

"Here." She shrugged her pack off. "We're making camp."

Kem shrugged his own pack off and the relief that passed across his face was so naked she almost looked away. He stood straighter. Rolled his shoulders. Winced at something in his lower back that he didn't mention.

"Sit," she said. Then: "No. Don't sit. You'll stiffen."

"I'm already — "

"You'll stiffen worse. Stay moving. We've got work."

She watched him absorb this — the visible negotiation between his body's desire to collapse and his recognition that she hadn't been wrong yet about anything physical. He stayed on his feet.

"What work?"

"Fire. Food. Shelter." She unrolled the fire kit from the top of her pack — a waxed-cloth wrap that held a steel striker, a knapped flint, and a bundle of char cloth she'd made three camps ago. "You're doing all of it."

He looked at the fire kit like it was written in a language he could almost read.

"I've seen fire," he said.

"You've seen Flame Charms."

"I've seen fire. In fireplaces. In the Vault."

"Someone else's fire."

"Yes." He picked up the striker, turned it in his hand. Soft fingers against hard steel. "So I — strike this against the stone?"

Vel gathered tinder without explaining, because he needed to see the result before the process would make sense. A loose nest of dried grass, birch bark she peeled from a fallen log, the smallest twigs she could find. She set the nest on a flat rock near the stream and crouched beside it.

"Char cloth on the flint." She showed him. "Strike down. Spark catches the cloth. Cloth goes into the tinder. You blow — small breaths. Don't blow it out."

"That seems straightforward."

"It's not."

He took the flint and the striker and tried. The first strike produced nothing — wrong angle, not enough force. He adjusted, struck again, and the steel skidded off the flint and caught the meat of his thumb. Blood welled up in a thin line across the blister there, bright against the pale, soft skin that had never gripped anything harder than a pen, and he hissed through his teeth.

"Shit. Okay."

He looked at his thumb. Looked at the striker. She could see him calculating — not the Ledger calculation, the simpler one. He could light this fire with a Flame Charm. One level. Probably less than one, at his current capacity. The temptation was sitting right there in his body, in the hum of two hundred and forty-odd levels of unused potential, and she watched him not reach for it.

He struck again. A spark — small, bright, gone before it touched the char cloth.

"Closer," she said.

He struck. Missed. Struck. The char cloth caught: a tiny orange glow at the edge, alive and improbable. He stared at it.

"Now the tinder. Careful."

He placed the glowing cloth into the nest with both hands, his fingers trembling slightly from the effort of gentleness after the violence of striking. He blew. Too hard. The ember flared orange and died.

He sat back on his heels.

"It was right there."

"It was."

"I had it."

"You did."

"That was — I can feel that that's supposed to work. I can feel the principle of it."

"The principle's fine. Your lungs need practice."

He looked at her. Not the look of an aristocrat expecting service. Not the look that said *fix this* or *I shouldn't have to do this*. She'd been waiting for that look for days. It hadn't come. She was starting to think it wasn't coming.

"Slow breath," she said. "Like you're warming your hands. Not like you're blowing out a candle."

He built a second tinder nest — worse than hers, loose in places, too tight in others, but he built it. Struck again. Spark. Catch. He blew, slow this time, and the char cloth smoked and the tinder smoked and a thread of flame climbed from the center of the nest and took hold of the birch bark, and Kem made a sound she hadn't heard from him before. A quiet, involuntary "oh" that had nothing to do with the Ledger and everything to do with what his hands had just done.

She fed the flame kindling while he watched, building it from thread to finger-width to wrist-width until the fire stood on its own. Then she sat back and pointed at the cookpot.

"Water first. Then there's a grouse in my pack, wrapped in dock leaves. You're cleaning it."

The look he gave her was roughly the look he'd given the striker, but worse.

---

He held the grouse by its feet and it hung there, gray-feathered, already cold, its head at the angle of a neck Vel had wrung that morning while he was still asleep. She'd kept it in the pack wrapped in leaves, waiting for this.

"I need to — what's first?"

"Pluck it."

He plucked it. This took a long time. The feathers stuck to the skin and came out in clumps and single barbs alternately, and he pulled too gently, then too hard, tearing skin along the breast so the pink flesh showed through in ragged strips. His hands, already blistered from the striker, were soon coated in a paste of blood and down that stuck to his raw palms and wouldn't shake free. He wiped them on his trousers. The down transferred to the fabric. He wiped harder and made it worse.

She said nothing. He kept plucking.

"Now gut it."

"I don't know where the — which part do I — "

She talked him through it. Belly cut, short and shallow, don't puncture the intestines. He punctured the intestines. The smell hit him and he turned his head and his jaw worked once and then he turned back and kept cutting. She showed him what to pull out and he pulled it out and his face was a careful blank she recognized from years of watching people meet the physical reality of food for the first time. The intestines came free with a wet sound and he set them on the rock beside him and looked at his hands.

"Everyone does this?" he said.

"Everyone who eats. Or someone does it for them."

"Someone did it for me. For twenty-six years." He said this quietly, without performance. A fact arriving in his body for the first time. "Someone killed the bird. Plucked it. Gutted it. Cooked it. Put it on a plate. Cleaned the plate. Every day. For twenty-six years."

"Yeah."

He rinsed the cavity with stream water. Spitted the bird on a green branch she cut for him — hazel, straight enough, the bark stripped. Set it over the fire on a forked stick. Too close. She didn't tell him. The residual feathers he'd missed singed and curled and the fat began to render and pop, spattering into the coals, and within five minutes the underside was charring black while the top stayed raw and pale.

"Turn it."

He turned it. The other side charred. He moved it higher, but the green branch bowed under the weight and the grouse slipped sideways and landed in the coals. He grabbed for it and the heel of his hand pressed into an ember and he jerked back, swearing — "God — that's — damn" — and she leaned over and fished the bird out with a stick and set it back on the spit.

A blister was already rising on his palm, next to the older ones. He held his hand out and looked at it — the accumulated record of one afternoon's work. Blisters on blisters, the knife cut, the ember burn, dried blood and grouse fat and ash.

"It's ruined," he said, looking at the bird.

"It's dinner."

"But it's — " He gestured. The grouse was blackened on two sides and raw on the third, ash embedded in the skin where it had fallen, the whole thing listing on the spit like a ship that had given up. "Nobody would eat that."

"I'm eating that."

He looked at her. She meant it. He could see that she meant it.

She showed him how to prop the spit at the right height and rotate it slowly, and the third side cooked through to something closer to edible while the charred sides went from black to blacker. When she judged the meat was done — or done enough that it wouldn't make them sick — she pulled it off the fire and set it on the flat rock between them.

---

They ate it. Vel tore the bird apart with her knife, cutting away the worst char, and what remained was tough and smoky and salty with ash. Kem chewed slowly, working through the texture of meat his blistered hands had made, and didn't complain. The stream ran behind them and the fire ticked and popped and the highlands darkened from green to gray to the blue-black of the canopy against the last light.

The food was the worst thing Vel had eaten in recent memory, and recent memory included a week of flatbread and creek water during a flux outbreak south of Greymarsh. She ate it anyway. He'd made it.

She was not going to examine why that mattered.

"In the Vault," Kem said, after a while, "food just appears. I mean — I knew there were kitchens. Somewhere. I knew people prepared it. But it appeared. On plates. With garnish. I never thought about what came before the plate."

"Mhm."

"This is what came before the plate." He held up a piece of charred grouse. Firelight caught the ash on his fingers.

"Every time."

"Every single meal."

"Yep."

He ate another piece. Chewed. The quiet between them was different than it had been — not the tactical silence of their first days, where every pause was Vel calculating and Kem filling space with nervous chatter. This was simpler. Two tired people eating at the end of a hard day.

A nightbird called from the canopy. Two notes, repeated, patient. The stream caught the last of the light and held it.

"Can I ask you something?" Kem said.

"You can ask."

"Why did you start?"

She knew what he meant. Not why she wanted zero — he'd asked that and she'd shut him down. This was different. Why she'd started spending. What made someone go from three hundred to twenty-eight, one level at a time, over forty years, in a world where every point spent was a permanent subtraction.

"That's not about the route."

"No."

"Then it's not your business."

"I know." He said this without resentment. Just acknowledgment. Then he went quiet. He picked at the grouse. The nightbird called again, the two-note phrase carrying in the damp air.

Vel pulled a strip of bark from the ground near her knee and tossed it into the coals. It caught and flared and she watched it burn down to a curl of ash.

"I was seventeen," she said.

She hadn't decided to say it. The words came out the way water finds a crack — not because the pressure was unbearable but because the resistance, for a moment, wasn't there. She was tired. The food was bad. The boy had burned his hands and eaten it anyway.

"There was a canal in Avermouth. South of the Vault, before you hit the Spent Towns. Market town. Big enough for two Hoarded representatives, small enough that everyone knew which streets were theirs. I was there with my mother — she sold cloth. End of market day. Sun going down. People heading home."

She paused. Not for effect. For the specific effort of uncovering something she'd packed away so long it had calcified into fact, and facts were easier than memories.

"A girl fell in the canal. Eight, maybe nine years old. She'd been running on the towpath and the rail was rotted through and she went in. The current runs fast where the canal narrows under the market bridge. Deep enough to pull an adult under. Cold — it was early spring, snowmelt still coming down from the hills."

"Was there — "

"There were forty people on that towpath. Maybe more. Half of them were Hoarded. One was at least two ninety-eight — I could see his House seal. They wore them pinned to their coats back then. He was standing six feet from where she went in."

Kem was very still.

"The Hoarded did nothing. Obviously. The seal-coat watched. Didn't even change his expression. The Spent — most of them were young, two-sixty, two-seventy. A Strength ability would've pulled her out. Three levels, maybe four. But they're looking at the Hoarded, and the Hoarded aren't moving, and there's this — " She could feel the shape of it still, forty-one years later. "This calculation happening on every face. The same one. *If they don't spend, why should I.*"

"And you — "

"I jumped in."

She said it flat. The fact.

"I was seventeen and at three hundred and I'd never spent a point in my life and I jumped into the canal after a girl I didn't know. The current took us both under the bridge and I — I don't even remember deciding. I used some kind of Grip ability. The Ledger calls it that — Grip. Your hands hold harder than a body should. I grabbed a bridge piling with one hand and her with the other and I held on until two men on the bank finally waded in."

"One level."

"One level. Three hundred to two ninety-nine. And I climbed out and I walked back past the seal-coat and he was still standing there and his face hadn't moved. Not once. And the girl's mother was crying — not at me, just crying, the way you cry when your body catches up to what almost happened — and nobody said thank you, and my hands were bleeding from the piling, and I was at two ninety-nine."

She stopped. She'd said more than she planned, and the silence that followed had the specific weight of too much honesty — not regret, but the exposure of having told a true thing to someone she hadn't fully decided to trust.

"I didn't plan the rest of it," she said. "I didn't have a philosophy. I just — once you've done it, you can't unsee the places where someone should do something. And nobody does. And you're standing there with two ninety-nine and the canal's right in front of you."

Kem didn't say anything for a while. He'd stopped eating. His face in the firelight was hard to read — younger than twenty-six, the way it went when something got past his composure and he didn't know what to do with it.

"The Hoarded would say you were reckless," he said.

"They'd say I was wasteful."

"Yes." He looked at the fire. "They'd say the girl would have been retrieved by natural means. Or that she should have been more careful on the towpath. Or that the loss of a level represents a permanent consequence disproportionate to the temporary risk of — " He caught himself. Stopped. "That's what my father would have said. Fifteen years ago, when he could still finish a sentence."

The fire popped. A coal split and the halves fell inward, glowing.

"Tell me about him," Vel said. She meant it, which surprised her.

Kem leaned back. His hands hung between his knees, palms up, and in the firelight she could see the blisters and the striker cut and the ash and the dried grouse blood crusted under his nails. Hands that were trying to learn what work was.

"My father's Ledger reads three hundred," he said. "It has always read three hundred. He's never used a single level on a single thing in sixty-one years of living."

"I know what the Hoarded are."

"You know the principle. You don't — " He stopped. Restarted, lower, less rehearsed. "I sat next to him at the Conservatory Rite every week for twenty-six years. That's the rite where we — where they — sit in a room full of objects that invite use and demonstrate the discipline of not touching them. Every week. My mother, my father, me. The great families. Everyone at three hundred. Everyone perfectly still."

"Sounds like hell."

"It was normal. It was just what you do on Seventhday. What I want to tell you about is what happened to him."

He picked up a twig and turned it in his fingers.

"He was sharp when I was small. I remember that. He had a voice that filled the dining hall. He argued with my mother about trade routes, southern policy, which families were gaining Council influence. He had opinions. Strong ones. He was — " A pause. "He was there. The way a person is when they're alive inside their own life."

"And he stopped."

"Not all at once. It's slow. The Hoarded call it Stillness. They call it the highest form of discipline — when someone achieves perfect nonaction so completely that the self quiets." He said this with the precise diction of something memorized young, and then his voice roughened, dropped into something less polished. "He was about forty-five when I first noticed. I was ten. His sentences got shorter. Not compressed the way yours are, where you're choosing to say less. Empty shorter. Like the words were drying up inside him. And his eyes — they started doing this thing where they'd fix on a point in the room and stay there. Not staring. Just — vacant. Gone somewhere I couldn't follow."

"The Hoarded celebrated it."

"Uncle Aldris — my father's brother — told me he was approaching perfect balance. The family physician called it deepening discipline. My mother — " He stopped. The twig snapped in his fingers. "My mother knew. She's not stupid. She could see what it was. But she's at three hundred too, and the alternative is to name it, and if you name it then the whole — everything the Hoarded have built, everything they've preserved for centuries — "

"Falls apart."

"Falls apart. So you don't name it. You sit next to your husband every Seventhday and watch his eyes go empty and call it virtue. And when your son stands up in the middle of the rite and walks out, you say his name once and let him go. Because stopping him would require action. Or because maybe part of you wanted someone to finally move. I don't know. Both."

He dropped the broken twig into the fire. It caught immediately, a bright flare that threw their shadows long against the trees and was gone.

"I didn't leave because I disagreed with the philosophy." His voice was steady. "I know that's what they'll say. Political dissent. Youthful confusion. A young lord under radical influence." He looked at her, and his eyes were wet, but his voice held. "I left because my father's dying in a room full of people who are calling it a gift. And I sat there for fifteen years watching it happen and doing nothing, which makes me exactly as Hoarded as the rest of them. And I don't — I can't do that anymore."

Vel sat with this. The fire burned. The stream ran. The nightbird went on calling its two notes into the dark.

She thought about the man in the seal-coat, standing six feet from a drowning girl. She thought about Lord Aldraste in the Conservatory, three hundred on the Ledger and the person draining out of him like water through a cracked jug. The same math, both times. Don't spend, don't act, don't reach into the water. The number stays full and the life stays empty.

"Can he still talk?" she asked.

"Some days. Fewer each year. When he does it's — the sentences aren't really sentences anymore. They're shapes. The grammar's there but the meaning's gone. Like he's reading off a page he can't see."

"And his Ledger."

"Three hundred. Still three hundred. Perfect."

She let the word sit between them. Perfect. She'd met people in the high Spent ranges — two-eighties, two-nineties — who had that same hollowed-out look. People who'd preserved almost everything and lost something the Ledger didn't track and didn't name. She'd never met a three hundred that far gone. She'd heard about it. She'd never had to sit next to it every week for twenty-six years.

"Eat your food," she said. It was the only thing she had that wasn't an opinion or a comfort or a lie.

He ate. The charred grouse, cold now, the rendered fat congealing white against the blackened skin. He ate it methodically, his blistered hands tearing the meat from the bone. When he was done he wiped his hands on the grass and stacked the bones in a small neat pile near the fire, the way someone stacks things when they need to do something with their hands and there's nothing else.

"That was the worst meal I've ever had," he said.

"Yeah."

"I made it."

"You did."

He looked at the bone pile. The fire had burned down to a bed of coals, the red glow painting the underside of the canopy in shifting copper light. His face was streaked with smoke and ash and the particular tiredness of someone who'd said a hard thing and wasn't sorry.

"Thank you," he said. "For the fire. And the — all of it. Even though I ruined the bird."

"You didn't ruin it. You cooked it badly. That's different."

"Is it?"

"Ruined is giving up halfway. Bad is finishing. You finished."

He almost smiled at that. Not the bright, manic grin from after the outrider fight, the one that meant he was terrified and papering over it with enthusiasm. This was smaller and quieter and sat on his face like something that belonged there.

"Tomorrow," she said, "you're building the fire again. And cleaning whatever we catch."

"All right."

"And you're carrying the heavier pack."

"I'm already carrying — " He stopped. Looked at her. "You gave me the light one."

"Twenty pounds."

"Twenty. And the heavy one is — "

"Thirty-five."

"You've been carrying thirty-five pounds through the highlands for two days."

"Forty years."

He was quiet for a moment. She could see him recalculating something — not the Ledger arithmetic but the simpler math of who she was. The seventeen-year-old who'd jumped into a canal. The fifty-eight-year-old who'd been carrying the heavy pack and hadn't mentioned it. The person between those two points who'd spent two hundred and seventy-two levels, one at a time, and was still walking.

"Thirty-five pounds," he said. "Yeah. Okay. I'll carry it."

Vel banked the coals. She laid out the bedrolls — his close enough to the fire's residual warmth, hers between him and the trail, habit more than any specific threat. The highlands were quiet. No pursuit. The Hoarded would reorganize, send more people, but not tonight. Tonight the world was the size of a campfire: a stream, two bedrolls, the smell of burned grouse and woodsmoke, and the weight of what they'd said to each other sitting between them like something solid they'd both have to carry from here.

She lay on her back. Through the canopy she could see a few stars in the gaps between leaves, small and far away. The stream sounded like the stream and nothing else. A good sound. One of the free ones.

Twenty-eight. The Ledger's hum was there, the way it was always there — not loud, just present, the awareness of what remained. Two hundred and seventy-two levels spent. Twenty-eight left. Every one of the spent ones had gone to something. Someone. Some moment where the water was rising and nobody was moving and she was standing there with what she had.

Twenty-eight was tight. Twenty-eight was enough.

She closed her eyes. In the morning she'd make him do it all again.


# Chapter 6

The Council chamber had seventeen chairs, and Sable Orm knew the occupant of each by the sound they made sitting down. Councilor Breve's knees cracked. Councilor Hain breathed through his mouth when he thought no one was listening. Lady Aldraste settled into her chair without producing any sound at all, which was its own kind of announcement.

Sable had spent twenty-three years in this room. She had organized its agendas, drafted its minutes, revised its protocols, and — on four occasions she did not discuss — corrected the arithmetic of Council members who outranked her. She knew the chamber the way a clerk knows a ledger: every column aligned, every entry predictable.

She did not know why she had been summoned to stand before it rather than sit behind the recording desk.

"The Aldraste situation," Councilor Breve began.

Sable waited. She had learned, across two decades of administrative service, that the first sentence of a Council address was rarely the one that mattered.

"Young Lord Aldraste has been outside the walls for approximately five days." Breve checked his notes, and the gesture itself was an indictment. Breve did not check notes for matters that did not horrify him. "He has spent an estimated twelve levels. In five days."

The stillness around the table changed quality. Twelve levels. In the Vault, the number was obscene. Sable's nearest colleague had spent four levels across an entire career — two on a Perception ability during an emergency audit, two on a Light charm during a power failure that threatened the record vaults. She had been spoken to about it.

"The House signet was taken from the ceremonial stand," Lady Aldraste said. Her voice carried the particular calm of a woman who had decided exactly how much of herself this room was going to get. "My son did not plan this. That is the charitable interpretation, and I will be offering no other."

The framing was deliberate. Sable had drafted enough institutional communications to recognize the structure: Lady Aldraste was establishing this as a breach of Council discipline, not a family crisis. A family retrieving a wayward son was gossip. The Council retrieving a defector was governance. The distinction protected House Aldraste's political standing while authorizing whatever came next.

"The question before the Council," Breve continued, "is the method of retrieval."

Sable waited. This was still not her meeting.

"There has been discussion" — a pause that filled the chamber like held breath — "regarding the deployment of specialized resources."

Specialized resources. Sable had encountered references to them in Council records three times across her career. Each reference had been redacted before filing. Each redaction had been performed by a different hand, which meant the information was sensitive enough to rotate the person responsible for suppressing it.

She thought of the sealed wing on the Vault's eastern corridor. Twenty-three years of walking past those doors. A different quality of silence behind them — not the Vault's cultivated stillness but something heavier, denser. The silence of rooms where things happened that the rest of the Vault pretended did not.

"The Red Reserve," Lady Aldraste said, and the room's quiet changed texture.

"For the benefit of those present," Breve said, with a look toward Sable that confirmed she was permitted to hear this, "the Red Reserve is a Council-maintained unit of Hoarded personnel who have been authorized to train in the use of abilities. For security purposes."

Authorized to train. Permitted to spend. Sable turned the words over. A civilization built on the principle that spending was degradation had, behind sealed doors, cultivated people who spent. The contradiction was precise in its hypocrisy and devastating in what it implied: the Hoarded had always known that total inaction was insufficient. They had simply arranged for that insufficiency to remain invisible.

"The Reserve is not a viable option for this operation," Councilor Hain said. He said it too quickly, which meant someone had raised it as an option before Sable entered the room.

Lady Aldraste spoke with the cadence of someone reading from a document, which was how she delivered her sharpest arguments. "Deploying trained spenders for a retrieval operation would constitute a public acknowledgment that the philosophy of preservation cannot address all institutional concerns. The existence of the Reserve is predicated on its non-existence. Using it visibly destroys the premise."

"Agreed," said Breve. "The Reserve is not deployable for this purpose."

Sable followed the logic. The Council could not send people who knew what they were doing, because people who knew what they were doing should not exist under Hoarded philosophy. Better to send people who didn't know. Better to send someone whose ignorance was itself a message: look, our administrators can handle this. No trained spenders required. No crack in the philosophy.

"Auditor Orm," Breve said.

Sable's stomach performed a maneuver she did not have an administrative category for.

"The Council would like to discuss your availability for a field assignment."

"I am available for any assignment the Council requires." The correct response. It came out before the incorrect one had finished forming.

"The retrieval of Kem Aldraste requires a lead operative with institutional credibility, administrative competence, and discretion. You have been recommended by" — Breve did not look at Lady Aldraste, which was itself a look — "multiple Council members."

"Councilor Breve. I have not left the capital in forty-seven years." Sable delivered this as an inventory of relevant data, not a protest. "I have never operated field equipment. I have never navigated terrain. I have not walked more than" — she performed the calculation — "approximately two kilometers in a single day, and that was during the record-room relocation of Year Eighteen."

"Which you managed with distinction," Breve said.

"The logistics of document transport across three buildings are not comparable to —"

"You are precisely what we need." Lady Aldraste's voice cut cleanly. She met Sable's eyes. The look was not warm and was not cold. It was the look of a woman who had solved an equation and found the answer acceptable. "Someone who represents what we are. Not what we have hidden."

The chamber held the statement. Three Council members who had not spoken exchanged looks that meant nothing and communicated everything — House Aldraste had produced the crisis and was now directing the response, and the cost of questioning Lady Aldraste's judgment was higher than the cost of sending an administrator into the wilderness. Hain cleared his throat but did not speak. Lady Aldraste's hands rested flat on the table, fingers aligned, perfectly still. One thumbnail had a raw edge where she had torn it. Sable noticed because Sable noticed everything that happened in this room, and in twenty-three years she had never seen Lady Aldraste's hands be anything less than immaculate. She filed the observation and did not remark on it. Whatever Lady Aldraste felt about her son — whatever a mother felt about her child walking out of the Vault with a stolen signet and a body burning through levels like kindling — was locked behind the institutional frame with professional precision. The Council would see a political crisis managed. Nothing else.

Sable understood. They were sending her because she was the opposite of the Red Reserve. A level-300 administrator who had never spent a single point. The pristine embodiment of Hoarded discipline. The fact that she could not retrieve a cat from a low wall, let alone an aristocratic defector from the southern wilds, was not an oversight. It was the point.

"I will need a team," Sable said.

"Of course. We have prepared a list of suitable operatives."

"Field-trained?"

The silence was its own answer.

"I see," Sable said.


The Council's list of suitable operatives contained eight names. Three declined immediately — one citing a joint condition, one citing a commitment to an ongoing inventory project, one submitting a formal written objection that referenced, by Sable's count, seven separate protocols governing voluntary assignment exemptions. The remaining five reported to her office at the designated hour.

They were all level 300. This was, Sable reflected, the defining characteristic of virtually everyone she had ever met. What distinguished these five was not their level but their willingness, which in at least two cases appeared to have been a misunderstanding.

"I was told this was a documentation assignment," said Operative Tarrell. He had brought three notebooks and a personal seal. He was a slender man in his fifties whose hands had the specific softness of someone who had never lifted anything heavier than a records binder. He had, Sable knew from his file, spent the last decade cataloguing the Vault's entire architectural archive — every pillar measurement, every sightline angle, every drainage gradient. His handwriting was famous among the filing clerks. He did not, at this moment, look like someone prepared to walk to the end of the corridor with purpose.

"It is a field retrieval," Sable said.

"In the field."

"Yes."

Tarrell looked at his notebooks with an expression of quiet betrayal. He did not put them away.

Operative Kess had volunteered. She had told Sable this twice, both times with the particular brightness of someone who had confused discomfort for enthusiasm. She wore her formal Hoarded garments — the kind designed for temperature-controlled rooms and minimal movement — and had packed a parasol. She was forty-two and had previously organized the Vault's seasonal floral rotation, a duty that required walking between seventeen courtyards and that she described as the most physically demanding experience of her career.

"Will we be walking the entire distance?" Kess asked. She was examining the map spread across Sable's desk with the expression of someone reading a language she did not speak.

"The target is traveling south on foot. We will need to match his route."

"On foot."

"On foot."

"For how long?"

Sable checked the briefing document, though she already knew. "The estimate is twelve to fifteen days, depending on terrain and the target's pace."

Kess looked at the parasol. She looked at the map. She set the parasol on the desk with the gentleness of someone putting down a small hope.

Operative Lenn stood by the window with his arms crossed and had not spoken. He was the youngest of the group — early thirties — and the set of his jaw was managing something he had no intention of naming. His pack was already assembled, compact, the compression straps retightened at least twice by the look of them. He had read the field manual. Sable recognized the approach because she shared it: when you could not prepare through experience, you prepared through text, and you held that text between yourself and the knowledge that text was not enough.

The remaining two — Operative Marris and Operative Chael — sat next to each other and shared an expression of careful administrative bewilderment. Marris was a quiet woman in her forties who kept checking her Ledger, a reflexive gesture Sable recognized because she did it herself. The number was always 300. It had been 300 for forty years. But the gesture was comfort, the way a person checks their pockets for keys already known to be there.

Chael had brought a compass. He was a large, slow-moving man who had served as a records porter for twenty years — the closest the Vault came to physical labor, which meant he carried files between buildings while everyone else had them sent. He held the compass flat in his palm and watched the needle with the focus of someone who had recently and urgently learned which direction was north.

"The field equipment kit," Sable began, and opened the crate the Council had provided.

She had reviewed the manifest the night before. She had not opened the crate. This was, she now understood, an error of institutional trust.

The kit contained: six ration packs sealed in waxed paper and labeled by nutritional category, six bedrolls rolled with institutional precision and tagged with serial numbers, one medical kit whose instruction sheet was printed in a font that assumed excellent lighting, six Communication charms sealed in individual lead-lined cases at a cost of one level each to activate, one hand-drawn map, and a four-page document titled *Standard Field Protocol for Council-Authorized Operations*.

Sable opened the protocol document and read it to her team.

"Section One: Departure Procedures. All operatives will confirm their current level prior to departure. Level expenditure during the assignment must be recorded in the field log at each rest interval. Total expenditure is not to exceed the pre-authorized operational limit of" — she paused, read the number, and paused again — "two levels per operative per day."

She looked at the document. She looked at her team.

"Two levels per day," she said. "For a pursuit operation involving potential contact with a target who has spent twelve levels in five days while being guided by a woman at level thirty-one who has survived four decades of continuous field work."

Silence.

"The protocol further specifies," Sable continued, maintaining the administrative register because losing it would require her to feel something she was not prepared to categorize, "that all ability use must be pre-authorized by the team leader through completion of Form 7-C: Justification of Level Expenditure. The form requires a written explanation of no fewer than two hundred words per instance of ability deployment."

"What if the situation requires immediate action?" Lenn asked. His voice was level, which took effort.

Sable consulted the appendix. "The Emergency Provision of Form 7-C allows the written justification to be reduced to one hundred words in circumstances of direct and immediate physical threat. The form must still be completed prior to deployment of the ability."

"Prior to," Lenn said.

"Prior to."

The room held a quality of stillness that was not the Vault's cultivated composure but something closer to collective disbelief.

Sable opened the medical kit. Inside: bandages in three widths, a suturing needle with thread pre-measured to six-inch lengths, a tin of wound salve, and a folded instruction card. The card's first line read: "In the event of injury, assess severity using the Standard Evaluation Grid (see Appendix D)." There was no Appendix D. There was no appendix of any kind. The card's reverse side displayed a diagram of the human body marked with zones — "critical," "moderate," "superficial" — rendered with the confident precision of someone who had never been cut.

Chael picked up the suturing needle and held it the way he held his compass: with intense focus and no evident understanding of its function.

"Has anyone here performed a field suture?" Sable asked.

Five variations of silence.

"I have read about the procedure," Lenn said.

"That will have to be sufficient," Sable said, and set the medical kit aside.

Sable turned to the map. The hand-drawn annotations had occupied her evening. The cartographer — the word was generous — had marked the Vault's main gate as "Departure Point Alpha" and the first rest position, twelve kilometers south, as "Manageable Day One Endpoint." The terrain between these points included a river ford and an elevation gain of four hundred meters. The annotation at the river read: "Fordable at standard administrative pace." There was no definition of standard administrative pace anywhere in the document, because no one had ever measured one.

Farther south, the annotations grew sparser and more hopeful. A cluster of settlements was marked as "Civilian territory — cooperation expected." A mountain pass was labeled "Navigable." A stretch of open country bore the single word "Flat," underlined, as though flatness were a guarantee of ease.

"The equipment includes no weapons," Sable noted.
