"We are not a military force," Tarrell said, with the reflexive certainty of someone quoting something he had filed.

"We are pursuing an individual who has already engaged Hoarded outriders in combat. The outriders were defeated. The woman guiding him has been spending abilities in the field for longer than most of us have held our current postings."

"The protocol does not authorize —"

"The protocol," Sable said, "was authored by someone who has never walked twelve kilometers." She heard the sentence leave her mouth. It sat in the room between administrative observation and something dangerously close to opinion.

She set the protocol document on the desk. She placed the map beside it.

"We depart at dawn," she said. "Pack what you can carry on your back. If you cannot carry it, you do not bring it."

Kess's eyes moved to the parasol.

"You do not bring it," Sable said.


Dawn arrived the way it arrived in the Vault: precisely, the light entering through windows engineered to illuminate without warming. Sable had slept two hours. She had spent the remaining six reading the field manual, then rereading it, then organizing her pack with the methodical attention she ordinarily reserved for year-end audits. Her bedroll was serial-tagged. Her ration packs were arranged by nutritional category. The medical kit's instructions were folded into the front pocket where she could reach them without opening the main compartment. None of this preparation would matter if the terrain did anything the field manual had not anticipated, which meant none of it would matter at all.

She met her team at the main gate.

They looked, collectively, like a committee that had taken a wrong turn and was too committed to admit it. Tarrell had the notebooks tucked into his pack, the corners protruding. Kess wore sensible boots that she stood in with the cautious unfamiliarity of borrowed shoes. Lenn's pack was squared and tight — the product of theoretical study, and it showed in how high he had cinched the straps, which would dig into his shoulders inside an hour. Marris carried her things in two bags, one balanced on each arm, because nobody had told her that a single pack distributed weight better and she had not thought to wonder. Chael held the compass.

The gate was where Kem Aldraste had walked out five days earlier. The ceremonial stand where House signets were displayed still held an empty slot — the Aldraste position, a small square of velvet with nothing on it. Sable looked at it and registered an irregularity. A gap in the record.

"Senior Auditor." The gate warden was an older man who had held the post for thirty years without incident, because incidents did not happen at the Vault's gate. "I am required to log your departure. How many in the party?"

"Six. Council-authorized retrieval operation. The reference number is in the briefing file."

"Estimated return date?"

"Three to four weeks."

He wrote it down. His handwriting was immaculate. Nothing in his manner suggested that six level-300 Hoarded citizens walking through the gate to pursue a defector across unknown terrain was anything unusual. The Vault maintained its composure. The Vault always maintained its composure.

Sable took the Wayfinder charm from its case.

A small disc. Cool metal, stamped with the tracking glyph. A Perception-class ability that attuned the user to a specific individual's energy signature and provided continuous directional awareness. Cost: two levels.

She held it in her palm and felt nothing, because it was an inert object and she had never activated anything and everything she knew about how this worked came from a manual she had memorized six hours ago.

Two levels. She had been at 300 for forty-seven years. Her mother had died at 296 — four levels spent across an entire life, three on healing and one on a Light charm during a night evacuation when Sable was small. Her mother had considered those four expenditures a source of private regret. Sable had grown up understanding that each was a failure of alternatives, not a choice. She had never spent one. Not one. Her Ledger had read the same number since the day she was born, and the constancy of that number was the foundation of everything she understood about herself: disciplined, preserved, whole.

She focused on Kem Aldraste. She had reviewed his file. She knew his face from the House records — formal, composed, the expression of a young man who had been still for twenty-six years and whose stillness had been considered exemplary right up until the moment he stood up.

She activated the glyph.

The world did not change. Nothing visible moved. But something inside her — something sealed, dormant, untouched for forty-seven years — cracked open with a force that buckled her knees. She caught the gate's stone archway with one hand. The Wayfinder's information was pouring in: direction, distance, the speed and bearing of the target. Kem Aldraste was south-southeast, roughly nine days at foot pace, moving steadily. The knowledge had not arrived through her eyes or ears. It had entered through a channel she did not have a name for, a sense in her body that had been closed her entire life and was now, without her permission, open.

Her hands shook. Not just her hands. She could feel the Ledger adjusting — the number shifting from 300 to 298 — and the sensation was not pain but it was not nothing. Something had been subtracted and something had been added and the arithmetic did not balance the way arithmetic was supposed to.

She sat down on the gate's threshold stone.

"Senior Auditor?" Lenn. Careful.

"A moment."

298. She was at 298. The number had been 300 for forty-seven years and now it was 298 and the difference was two points and a Wayfinder humming at the base of her skull, feeding her the continuous direction of a man she had never met, and she could not organize the feeling into a category she could file because nobody had ever described to her what spending felt like. They described what it cost. They measured the loss. Nobody in the Vault had ever said: when you use yourself for the first time, the body answers, and the answer is louder than the price.

She breathed. She made the breathing slower. She was a Senior Auditor. She had managed seventeen crisis meetings in her career, including the Year Twelve audit that had uncovered a forty-year filing error in the Treasury archives. She could manage this. She could manage her own lungs. What she could not manage was the Wayfinder. It continued — south-southeast, south-southeast — regardless of what she wanted. She had activated it and now it was part of her the way her heartbeat was part of her, running without permission, and she could not dismiss it because dismissal required a second expenditure and the protocol she had just dismantled in front of her team authorized two levels per day, and she had already spent both.

"The target is south-southeast," she said. Her voice held. The rest of her did not match her voice. "Approximately nine days at our pace. Assuming our pace is" — she looked at her team, five level-300 Hoarded citizens who had never walked farther than the filing annex — "what it is."

She stood. Her legs held. The Wayfinder pulsed steadily — south-southeast, south-southeast — a new sense layered onto the ones she had lived with for forty-seven years, and she could not determine whether what she felt was violation or release or both of those at once or something she would need a word for that nobody in the Vault had ever thought to invent.

"We proceed south," she said.

They walked through the gate. Sable Orm, forty-seven years old, level 298, stepped over the Vault's threshold and into the world.

The morning was warm. Early summer. The air moved — it actually moved, touched her face, carried smells she had no names for because the Vault's air was filtered and temperature-regulated and carried nothing. Birdsong, from actual birds, not the decorative ones in the Vault's inner courtyards but birds that lived in trees and had no regard for institutional composure. The ground under her boots was uneven — not polished stone but packed earth with roots crossing it, and she had to watch where she put her feet, which was a problem she had never considered having.

Behind them, the Vault's walls rose in clean vertical lines, immaculate, silent. The architecture that had contained her entire life. She did not look back. She was a Senior Auditor on an authorized assignment, and authorized assignments faced forward.

Kess had left the parasol. Tarrell's notebooks made a papery rustle against his hip with each step. Lenn set a pace that was clearly the product of theoretical calculation — too fast for everyone except Chael, whose twenty years of carrying files between buildings had given him, if nothing else, legs that were accustomed to use. Marris shifted her two bags from arm to arm every few minutes, her face composing what Sable suspected was a mental draft of a formal review of the packing protocol. Chael consulted the compass every thirty paces, confirming they were still heading south, which they were, because the road went south and they were on it.

The road turned. The Vault disappeared behind a low ridge. Sable felt it go — the quiet, the geometry, the controlled air, the certainty of walls. Ahead, the terrain opened into rolling country under a sky that was larger than any sky had a right to be.

By the first hour, the problems were arriving. Lenn's straps were cutting where Sable had predicted. He adjusted them without stopping, jaw tight, refusing to fall behind a pace he had set himself. Kess's boots had begun to rub — she walked with an increasingly deliberate gait, placing each foot like a question she was not sure she wanted answered. Tarrell had discovered that notebooks and field packs were structurally incompatible and was carrying one in his hand, the other two wedged against the bedroll in an arrangement that would not survive the afternoon. Marris had shifted both bags into a single carry over her left shoulder and was listing slightly to port. Chael alone walked without visible distress. Twenty years of records-porter work had given him the one gift the Vault could accidentally bestow: a body accustomed to carrying weight from one place to another.

Sable tallied the column. Six operatives. Eighteen hundred combined levels. Zero field experience between them. The arithmetic was absurd in every direction.

The Wayfinder pulsed. South-southeast. Two levels gone and something opened that she did not know how to close. The number was wrong. She was walking toward a man she had never met because the institution she had served for twenty-three years could not send the people it had trained for this without admitting they existed, so it had sent her instead. An administrator. A woman who had never left the capital. A woman who, as of seven hours ago, had maintained a perfect record of zero lifetime expenditure, and who was now at 298 and could feel the difference the way you feel a tooth gone from the jaw — not with pain but with the tongue's helpless return to the gap, again and again, confirming what was already known.

The road went south. She followed it.


# Chapter 7

The road improved before the town appeared. For six days Kem had been walking on dirt tracks, game trails, whatever path Vel chose that kept them below ridgelines and out of sight. Now the ground under his boots was packed and graded — not cobblestone, nothing the Vault would recognize as a proper surface, but someone had used a Shaping ability on this stretch of earth. The surface was compressed and even, rainwater channels cut along both sides. Maintained. A road built by spending.

Vel didn't comment on it. She adjusted her pack and kept walking, her pace the same efficient tempo she held whether the ground was flat or climbing, and Kem had stopped trying to match it and settled into his own stride, which was longer but less economical. His blisters had hardened into calluses over the last four days. That counted as progress.

The settlement came into view as they crested a long rise: a cluster of buildings in a shallow valley, sixty or seventy structures, with fields spreading out on three sides and a line of low hills to the south. Early summer light sat heavy on the rooftops, and the heat had a different quality here than in the forested highlands — drier, closer, the kind of warmth that pressed against exposed skin and stayed. It was the largest thing Kem had seen since the Vault, and it looked nothing like the Vault. The buildings were stone and timber, shaped but not uniform — some walls curved where a Shaping ability had been applied freehand, others squared off with hand-laid block. Repairs showed as visible seams between ability-work and manual labor, and nobody had bothered to make the seams invisible. The Vault's architecture existed to deny that maintenance was necessary. This place wore its maintenance like working clothes.

What struck him was the sound.

The Vault was quiet. That was the first principle of Hoarded living — stillness, silence, the absence of exertion. Sound meant someone was doing something, and doing something meant spending, and spending was failure. Kem had grown up in a place where raised voices were a scandal and footsteps were padded by design.

This settlement hummed.

A low, warm vibration ran under everything — Fertility Charms, he realized, active in the fields to the east. Not one but dozens, the combined output of farmers spending levels daily to make soil yield more than it would on its own. The sound was like standing near a hive, a thrumming he felt in his back teeth before he heard it with his ears. Layered over that: the crack and grind of Shaping work, someone reshaping stone on the settlement's far side, a distant rhythm of ability striking material. And closer, something else — a pulse he couldn't identify at first. Rhythmic, gentle, warm. It rose and fell like breathing.

"What's that?" he asked.

Vel had stopped to let him look. She followed his gaze toward a low building on the settlement's southern edge, its door propped open with a stone. "Healing ward. They've got a healer who takes patients. That pulse is a Restoration Charm running."

"Running. As in, continuously?"

"She keeps it going for an hour each morning. Costs her a level every few weeks, depending on how many people she's working on."

A level every few weeks. If the healer was at, say, 180, she'd been spending steadily for years. Decades, maybe. Her entire adult life directed at making other people's bodies work properly. In the Vault, that woman would be considered reckless. Depleted. The kind of person polite families mentioned as a cautionary example — *she had such potential, but she spent it all.* In the Conservatory Rite, they would have seated her near the door, if they'd admitted her at all.

From where Kem stood, listening to the warm pulse of her ability carrying over the morning air, she sounded like the most useful person in the valley.

They walked in. The settlement's main road was wider than the approach, lined with buildings that served as both homes and businesses — a tanner's workshop with hides stretched on a frame outside, a grain store with its doors open and the smell of dry seed carrying out, a place that sold tools and rope and had been expanded three times in different decades, each addition visible in the stonework. People were out. Working. Moving with the unhurried competence of people doing familiar jobs, calling to each other across the road, carrying loads. A man with a crate on his shoulder nodded at Vel as he passed. She nodded back. Two women sat on a bench outside a doorway, shelling something into a bowl between them, talking in voices too low for Kem to catch. One of them looked up at him, registered his height and his pallor, and went back to her work.

Nobody nodded at Kem. They looked, though. Briefly, the way you look at something unfamiliar — the height, the pale indoor skin that was only now starting to redden across his nose and forearms, the clothes that hadn't been mended because they'd never been worn long enough to need it. He could feel himself being assessed and sorted: outsider, soft, sheltered. The last one was accurate enough that it landed.

A woman was repairing a section of wall on a building to their left. She had her hand flat against the stone and the material was moving under her fingers, bulging outward to fill a crack, settling back to flush. Her face was concentrated, her free arm braced against the wall for balance, and when she finished she stepped back and shook her hand out like the joints ached. A man on a ladder above her said something Kem couldn't catch, and she laughed and told him to do it himself if he was going to criticize her pointing.

She moved to the next section and started again. Each repair cost something — a fraction of a level, a fraction of a fraction, but it accumulated. Every time she pressed her hand to the stone she was permanently diminished by some small measure. She was choosing to do it because the wall needed fixing.

Kem looked at his own hands. His Ledger pulsed at the edge of his awareness: 228. He'd bled levels on trivial things all week — a Flame Charm two nights ago because Vel's flint was damp and he hadn't wanted to wait, a Light Charm in a cave they'd sheltered in, a minor Strength boost on a steep climb that his legs could have managed if he'd been willing to hurt for it. Twenty-odd levels gone on problems that matched what this woman was solving with her hands and a sore wrist.

"We need supplies," Vel said. She'd been watching him watch the settlement, but she didn't say anything about it. "Food for the next stretch south, and I need to ask about route conditions."

She led them to a tavern — a broad, low building with a clay-tile roof and a yard where someone had set up benches under a canvas shade. A few chickens scratched in the dirt near the entrance. Inside was dim and cool, smelling of grain beer and cooked onions and the specific sourness of a space where many people had eaten and drunk and the wood had absorbed decades of it. Half a dozen patrons sat at tables — men and women in working clothes, eating midday meals or drinking slowly. They looked up when Vel and Kem entered. The looks settled on Kem in the same measuring way they had outside, held for a beat, and moved on. One man went back to his food. A woman at the bar turned fully to study him, then turned away.

The tavern keeper came from a back room, a crate of clay bottles braced against her hip. She was perhaps fifty, heavy-set, with her hair pulled back under a cloth and forearms that showed the specific muscle of years spent lifting, pouring, and hauling stock. A leather brace wrapped her left knee — the kind of thing you wore for a joint that had been complaining for years and you'd stopped trying to fix. She set the crate on the bar with a practiced thud and looked at them.

"Passing through?" she said to Vel.

"Couple of days. We need dried food for six days' travel south. Whatever you've got."

"I can put that together." The keeper's eyes moved to Kem. Took in the height, the posture. "Him too?"

"Him too."

She started pulling items from below the bar — wrapped packages of dried meat, hard bread, a pouch of grain that smelled of something nutty. Her hands were quick and practiced, no abilities, the motions of someone who'd stocked this bar a thousand times. Kem stood back while Vel negotiated quantities and price, and he let his eyes move around the room. The tavern was plain but swept clean. Someone had shaped decorative grooves into the stone walls near the ceiling — a small touch of craft that had cost a fraction of a level and was worth it to whoever lived here. The tables were scarred from use. A cat slept on a chair near the window, its tail hanging off the edge.

The keeper set the food packages on the bar and named a price. Vel counted marks from a leather pouch — Kem's marks, technically, converted from the portable valuables he'd taken from the Vault. Coins and small trade goods that amounted to more money than most people in this room earned in a season. Vel paid without comment, and the keeper sorted the coins into a box beneath the counter.

Then her eyes dropped to Kem's right hand, resting on the bar.

His House signet. Silver, heavy, carved with the Aldraste crest — the still flame, the mark of preservation. He'd taken it from the ceremonial stand at the Vault gate ten days ago, not because he'd decided to bring it but because leaving it would have meant he'd thought the whole thing through. He'd been wearing it since. It was his father's crest and his grandfather's, and it meant: we are the family that never acts, and we have made a philosophy of it.

The keeper's face changed.

Not anger. Something more practiced — a closing, a withdrawal. The professional courtesy she'd been showing Vel drained out of her expression and what replaced it was flat and careful. She looked at the signet, then at Kem's face, then at the signet again, and her mouth became a line. The crease between her eyebrows deepened. She was a woman who had seen that crest before, or crests like it, and had concluded something about its bearers long ago, and nothing about Kem's sunburned face or his awkward posture in her tavern was going to reopen the question.

She finished stacking the food packages. She pushed them across the bar toward Vel — not toward Kem. "Anything else?" she said, and the temperature of her voice had dropped to a place where *anything else* meant *please leave*.

"No," Vel said. "That's fine."

Kem opened his mouth. He didn't know what he was going to say — an explanation, an apology, some version of *I'm not what you think,* which was absurd because she was looking at the physical proof of exactly what he was. He closed his mouth. The keeper picked up her empty crate and went back through the doorway without looking at him again.

He stood there with his hand on the bar and the signet on his finger and the silence the keeper had left in her wake. The man at the nearest table glanced over, saw the ring, and looked away — not hostile, just finished with him.

Kem looked down at the signet. Silver, clean, precisely cast. In the Vault it had been a marker of belonging — lineage, status, the family's centuries-long commitment to the highest possible discipline. Here, in a settlement where people spent their lives level by level to build walls and heal neighbors and grow food, the still flame said something else entirely. It said: *we watched you labor and kept our hands still. We own the trade routes your livelihood depends on. We built a civilization on the principle that your work makes you lesser and our inaction makes us great.*

He covered the signet with his other hand. The metal was warm against his palm.

Vel was shouldering her pack. She'd seen the whole thing — he could tell by the specific quality of her not-looking, the way she didn't turn toward him or offer commentary. She was giving him room to sit with it.

"I didn't know," he said, once they were outside.

Vel squinted against the midday sun. "You didn't know what?"

He started to answer, then stopped. What didn't he know? That the signet meant something different outside the Vault. That the Hoarded's symbol of discipline was, to people who lived under Hoarded economic control, a mark of the system that had decided their labor was inferior to inaction. That a woman who worked a bar every day would see his family's crest and read in it a specific, justified contempt.

"Any of it," he said. "What any of it looked like from out here."

"That's what outside is for." She adjusted a strap on her pack. "Come on. I need to find someone about the southern routes."

---

They found Dren at the market yard.

Vel had stopped at the settlement's well and asked a man drawing water about a trader who knew the country south of the Spent Towns. The man pointed them west. "Dren. He's loading up over there. Supposed to head east tomorrow, I think."

"We need him heading south."

The man shrugged. "Good luck. He goes where the money is."

The market yard was a packed-dirt clearing on the settlement's western edge where carts and pack animals and stacked goods created a working chaos Kem found difficult to parse. People argued over quantities, loaded crates, inspected produce. A boy chased a loose goat between two carts and crashed into a woman carrying a bolt of cloth, who swore at him with the rote exhaustion of someone who'd chased that same goat herself last week and would probably chase it again tomorrow. Someone was using a minor Shaping ability to repair a cracked cart wheel — the wood swelling and sealing under his palm, a process that would have taken a carpenter half a day accomplished in thirty seconds at the cost of a fraction of a level. The spending was constant and casual, part of the working rhythm, and nobody around treated it as anything other than getting things done.

Dren was loading his cart near the yard's far fence. He was shorter than Kem had assumed — stocky, road-darkened, with hands that looked like they'd been tanned alongside the leather goods stacked behind him. A scar ran along the outside of his left forearm, old and faded, the kind of thing you got from a rope that slipped or a crate edge that caught you wrong. He worked with the efficiency of a man who'd done this job for twenty years, each item placed where it balanced the load, each tie cinched with a knot that looked permanent. His boots were battered, resoled at least once, the leather cracking at the ankles. He wore a belt with too many things on it — a knife, a water flask, a coil of cord, a pouch Kem couldn't identify — the equipment of someone who solved problems with whatever was at hand.

Vel walked up to him directly. "You Dren? I'm told you know the routes south."

He looked up from a strap he was tightening. Assessed Vel — took in the weathered face, the scarred hands, the pack that sat on her frame like it belonged there. Then his eyes moved to Kem, and the assessment took longer. The height. The jaw that had never been hit. The hands that had started to callus but still showed the softness of a life spent indoors. The posture.

"I know them," Dren said. He went back to his strap. "Southern routes aren't popular. Spent Towns thin out after two days. Then it's rough hill country and then you're in the Margins, and nobody goes to the Margins who doesn't have to."

"We're going on purpose," Vel said.

"Figured." He tested the strap with a sharp tug. "You and the lord."

"He's not —"

"He's Hoarded. I can tell by the way he's standing. He's standing in my market yard like he's waiting for someone to bring him a chair." Dren looked at Kem. "No offense."

"I'm not waiting for a chair," Kem said.

"Sure. What are you paying?"

Vel named the sum. Kem watched Dren's face as the number landed — a flicker around the eyes, the mental arithmetic of a man who measured every offer against a specific need. The amount was generous. Better than an eastern trading run. Better than two.

Dren straightened up from his cart. He was doing calculations behind his eyes, and Kem recognized the process because he'd watched Vel do it — the silent weighing of what you could afford against what you needed. But where Vel's arithmetic was about levels, Dren's was about money, and behind the money was something else.

"South to where?" Dren asked.

"Past the settlements' southern edge. Through the hill country to the rougher terrain beyond. We need someone who knows the water sources and the safe camps."

"I know the water. Terrain's not the hard part — the hard part's what's in the terrain. Bandits. Desperate folks drifting north from the Margins. People who'll take your pack while you sleep and feel bad about it later." He squinted at Kem. "He know how to handle himself?"

"He's learning," Vel said.

"How fast?"

"Fast enough."

Dren didn't look convinced. He rubbed his jaw with the back of his wrist, leaving a streak of road dust on his cheek that he didn't bother with. "How long are we talking?"

"Five, six days to the edge. Then you're done and you head back."

"I was heading east tomorrow. Buyers in Callowfield, reliable contract. That's money I can count on. You're asking me to dump a sure job for a one-off into country I don't especially like."

"I'm asking you to take good money for a week's work. You know the route."

"I know it. That's why I don't like it." He leaned against his cart and crossed his arms. His forearms were thick from years of loading cargo, and the old scar caught the light. "Look. My situation's specific. I've got a daughter."

He said it the way you'd mention a fact about the road — which turn to take, where the water was. Flat, informational. As if his daughter were one of many factors in an equation rather than the weight at the center of everything. Kem recognized the flatness because he'd spent twenty-six years around people who'd perfected the art of talking about the things that mattered most as though they mattered least. The Hoarded did it with philosophy. Dren did it with the blunt economy of a man who couldn't afford to let his vulnerability show because someone might use it as leverage.

"Mira. She's eight. Got a bone thing that's been eating at her legs for the past year. There's a healer three towns north who can treat it — a deep Restoration, takes multiple sessions. It costs." He looked at the space between himself and Vel as if the money she'd offered were sitting there, visible. "What you're paying covers about half. The other half I've been scraping together run by run. Callowfield gets me maybe a quarter. So the math on your offer is good. The math on going south with —" He nodded at Kem. "— with whatever this is, that math is less clear."

"It's six days," Vel said. "We're not looking for trouble."

"Lady." He said it without irony, without deference — just a word. "You're heading into rough country with a man wearing a signet that gets faces shut on him in a Spent Town tavern. I've been at this long enough to know what that means. It means trouble's already found you and you're moving to stay ahead of it."

A silence opened. The market yard moved around them — carts loading, people arguing, the boy chasing his goat back the other way. Dren waited in the silence with the patience of someone who'd spent years in negotiations and knew that the person who talked first usually gave something away.

"There are people looking for him," Vel said. "They're behind us. If we move smart, they stay behind us."

Dren absorbed this. His expression didn't change. "Hoarded retrieval?"

"Something like that."

"Great." He scratched the back of his neck. The gesture pulled his collar aside and showed more road-darkened skin, a life lived outdoors. "So I'd be guiding a Hoarded defector through territory where Hoarded agents might show up, and if they do, I'm standing next to the thing they're looking for."

"We've handled them before," Vel said.

"How many levels did 'handling them' cost?"

Vel didn't answer immediately, and that was its own kind of answer. Dren caught it.

"Right." He uncrossed his arms. "Your price, six days, south route. I want half up front and the other half before I turn back. And I need the morning to offload what I can from the cart and store the rest."

"We leave tomorrow morning," Vel said.

"Tomorrow morning means I'm selling at a loss. Anything I can't move by tonight sits in someone's shed and I'll be lucky to get two-thirds of it back. That cost comes out of your side."

Vel looked at Kem. Kem looked at the pouch on her belt — his Vault money, converted into the currency of a world where people worked for what they had.

"Agreed," Kem said.

"Agreed," Dren said. He stood away from his cart and studied Kem properly for the first time — not the quick assessment from before but something longer, more specific. The look of a man calculating whether a piece of cargo was worth the trouble of hauling it. "You ever carried a pack for more than a day before this?"

"Ten days now."

"How's it feel?"

"My shoulders hurt."

Dren's mouth moved. Not a smile — more like the acknowledgment that something had met his lowest expectations. "Tomorrow morning. I'll be here at first light." He turned back to his cart, then stopped. "And put that ring somewhere it can't catch the light. I don't need the southern settlements deciding I'm running escort for a Hoarded inspection."

He started untying the ropes on his cargo, already doing the math on what to sell and what to store. Vel turned and headed for the edge of the market yard. Kem followed, adjusting his pack on the shoulders that did, specifically, hurt — a deep ache in the muscle between his neck and the joint, the kind that Vel had told him would eventually become background noise. It hadn't yet.

They found a spot outside the settlement to camp for the night, in a grove of trees where the ground was soft with leaf litter and the air smelled of warm earth and dry grass. Vel built a fire the way she always did — flint and dry tinder, no abilities, each motion practiced. The settlement sat below them in the fading light, its shapes softening as evening came on. The Fertility Charm hum had quieted as the farmers stopped for the day, but the healing ward's pulse still ran, faint and steady, carrying over the distance.

Kem sat on a rock near the fire and turned the signet on his finger. The metal was warm from his skin and from the long day's sun. He thought about taking it off, putting it in his pocket. Something stopped him — not attachment, not pride. The keeper's closed face had been a judgment of the signet's meaning, not of him specifically. Hiding it would be about his comfort. It wouldn't change what the symbol represented to every person in that settlement, or to Dren, or to the people in the southern country they were heading into tomorrow.

He left it on.

"Vel."

She looked up from feeding a stick into the fire. The flames caught the lines around her eyes, the gray in her hair, the old scars on her knuckles.

"How much have you spent since you found me? Total."

She considered. "Three in the outrider fight. Couple more on small things through the week. Five, give or take."

"So you're at — twenty-six? Twenty-seven?"

"Around there."

Kem checked his own Ledger. The number sat at the edge of his awareness and came into focus when he reached for it: 227. He'd been 300 eleven days ago. Seventy-three levels gone. Vel had spent five in the same period, and every single one had been deliberate.

"I used a Flame Charm two nights ago," he said. "Because the flint was damp."

"I know."

"That was a level. Maybe a little less."

"Maybe."

"That's a level the woman fixing the wall would've spent on a wall."

Vel poked the fire. A log shifted, sending sparks upward into the dark. She didn't say anything for a while, and he let the silence sit because he was starting to learn that Vel's silences weren't empty — they were the space she left for things that didn't need to be said out loud.

"Yeah," she said, finally.

He waited for the rest. The reassurance, the lesson, the framing of what he'd wasted into something he could learn from. She didn't offer any of it. She sat by the fire and let the silence do the work that comfort would have interrupted, and what the silence said was: yes, you spent them on nothing, and yes, they're gone, and that is what this is.

The healing ward's pulse faded as the evening deepened. Somewhere in the settlement, a door closed. A dog barked once and stopped. Insects started in the grass around them, the night sounds of a place where people lived by choosing, daily, to spend themselves for each other and for the things they were building.

Tomorrow they'd head south with a man whose daughter was sick and whose price for helping them was exactly the kind of calculation the Hoarded never had to make. Kem sat with his signet and his seventy-three missing levels and the settling quiet of the Spent Towns, and when the dark came fully he did not reach for a Light Charm. He sat in it and watched the fire burn down on its own.


# Chapter 8

Vel counted the road south in levels.

The Spent Towns fell behind them over three days — the last of the managed farmland, the last settlements where a Fertility Charm still turned the soil green. The road narrowed from packed trade-route to dirt track, winding through fields that had been good once and were merely adequate now, land where the charm had been used up within its cycle and the ground was doing its own work for a change. Spring had settled into the warm part of itself. The air smelled like cut grain and drying mud.

Twenty-eight. That was the number she carried, the number she'd budgeted from, the number that had to stretch across the southern routes and whatever they found on them. She checked it the way she checked her pack straps — habitual, involuntary, a background process that ran beneath everything else. Twenty-eight.

Dren walked point. He read the road without thinking about it, the way a person reads a language they've spoken since birth — which ruts were fresh, which bootprints were local, where the track was solid and where yesterday's rain had softened it into something that would eat your ankle if you stepped wrong. He'd been doing this for twenty years, and it showed in how he moved: unhurried, efficient, his weight centered, his attention spread across the landscape without concentrating on any one part of it. He had a habit of touching the strap of his satchel when he was thinking — thumb and forefinger working the leather like a merchant counting coins in his pocket.

"There's a depot settlement about an hour ahead," Dren said, not turning around. "Grain storage, maybe an inn. We can top off the water and keep going."

"How big?" Vel asked.

"Small. Thirty, forty people. They service the farms around here."

Kem walked behind Vel, carrying his own pack for the fourth consecutive day without complaint. He'd stopped adjusting the straps every ten minutes, which meant his shoulders had either toughened or gone numb. He was watching the fields with the quiet attention of someone still learning what open land looked like — how light moved across grain, the patterns birds made when they lifted from a hedgerow. He'd started seeing things without needing to name them, and Vel had begun to find this less irritating and more like the beginning of something useful.

"Is that wheat?" Kem asked, nodding at a field to the left.

"Barley," Dren said.

"Right."

"You can tell by the—"

"By the awns. I know. Vel told me yesterday."

"Hm." Dren didn't look back. "She teach you what good barley looks like yet?"

"She told me not to worry about it."

"Smart." A pause. "That's not good barley, by the way. Fertility Charm's worn thin. You can see where it stops — the color changes about fifty meters in."

Vel could see it. A line across the field where the green darkened, the stalks thickened, and then — nothing dramatic, just a gradient — the barley became thinner, paler, working harder for less. The line between charmed land and uncharmed land. Every farmer in the Spent Towns knew that line. It was the border between what you could afford and what you couldn't.

They walked. The road dust settled on Vel's boots and the sun was warm on her neck and the only sounds were their footsteps, the insects, and the distant report of someone using a Shaping ability at one of the farms — a rhythmic crack that meant construction or repair, the workaday sound of levels being spent on things that needed doing.

She was listening to the wrong sounds.

What she expected, if pursuit came, was precision. The Hoarded pursued the way they lived — with minimum expenditure, maximum efficiency, the smallest application of force that could solve the problem. She'd been waiting for a Perception sweep: one clean pulse from a trained tracker, reading the landscape at range, locating them without spending more than a level. That was how the Hoarded hunted people. Quiet. Cheap. Effective.

What came instead was the sound of someone ripping a forest apart.

It wasn't literally fire. Vel's ears parsed the noise before her mind caught up — crashing through heavy undergrowth from the north, multiple sources, branch snaps amplified by residual kinetic discharge, the stale buzz an ability left in the air when it had been used carelessly and the energy hadn't finished dissipating. Then a tree falling. Then another tree falling. Then a voice she couldn't make out saying something that ended in a question mark.

"Off the road," Vel said.

Dren was already moving — a single sideways step into the tall grass bordering the track, his body dropping into a crouch with the economy of a man who'd been avoiding problems since before Kem was born. Kem was slower. His pack caught on a fencepost. He pulled, the post shifted, and the fence wire sang a note that traveled twenty meters in both directions.

Too slow. Too loud. It didn't matter.

They came out of the treeline at the edge of the grain fields in a loose cluster that no one would have mistaken for a formation. Five figures in matching gray cloaks — standardized equipment that said institutional procurement, not personal choice. Three of them were wearing the cloaks wrong: one fastened at the waist with the hem pooling around his boots, one with the hood cinched so tight his face was a red circle of sweat, and one who'd cut armholes in the sides and was using it as a vest. The fourth had abandoned her cloak somewhere in the woods, her bare arms still shimmering with the residue of whatever speed ability she'd been burning to keep pace. The fifth had his cloak on correctly and looked miserable about it.

Level 300. All five of them.

Vel felt it in the displacement — the sheer mass of stored potential radiating off them like heat from a forge. Five Ledgers at maximum capacity, five bodies that had never burned a significant level in their lives, five repositories of power so dense it distorted the Ledger's background hum the way a heavy object warps the surface it sits on. She'd felt level-300 presence before — every Hoarded aristocrat carried that weightiness, the fullness of unused capacity. But five at once, in an open field, moving toward them with the coordinated precision of a herd of startled cattle, was something else entirely.

Sable Orm's retrieval team.

"That's — I think that's — yes." The one with the tight hood pointed at Kem. "That's the Aldraste heir. I'm relatively certain."

He was pointing at the only tall, pale, obviously-Hoarded person standing in a grain field south of the Spent Towns.

Vel calculated. Five at 300, untrained. Herself at 28, four decades of fieldwork. Kem at approximately 225, improving but still wasteful. Dren at 138, a trader, not a combatant, crouched in the grass with an expression that said he'd known this job would go wrong and he'd taken it anyway. The settlement's grain depot was visible ahead — silo, a few structures, people moving between them. Civilians in range.

The math said run. The geography said: flat open ground, grain fields in every direction, the road straight and exposed, the treeline behind the agents and the settlement ahead. No cover worth the name.

"Kem," Vel said. "Behind me. Don't do anything until I tell you."

"I can fight—"

"Behind me."

***

The hooded agent went first.

His hands moved with the careful precision of someone who had studied a technique in a manual and was now attempting it in the field — fingers curved, wrists rotated inward, the Binding ability's somatic components executed with textbook accuracy and zero practical instinct. At level 300, a Binding could have pinned all three of them to the ground like insects on a board. A trained Binder would have spread the effect across multiple targets, calibrated the force, maintained the hold while colleagues closed in.

This agent missed all three of them and hit a boulder.

The Binding was designed for flesh. Applied to stone, it didn't pin — it fused. The agent's palm sealed flat against the rock surface with a sound like wet clay hitting a wall. His body wrenched sideways, his arm going taut, his feet leaving the ground for a moment before gravity and the Binding's hold found an equilibrium that left him half-hanging off the boulder with his face pressed against the stone.

"That's not—" He pulled. The Binding held. He pulled harder. The boulder didn't care. "My hand is — I can't—"

The bare-armed woman went second. A Perception Sweep — Vel recognized the posture, the widening of the eyes, the stillness that preceded the ability's activation. At level 300, a Perception Sweep deployed by a trained scout could read every heartbeat on a battlefield, map structural weaknesses in architecture, track a target through walls. It was one of the most useful abilities in the Ledger.

At level 300, deployed by someone who had never used it before, it read everything. Every insect in the grain field. Every mole in the earth beneath it. Every stress fracture in the silo's mortar. Every grain of pollen suspended in the air. The structural composition of every building in the settlement. The respiration rate of every person within two hundred meters. All of it, simultaneously, at maximum resolution, with no filter and no training in how to shut it off.

The woman made a sound — not a scream, something smaller and more damaged, a sound a mind produced when it received a quantity of information it had no framework for processing — and sat down hard in the dirt. Both hands went to her temples. Her eyes were open and seeing nothing useful.

"Too much," she said. "The — there are seventeen — the silo's third course has a — I can hear the worms — too much too much—"

Two down. Vel felt something that wasn't amusement — closer to the grim recognition that the Hoarded's most expensive field deployment was performing exactly as anyone with field experience would have predicted. She'd started to move, to use the distraction, to get Kem and Dren off the road while three remaining agents processed their colleagues' failures.

The vest agent threw a Combat Pulse.

He threw it at Kem. His aim was that of a person who'd been told where to point but had no practice pointing — the Pulse left his hands headed in approximately the right direction and arrived approximately forty meters to the left, where the settlement's grain silo stood.

The silo came apart.

It wasn't an explosion — Combat Pulses didn't burn. Concussive force hit the structure like a fist hitting a sandcastle. The upper third of the cylinder separated from the lower two-thirds in a spray of mortared stone, the blocks scattering outward in an arc that showered the road and the nearest field with debris. Grain — thousands of kilos of stored barley — erupted from the ruptured cylinder in a cloud that turned the air yellow-brown and smelled like a bakery caught in a windstorm. Stone fragments flew.

A piece hit Dren.

He'd been crouched in the grass six meters from the silo, exactly where a person would be if he'd stepped off a road next to a grain depot because five lunatics had just come out of a treeline. He wasn't in the fight. He wasn't near the fight. He was a trader crouching in grass, and a chunk of mortared stone trailing grain dust caught his left arm between the elbow and the wrist and opened it.

The sound was a wet smack, followed by Dren saying "Shit" in a tone that was more irritation than pain. Then the pain arrived and his voice changed. He looked at his arm. The gash ran from the inside of his elbow halfway to his wrist, deep enough that the edges gaped, deep enough that the meat underneath was visible, pink and then rapidly red as the blood found the opening. Stone dust and grain chaff were already in the wound, ground into the raw tissue by the impact.

"Shit," he said again, quieter, pressing his right hand over the gash. Blood pushed through his fingers immediately.

Vel marked it — Dren's wound, Dren's arm, a civilian hit by debris from an ability nobody had aimed at anything, because this was what happened when level-300 power was wielded by people who'd never hit a target in their lives. It wasn't a combat injury or a tactical outcome. A piece of building had fallen on him.

She couldn't help him now.

The fourth agent — the one who'd been watching his colleagues fail — decided to test his Strength augmentation. He was standing at the back of the group, and he didn't aim it at anyone. He raised his fist and swung at the air, testing the ability the way you'd test a tool before using it.

The air moved — not wind, but displacement. The Strength-augmented fist pushed a wall of compressed air outward at a speed Vel's body registered before her mind caught up — a pressure wave that bent the standing grain flat in a ring around the agent, that lifted the dust and grain particles from the silo explosion and drove them horizontal, that hit the two nearest trees and snapped their trunks at the midpoint. The wood broke with a sound like bone — two cracks, fast, the trees folding and going down in a tangle of branches and torn leaves.

Fifteen meters away, Vel felt the pressure wave in her sternum. It wasn't a hit — it was a preview, a casual demonstration of what that fist would do if it connected with something softer than air.

The comedy was over.

The fifth agent — the one who'd gotten his cloak right — had been standing still, processing. Watching the Binding fail, the Perception overwhelm, the Combat Pulse miss, the Strength concuss the air. He was the one who learned. His hands came up with a deliberate motion, fingers spread, and Vel recognized the Force Push a half-second before it left his hands.

She was already turning. The Force Push at level 300 was a directed compression wave — not the unfocused blast of the Strength test, but a channel of force aimed at a target. She turned sideways to narrow her profile, and the edge of the Push caught her across the ribs on the left side instead of square in the chest. The difference between those two outcomes was the difference between cracked ribs and a stopped heart.

She heard two cracks before she felt them — the same sound the trees had made but closer, inside her, bone giving way under lateral compression. It wasn't a clean snap but a grinding flex, ribs bending past their tolerance, costal cartilage separating from the sternum on the left side where the force concentrated. Her left side went hot. Then it went wrong — a rolling wrongness, the bones rearranging into a configuration that didn't support breathing, which was a problem because she was currently trying to breathe.

She hit the ground on her right shoulder. Drew one breath — shallow, because anything deeper pulled the separated ribs apart and the sensation of bone ends moving against each other whited the edges of her vision — and got up.

Getting up cost more than any ability she'd used in a month. Her left arm went to her side involuntarily, pressing against the fracture site, and the pressure helped and also didn't help because the ribs were wrong under her hand, the contour of her chest altered in a way her fingers could map — two ribs, maybe a third. The cartilage felt torn rather than broken — a softer failure, which meant it would hurt more, heal slower, and she wasn't going to spend on it.

Twenty-eight. She'd walked onto this road at twenty-eight.

She burned a Combat ability. A kinetic strike — focused, narrow, the precise application of force she'd been practicing for forty years — caught the cloak agent in the hip and turned him sideways. At 300, his body absorbed the damage the way a wall absorbed a thrown ball: the physics registered, the structure didn't care. But his stance broke and his second Push went into the dirt.

Cost: one level. Twenty-seven.

She burned an Evasion charm. A half-second of sharpened reflexes — her body reading the Strength agent's wind-up before her eyes finished tracking his arm. She sidestepped the fist's displacement wave by two feet. The air where she'd been standing compressed hard enough to kick up a wall of dust that pelted the back of her neck with grit.

Cost: one level. Twenty-six.

Kem was behind her. She could feel his Ledger displacement shifting — the signature of someone gathering an ability, pulling it from reserves. "Kem. Perimeter. Now."

The perimeter went up. She felt it snap into place — a dome of shimmering force, blue-white at the edges, covering a radius of at least thirty meters. It crackled where the grain dust met the barrier. It was enormous. It was triple what they needed. Kem had pulled it from the same place he pulled everything: the deep well of a level-300 Ledger that was learning to spend but hadn't learned to measure.

The cloak agent's third Push hit the perimeter and dispersed. The barrier hummed, held, shed the force in a ripple that flattened barley in a circle around them.

Vel burned a second Combat ability — aimed at the ground in front of the Strength agent, kicking up a spray of dirt and stone that caught him in the eyes. He staggered back, one hand going to his face, the augmented fist swinging blind. The swing connected with air and the concussion tore a furrow in the field twenty meters long.

Cost: one level. Twenty-five.

She burned a third Combat ability at the cloak agent's knee. Not to injure — at 300, she couldn't injure him with a single strike from 25. But the knee buckled, his weight shifted, and the Push he was building collapsed in his hands, the energy discharging harmlessly into the barley.

Cost: one level. Twenty-four.

She burned a second Evasion as the hooded agent — who had finally freed himself from the boulder, his palm raw and bleeding where the Binding had torn away from skin — threw a second Binding at her. The charm pulled her sideways and the Binding passed through empty air and hit a fencepost, which it consumed.

Cost: one level. Twenty-three.

She burned her last Combat ability and the rotation required to cast it nearly dropped her. The somatic component demanded a twist of the torso that her ribs vetoed — pain flared through her left side, bright and total, her breath catching on the fracture. She forced the rotation anyway. The strike caught the Strength agent in the shoulder, and something in the joint gave under the focused force. His arm went limp. He dropped to one knee, and his face registered the discovery that pain was real and currently interested in him personally.

Cost: one level. Twenty-two.

Twenty-two.

Six levels. She'd burned six levels in under three minutes against five people who couldn't aim, couldn't coordinate, couldn't hold a formation, couldn't do a single thing that a week of field training would have made basic. She'd burned her budget for two weeks because raw power didn't need skill to break things. Raw power just needed to exist in sufficient quantity, and five level-300 agents had quantity enough to rearrange the landscape.

Her ribs were grinding with every breath. She could feel the bone ends moving — a subtle, nauseating shift each time her chest expanded, the cartilage separation making the left side of her ribcage a structure that moved in ways ribcages shouldn't. Breathing was a negotiation between oxygen and structural integrity, and structural integrity was losing.

***

"We move," Vel said. Her voice came out quiet and flat, which was what happened when speaking at normal volume required the ribcage expansion she couldn't currently afford.

Dren was on his feet. His left arm was pressed against his body, his right hand clamped over the wound, and the blood had soaked through his shirt and reached his fingers. His face was the color of old paper. But he was standing, his eyes were clear, and he was looking south with the focus of a man evaluating an exit.

"Tree cover," Dren said, jerking his chin toward the road ahead where the track entered a stretch of mixed woodland. "Half a kilometer. Maybe less."

"Can you run?"

He looked at his arm. Looked at Vel — at the way she held her left side, the shallow breathing, the posture of someone managing damage she wasn't mentioning. "Can you?"

Vel couldn't run. She could walk fast, in a controlled manner that kept her torso rigid and her arms close, movement that minimized every rotation her ribs would punish. It wasn't running — it was the ambulatory equivalent of the shallow breathing she'd adopted: the minimum required for forward progress.

"Kem. Drop the north side. Reinforce south. We're moving."

The perimeter shifted. The northern face dissolved and the energy redistributed to the south, creating a wall of force between them and the agents. Kem was walking backward, his hands out, his face showing the concentration of someone maintaining an ability while in motion — a skill he hadn't had two weeks ago.

They moved. Dren first — his stride shortened, his balance off, but his legs worked and his sense of direction was a thing no arm wound could take. He found the fastest line to the tree cover without being told, the trader's instinct for a clean route asserting itself even with blood running down his hand and dripping off his fingertips into the road dust. Vel followed, each step a negotiation with her ribs. Kem last, walking backward, the barrier rippling as a Binding and a half-formed Combat Pulse hit it and dissipated.

Behind them, the agents were not pursuing. The Perception woman was still on the ground, hands over her ears, her eyes squeezed shut. The boulder agent was examining his torn palm. The vest agent had run out of Combat Pulses — or, more accurately, had realized that his second Pulse had destroyed a fence instead of a person and was staring at the wreckage in what appeared to be vocational crisis. The Strength agent was kneeling in the dirt, cradling his shoulder. The cloak agent was standing still, watching them retreat, his hands at his sides.

He was the only one Vel kept her eyes on.

They reached the tree cover. The road entered the wood and the canopy closed overhead, filtering the light into green shadow. Vel put her back against an oak trunk and let herself breathe — still shallow, still managed, but the adrenaline was dropping and the ribs were presenting their full invoice. The left side of her chest was a topology of wrong. She probed with her fingers and found the two fracture sites — one at the fifth rib, one at the sixth, both in the anterolateral line where the Push had compressed the bone. The cartilage separation was higher, near the sternum, and it shifted when she pressed, a soft grinding that made her hand flinch away before she decided to stop.

She could heal this. A Restoration ability, targeted and minimal, would knit the bone and reattach the cartilage in minutes. Cost: two levels. She'd be at twenty. Twenty, with the Margins ahead, with unknown terrain and unknown threats and the Scour beyond that, and every level between here and zero already spoken for in a budget she'd been building since before Kem Aldraste had ever heard of her.

She pressed her palm flat against the fracture site and breathed through her nose.

Twenty-two. The margin was gone. Not the levels — the buffer. The space between what she'd planned to spend and what she'd actually spent, the cushion that was supposed to absorb exactly this kind of problem. Six levels, burned in a single engagement, against people fumbling their own abilities. She'd accounted for pursuit. She'd accounted for combat. She hadn't accounted for the blunt arithmetic of five level-300 Ledgers wielded by people who couldn't hit what they aimed at but could destroy what they didn't.

"Kem."

He was bent over, hands on his thighs, breathing hard. The perimeter had cost him — not physically, he had the reserves, but maintaining an ability while walking backward across open ground was draining in the way new skills drained a person: concentration, focus, the effort of doing something the body hadn't automated yet.

"How much?" she said.

He checked. The inward focus, the slight defocusing of the eyes. "One-eighty-three."

Forty-two levels. On a perimeter that had been three times the necessary size, maintained for four times the necessary duration, against agents who'd been too disorganized to mount a real pursuit. The perimeter could have been done for fifteen levels by a trained spender. Twenty at the outside. Twenty-two levels of pure waste.

She didn't say this. The perimeter had worked. The Pushes and Bindings had hit the barrier instead of them. They were alive because Kem had thrown up a wall of force that was excessive and expensive and exactly what they'd needed.

"Okay," she said.

Dren sat down with his back against a birch. He moved his right hand from the wound long enough to look at it, and the gash started bleeding freely again — a thick seep that ran down his forearm and dripped from his wrist into the dead leaves. The wound was ragged, the edges dark with stone dust and grain chaff ground into raw tissue by the impact.

"Hold that shut," Vel said. She dug into her torn pack — the Force Push had ripped it open, scattering half her supplies across a grain field she'd never see again — and found a strip of clean cloth. "Press this over it. Hard. We'll clean it when we stop."

Dren took the cloth. Pressed it against the gash. His jaw was set and his breathing was controlled in the way of a man who handled pain by denying it his full attention. He wrapped the cloth around his forearm and pulled it tight with his teeth and his good hand. A knot, practiced, the kind of self-bandaging a trader learned after enough years of loading cargo alone.

"That'll hold for now," he said. He flexed his fingers experimentally. Winced. Stopped flexing. "Some fucking professionals."

It wasn't anger. It was the flat verdict of a man who evaluated goods and services for a living. He'd been hired to guide them through territory he knew, and the service he'd received in return was a chunk of silo in his arm from an ability that hadn't been aimed at him, or at the building, or at anything specific at all. His daughter was waiting for him back in the Spent Towns, and he'd just lost blood and functionality to collateral damage from a fight between people whose combined Ledger readings could have funded a small nation.

Vel leaned her head against the oak bark. The rough surface pressed into her skull, and the pressure was the only comfortable thing about her body. Grain dust had settled into her hair, her clothing, the torn seams of her pack. The stale, floury smell of the destroyed silo clung to everything. In two hours, when the dust dried, it would be a fine grit in every fold and crease. She'd be picking barley out of her ears for days.

Twenty-two levels and cracked ribs and a wounded guide and a partner forty-two levels lighter than he'd been half an hour ago. The budget for two weeks of southern travel, spent in three minutes of chaos.

She opened her eyes and looked back through the trees.

The agents were visible at the edge of the grain field — scattered, useless, the Perception woman still down, the boulder agent sitting next to his boulder, the vest agent having what appeared to be a quiet crisis near the ruined silo. The settlement's people had come out and were standing at a careful distance, watching the agents with the patience of civilians who had seen powerful idiots before and knew the safest response was distance and time.

There was a sixth figure.

Vel had counted five during the fight. Five gray cloaks, five sets of 300, five flailing displays of power that had produced one destroyed silo, two broken ribs, one civilian wound, and zero successful restraints. But there, at the treeline where the agents had first emerged, stood a sixth person — not in gray, standing apart from the group, closer to the trees than the field, positioned as if she'd chosen the spot deliberately — observation range, not engagement range. Dark hair, a formal Hoarded arrangement coming loose. The posture of someone who'd spent decades sitting correctly in official chambers and was only now learning what it felt like to stand in open air for hours.

She was holding a small notebook. She was writing in it.

She had watched the entire fight. Vel's combat — the strikes, the evasion timing, the way Vel moved to minimize exposure. Kem's perimeter — the radius, the energy output, the direction of retreat. Dren going down and Vel not stopping. Five agents failing in five different ways. All of it recorded in a notebook she'd brought from the Vault because she was the kind of person who brought notebooks to field operations.

As Vel watched, the figure finished writing and closed the notebook. She looked south — not at the agents, not at the silo wreckage, south, down the road, into the trees where Vel and Kem and Dren had disappeared.

Then she turned and walked back to the agents — she wasn't hurrying or running, just walking with the unhurried gait of someone on her own schedule, crossing the trampled barley toward the five people she'd been assigned to lead and had apparently decided to observe instead.

Five agents who couldn't fight. That was a storm. Storms passed. Storms burned their own energy and stumbled home.

One person who watched and learned and wrote it down. That was someone building a curriculum from wreckage, cataloging what had worked and what hadn't, studying Vel's patterns and Kem's output and the geometry of their escape. That was someone who would be better next time because she had the resource no one in the Hoarded system was supposed to possess: the willingness to spend her own ignorance.

Vel turned away.

"We need to move," she said. She pushed off the tree. The motion sent a flare through her left side — the ribs shifting, the cartilage grinding, the fracture reasserting itself with the insistence of an injury that had been briefly forgotten and resented the oversight. She breathed through it. Shallow. Controlled. Her left arm found its place against her ribs, holding them in, holding herself together through pressure she applied because the pressure she refused to spend two levels on would have done the job better.

Kem straightened up. He was watching her face, and his eyes had the look of someone who'd recently learned to read pain in other people — not innate, a developing skill. He didn't ask if she was okay. He'd learned that Vel didn't answer that question.

Dren was already on his feet, his left arm held close, the cloth darkening. He picked a direction through the trees and started walking. "There's a creek crossing about three kilometers in. We can clean this up there."

Vel adjusted her pack on her right shoulder. The left side was territory she'd be navigating around rather than through for weeks — the ribs had made that decision for her, and the two levels she wouldn't spend on healing them were a tax she'd be paying in pain for as long as the bone needed to knit on its own. Every step was a small negotiation. Every breath was a line item.

Twenty-two. She was heading south at twenty-two, with fractures she wouldn't heal and a budget she couldn't rebuild and a guide bleeding through improvised bandaging and a partner who'd just burned forty-two levels she'd been counting on. The five agents behind them were a problem that would solve itself — they'd regroup, they'd complain, they'd limp back to the Vault with a report that field operations were harder than the briefing suggested.

But the sixth one. The one with the notebook. The one who'd stood at the treeline and watched five people fail and hadn't failed herself because she hadn't tried anything yet. The one who was already better than she'd been an hour ago, simply from standing still and paying attention.

That one wasn't going home.

Vel walked south. She counted her steps because counting was what she did, and each step cost exactly what it cost, and the ribs kept their own tally alongside hers.


# Chapter 9

The needle went in wrong the first time. Kem felt the resistance of Dren's skin, the rubbery give of it, and his hand jerked sideways. Dren hissed through his teeth.

"Sorry. I'm sorry."

"Just do it," Dren said. "Don't talk about it."

Kem repositioned the needle — a curved sewing needle Vel had bent with pliers somewhere in the first week of travel, kept in a cloth wrap in her pack alongside thread she'd waxed with tallow. The wound ran from Dren's forearm nearly to his elbow, a ragged opening where silo shrapnel had torn through the sleeve and the flesh underneath. The blood had slowed to a dark seep. Vel had cleaned it with water and a rag that smelled of spirits, and Dren had sat through that with nothing more than a tightening around his eyes.

Now it was Kem's turn. Vel couldn't do it — her ribs, cracked from the fight, made the work impossible when she had to lean forward and hold the wound edges together. She'd tried and stopped after the first attempt, pressing her elbow against her side with a controlled exhale that was worse than a grimace.

"Straight through," she'd told him, demonstrating the motion in the air. "Both sides of the wound, equal depth. Pull it snug but not tight. You're closing skin, not cinching a bag."

He understood the principle. He'd been educated in principles. The problem was his hands, which wouldn't stop shaking.

The second pass of the needle was better. It caught both edges of the wound and he pulled the waxed thread through, watching the skin draw together in a puckered line. It looked nothing like the neat stitching Vel had described. It looked like a child had sewn a doll shut.

"Christ," Dren said, looking at it.

"I know."

"Keep going."

Kem kept going. The third stitch was crooked. The fourth caught too deep on one side and too shallow on the other, creating a bunched ridge of skin that would scar badly. Dren's forearm was tanned and ropey from years of loading cargo, and the muscle twitched each time the needle went in. Kem felt every flinch through the thread, a tiny telegraph of pain running from Dren's body into his fingers.

The fifth stitch was the first one he got right. Both sides even, the thread snug, the wound edges meeting cleanly. He stared at it for a moment — one decent stitch among bad ones — and moved on. The sixth was worse, the needle catching on something fibrous under the skin that made Dren grunt and turn his head away. Kem's stomach lurched but he pulled through and tied it off. The seventh was rushed because his fingers were cramping and he wanted to be done.

He was kneeling on packed dirt in a clearing half a mile south of where they'd stopped running. The late-afternoon light came through the trees at an angle that made the blood on Dren's arm look almost black. Vel sat against a tree trunk with her arms crossed carefully over her torso, breathing in the measured way she'd been breathing since the fight — shallow, controlled, the kind of breathing that said something was wrong inside her and she was managing it through force of will. She watched Kem work without commenting.

By the eighth stitch, he'd found something like a rhythm. Not skill — nothing close to it — but a sequence he could repeat: push through, pull, check the gap, tie. His hands still trembled, but the trembling had become part of the motion rather than an obstacle to it. He wiped blood and wax residue from his fingers on his trousers and repositioned for the ninth.

"You're pulling them uneven," Vel said from the tree.

"I know."

"The last two are going to scar worse than they need to."

"I know."

"Keep going."

He pulled the ninth stitch snug. The wound was mostly closed now — an ugly seam running up Dren's forearm, raised and irregular, the thread making dark X shapes against skin that was beginning to swell around the punctures. There were twelve stitches when he finished. Some of them were acceptable. Some of them were terrible. All of them held.

Dren turned his arm, examining the work. He flexed his hand experimentally, and the stitches pulled but didn't tear.

"That's bad," he said.

"Yeah."

Dren rotated his wrist again. The gash was closed. The arm was functional. "My arm's still on, though. So." He looked at Kem, and something shifted in his expression — not warmth exactly, but a recognition of effort that wasn't pretending to be expertise. "Thanks."

It was the first time anyone had thanked Kem for something he'd done badly. He'd been thanked at the Vault — for existing, for attending rites, for carrying the Aldraste name with the proper bearing. Social acknowledgment of his function in a system that required his stillness. Nobody had ever looked at work his hands produced and said thanks while the work was visibly, undeniably poor.

He didn't know what to do with the feeling, so he cleaned the needle with the remains of the spirits and wrapped it back in its cloth.

Vel hadn't moved from the tree. "It'll scar," she said.

"Everything scars." Dren wrapped a strip of cloth around his forearm, tucking the end under itself one-handed with the automatic competence of someone who'd bandaged road injuries for twenty years. The cloth turned pink immediately where it pressed against the stitches. He held his arm against his stomach and leaned back against his pack. "We staying here?"

"Tonight," Vel said. "Move at first light."

Dren tested his grip on the pack strap with the bad arm, pulling carefully. The stitches held but his jaw tightened. He let go and used the good hand instead, adjusting the pack behind his back. Kem watched him work through the calculation — not levels but capacity. The arm functioned. The arm would slow him. The arm changed what he could carry and how fast he could move and how many days he could keep doing this before the math stopped working. Dren did that kind of assessment the way other people breathed — constantly, invisibly, with the automatic precision of someone who'd been balancing risk against return since before Kem was born.

"Arm's fine," Dren said, to nobody in particular.

Kem sat back on his heels and looked at his hands. Blood under his nails. Wax on his fingers. A shallow puncture on his left thumb where the needle had skipped. He tried to remember the last time his hands had looked like this — used, marked, involved in something — and couldn't think of a single occasion. In the Vault, his hands had been clean for twenty-six years. Unblemished, uncalloused. Ready for nothing.

He checked his Ledger. The number hung in his awareness the way it always did — not visible exactly, but present, the way you knew the temperature of a room without looking at anything specific. 178.

He'd been 300 when he walked out of the Vault. That was — three weeks ago? Less? The days blurred into each other, separated by terrain and weather rather than any structure he recognized. Three hundred to 178. A hundred and twenty-two levels gone. More than a third of everything he'd had.

The number felt different now than it had at 245, after the first fight, when the drop had been exhilarating — the rush of finally using what he'd hoarded, the revelation that his body could do things besides sit in ceremonial halls. At 245 he'd felt lighter. At 178 the lightness had an edge to it. He could feel the gaps in his roster — abilities that used to sit in the background like furniture in a room he'd memorized. Combat Pulse, gone. Three Utility charms, gone. The Sustenance Charm that had fed forty people on his first night outside — he still had that one, but activating it now would cost a level he could feel the weight of, the way you'd feel the weight of a coin when you were checking what was left in your pocket.

This was what Vel had been living with for forty years. Not the poverty of it — that word was wrong, too abstract — but the arithmetic. The constant background calculation: what can I afford, what will this cost, what do I lose by choosing this over that. He'd understood it as a concept when she'd explained it. He was starting to understand it as a texture that ran through every decision in a day.

Dren was watching him. Kem realized he'd been staring at his hands for too long.

"How many?" Dren asked.

"What?"

"Levels. How many you got left."

"178."

Dren didn't whistle or wince. He just nodded, once, the nod of someone who understood numbers as facts rather than as things to react to. At 138 — or wherever Dren was now after whatever incidentals the road demanded — he lived inside the same arithmetic every day. The difference was that Dren had been doing it for decades and Kem had been doing it for three weeks.

"That'll get you a ways," Dren said. Which was as close to comfort as Dren ever came.

The quiet settled. Vel had closed her eyes against the tree. Not sleeping — her breathing was too careful for sleep, too conscious of the ribs. But resting in the way she rested, which was never fully. Dren adjusted the wrapped arm against his stomach, found a position that didn't press the stitches against anything, and went still.

Kem wiped his hands on the damp grass beside him, and when the blood didn't come out of the creases he rubbed them with dirt and water until his skin was raw and mostly clean. The puncture on his thumb stung. He left it alone.

The light was going. Through the trees he could see the sky turning orange at the western edge and darkening overhead. They hadn't eaten. Kem's stomach felt tight, not from hunger but from the sustained effort of keeping his hands steady for an hour. He was tired in a way that had nothing to do with abilities — a physical tiredness from concentration and the long tail of adrenaline, the kind of fatigue that settled into his forearms and his jaw from clenching it.

He started collecting wood for a fire. Without a Flame Charm — he still had one, tucked in the thinning roster like a single match in an otherwise empty box, but the idea of using it for a campfire now felt wasteful in a way it hadn't a week ago. He'd used Flame Charms for campfires three times in the first week of travel and Vel hadn't said anything, which he now understood was a form of letting him learn the cost on his own schedule. He found dry wood, shaved bark with the knife Vel had given him, and built the fire the way she'd taught him in the highlands — small, contained, starting with the finest shavings and adding sticks in order of thickness.

It took him four tries. The third pile of shavings caught but he smothered it by adding a branch too soon. The fourth time he was patient enough, feeding the flame twig by twig until it could handle the small wood, then the larger pieces. By the time the fire was steady, the sky was fully dark and Dren was dozing against his pack with his injured arm held close to his body.

Vel opened her eyes when the fire caught. She looked at it, then at Kem, and the corner of her mouth moved — not quite a smile, but the acknowledgment of something. She didn't comment on the fire. She just moved, carefully, wincing once at the ribs, until she was closer to the heat.

---

They ate dried meat and a heel of bread from Dren's pack. The meat was tough and oversalted, the bread going stale at the edges. Nobody complained. Dren ate one-handed, chewing slowly, his jaw working with the mechanical patience of someone who'd eaten worse things on worse nights. He went back to sleep almost immediately after, turned on his good side with his pack under his head.
