His breathing evened out quickly — the sleep of someone who could rest anywhere, a road-earned skill Kem envied with a specificity that surprised him. Three weeks ago he wouldn't have noticed how another person slept.

Vel sat across the fire from Kem. She'd pulled her knees up, arms wrapped around them in a way that was half warmth and half protecting the ribs. The firelight caught the scars on her hands — a topography of forty years of use, small pale lines across her knuckles and fingers from work Kem couldn't catalog but was beginning to understand as accumulated cost. She was fifty-eight. She looked older. She'd looked older since the day he'd met her, but he was starting to read the age differently — not as damage but as evidence.

"Where does it go?" Kem asked.

He hadn't planned the question. It arrived the way his questions arrived lately — not from curiosity in the abstract but from a specific pressure that had been building since the fight, or since the stitching, or since he'd checked his Ledger and felt the weight of 178 settle into him.

Vel looked at him. The firelight moved on her face.

"Zero," he said. "When you hit zero. What happens."

She was quiet for long enough that he thought she wouldn't answer. She did this — let silences run until the other person either repeated the question or gave up. He'd learned not to give up.

"It's not death," she said.

"You've said that. You haven't said what it is."

"No." She looked at the fire. Then at Dren, checking his sleep. Then at Kem. Something was moving behind her expression — a calculation, maybe, about how much to share and when. He recognized it because he'd been watching her make those calculations since the first day of their alliance.

"Zero is a threshold," Vel said. "Not an ending. When you hit it — when you actually reach zero, all of it gone, nothing left in the Ledger — you don't die. You go somewhere."

Kem waited. His hands were resting on his knees and he could feel the dried blood still in the creases of his knuckles.

"There's a place," she said. "On the other side. People live there. Without the Ledger." She said it the way she said most things that mattered — flat, without decoration, as if the words were sufficient and anything added to them would be waste. "No levels. No abilities. No number. They just live."

The fire cracked. A piece of wood shifted and sent a scatter of sparks upward into the dark. Kem watched them rise and die.

"A place without the Ledger," he said.

"Yeah."

"That's real."

"Yeah."

He looked at Dren's sleeping form. The bandaged arm. The steady breathing. Dren was at 138 — or near it, whatever the road had cost him since he'd signed on. A number worn down through years of trade routes and small practical spends. Dren would never reach zero. Dren wasn't trying to. Dren had a daughter and a route and a number he managed the way he managed cargo weight, carefully, fearfully, always knowing the load limit. The idea that zero could be a destination rather than a catastrophe — Kem didn't think that would mean to Dren what it was starting to mean to him.

He sat with it. The concept was enormous and he could feel his mind working at the edges of it, trying to find something to hold on to, a frame of reference. A world without the Ledger. Without the number. Without the constant presence in his awareness that told him what he had and what he could do and what it would cost. He tried to imagine that presence gone — not reduced, not diminished the way it had been diminishing every day, but absent — and couldn't manage it. It was like trying to imagine silence when you'd never been in a room without sound.

"How do you know?" he asked.

"I watched someone cross."

The words landed simply. She didn't elaborate. Kem heard the restraint in it — the careful verb, *watched*, the implication of someone else reaching zero while Vel stood on this side. There was a story in that sentence she wasn't telling him, and he could feel its weight the way you could feel a room's dimensions in the dark before your eyes adjusted. Large. Old. Full of things he hadn't been shown yet.

"Who?"

Vel's jaw moved. Not a tell exactly — she was chewing on the inside of her cheek, a habit he'd noticed only at moments when she was deciding how much truth to let out. "Someone I traveled with. Years ago."

"And they—"

"They reached zero. At a specific place, under specific conditions. And they weren't there anymore. Not dead. Just — gone. Through."

"Through to what?"

"That's what I'm going to find out."

Kem's mind kept circling back to the crossing itself. Someone had been there — alive, present, with a Ledger and a number — and then they were somewhere the Ledger didn't reach. He tried to imagine the moment of it. The last ability gone. The last number winking out. And then just your body. Just your hands and your weight and whatever you'd learned to do without the thing that had defined every choice you'd ever made.

He thought about the stitching. His shaking hands on the needle, the waxed thread pulling through skin, the trembling he couldn't stop but had learned to work around. That was what all of it would be like, on the other side. No fallback. No option to spend your way past a problem you couldn't solve with time and effort and your own two hands.

The thought should have been terrifying. It was, partly. But there was something else underneath the fear — a relief he couldn't fully name, like the prospect of putting down a weight he hadn't realized he was carrying until it began to lighten.

The fire had burned down to a concentrated heat, the flames low and steady now, the surrounding darkness pressing closer. Dren's breathing was a slow rhythm behind him — steady, anchoring, the kind of sound you stopped hearing and then noticed again. Insects in the trees. The night sounds that had terrified Kem his first evening outside the Vault and now registered as the texture of the world settling in around itself.

"A specific place," Kem said. "Specific conditions."

"Yeah."

"So it's not just — reach zero and vanish."

"No. There's a location. A mechanism. It doesn't happen by accident."

He was asking questions because that was what he did under pressure — filled silence with words, tried to assemble understanding from enough small pieces stacked end to end. But the questions were real. He needed to know. Not because the knowledge changed his next step — he'd already committed to the journey in ways he couldn't fully articulate, somewhere between the outrider fight and the stitching of Dren's arm — but because the shape of what they were walking toward had just changed, and he needed his mind to catch up with it.

"Why didn't you tell me before?"

Vel looked at him across the low fire. "Because you didn't need to know before."

That was a Vel answer. Information released on her schedule, in her judgment, according to her assessment of what was necessary and when. He'd accepted it at the beginning because he'd had nothing — no skills, no knowledge, no leverage to demand more. He was starting to accept it differently now. Not because he thought her judgment was wrong but because he'd begun to see what it cost her to carry the information alone. She'd been holding this — the knowledge that zero was a door, that there was a world behind it, that someone she'd traveled with had walked through and not come back — for years. By herself.

"Is that why you keep going?" he asked. "To get to the other side?"

"Yeah."

"Not to prove something. Not a philosophy. You just want to get there."

"I want to get there."

There it was. Under the teaching, the route planning, the careful management of his spending and her budget and the hundred small decisions that kept them moving south each day — a want. Not abstract. Not ideological. Personal. She wanted to reach a specific place for reasons she hadn't told him, and the force of that want had carried her for forty years and brought her here, sitting across a campfire from a twenty-six-year-old former aristocrat she'd recruited because she needed his levels and who was now looking at her with blood under his nails.

He didn't push further. He could see the edge of what she was willing to say, and he'd learned — was still learning — that pushing Vel past that edge produced silence, not answers.

"A world without the Ledger," he said again. Not a question this time. He was turning it over, letting it sit in his mind, looking at it from different sides the way you'd turn an unfamiliar object in your hands. "No levels."

"No levels."

"No abilities."

"No abilities."

"How do they live?"

"With their hands. With whatever they can build and grow and carry. The way people lived before the Ledger, I'd guess, or the way people in the Margins almost live now — except without the system pressing on them. Without the number."

Kem's face was hot on the fire side and cold on the other. He thought about the Vault — the ceremonial halls where nobody did anything, the Conservatory Rite where his father sat with blank eyes, the sealed wing that nobody discussed. He thought about 300, what it had felt like to have everything and do nothing with it. Then he thought about 178, and his hands, and the ugly stitches holding Dren's arm together.

"I can't tell," he said, "if that sounds like the best thing I've ever heard or the worst."

Vel almost laughed. Not quite — a sound through her nose, a small expulsion of air that was as close to amusement as the cracked ribs would allow. "Yeah," she said. "That's about right."

They sat with the fire between them. Dren slept. The insects kept their rhythm in the trees. Somewhere to the north, five disorganized agents were presumably regrouping, and Sable Orm was presumably learning from everything she'd watched, and the Hoarded Council was presumably drafting retrieval protocols in language that classified human beings as logistical concerns, and none of it felt as immediate as the fact that there was a place where the number went silent and people lived without it and Vel had spent forty years of her life trying to get there.

Kem looked at his hands in the firelight. Blood in the creases. A puncture on his thumb. The beginning of calluses in places that had been soft three weeks ago. He closed his fists and opened them.

"I'm going to sleep," he said.

"I'll take first watch."

"You should sleep. Your ribs."

"I'll take first watch," Vel said again, and the repetition closed the subject.

He lay down on the ground with his pack under his head, the way Dren had shown him on the third night of travel. The earth was cold and uneven and a root pressed into his shoulder blade. He shifted, didn't find anything better, and stopped trying. Above him through the tree canopy, stars. He didn't know their names. He'd never needed to — in the Vault, the ceiling was carved stone, and the sky was something that existed in paintings.

He fell asleep thinking about a world where you couldn't do anything except what your body and your hands could manage, and he dreamed about his father sitting in the Conservatory with empty eyes, except in the dream his father's Ledger read zero and his face wasn't empty at all, and Kem woke in the gray light before dawn with the image already dissolving and the fire burned down to white ash and Vel still sitting where he'd left her, watching the dark between the trees.


# Chapter 10

The settlement looked the same.

That was the first thing Vel noticed from the ridge — the careful placement of it, the stone-and-timber structures arranged around the communal well, the gardens along the south edge where the light was best. Twelve years, and the rooflines hadn't changed. Someone had added a second story to the storehouse near the eastern road, and there was a new fence around what used to be open pasture, but the bones of the place were exactly as she'd left them. Thessa had always been good at maintenance.

"How far?" Dren asked. His left arm hung at his side, the stitches Kem had given him pulling every time he shifted his pack. The wound wasn't infected, which was the best thing Vel could say about it.

"Half an hour down."

"And this person — she's expecting us?"

"No."

Kem was quiet for once. He'd been watching Vel since she'd angled their route west that morning, deviating from the path she'd marked on Maren's journal the night before. He didn't ask why. He was learning when to ask and when to wait, which was either progress or a problem, depending on what was growing in his silence.

The path down from the ridge was switchback gravel, eroded in the runoff channels where spring melt had cut through. Vel's feet remembered the route before her mind agreed to make it — the footing at the second switchback, the place where the gravel thinned and the rock underneath slipped if you hit it wrong. Twelve years, and her body still knew the grade. The heat of early summer sat on the valley like a hand. Dry air, the smell of baked clay, and something growing in the gardens below — beans, maybe, or the low herb rows Thessa had always kept near the south wall.

They reached the settlement's northern edge and Vel saw the cart.

It was parked outside the storehouse, clean-sided, well-built, and entirely wrong for this place. Not a trader's cart — the hardware was too precise, the wood lacquered rather than oiled, the axle bearing Hoarded-standard machining that no local smith could produce. A logistics cart, the kind that showed up when someone institutional wanted to look like they belonged.

She put her hand out and Kem stopped. Dren was already reading the cart the way he read everything — calculating its cost, its origin, its implications for them.

"Hoarded," he said.

"Yeah."

"Representative?"

"Looks like."

They circled wide, approaching from the south along the garden rows where the bean frames gave partial cover. The settlement was small enough that they'd be seen eventually, but Vel wanted to see first. She always wanted to see first. The arithmetic of observation before action — the cheapest intelligence you could buy.

She saw Thessa before Thessa saw her.

Thessa was standing near the well, talking to a woman in Hoarded trade dress — the tailored linen, the clean hemline, the leather document case that screamed institutional authority. The woman was maybe forty-five, average build, dark hair pulled back in a style that looked like it had been set that morning with more care than the rest of her appearance warranted. She was talking about delivery schedules and settlement quota adjustments, her voice carrying in the clear air, professional and unhurried.

Then her voice slowed.

Not like someone choosing words carefully. Not a pause for thought. The deceleration was mechanical, as if the process producing speech had lost a gear somewhere behind her eyes. Her mouth kept moving for a beat after the sound dropped away, and her gaze — which had been fixed on Thessa's face with focused attention — drifted sideways to a point approximately three feet to the left of anything. Not blank. Vacant in the specific, particular way that Vel had seen only once before, in a man at a trade post three years back who'd left the Vault at two-ninety and carried something with him he couldn't name.

Stillness. Walking around in a settlement three weeks south of the Vault, negotiating bean quotas.

The episode lasted maybe four seconds. The woman blinked. Her eyes reseated. She picked up her sentence from roughly where she'd left it, smoothing over the gap with the practiced ease of someone who'd learned to paper over these absences the way you'd patch a crack in a wall — quickly, before anyone looked too closely. Her hand, holding a stylus over her document case, hadn't moved during the whole episode. She made a note and smiled.

Thessa's expression didn't change. She'd seen this before. She'd been watching it happen, visit after visit, and she'd decided something about it that Vel would discover soon enough.

Vel's hands had gone cold. Not from fear — from the particular chill of recognition. She'd spent forty years in the field. She'd seen what spending cost. But this was the other thing, the thing the Hoarded didn't talk about: what preservation cost when you carried it long enough. The condition they called discipline, eating its way through a woman who was out here negotiating settlement quotas because the system needed someone to do it, and the system didn't care that the someone was going intermittently absent behind her own eyes.

Kem was standing close enough behind Vel that she could hear his breathing change. He'd gone very still — not Hoarded still, but the rigid stillness of someone seeing a familiar nightmare in an unfamiliar place. His father's empty eyes. Here, in a dusty settlement, in a woman who was writing notes about bean deliveries.

"That's —" he started.

"I know."

"My father does that. That exact —"

"I know what it is."

The Hoarded representative finished her note, tucked the stylus into her case, and said something about returning next month for the updated figures. Thessa nodded. The representative walked toward her cart with the measured steps of someone conserving something she might not know she was losing.

Thessa watched her go. Then she looked up, past the well, past the garden fence, toward the bean frames where Vel was standing with a Hoarded boy and a wounded trader, and her face changed twice in the space of a breath.

First: recognition. The jolt of it in her body, shoulders pulling back, hands coming away from her sides. Then the training kicked in — the persona she'd built for settlement life, the practiced approachability that had kept her safe and useful for twelve years. Her face rearranged into welcome. She said something to a passing neighbor — something light, something about needing a moment — and walked toward them.

The composure held until she was close enough for Vel to see her eyes. Under the welcome, they were furious.

"Vel." It came out like she'd been storing it. Not rehearsing — Thessa hadn't rehearsed this. She'd spent twelve years not preparing for it, and the unpreparedness was in every syllable. "Well. Come in, I've got — sit down. Let me put water on. You look —" Her eyes cut to Kem, to Dren, to the packs and the road dust and the specific wear of people who'd been moving fast. "Sit down."

"Thessa."

"I said come in." The pleasantness cranked up a notch, which meant the anger underneath had too. She was already turning toward her house — the same stone-fronted house at the northwest corner, the one she'd been living in when Vel left twelve years ago. "Bring your — are they with you?"

"Yeah."

"Bring them. There's food. Do you want bread? The bread here's actually good, we've got a — it doesn't matter. Come in."

Vel followed her. Behind her, Kem's silence had a different quality than the one he'd carried down from the ridge. And Dren's boots scuffed gravel in a rhythm that said he'd decided to reserve judgment until judgment was useful.

---

Thessa's house smelled like dried herbs and warm stone. The front room was the right size for one person who'd decided exactly how much space she needed — a table, four chairs, a cooking alcove, shelves lined with labeled jars. Everything maintained. Everything placed. The geometry of a life calculated for minimum cost and maximum stability. Level seventy-eight, spending maybe one per year. Forty years of arithmetic producing this: a clean house, a reputation, and a front door that stayed shut against the things that couldn't be calculated.

She put a kettle on the fire without asking. Cut bread from a round loaf — thick slices, the crust dark and cracked from a proper stone oven. Found cups. Poured water for the kettle. The hospitality was so practiced it was almost an ability in itself, and it served the same function: it cost something small and produced something useful and kept the larger costs at bay.

Kem sat where she pointed. Dren leaned against the wall near the door, his bad arm held close to his body, and said nothing. Vel stood.

"Sit down," Thessa said.

"I'm fine."

"You're in my house. Sit."

Vel sat.

The kettle worked. Thessa poured — real tea, not the boiled-root approximation they'd been drinking for weeks. She set cups in front of Vel, then Kem, then Dren, and sat down across from them with her hands wrapped around her own, and for a long moment the only sound was the fire popping and the settlement's ambient noise through the window: someone calling a name, the scrape of a tool, a gate swinging.

"Twelve years," Thessa said.

"Yeah."

"Twelve years, and you show up with a Hoarded boy and a man who looks like he lost a fight with a grain silo."

Dren lifted his bad arm. "Grain silo won."

Thessa didn't smile. She was studying Vel with the specific attention of someone who'd spent eight years traveling beside her and twelve years refusing to think about it. Running her own calculations. What this visit cost. What it would require. What was being asked underneath the asking.

"You're heading south."

"I need route information. The paths through to the Margins interior."

"Of course you do." Thessa sipped her tea. Then again. The pauses were structural, the cordiality a wall built of offered cups and good bread. "The southern routes have changed since you were through. The old paths past the river are washed out — flooding three years ago. There's a passable crossing two miles east of where the bridge used to be, but it's rough."

"What about the Margins interior? Past the —"

"Have some bread." The plate pushed toward her. "It's good bread. A woman here bakes it in a stone oven she built herself. No abilities, just — have some."

Vel took a piece. It was good. She chewed and swallowed and the specific cruelty of Thessa's hospitality settled over the table: it was sincere. The bread was offered because Thessa wanted her fed, and also because every cup of tea and slice of bread was a delay, a redirection, a way to fill the room with small kindnesses until there was no space left for the conversation they were both circling.

"I can't stay long," Vel said.

"You never could." The first cut through the pleasantness, so quick Thessa sealed it before the blood showed. "I mean — you've been traveling. You must be tired."

"Almost three weeks on the road."

"With him?" A nod toward Kem. Thessa studied him the way she'd studied Vel — reading the signs, the level arithmetic, the body's testimony. "You're Hoarded."

"I was," Kem said. He was sitting with the old Vault posture, straight-backed, hands on the table — the training mixed with the new directness. "It's — I'm. That's complicated."

"Always is." Thessa's gaze lingered on his hands — the particular smoothness that only Vault hands had, though calluses were roughening the palms now. She looked at his face. Then at Vel. "Vel."

"What."

"What level."

Kem opened his mouth and Vel cut in. "His business."

"I'm at one-seventy-eight," Kem said. He glanced at Vel. "It's fine."

Thessa's cup stopped halfway to her mouth. Seventy-eight herself. She knew what numbers meant. Her face did something complicated — surprise, then calculation, then a settling into exhausted recognition, like watching someone confirm a diagnosis she'd been dreading. "You found one."

"Thessa —"

"You actually found one." She set the cup down. The ceramic hit the wood harder than she'd meant. "After all that."

Vel felt the ground shift underneath the conversation. Not the stone floor, which was solid as it had been for twenty years, but the terrain between them — the accumulated sediment of twelve years cracking along fault lines that had been waiting for exactly this weight.

"I need the southern routes," Vel said. "That's all I came for."

"That's never all you come for." Thessa's voice was still shaped like accommodation, but the structure underneath was thinner now, pulled taut. "You've never once shown up somewhere just for information. There's always the thing past the thing. The next route. The next person who needs you to spend on them, or —"

She stopped herself. Took a breath. Pushed the bread plate two inches to the left — an adjustment that meant nothing except that her hands needed something to do.

"The southern routes," Thessa said, her voice reset to functional. "I can give you what I have. But the information's partial. I haven't gone south in twelve years. Because twelve years ago I decided I wanted to be alive at seventy-eight instead of dead at nothing, and you decided that wasn't brave enough for you."

"I'm not asking you to come."

"No. You just leave, and the leaving does the asking for you."

Kem was watching this with his head tilted slightly, processing. Dren was watching the door — and the window, and the path the Hoarded representative had taken — because Dren always watched exits the way Vel watched numbers, with the attentive pessimism of someone who'd learned that knowing where to run was cheaper than fighting.

Thessa stood and went to a shelf. She pulled down a folded cloth that turned out to be a hand-drawn map — the paths south of the settlement, marked in the careful hand of someone who'd gathered secondhand information from traders and travelers over years without going herself. Trade routes, water sources, danger zones.

"This is what I have. It's secondhand. I haven't verified anything past the river." She spread it on the table. Her hand lingered on the northwest corner, where the route from the settlement was drawn. "The Margins are worse than when you — when you went through before. Hoarded enforcement's heavier. Contracted labor operations that weren't there ten years ago."

Vel leaned forward. The map was useful but incomplete — distances without terrain detail, water sources marked but not confirmed. She needed to cross-reference.

She pulled Maren's journal from the inside pocket of her pack.

The motion was habitual. Eight years of consulting these pages, comparing routes, tracing the handwriting that wasn't hers along margins crowded with annotations in two different hands. She opened to the pages covering the southern approaches — Maren's careful cartography overlapping with Vel's own additions — and started matching coordinates to Thessa's map.

Thessa was saying something about the river crossing when she stopped.

She was staring at the journal. At the battered cover, the bent spine, the pages showing eight years of handling and the weather they'd taken before that. She recognized it the way you'd recognize a face — not piece by piece but whole, the accumulated familiarity of something you'd once known well.

"You're still carrying that."

Vel's hands stilled on the pages.

"Maren's." Thessa said it like a stone she'd been holding in her mouth. "That's her journal. I'd recognize that binding — she repaired it with gut thread from that trader outside Hallson's, the one who couldn't tie a knot to save his life." She let out a breath that was almost a laugh and entirely wasn't. "Twelve years, Vel. Twelve years since you walked out of here, and you're still carrying her journal, still heading south, still —"

"Thessa."

"Still chasing the same thing." The accommodation was gone now. Not cracked — lifted off, like a mask set down carefully on the table next to the bread plate. What was underneath wasn't rage. It was grief so old it had calcified into something load-bearing, something that held up the rest of her life, and Vel was standing in her kitchen pulling it out of the wall. "She crossed. I know she crossed. You told me — no. You didn't tell me. You told a trader who told Barrens who told me because you couldn't walk ten minutes to my door and say it yourself." She pressed her lips together. "She crossed and you've been trying to follow ever since."

Vel didn't answer. The journal was open on the table, Maren's handwriting beside Thessa's map, and the convergence of those two documents in this room was more than she'd been ready for. The herbs drying on the shelf above the alcove filled the air with rosemary and something sharper — thyme, maybe — and the smell was the same as twelve years ago, and Vel's ribs ached where she'd cracked them two weeks before, and she had twenty-two levels left, and the journal's pages were warm from being carried against her body.

"I don't need to explain this to you," Vel said.

"No. You never do." Thessa turned to Kem. The shift in her attention was deliberate — a blade aimed with precision. "Do you know how long she's been at this?"

Kem's eyes moved between them. "The journey started three weeks ago."

"Not the journey." Thessa's voice was calm now, dangerously calm, the kind that came only after the performed kindness had been fully stripped and what was left was bone. "Her. This. Looking for someone like you." She paused. "A Hoarded lord willing to burn down. She's been looking for years."

The room went quiet. The fire popped. Outside, someone's gate swung on its hinge.

"She came through here," Thessa continued. She was looking at Kem, not Vel, and her voice was steady in the way of a support beam under too much load — holding, but the wood grain showed. "Five years ago. Sat in that chair you're sitting in. And she asked me about Hoarded defectors. Whether I'd heard of any. Whether any lords had walked out. Whether anyone at two-fifty or above might be persuaded to spend down."

Kem didn't move.

"Five years ago," Thessa said. "She wasn't asking because she was curious. She was looking for a component. Someone with enough levels to make the crossing work." She looked at Vel. "You didn't find one then. You kept looking. And here you are with a lord at one-seventy-eight, and you expect me to —"

"That's enough." Vel's voice was quiet, which was the wrong kind of quiet — the budgeted kind, the flat practical register that meant the cost of continuing this conversation was higher than she could afford.

"It isn't." Thessa was shaking. Not the tremor of immediate anger — the deep structural vibration of something that had been bearing weight for twelve years and had just been asked to carry this too. "It isn't enough. You left me. You left everyone who loved you because the spending mattered more, and now you're doing it again with him, and he deserves to know what you are."

"What I am."

"Someone who sees levels before she sees people." Thessa's eyes were wet and she wasn't bothering with it. The tears tracked down her face and she let them, because hiding would have been another accommodation and she was done accommodating. "Who's been running the numbers on every spender she's met for five years, looking for someone tall enough to reach the shelf. I loved you. Maren loved you. And you used us both — me to learn the routes, her to find the threshold, and him —" a gesture toward Kem — "to make the crossing. You're the most generous person I've ever met and the cruelest, and you don't even know the difference."

The silence that followed was a physical presence in the room. Vel could feel it pressing against her cracked ribs. The house smelled like rosemary and thyme and bread and warm stone, and Thessa was crying — not the loud kind, not performance — the exhausted, quiet kind that came after years of words held back finally let go, the cost of twelve years of silence paid all at once.

Kem was watching Vel. His face had changed. Not anger — he didn't have enough context for anger, not yet. But something had shifted behind his eyes, a question assembling itself that he wasn't going to voice in this room. Something filed away. Troubling but not yet catastrophic, the way a hairline crack in a load-bearing wall is troubling — you don't know yet whether it's structural or surface, but you know you can't stop looking at it.

"The southern routes," Vel said, and her voice was wrong — flattened, over-controlled, the voice she used when the cost of being in a room had exceeded what she'd budgeted. "You said the information was partial."

"Don't you dare." Thessa wiped her face with the back of her hand. "Don't sit in my house and redirect to logistics after I just —"

"The Hoarded rep." Dren's voice from the wall, carrying the room the way a blunt object carries weight. He hadn't moved, but he was looking through the window with the attention of a man who'd been watching something develop for the last thirty seconds. "She's coming this way."

Thessa went still. A different kind of still — the kind Vel recognized from their years running routes together, when something practical interrupted something emotional and the practical won because the practical had teeth.

"She comes once a month," Thessa said. Her voice was unsteady but operational. "Quota reviews. Settlement reports. And —" She stopped.

"And what," Vel said.

Thessa looked at the table. At the bread she'd cut, the tea she'd poured, the map and the journal lying side by side. Then she said it, the thing she'd been circling: "They offered me a deal. The Hoarded. Three years ago. Economic security for the settlement — guaranteed supply routes, subsidized materials, a standing medical allocation. Enough to stop worrying."

"In exchange for what."

"Route information. Traveler reports. Who comes through, where they're heading, whether anyone asks about the southern paths." She swallowed. "I took it."

The information landed in Vel's chest the way a stone lands in a pack you're already carrying — not surprising, just heavier. She'd stopped being surprised by the Hoarded's reach years ago. But the weight of this was specific: Thessa, who had loved her, who had stayed behind out of a justified and reasonable fear, had been trading information to the same system Vel had spent her whole life pushing against. And the deal wasn't cowardice. The deal was survival math performed by a woman at seventy-eight in a settlement that needed a medical allocation more than it needed principles.

"Does she know we're here," Vel said.

"She saw you from the well. I don't — she's not quick, Vel. Her episodes have gotten worse. Last two visits, she lost a full minute during quota review. Just stood there, eyes gone." Through the window, the representative was crossing the open ground toward Thessa's house, her steps measured, her document case under her arm. "I've been bargaining with a woman the system's eating alive. I know what that looks like. I know what that makes me."

"It makes you someone who kept three families fed through winter."

"Don't." Thessa shook her head. "Don't make it clean. It isn't clean. She sits in my house and her speech stops and her eyes go empty and I hand her reports about people like you because if I don't, the supply routes close and this settlement starves." She pushed back from the table. "That's what stopping gets you. That's the price of being alive at seventy-eight."

The representative was forty yards from the door. She was moving with the deliberate pace of someone whose body ran on schedule even when the person inside it didn't, and there was a communication scroll in her free hand — the short-range kind that could reach a Hoarded relay station within thirty miles. If she'd already sent a message from the well, the response could be close.

"We go now," Vel said. She stood. Folded the journal closed and slid it into her pack, the pages aligned, the cover pressed flat — the habitual care she showed the object, and she could see Thessa watching her do it, watching the tenderness she applied to something that wasn't hers, and Thessa's face did something Vel couldn't afford to look at.

"Back door," Thessa said. Her voice had gone operational — the route-runner's voice, the one Vel remembered from a decade of shared decisions made fast. "Through the kitchen. She parks east — you exit west, take the lane between my place and Garran's, cut to the orchards. There's an irrigation ditch that runs south for a mile. Stay below the lip."

"Thank you," Kem said. Quiet. He was looking at Thessa with an attention that Vel found hard to watch — not because it was wrong, but because she recognized it. Kem looked at people and saw them. It was the thing she'd first noticed about him in the forest weeks ago, and it was the thing that made him irreplaceable in her plans and dangerous to them, and right now he was seeing a woman who'd just dismantled Vel's authority in his eyes and was still, in this moment, helping them escape.

"Don't thank me." Thessa was looking at Vel. "Just go. You were always going to go."

---

They went through Thessa's kitchen, past the shelves of labeled jars and a window box of herbs, through a back door into the narrow lane between houses. The settlement was small and the lane was empty and the sun was white overhead, pressing down.

Vel ran a Perception sweep as they cleared the lane. One level — quick, minimal, the most compressed version of the ability she could manage. The information hit her like a brief headache: the representative was at Thessa's front door, her communication scroll active, a Hoarded relay signal already pulsing east. Two people moving on the eastern road, fast, in the nondescript clothes that settlement enforcers wore when they wanted to blend. Response time: fifteen, maybe twenty minutes before they reached the settlement and got a direction.

Twenty-one. She was at twenty-one.

They cut through the orchards — low fruit trees, not well-tended, the branches overgrown in the way of trees that had been Fertility-Charmed once and left to manage on their own. The irrigation ditch ran along the eastern edge, a channel dug into dry earth, deep enough to walk in without showing above the lip if you kept your head down. Dren dropped in first. Kem followed, his height a problem — he had to hunch, his back curved to keep his head below the ditch edge. Vel went last and the clay under her boots was dry and cracked and the channel smelled like standing water and hot earth.

They moved south. The ditch offered cover for maybe a mile. Vel kept the Perception information running in the back of her awareness — the two enforcers approaching from the east, the representative's signal still pulsing, the general shape of what was coming — and calculated. The enforcers would reach the settlement, get a direction from the representative or from whoever had seen them leave, and follow south. Level two-hundred range, probably. Settlement-grade abilities. Not trained for pursuit, but they'd have numbers and reserves that Vel couldn't match.

"How long?" Dren asked, low.

"Fifteen minutes before they're pointed our way. Maybe less if the rep had a better look than I think."

"The concealment from before won't work again?"

Vel looked at Kem. He was moving through the ditch with his shoulders pulled in, concentrating on the terrain — the footing, the turns, the places where the ditch wall had eroded. His face was the one she'd come to know as his working face: calculating expenditure against need. The arithmetic she'd taught him.

"I can do it," Kem said. "Concealment overlay. Shield if they get close. Evasion shimmer at the edges." He said it without looking back. "Eighteen levels, give or take. Down to one-sixty."

"I know what it costs."

"So do I."

There it was — not defiance, not the exhilarated recklessness of his early spending. Just the flat statement of someone who'd learned to count and was counting. A month ago he'd have thrown power at the problem and worried about the arithmetic afterward. Now he was leading with the numbers. Vel wasn't sure if she'd taught him that or if he'd learned it by losing half of everything he had.

"Do it," she said.

His hands came up. The concealment overlay went first — not the wild, blown-out burst he'd have produced three weeks ago, but something shaped, fitted around the three of them and the ditch like a shell pulled over the lip of the earth. The light bent. The footfalls muffled. The air around them took on the visual quality of empty ground, unremarkable, nothing to see.

Seven levels. Vel felt it settle around them — the blur, the dampened sound, the shimmer at the edges where the effect met unaltered air. Not perfect. A trained eye would catch the warp. But competent, and competence from Kem still had the faint surprise of a new thing.

Vel dropped a small concealment of her own over their trail — a one-level wisp that obscured the boot prints in the ditch clay, the disturbed dust at the channel's lip, the residual signs of three people passing. It wouldn't hold for more than twenty minutes, but twenty minutes was the difference between enforcers following tracks and enforcers following guesses.

Twenty. She was at twenty. The number sat in her awareness like a word she couldn't stop hearing.

They moved faster. The ditch curved away from the settlement road into open ground, angling toward a dry creek bed that ran south through scrubby lowlands. Dren kept pace despite his arm, his face rigid with the effort of not using it for balance. The poorly healed stitches pulled at the wound every time the footing shifted, and Vel could see his jaw working, the specific expression of a man converting pain into motion because the alternative was stopping.

Behind them, the enforcers reached the settlement. Vel felt it through the Perception data still fading in her awareness — the disturbance pattern, the way ability use rippled through ambient fields when someone activated a scan. The scan passed over the ditch. Passed over the concealment. Moved on, either because the overlay held or because they weren't looking in the right place or because Thessa had sent them the wrong direction.

Vel didn't want to think about which one.

Forty minutes south, the concealment flickered. Kem's face showed the strain — not of raw power but of sustained precision, the fatigue of holding something careful rather than throwing something large.

"It's going," he said.

"How much longer?"

"Minutes."

A burst shield went up as the concealment thinned — five levels, reflexive, a defensive shell that would absorb one hit if it came. The transition from stealth to defense was smoother than anything Kem had managed before, the kind of tactical pivot that required thinking two moves ahead instead of reacting to the last one. Then the evasion shimmer — six levels layered at the edges of their movement, blurring their position if anyone used a tracking ability. The shimmer rippled and settled.

Eighteen total. His hands were shaking — not from exhaustion, from the specific fatigue of precision work, the way a carpenter's hands shake after dovetailing rather than hammering.

They cleared the ditch and dropped into the creek bed. Open ground stretched south — low scrub, dry grass, the hills rising ahead toward the river and whatever lay past it. No pursuit visible. The enforcers either hadn't found the trail or were still searching the settlement or were heading the wrong way.

Kem checked his Ledger. The pause was brief, internal, the flicker of attention that came with reading a number that mattered.

"One-sixty," he said.

One-sixty. He'd been near three hundred a month ago. The arithmetic of the descent was written in the flatness of his voice, in his steady hands even as they shook, in the way he reported the number without the frustration that would have been there four weeks ago or the exhilaration that had been there six. Just cost. Just the next number down.

They kept moving south. The creek bed turned into a trail and the trail curved toward low hills that would take them to the river and the Margins' edge beyond it. The afternoon sun was white and merciless on the dry ground, and the heat rose from the earth in visible distortion, and the air tasted like dust.

Dren walked ahead, reading the terrain with the practiced eye of a man who'd been on these roads his whole working life. His arm was bad and getting worse — the gait adjustments more pronounced now, the way he unconsciously shielded the injured side with his body turning the walk into a slow, lopsided march. He needed a healer. He needed his daughter. He was here, moving south through the heat, for reasons that had stopped being purely transactional and that he hadn't, Vel suspected, fully examined.

Vel walked behind Kem and watched the back of his head and thought about Thessa. About the word *component*. About the fact that five years ago she had sat in that chair and asked about Hoarded defectors, and the woman who had loved her — who had spent eight years beside her and twelve years refusing to think about her — had watched her do it and understood exactly what it meant.

Thessa wasn't wrong. That was the weight of it — not that Thessa was bitter, or unfair, or speaking from hurt rather than truth. She was doing all of those things, and she was also right. Vel had been looking for a Kem. For years. Not this specific person — not the particular man walking ahead of her with dust in his hair and shaking hands and a Ledger that would have been an insult in the Vault a month ago. She'd been looking for anyone with enough levels and enough willingness to burn down. A component. She'd found him, and she'd guided him, and she'd taught him to spend with economy and to fight with precision and to value each level as it went. And all of that was real. And all of that was also, underneath, the cultivation of a tool she needed.

Kem didn't turn around. He didn't ask the question Vel could feel forming in the space between them — the question Thessa had planted with the accuracy of someone who knew exactly where the foundation was weakest. *Five years. She's been looking for someone like you.* The question would grow. It would grow through the river crossing, through the Margins, through whatever came after. And when it arrived, Vel didn't know what she'd say, because the truth — that she'd needed him before she'd known him, and that knowing him hadn't changed the need, only made the need into something that could hurt — was the kind of answer that explained everything and repaired nothing.

The trail widened. The hills rose. South, toward the river and the Margins and the place where numbers ran out and something else began. The journal pressed against Vel's spine through the pack, Maren's handwriting carried on her back the way you carry something that is either a promise or a debt and might be both.

Her Ledger read twenty. Her ribs ached. The settlement where Thessa lived was shrinking behind them into the valley's haze, and Vel thought about Thessa standing in her kitchen with her labeled jars and her good bread and her deal with a woman the system was eating alive, and she did not look back, because looking back was an expenditure she had never learned to budget for.


# Chapter 11

The bridge had been gone a long time. What remained — gray timbers slumped into the current, iron bolts orange with rust — suggested years of neglect rather than sudden collapse. The southern end had rotted away entirely. The northern pilings stood like teeth in a broken jaw, water rushing between them in brown surges that carried sticks and clods of soil.

Kem stood at the bank and watched the river. It was wider than he'd expected, twenty meters at least, loud with the kind of sound that water made when it was working rather than resting. The surface moved fast and uneven, cresting over submerged rocks, carrying silt from somewhere upstream where it had rained recently. South of Thessa's settlement the terrain had changed — less cultivated, fewer fences, the road itself narrowing from packed earth to a track that was mostly suggestion. This was the boundary between the Spent lands and whatever lay beyond them.

"How deep?" he asked.

Vel was crouching at the bank, testing the current with a stripped branch. She pulled it out and studied the wet line. "It'll be waist-deep at the edges. Chest-high in the middle, maybe higher. Rain upstream pushed it up."

"So we spend."

"No."

"Bridge Conjuration would take three levels. I could span it in—"

"No."

"I've got Water Walking at—"

"You're going to cross it." She pulled the branch out of the water. The current had already stripped the bark below the waterline, leaving white wood that looked gnawed. "With your legs."

Dren was sitting on a flat rock a few meters back, working his boots off with his good hand. His left arm moved carefully — the silo wound had healed into a knotted ridge of scar that pulled tight when he extended the elbow, Kem's inexpert stitches leaving puckered seams in the skin like a badly mended seam in leather. The arm worked, but not easily. He tucked the laces inside the boots and lashed them to the top of his pack in a configuration Kem had watched him perform half a dozen times — the leather soles turned outward, the knot cinched with a trader's twist that left a tail he could pull to release. He'd seen Dren do the same knot on cargo bales, saddlebags, and once on a loose tent stake that wouldn't hold in soft ground.

"Current's stronger than it looks," Dren said, without looking up. "Don't try to go straight across. You'll end up half a kilometer downstream. Angle with it."

"Right." Kem was already assessing his options. Bridge Conjuration, three levels. He could span the river in twenty seconds. Flight Charm, two levels, but he'd never used one and the calibration problems from his first weeks still haunted him. Strength boost and just bull through—

"You heard me," Vel said.

He looked at her. She was standing now, the branch discarded, her expression the flat practical one she used when she'd already made a decision and considered the discussion administrative.

"The river's dangerous."

"The river's crossable."

He wanted to say more. He had arguments — specific, reasonable arguments about risk and resource management that she herself had taught him. But the branch of his thinking that might have pressed the point was thin and new, and the branch that said *she knows what she's doing, she's been doing this for forty years* was thick and established. He closed his mouth.

Dren went first. He hoisted his pack, adjusted the strap to favor his bad arm, and walked to the bank without ceremony. He'd done this before — river crossings were part of trade routes the way stairs were part of buildings. He stepped in and the water came to his thighs. He moved with the unhurried steadiness of a man reading terrain, each foot planted, tested, weighted. The current pushed against him and his scarred arm swung for balance, the ridge of tissue pulling visibly, but his face didn't change. He was watching the water the way Vel watched ability-use situations — where the pressure eased, where the footing was solid, where the cost was lowest.

Halfway across, the water reached his chest. He lifted his pack above his head with both arms, the bad one protesting in the set of his jaw but holding. He paused there for three breaths, adjusted his angle, and continued.

Twenty minutes from bank to bank. He pulled himself out on the far side, shook water from his pack, and sat down to wring out his shirt. His hand went to his left arm and ran a thumb along the scar — a gesture Kem had seen him make a dozen times since the healing, touching the ridge the way you'd touch a pocket to check your coin was still there.

Vel went next. She stripped her boots, tied them to her pack with a knot that was not a trader's knot but served the same function, and entered the water as if she were stepping into a room she'd visited before. She was shorter than Dren and the water hit her ribs sooner, but she compensated with a lower stance and a reading of current that came from decades of this kind of work. She didn't pause in the middle. Didn't lift her pack. Just moved across the water with the economy she applied to everything — no wasted motion, no wasted effort, the body doing exactly what was required and not one thing more.

Ten minutes. She climbed out on the far bank and wrung her shirt without looking back.

Both of them were across. The river ran between Kem and the rest of his group, loud and brown and indifferent to his situation.

He took his boots off.

---

He entered the river at the point Dren had recommended, twenty meters upstream of a flat rock on the far bank. The water was colder than he'd prepared for — mountain-fed, carrying something that felt like January even now, a cold that went past skin and settled in the bones of his feet. His ankles went rigid. He took another step. His foot found a rock slick with something green and he adjusted, testing, the way he'd watched Dren do it.

The water reached his waist. The current pressed against his thighs with a flat, steady insistence — not violent, just certain. His pack shifted on his shoulders. He gripped the straps and leaned into the push, his feet working at the riverbed, finding gravel and stone that was uneven and smooth in the wrong places.

He was a third of the way across when the bottom dropped.

It wasn't a dramatic plunge — six inches, maybe eight. But it put the water at his chest and changed the balance of the situation entirely. The current that had been pushing at his legs now pressed against his torso, and the difference between thigh-deep resistance and chest-deep resistance was the difference between leaning into a wind and leaning into a wall. He staggered, caught himself, took a step that found nothing solid and another before the first resolved.

"Angle more," Dren called from the far bank. "You're going too straight."

Kem angled. His foot slipped on something underneath and his knee went sideways and the river took the opportunity to suggest, firmly, that he lie down. He didn't. He got his balance back through a motion that involved both arms and no dignity. The pack was soaked. His clothes pulled at him, heavy in a way that made the Strength boost he'd been using to carry the pack for the last three days seem less like waste and more like basic sense.

He was halfway across. The water was at his shoulders now and the current was a presence — not the metaphorical kind, the literal kind, the way any sustained physical force becomes something you bargain with. He could feel it shifting in patterns that had nothing to do with him and would continue long after he was gone. His feet found a ledge of rock and he stopped, breathing hard, the water lapping at his chin when the current surged.

He could see Vel on the far bank. She was watching with the particular attention she gave to things she was evaluating. Dren was sitting nearby, squeezing water from his shirt.

Kem took another step. The ledge ended and his foot went into nothing and the current took him.

It wasn't dramatic. He didn't go under in a crash of white water. The river simply moved him sideways, his feet losing the bottom, his body becoming a thing being carried rather than a thing walking. He reached for the bottom with his toes and couldn't find it. His pack pulled him backward. He swallowed water — not a mouthful, a gulp he hadn't intended, and it burned in his throat and he coughed and the coughing pulled more in.

He went under.

The sound changed. The river closed over his head and everything became brown and churning and loud in a way that was not the loud of the surface but the loud of being inside something. The cold was enormous. He kicked and his head broke the surface and he gasped and the river put him under again, casually, the way you'd push a floating stick back down to watch it bob.

His shin cracked against a rock. The pain was sharp and particular — not the generalized hurt of exertion but the specific wrongness of bone meeting stone through thin skin. He used it. Pushed off the rock with his battered leg, got his head up, coughed water until his ribs ached from the coughing.

He was ten meters downstream of where he'd been.

"Get your feet downstream!" Vel shouted. Her voice competed with the river and mostly lost, but the shape of the words carried. "Feet first — don't fight it, ride it to the shallows!"

He got his feet downstream. The river dragged him over rocks — his back scraped against something that caught the fabric of his shirt and tore it and scraped the skin underneath in a long hot line from shoulder blade to ribs. Another rock caught his pack and spun him and he went under again, briefly, long enough for the cold to fill his ears and the certainty to fill everything else: the river could kill him and it was not bothering to try.

When he surfaced this time, he was near the far bank — the current had deposited him in a shallow where the water was only waist-deep and he could stand. He stood. His legs shook from cold and effort. His shin was bleeding freely, the water around it going pink.

He was forty meters downstream of where Vel and Dren waited. The crossing point with the flat rock was behind him. Between his position and theirs, the river deepened again — a channel close to the bank where the water moved fast and dark.

He waded upstream through the shallows. They were deceptive — waist-deep becoming chest-deep in two steps, the bottom shifting from gravel to clay that sucked at his feet with each stride. He slipped, caught himself on a half-submerged branch, and pulled forward.

The branch snapped.

He went down to his neck and the current in the shallow pushed him sideways and he grabbed at the bank — mud and roots, nothing solid, his numb fingers sliding off. He got his feet under him and pushed up and the clay bottom gave way and he went down again.

On the far bank, Vel took a step toward the water.

He came up. He saw her standing with one foot in the shallows, her weight shifted forward, and something about the way she was standing — not panicked, not yet, but close to the edge of her own assessment — made the situation more real than the water in his lungs had.

She was wrong. The river was more dangerous than she'd thought, and she knew it, and she was deciding whether to come in after him.

He got his feet under him on a submerged root and pushed upstream. Vel stepped back.

The next thirty minutes were the worst. The shallows grew shallower and then deepened again where a tributary fed in, carrying colder water and a raw clay smell. He crossed the tributary at neck depth, his feet barely touching, his hands grabbing rocks that were slick with green growth and offered nothing. His fingers were so numb he couldn't feel what he was gripping. He crossed by faith — the faith that the next handhold would hold, the next footfall would land, the next breath would come before the water did.

It came before the water did. Every time, by margins that felt less and less comfortable.

When he reached the crossing point, the flat rock Dren had indicated was a meter above the waterline. He climbed onto it, slipping twice on the wet surface, his hands leaving bloody smears from scrapes he hadn't noticed getting. He sat there and breathed. The river ran past him, unchanged.

He was still on the wrong side.

The final section was eight meters of deep water between his rock and the far bank. He could see Dren watching from the shore, arms folded. Vel had moved to the water's edge and was standing with her feet in the shallows, her body angled as if she might wade in at any moment and was holding herself back by conscious decision.

Kem slid off the rock. The water took him immediately and he fought it — badly, with frozen hands and leaden legs and the last reserves of a body that had never been asked to do anything like this. The current spun him. He kicked. His foot hit bottom — gravel, solid, shallow enough — and he lunged forward and the bottom held and he lunged again and the water was at his waist, his thighs, his knees.

He crawled onto the far bank on his hands and knees. Two hours after he'd entered the river. He lay face-down in the dirt and breathed mud and didn't move.

---

Dren said, quietly: "Better than I expected, honestly."

Kem did not find this encouraging.

He rolled onto his back. The sky was pale and ordinary, a few thin clouds sliding east. His body was a collection of specific complaints — the shin where the rock had cracked it, seeping blood into the dirt; the long scrape on his back where the torn shirt stuck to raw skin; the numbness in his hands that was turning to a painful tingling as feeling returned; the deep ache in his thighs and lower back from fighting current for two hours. All of it earned. None of it permanent.

He sat up. Vel was standing three feet away, wringing out the hem of her shirt. Her legs were wet to the knee. She'd been in the water. Ready to come in after him, or at least ready enough that the readiness had gotten her wet.

"You were going to come in," he said.

"You were struggling."

"You told me not to spend."

"I did."

"That could've killed me."

"Yes."

The fury came slowly. Not hot — cold, the way his body was cold, building from the specific memory of brown water closing over his head and the bottom dropping away and the long second where he'd known, with absolute physical certainty, that the river could take him and his Ledger would never change.

"Why?" he said.

Vel sat down. Not next to him — across from him, on a rock, the way she sat when she intended to say something she'd been thinking about for a while. Dren was further up the bank, laying out his pack's contents to dry, giving the conversation the studied distance of a man who had opinions he wasn't being paid to share.

"Because I thought you could make it," Vel said. "I was wrong about how hard it'd be for you."

"So I nearly drowned because you made a bad call."

"You nearly drowned because I made a bad call and you followed it."

He waited.

"You didn't look at the river," she said. "I said don't spend, and you didn't spend. You didn't check the current yourself. You didn't watch how Dren and I crossed and figure out where you'd have trouble. You didn't look at the depth markers and decide I was right. You just did what I told you."

"Because you told me—"

"I know what I told you. I'm telling you I was wrong. And you should've seen it."

He stared at her. This wasn't what he'd expected — not the admission, which was rare enough, but the direction. She wasn't apologizing. She was telling him he should have overruled her.

"The river was too much for a straight physical crossing," she said. "I should've caught that. The rain, the current speed, the width. I miscalled it. But you'd been walking next to water for weeks. You've seen what current does. You had enough to make your own judgment and you didn't use any of it."

"Because you said not to spend."

"Because I said. That's the problem."

She picked up a stone from the bank and turned it in her hands. Not looking at him. The compressed voice she used when she was working through something difficult and practical at the same time.

"Camp was different. I taught you to make a fire and you learned to make a fire. That was practice. This wasn't practice. This was a real river that could've killed you, and I said cross it and you crossed it because I said so. Not because you looked at the situation and decided restraint was the right call. You didn't spend because I told you not to. That's the same thing your father did at the Rite — just from the other direction."

The connection landed in a place that was already tender. He thought of the Conservatory — his father sitting in perfect stillness, preserving as a matter of principle. He thought of himself in the river, not spending as a matter of obedience. The architecture was the same. Only the furniture was different.

"Discipline isn't following a rule," Vel said. "If you turn 'don't spend' into something you obey instead of something you judge, you're hoarding. You're preserving restraint instead of preserving levels, and it's the same thing. It's ideology."

Kem sat with the scraped back and the bleeding shin and the sound of the river behind him, which hadn't changed at all. He wanted to argue. The river had made the argument already, and it was difficult to dispute evidence that had been inside his lungs.

"So what do I do?" His voice came out rough from the water he'd swallowed.

"You look at the situation. You decide. Sometimes spending's right. Sometimes it isn't. I can't give you a rule for which is which. You have to build that yourself."

She dropped the stone. Something in her expression shifted — not warmer, but less compressed. The look of someone who'd made an error and wasn't finished accounting for it.

"I made the wrong call today," she said. "Don't let me do it again."

He held her gaze. There was a time — recent, before Thessa's settlement — when this would have settled something between them. Vel admitting error, trusting him with the right to override her. He would have felt the reciprocity of it, the deepening.

What he felt instead was a question he couldn't resolve. Thessa's voice from five days ago, describing how Vel had come through years earlier, asking about Hoarded defectors, high-level spenders who might be willing to burn down. He didn't know if Vel teaching him to judge for himself was generosity or management. Whether building his independence served him or served her plan. The two possibilities existed side by side, and he didn't yet have the tools to tell them apart.

"Fine," he said.

If Vel noticed the flatness in the word, she didn't address it.

"One more thing." She smoothed a piece of bark flat on her knee — a habit she had when she was organizing a practical argument. "Since the Spent Towns. Your incidentals."

"What about them?"

"Flame Charms for cooking fires when you've got flint in your kit. Strength boosts for carrying your pack uphill when your shoulders can carry your pack. Light abilities in the dark when there's a torch three feet away." She ticked each one without looking at him. "You've been bleeding levels on things a match and a strong back could handle. Since the Spent Towns. Eighteen."

He opened his mouth. Closed it. He hadn't been counting.

"Check your Ledger," she said.

He checked. The number was there — persistent, precise, the Ledger's one reliable quality. He hadn't looked at it closely since Thessa's settlement.

One hundred and forty-eight.

He'd been at 160 after the escape. Twelve levels in the days since, spent on things he hadn't even registered as spending. And Vel had been watching the whole time, keeping a tally he hadn't known she was keeping.

"Eighteen levels since the Spent Towns on things a match, a torch, and a strong shoulder could've handled," Vel said. "You're at 148 not because of the fights. Not because of the escape. Because you still reach for an ability before you reach for anything else."

Eighteen. He thought about each one he could remember — the cooking fire two nights ago when his hands were cold and the flint seemed like so much effort compared to a Flame Charm. The Strength boost on the long hill because his pack straps were cutting grooves in his shoulders. The Light ability in the cave they'd sheltered in because the dark was absolute and a torch smelled bad and three levels seemed like nothing against the relief of sight. Each one small. Each one a permanent subtraction he'd made without conscious decision.

"The river says sometimes you should spend," Vel said. "The eighteen says most of the time you shouldn't. Both of those are true at the same time."

She stood up and began unpacking her wet things. The conversation was finished. She'd said what she had to say — once, as always, expecting it to land.

Dren came over with a strip of cloth he'd torn from something in his pack. "Your shin," he said. "Before it fills with dirt."

Kem took the cloth. "Thanks."

"Wasn't what I signed on for," Dren said. He ran his thumb along the scar on his left arm, the habitual gesture, checking. "The motivational drowning."

"She said she was wrong about the river."

"She was." Dren sat on his heels and looked at the water. His face had the expression Kem was learning to read — the one that surfaced when he was doing math that involved his daughter, his Ledger, the distance between where he was and where she was waiting. "She's usually not, though. Wrong, I mean. That's the thing about traveling with someone who's been doing this forty years. When they're right, you learn something. When they're wrong, it almost kills you."

He stood, brushed dirt from his knees, and went back to his things.

Kem wrapped his shin and sat on the bank and looked south. The terrain on this side of the river was different — drier, the vegetation thinner, the soil lighter in color as if something had been drawn out of it. The road, what there was of it, continued as two ruts in pale dirt. Whatever lay ahead didn't look like the Spent Towns.

He didn't check his Ledger again. The number was there whether he looked at it or not.

---

Sable built the fire herself.

Not with an ability. With a striker and steel she'd taken from the field equipment kit six weeks ago, back when the kit had been an object of institutional comedy — designed by administrators who had never lit a fire, packed by clerks who could not tell kindling from deadfall. She had learned the difference since then. She had learned it the way she learned everything: systematically, with attention to process, noting which materials caught and which smoked and died.

The sparks caught the shredded bark and she cupped her hands around the small flame, feeding it dried grass, then twigs, then larger pieces in the order she had established through five weeks of trial and revision. The fire grew. She fed it with the methodical care she once applied to quarterly audit reports, and she noticed the comparison forming and did not suppress it.

She was alone. She had been alone for three weeks, since she'd sent the remaining agents back toward the capital. Two of the five had been unable to continue — the spending had unsettled something in them that decades of Hoarded propriety had not prepared them for. The other three could still function, but their growing competence was making them harder to predict, and unpredictable spenders at level 280 were a liability she did not need. She tracked better alone. She was better at it than she had expected to be, which was itself information she was still processing.

Her camp was organized. Boots aligned by the fire. Provisions inventoried — another week at her current pace before she would need to resupply. She had a map she'd annotated in her own hand, precise small notes in the margins, and she had stopped using the Council-issued terminology for geographic features. The landmark the official map labeled "Terrain Feature 7" was a ridge. She called it a ridge in her notes now. The shift in language felt disproportionately significant.

She activated the Communication ability.

The cost was two levels. She felt it go — a precise subtraction, the specific sensation of capacity leaving, which she had learned was not pain and not loss but something between the two that the Hoarded vocabulary did not have a word for. Her Ledger adjusted: 270. She'd been 300 six weeks ago. Thirty levels spent on pursuit, on tracking, on travel, on the accumulated expenses of being alive outside a building for the first time in her adult life.

The ability connected. Lady Aldraste's voice arrived clean and cold, carrying the authority of a woman who had chaired institutional meetings for three decades and had learned to compress command into the pitch of her syllables.

"Report."

"The subjects are moving south," Sable said. "Past the boundary settlements. Direction is consistent with a Scour approach."

"Rate of travel."

"Reduced. The guide has an arm injury that limits their pace." She paused. The next sentence required her to choose between the institutional frame and the accurate one. "The Aldraste subject — Kem — his incidental ability use has declined. He is spending less on routine tasks."

"Meaning?"

"He is being trained. The Ashwick woman is teaching him to function without abilities." Sable heard herself say it and heard, also, what she was not saying — that the training was working, that the reckless spender she'd watched fight with the exhilaration of a child was becoming something more deliberate, and that she found the transformation difficult to categorize as decline.

"His current level?"

"Approximately 160. Down from 225 when he left the Spent Towns."

"That is a considerable expenditure." The administrative frost. Numbers that meant money, waste, institutional failure. But underneath it — and Sable had spent twenty years in meetings with Lady Aldraste, long enough to hear what ran beneath the surface like water under ice — there was a different arithmetic. A mother counting how much of her son was left.

"The majority was combat and defensive spending," Sable said. "The incidental losses are decreasing."

"Your instructions remain unchanged. Retrieve the subject with minimal force and minimal additional expenditure. The Council's patience with this operation is not without limit."

"Understood."

"And Auditor Orm."

"Yes."

"Your current level."

Sable looked at the fire she had built with her own hands. The flames were steady, the fuel well-arranged, the heat banking the way she had taught herself to make it bank through weeks of practice. Six weeks ago she could not have built this fire. Could not have walked eight hours on rough terrain. Could not have slept outdoors, navigated by stars, read weather from cloud patterns, or made a single operational decision that was not routed through a committee and countersigned by two superiors.

"Two hundred and eighty-five," she said.

She was at 270. The lie came out in her official voice, the voice that filed reports and presented audit findings, and it sounded true in that voice the way all official statements sounded true. She understood the institutional reason for the lie — a 30-level expenditure on a retrieval would trigger a spending review, and a review would expose the gap between what the Council had authorized and what she had done with the authorization. But the institutional reason felt like a shell around the real one, which she could not have named if pressed: the fifteen levels she had hidden represented choices she had made for herself, spent on becoming someone the Council had not approved, and they were hers in a way that nothing she had done in forty-seven years of institutional service had been hers.

"Noted," Lady Aldraste said. "Continue southward. Report at standard intervals."

The connection held for a moment longer than protocol required. Sable waited. In the silence she could hear the specific quality of a woman deciding whether to step outside the institutional frame — a frame that had contained Lady Aldraste for as long as Sable had known her, as long as Lady Aldraste had watched her husband go still and her son walk out and her house become a political problem rather than a family.

"Is he well?" Lady Aldraste asked. The words were quiet. They did not sound like a Council directive.

"He is functional," Sable said. "He is adapting to field conditions."

"That is not what I asked."

Sable thought of Kem in the fight — burning levels with the efficiency of a dropped torch, grinning afterward with a happiness that nobody in the Vault had displayed in her presence, ever. She thought of the way he asked questions — interrupting, circling back, genuine. She thought of the trader's wound and the ugly stitches and how the trader had said *my arm's still on, so thanks* and how Kem had looked when he heard it, as if someone had handed him a thing he had not known he wanted.

"He seems well," Sable said. "That is not in my report."

The connection ended.

Sable sat by the fire and watched the flames work through the fuel she had arranged. Her hands were calloused now — new calluses, still pink and tender, forming over skin that had never gripped anything rougher than a pen or the spine of an audit ledger. Her hair was pulled back in a knot she had copied from a Spent Town woman three weeks earlier, nothing like the formal arrangement she had maintained for thirty years. She was not sure what she looked like. She had not seen a mirror since the capital.

The question had been forming for weeks. Not arriving whole — building, the way pressure builds in a structure, incremental and invisible until the crack appears.

Every ability she used made her better at something. The Wayfinder had given her directional sense. The tracking abilities had given her fluency with terrain and weather. The Communication ability gave her reach. Each one subtracted from her Ledger and added to her capacity in ways the Ledger did not measure and the Council did not acknowledge.

The Hoarded said spending diminished you. That every level lost was a reduction, a step toward emptiness, toward the condition they named preservation and enforced as virtue. She had believed this for forty-seven years. She had organized her career, her identity, and her understanding of what a person was around the principle that keeping was strength and spending was erosion.

But she was more capable than she had ever been. She could build a fire, read terrain, walk a full day on bad ground, make decisions without a committee, and sleep on the earth. She could track a man across a landscape she had never seen. None of these capacities appeared on a Ledger. All of them were real.

She reached for the institutional frame out of habit, the way she reached for the striker when she needed fire.

*Preliminary finding: expenditure of levels correlates with increased functional capacity in the field. This observation is inconsistent with the Council's preservation framework. Recommend further assessment.*

The language sat on top of the real question like a report cover on an audit. Keeping the contents contained, presentable, deniable.

She let the cover slide.

*Does spending make you more?*

She did not write it in her field notes. She did not file it in the observation log she had been maintaining out of bureaucratic reflex. She sat with the fire she had built and the calluses on her hands and the number 270 that was hers, and she let the question remain where it was — unprocessed, uncategorized, without a protocol for what to do next.

The fire cracked. A log shifted and sent a constellation of sparks upward into the dark. She watched them rise and vanish and did not reach for an ability to track their path, though she had one that would have worked. She let them go.


# Chapter 12

The soil was the color of chalk. Not clay-pale or drought-faded — chalk-pale, the specific exhaustion of ground that had been asked for everything it could give and then asked again. Vel had seen depleted farmland before, in settlements where Fertility Charms had been overworked for a generation. This was different in scale. The depletion went to the horizon, south and east and west, the fields marked by wooden stakes driven into ground too tired to hold them upright. Half the stakes leaned. Nobody had straightened them. Nobody had the levels to care about stakes.

They'd crossed the river two days ago. Since then the land had changed in stages — first the forest thinning, then the soil lightening, then the structures getting worse. Now there was no pretending they were anywhere but the Margins.

The heat didn't help. Early summer in the south was dry and heavy, the kind that pulled moisture from skin and pack straps and road alike. Dren's canteen was half-empty by midday and he hadn't mentioned it.
