Kem had stopped reaching for Sustenance Charms weeks ago — one of the economies Vel had hammered into him — but she could see his hand drift toward his belt pouch and stop, drift and stop, the old reflex surfacing and being caught. He drank water and kept walking. She gave him that much credit.

The first settlement appeared around noon: a cluster of low buildings arranged around a central well, the structures showing the seams of cheap Shaping work. Walls that met at angles that weren't quite right. Rooflines that sagged where the original builder had run out of levels mid-construction. The Shaping itself had been competent. The specifications hadn't cared about the people living under them.

A woman sat outside the nearest building on a stool, shelling dried beans into a wooden bowl. She looked up when they approached. Her eyes went to Kem first — his height, his hands, the boots that were battered now but still identifiably Vault-made — and her face arranged itself into something neutral and practiced, the expression of someone who'd learned that strangers with good boots didn't usually bring good news.

"Water?" Vel asked.

"Well's there." The woman nodded toward the cistern. "Thirty-two people drawing from it, so take what you need and move on."

"We're passing through," Vel said.

"That's what they all say."

Vel filled their canteens. Seven people were visible in the settlement. All of them moved with a specific economy she recognized — careful, minimum-effort, the motion of people who'd spent most of what they had and felt the cost of every gesture. She'd moved like that herself for years. The careful walk, the deliberate hands. Before Kem, before she'd had someone else's levels to budget alongside her own.

The notice board was by the well. Proper construction — Hoarded administrative hardwood with iron fittings, the kind of thing a district overseer would have had installed. The notices on it were layered deep, older ones buried under newer ones, the whole thing a geological record of bureaucratic attention paid to a place that had never received any other kind.

The top notice was current. Hoarded administrative stock, cream-colored paper:

NOTICE OF PRODUCTIVITY RECLASSIFICATION — MARGINS DISTRICT 7, SECTOR 14-B

Pursuant to Directive 1172 (amended), all residents of the above-designated sector are hereby classified under the revised Productivity Tier system:

TIER ONE (Levels 40+): Full daily output expected. 0.3 levels-per-annum standard rate. Interest on existing ability-debt accrues per schedule (Appendix C-7, Table 3, subsection ii).

TIER TWO (Levels 15–39): Reduced output. 0.15 levels-per-annum. Supplementary manual labor allocation may be assigned. Interest rate adjustment per Formula 9-B (revised).

TIER THREE (Levels 5–14): Maintenance category. Residents classified as Renewable Expenditure Units and may be reassigned to Development Opportunity programs at District Overseer discretion.

TIER FOUR (Levels 1–4): Non-productive. Account closure processing per Standard Protocol 6.

APPEALS: Reclassification appeals may be submitted to the Bureau of Expenditure Oversight, Northern Administrative Center, during standard business hours (first and third Wedgeday of each month, 10:00–14:00). Filing fee: 2 levels.

Vel read it twice. The first time as language, the second as math.

"Renewable Expenditure Units," Kem said. He was reading over her shoulder. His voice had that careful, too-even quality it took on when something from the world outside the Vault didn't fit any framework he'd brought from home. "That means people."

"Renewable because they have children," Vel said. "Children are born at three hundred."

Kem's hand closed on the edge of the board. "And the filing fee. Two levels. If you're Tier Four, you've got one to four levels left and the fee to appeal your classification is —"

"Half of everything you have."

He stared at the notice. She could see him working the rest of the math — the interest formulas in Appendix C that no one here could read, the daily output quotas, the five-year Development Opportunity programs that reduced your debt by two percent per year so that after thirty years of labor you'd still owe more than forty percent of the original amount. The system wasn't designed to pay off. The system was designed to run.

Dren hadn't stopped at the board. He was standing thirty feet down the road, watching south. His bad arm hung slightly forward, the way he carried it now — Kem's stitches had held but the flesh underneath had healed tight and wrong, and Dren protected the arm like a thing he couldn't afford to break a second time.

"We moving?" he said.

Vel pulled the notice from the board. It tore along a nail hole. Underneath was an older one, its ink fading to brown:

DEVELOPMENT OPPORTUNITY PROGRAM — COHORT 47
Duration: 5-year rotating assignment (renewable)
Daily ability expenditure requirement: Per Tier classification
Compensation: Room, board, and 2% annual reduction in outstanding ability-debt balance

She folded both notices and put them in her pack. She didn't have a reason. She took them anyway.

***

They passed two more settlements by mid-afternoon. Each showed the same marks: cheap Shaping, depleted soil, careful people. The notice boards varied in age but not in content — Productivity Tiers, Expenditure classifications, debt schedules referencing appendices nobody here had ever seen. In one settlement, someone had written across the bottom of a notice in charcoal: *formula doesn't balance — asked district office — they said resubmit next quarter*. The handwriting was shaky. Tier Three, Vel guessed. Or Four.

In the third settlement, they found the labor operation.

It was visible from a quarter mile: a compound surrounded by a wall of Shaped stone, about eight feet high, the Shaping layered thick enough that the stone had that fused, seamless look of serious ability investment. Inside, the tops of low buildings were visible, and people moving between them. Workers. They came and went through a single gate that a woman in her fifties controlled.

The enforcer sat on a chair by the gate with a ledger book in her lap and a clay cup on a crate beside her. When workers passed through, she checked names. When they came back in, she checked again.

"Debt work," Dren said. He'd seen it before. "Hoarded contract. Workers spend their daily quota, she marks it, end of the season the district office processes the reduction. In theory."

"In theory," Vel said.

"In theory."

A child sat against the compound's outer wall, in its shade. Maybe eight years old, small for the age, with the wrists of someone who'd never had enough of anything. His face was flushed — not sun-flushed but fever-flushed, the glassy heat of infection. An older girl sat beside him, twelve or thirteen, with the watchful, used-up face of someone who'd been responsible for too long. She had her hand on the boy's forehead. She'd been sitting like that for a while. You could see it in the set of her back.

Kem saw the child at the same time Vel did. She watched his stride change — the slight hitch, the redirection, his body deciding before his mind had caught up.

"Don't," Vel said. Quietly. To herself or to him — she wasn't certain which.

***

Kem knelt beside the child. The girl pulled her brother closer.

"He's got a fever," Kem said.

"I know he's got a fever." The girl looked at Kem's boots, his hands, the remains of Vault that he still carried. "He's had it three days."

"Three days is too long. Has anyone —"

"Has anyone what? Healed him?" She said it the way you'd say *has anyone flown him to the moon*. "With what?"

Kem looked at Vel. She knew what he was asking. She also knew what the answer cost — not to the boy, but to the cascade, the mechanism, the plan she'd spent eight years building. She didn't say yes. She didn't say no.

He put his hands on the child's forehead and closed his eyes. The Curative ability came as a clean pulse of warmth that Vel could feel from six feet away, like a door opening into a heated room. Kem's calibration had improved — this was targeted, precise, the fever and infection only. Eight levels. Not twenty. Not forty. The boy's face lost its feverish tightness and relaxed into something that looked like ordinary sleep.

The girl stared at Kem. She touched her brother's forehead, then his cheek. Her hand was shaking. "How many was that?"

"Eight," Kem said. He stood up, and Vel could see the change — slight, the way spending always registered as a simultaneous lightening and heaviness. He was at 140.

"Thank you," the girl said. It didn't sound like thank you. It sounded like something much harder — gratitude pressed up against the fury of having needed a stranger's charity in the first place.

Vel did the math. Her cascade projection assumed Kem arriving at the Scour with approximately 90 levels — enough for a sustained descent to stabilize the threshold alongside her own. He was ten below budget. She could adjust. She'd adjusted before.

Then Kem walked toward the compound gate.

"What is this?" he asked the enforcer.

The enforcer looked up from her ledger. "Labor compound. Contract work under District authority."

"And the wall?"

"Compound perimeter. Workers stay on-site during assigned shift. Per regulation."

"How long's a shift?"

"Sunrise to sunset. Per regulation."

"And if someone needs to leave? If their kid's sick?"

The enforcer's face didn't change. She was practiced at this. "Shift completion is required before exit authorization. Per regulation."

Kem looked at the wall. Vel saw his hand flex at his side — the reflex that preceded every ability use. He'd done it before the river, before every fight, before every decision to spend.

"Kem," she said.

He didn't look at her. He put both hands flat against the Shaped stone and pushed.

The Disruption came as a sustained wave — not a single blast but a modulated pressure, weeks of Vel's teaching translating into something close to control. The Shaping cracked along its seams first, the fused layers separating with a sound like ice breaking on a river. Then the whole section — fifteen feet of wall, the gate, its mechanism — dissolved into rubble and pale dust. The enforcer fell off her chair. Workers on the other side stood blinking in the sudden daylight where the wall had been.

Twenty levels. He was at 120.

Vel's cascade math was now deeply, specifically wrong.

"You can leave," Kem told the workers. Some were already moving. Others stood where they were, staring at the gap as if it might rebuild itself. One woman picked up a tool she'd been using and set it down again, carefully, in the exact spot where it had been, as if that mattered. Then she walked through the opening without looking back.

The enforcer got up from the dust, ledger book clutched to her chest. "You've destroyed Hoarded-contracted infrastructure. There'll be — the district office will —"

"Send the invoice to House Aldraste," Kem said.

Vel did not laugh. She wanted to. She was also watching the number 120 settle into her calculations like a stone dropped into water, the ripples spreading through every projection she'd built.

The backup arrived within the hour. Three enforcers from a larger compound to the south — two men and a woman, all in the fifty-to-seventy range by their movement, all with the hard-use build of people who'd spent careers on enforcement rather than labor. They assessed the rubble, the dispersing workers, and Kem sitting on a chunk of fallen wall drinking water, and drew the correct conclusions.

The first one opened with a Combat Pulse aimed at Kem's chest. Kem threw up a shield that caught it — the detonation punched the air and blew dust in a circle around him, and Vel felt the shield's cost like a flinch: four levels, maybe five. The second enforcer was already flanking left, and Kem read it — a Perception flash, two levels, his head snapping toward the movement before the man had committed. The third enforcer hung back, gauging.

Then all three pressed at once. Kem's defensive barrier was rough but solid, a sustained field that held while the enforcers hammered it with everything they had. The air around the barrier rippled with spent energy. Nine levels to hold it. Vel counted them in the pressure fluctuations.

He dropped the barrier and hit the first enforcer with a targeted Force Strike before the man could reset — three levels, clean, the enforcer going down hard and staying down. The second caught a Strike to the shoulder — three more levels, and the man's arm went slack. The third enforcer looked at her two colleagues on the ground and at Kem, who was breathing hard and bleeding from a gash on his forearm where a glancing pulse had opened the skin, and she turned and ran.

Thirty-five levels in combat. He was at 85.

Kem sat down in the rubble. He tore a strip from the hem of his shirt and wrapped his forearm, the blood soaking through immediately — a dark, spreading stain. Two months ago he would have healed the cut with an ability and not thought about the cost. Now he tied the cloth tight with his teeth and flexed the hand to check the tendons. Field triage. Vel had taught him that, in the first week, kneeling over a scraped knee he'd wanted to burn three levels on.

A worker came back through the gap in the wall. An old man, sixty or seventy, stooped and slow. He walked to the enforcer's chair, which was lying on its side in the dust, and he righted it. Then he sat down in it. He didn't do anything else. He just sat there, in the enforcer's chair, and looked out at the road where the enforcers had gone.

Nobody said anything about it. Vel watched the man sit in the chair and thought about power and what it looked like when it shifted — not in a grand way, not in a way anyone would write down, but in a sixty-year-old man sitting in a chair he hadn't been allowed to sit in that morning.

Vel stood at the edge of the rubble and did the arithmetic. Her cascade projection needed Kem at 90. He was at 85. She was five levels below the minimum she'd calculated, and Kem was wrapping his arm with torn cloth and wearing the expression of someone who wasn't finished.

She needed to tell him. She needed to say: *every level you spend on these people is a level we need at the Scour.* She needed to explain that the cascade mechanism wasn't elastic, that the threshold required a minimum sustained descent, that his charity was consuming the only resource that stood between them and failure.

She opened her mouth.

And closed it. Because she looked at the workers walking through the gap in the wall. At the girl sitting with her sleeping brother, his fever broken. At the enforcer's ledger lying open in the rubble, its columns of names and daily ability-debt calculations baking in the sun.

Stop spending on people who need help. That was what the Hoarded said. Preserve. Husband your resources. Let the expendable be expended. She'd built her entire life in opposition to that sentence. She couldn't say it now because the person she'd need to become to say it was the person she'd spent forty years refusing to be.

Kem spent the rest of the day.

The collapsed irrigation channel came first: a section that served four nearby settlements, built by Hoarded contractors twenty years ago and left to rot when the contract expired. Kem knelt in the dry ditch and poured sustained Shaping energy into packed earth and stone, rebuilding the channel with the blunt, functional craftsmanship of someone who'd learned by breaking things for weeks before trying to build them. It wasn't elegant. When upstream water began flowing through for the first time in what the settlement people said was three years, a woman waded into the ditch and stood with the water running over her ankles, touching the rebuilt wall of the channel with her palm. She stood there for a long time. She didn't say anything to Kem. She didn't need to.

Fifteen levels. He was at 70.

Then the smaller things, which weren't smaller to the people who needed them. A man with a broken finger he'd been splinting with sticks for two weeks — three levels of healing, twice what a competent healer would charge but half what it would have cost Kem a month ago. A wall about to collapse onto a house where four people slept — five levels of structural reinforcement, rough but solid. A woman whose vision had been clouding for years, cataracts thickening because nobody in the Margins could afford the Ocular ability. Kem hesitated on that one. He'd never done it before. He did it anyway — six levels, and it wasn't perfect, the woman said she could see shapes now and colors and her daughter's face but the edges were still soft. Kem told her he was sorry it wasn't better. She said he shouldn't be sorry. She said *shapes and colors* like she was holding something she'd expected never to hold again.

A child's infected tooth: two levels. The child cried during the healing — not from pain but from the strangeness of an ability being used on her for the first time in her life, the warm invasive pulse of someone else's energy moving through her jaw. Her father held her and looked at Kem the way the girl with the feverish brother had looked at him — that complicated expression, gratitude and anger occupying the same face.

A man's chronic pain from a years-old injury that had healed crooked and wrong: four levels. Kem asked the man where it hurt. The man showed him — a hip, twisted in a fall from a half-collapsed structure, the kind of workplace accident that would have been healed in ten minutes in the Spent Towns and in thirty seconds in the Vault and that here, in the Margins, had become four years of limping. Kem fixed what he could. The man walked three steps and stopped and walked three more, testing, the way someone tests ice before committing their weight.

Twenty levels in small interventions. He was at 50.

Vel spent one of her own — a Perception sweep, mapping the local terrain and the road south, filing landmarks and distances. A level she could barely afford, but she worked better when she could count things. Nineteen to eighteen.

Dren had been quiet all day. He'd watched Kem heal the child and said nothing. He'd watched the wall come down and said nothing. When Kem was rebuilding the irrigation channel, Dren had walked into the ditch and started carrying rubble out of the way — manually, his bad arm tucked against his body, his good arm doing the lifting. He didn't ask. He just worked.

Now he sat on a stone at the edge of the repaired channel, watching the water run. His face had the specific look of a man doing arithmetic he didn't want to finish. He was at 130. The people around him were at 4, at 8, at 15. The distance between Dren and the floor of the Margins was 126 levels. A month ago that would have felt like wealth. Sitting here, watching a sick boy sleep because a stranger had spent eight levels on him and watching a woman wade in running water because a stranger had spent fifteen on a ditch, the number 130 looked different. It looked like the distance between his daughter and the people in this settlement — a distance measured not in levels but in the presence or absence of someone willing to spend.

***

They found Fallow an hour before dark, at the edge of the furthest settlement.

Fallow's structure was smaller than the others and better maintained, in the particular way of someone who had almost nothing and kept it precise. A patched roof. A swept floor visible through the open door. A wooden chair with one leg shorter than the others and a folded cloth wedged under it, the cloth re-folded and repositioned enough times that it had worn thin in the middle. Outside, a garden held three rows of something stubborn growing in the chalky soil — each plant staked with a twig and tied with strips of rag, the rows dead-straight, the spacing exact. The garden hadn't produced much. But it was tended with a discipline that put most of the Hoarded to shame.

Fallow was sitting in the chair when they arrived. Level 4. Vel didn't need a Perception ability to tell — at that level, the conservation was visible in everything. The way Fallow turned their head to look at the visitors, the rotation itself measured. The way their hands rested on their knees without fidgeting, because fidgeting was wasted motion.

Sixty years old. Gaunt past their years. Skin creased deep by three decades of field sun. Hands that were mostly tendon and callus, the fingers permanently curved from tools they'd held so long the shape had become structural. Fallow wore a shirt mended in seven visible places, each repair in a different-colored thread — brown, white, blue, red, green, yellow, brown again — as if the available thread changed with the years. The effect was almost festive, in a way Fallow clearly didn't intend or notice.

"Sit," Fallow said. There was one chair and they didn't gesture at it. "Ground or wall. Chair's mine."

They sat. Dren leaned in the doorframe.

"Dren said you know the routes south," Vel said.

"I know the routes everywhere around here. I've walked most of them at one point or another." Fallow's eyes moved from Vel to Kem. They lingered the way everyone in the Margins lingered on Kem — assessing, categorizing, placing him in the ledger of who had what and who didn't. "You're the one who broke Danner's wall."

"Danner?"

"The enforcer. She's been running that compound six years. Longest operation in this district." Fallow said it without judgment. The wall was a fact. Its absence was another fact. "She'll file a reconstruction request with the district office. Takes three weeks to process. Four more for a Shaper to come down. Your people get maybe seven weeks before it's back up."

Vel felt that land. Kem had spent twenty levels and bought seven weeks.

"What were you before this?" Kem asked. He was leaning forward, the way he did when he was genuinely working through something — not performing interest, just present.

"I was at a hundred and forty when they put me in the fields. Development Opportunity, they called it." Fallow spread their hands on their knees. The calluses had their own geography — ridges and valleys from decades of tools and soil. "Thirty years of development." They looked at their hands the way someone might look at a receipt for something they hadn't ordered. "Very developed. Look at me."

The line was flat. Not delivered for effect — offered with the weary timing of something said too many times to too few people who could change anything. The comedy was in the distance between the Hoarded's language and the body in the chair. Development Opportunity. A hundred and forty to four.

Kem was quiet for a long time. Vel watched him let go of the impulse to categorize, to respond, to make it mean something. He just sat with it.

"My mother grows the same thing," Dren said. He was looking at the garden. "In her yard back home. Those're ash-beans, right? Alkaline soil?"

Fallow glanced at him. Something shifted in their face — faint, a flicker of the person they'd been before thirty years of development had compressed them down to facts and silence. "They don't produce much."

"Hers either. She says they're stubborn enough to be worth the effort."

"Yeah. They're worth it." Fallow turned back to Vel. The flicker was gone. "You want the south routes."

"I want to know about the Scour," Vel said. She kept her voice even.

"Three days south. The land drops into a basin — big, all rock and wind and nothing growing. Old mining territory. The Hoarded ran Shaping crews down there sixty, seventy years back. Stripped it and left."

"What happens there?"

Fallow was quiet for a moment. Not for drama — the silence of someone conserving the energy of speech. "People go to zero. It isn't common. Couple times a generation, maybe. Someone's so far down there's nothing left, and they end up at the Scour because there's nowhere lower to go. And they hit zero and they're gone."

"Gone," Kem said.

"Not dead. Not a body on the ground." Fallow's voice didn't change register. They could have been describing rain. "They're standing there and then they're not. I've seen it twice. First time was a woman named Sera. She'd been at two for months, just barely holding on. She walked south one morning. I was on a field crew near the basin's edge and saw her from a distance. She was standing on the rock floor, and then she wasn't. Like she stepped through something I couldn't see."

"And the second?"

"Man called Hoster. That was maybe eight years ago." Fallow rubbed the side of their jaw — a gesture that looked habitual, something to do with their hands when a memory required effort. "Same thing. He walked down, stood on the rock, and he was gone. There was a flash of something — light coming through a gap, like a door closing. I was further away that time. Could've been sun on the stone. Wasn't, though."

Eight years ago. Vel's chest did something complicated — not a lurch, not a tightening, but a kind of settling, like a weight she'd been holding shifted to a position she could carry.

"Nobody talks about it," Fallow said. "What's the point? People at zero disappear. It's another thing that happens to people nobody protects. The Hoarded don't come down here to investigate. Margins people don't have the levels to care about mysteries." They looked at the garden through the open door. "Sera and Hoster are just names now. Another thing that happened."

Vel reached into her pack and pulled out Maren's route journal. The leather was soft from years of handling, the pages warped from humidity and sweat and the oils of hands that had held it across hundreds of miles. She opened it to the southern section — the Margins passages, the pages where Maren's handwriting was densest.

The annotations for this region were detailed. Maren had come through here. Vel traced the routes in handwriting she knew better than her own — distances between water sources, terrain notes, settlement names that might not exist anymore. On one page, Maren had drawn a rough map of the basin Fallow was describing. An oval, hash marks for the descent, a small x on the basin floor. Beside the x, in Maren's careful script: *here*.

"Route journal?" Fallow asked.

"Yeah."

"That handwriting's not yours."

"No."

Fallow watched Vel handle the pages. They didn't push. They turned to Kem instead. "You're the one at — what, fifty now? After today?"

"Fifty," Kem said.

"Going to zero on purpose." Not a question.

"Yeah."

"Hm." Fallow's mouth moved — not quite a smile, more the shape a face makes when it doesn't have the energy for the expression it wants. "That's new. Most people around here, zero's what happens when there's nothing left of you to take."

Vel closed the journal. She put it back in her pack with the care she applied to nothing else she owned — the leather settled against the folded notices from the board, and she fastened the buckle.

Three days south. She could feel the distance in her body, the way she felt every distance now — as cost, as arithmetic, as the narrowing space between what she needed and what she had.

***

They left Fallow's settlement in the failing light. Vel wanted hours — three days to the Scour, and every hour mattered now that her cascade math had come apart in her hands. They walked in the formation they'd developed over weeks: Vel reading the terrain ahead, Kem carrying the heavier pack because his body could take it, Dren at the rear watching behind.

The road was empty. A footbridge over a dry creek bed was about to give — Vel reinforced it with a Structural ability, one level, the wood stiffening under their weight. Eighteen now. The cost barely registered against the wreck of everything else.

They walked in silence for a while. The pale soil cooled under their feet as the light faded. Somewhere to the west, a settlement's cooking fires put a thin haze against the sky.

Dren caught up and walked beside Vel. This was unusual. He kept rear guard and didn't seek company unless he had logistics to report.

"My agreement with you was to the edge of the Margins," he said. "We're past that."

"Yeah."

"Payment was for guide work. Routes, getting you through the Spent Towns and pointed south. That's done."

"Yeah, it's done."

He was quiet for another fifty paces. Vel could hear his breathing — steady, a walker's breath, but with a hitch on the left side where his bad arm moved against his ribs.

"I'm not leaving yet," he said.

Vel looked at him. His face was set in the dying light — not resolved exactly, but committed, like a man who'd finished a calculation and didn't like the answer but wasn't going to run it again.

"I'm not asking for more payment," he said. "I'm just — I'm staying on."

"Okay."

"Don't ask me why."

"I won't."

They walked. Behind them, Kem was a dark shape against the darkening road. Vel didn't look back. She could feel his depleted Ledger in the space between them like a change in air pressure — 50 where there had been 148 this morning, 98 levels poured into strangers and structures and suffering that hadn't been part of her plan.

Her plan. She'd built it over eight years, and it had assumed a version of this journey where the human cost of the Ledger system stayed at a distance — a political fact, a moral position, something she could manage alongside the cascade projections. The Margins were not at a distance. The Margins were a girl who couldn't afford to save her brother, and a wall built to keep people productive, and a sixty-year-old at level 4 tending three rows of ash-beans in soil that had nothing left to give.

She'd spent her life treating spending as the brave choice. The right thing. Jump in. Heal the sick. Break what needs breaking. The Hoarded hoarded and Vel spent and that had been the axis her whole life turned on.

But the people here hadn't chosen to spend. They'd been spent. The system had extracted from them the way it extracted from the soil — on schedule, under administrative oversight, with appendices. Fallow hadn't spent bravely. Fallow had been spent productively, for thirty years, under a program the Hoarded called a Development Opportunity.

Vel adjusted her pack. The route journal pressed against her spine through the fabric, Maren's handwriting a weight she carried the way she carried everything Maren had left behind.

Kem's footsteps were steady behind her. Fifty levels. She couldn't fix the math by counting, and she couldn't tell him the math was broken, and she couldn't tell him to stop because stopping was the thing she'd sworn she'd never do.

The Margins stretched ahead of them in the dark. Three days to the Scour. Somewhere south, in a basin of wind-scoured rock, people disappeared when they hit zero — not dead, not gone, but elsewhere. Vel had watched it happen once. Eight years ago. A flash of light like a door closing. Maren there and then not there.

She walked south, and the arithmetic walked with her.


# Chapter 13

The road had been bad for two days. Not road — packed earth over old Shaping work, the kind of infrastructure the Hoarded contracted fast in the Margins and never maintained. Kem could feel it through his boots: shallow subsidence where structural charms had eroded, cracks running through compacted dirt, root systems pushing up from plants that should have been dead years ago but kept going anyway because nothing in the Margins died cleanly.

He was at forty-three.

Seven levels gone in two days. He could account for each one. A Light ability in a stretch of collapsed culvert on the first evening, where even Dren couldn't navigate the rubble blind. Two levels on a healing touch for a woman they'd found sitting in the brush with a broken wrist, cradling the arm in her lap like something she was carrying for someone else — she'd been there two days, she said, which meant two days of nobody coming, which meant the Margins. A Warmth Charm on a night when the basin wind found every seam in their camp. A minor Strength boost to help Dren shift a fallen crossbeam blocking the only passable track, because Dren's bad arm couldn't take the weight alone and going around would have cost them half a day they didn't have. Two more on a Flame Charm and a Water Purification when they found a silted well and no dry kindling.

Seven levels. Some of them he could have saved. The Warmth Charm, maybe — Vel could have rigged a windbreak. The Strength boost — they could have gone around. But the woman's wrist had been broken and nobody was coming, and the well water would have given them cramps they couldn't afford, and some of the decisions that looked like waste were just the decisions that kept them alive and moving.

Vel hadn't commented on the spending. That was new. In the first weeks she'd have taken each unnecessary spend apart with surgical attention — the cost, the alternative, the principle. But since the labor camp, since she'd watched him burn ninety-eight levels on strangers and couldn't bring herself to tell him to stop, something in her economy of correction had gone quiet. She still tracked each spend with that flat assessment, the look that made his skin prickle because he could never tell what calculation was running behind it. But the lectures had stopped. He didn't know what that meant. He'd stopped assuming he knew what anything about Vel meant.

Dren walked ahead, favoring his left arm — the one Kem had stitched after the silo explosion, weeks ago now. The wound had healed wrong, the scar tissue pulling tight in the heat, and Dren carried the limb slightly off his body as though keeping distance between it and the rest of himself might make it someone else's problem. He hadn't complained about it. That was Dren — you mentioned a thing when something could be done about it, and when nothing could, you adjusted and kept going.

The Margins around them were what the Margins always were. Depleted farmland the color of bone dust, where Fertility Charms had been cast and recast until the soil couldn't hold anything anymore. Low structures with Shaping-smooth walls going to ruin — built straight and cheap by workers on ability-debt contracts, built to last exactly long enough for the Hoarded to extract value and not a season past that. Posted notices on stone pillars in faded ink: PRODUCTIVITY TIER REASSIGNMENT — ALL EXPENDITURE UNITS REPORT TO SECTOR DESIGNATE. Nobody had taken them down. The energy required to peel ink off a stone pillar was energy nobody here could spare.

Mid-summer heat pressed on everything. The air was thick and dry, the sun a flat white weight on pale sky. Kem's skin had changed over the weeks of travel — browned, peeling across his nose and the backs of his hands, nothing like the indoor pallor he'd carried out of the Vault. His body too. Leaner. Harder at the shoulders and hands, where new callus and muscle sat on top of bone they'd never met. He caught himself sometimes in the unfamiliar fit of his own clothes and thought: I don't recognize this person. Then he'd check his Ledger and think the same thing about the number.

Forty-three. Low enough that he could feel each remaining ability as a distinct presence — when he'd been at three hundred, the Ledger had been a wall of potential, vast and undifferentiated, everything available at once. At forty-three, it was a list he could read end to end in a few breaths. A Stabilization Charm. A defensive Barrier. A Healing Touch. A handful of combat applications he'd learned to use with something close to precision. Each one with a cost beside it in his mind, each cost now significant against the total. Spending one level at three hundred had been invisible. Spending one level at forty-three was arithmetic you did in your chest.

The ground gave out twenty yards ahead of them.

No warning — or rather, warnings that had been there for decades, legible to anyone who understood what cheap Shaping work looked like at the end of its lifespan. The structural charms holding this section of road over a shallow drainage channel had been thinning for years, the old magic eroding the way everything in the Margins eroded, and when the last thread gave the road surface folded inward with a sound that was wrong. Not a crack. A long tearing exhalation, as if the ground had been holding together through sheer habit and finally let go.

A section fifteen feet across dropped. The edges kept eating outward — road surface breaking away in bites, the collapse spreading in slow cascading failure.

Dren stopped. Vel stopped.

There was a man on the failing section. A Margins farmer by his clothes and his thinness, a pack of dried herbs on his back that he'd been hauling somewhere to trade. The ground tilted under him and he went to hands and knees and the herb pack slid sideways and he grabbed for it because it was worth something, and the grabbing shifted his weight, and the edge nearest to him gave another foot.

Kem didn't decide. He'd learned that about spending — there was a moment before the arithmetic, a place where the body moved because the situation required it and the numbers ran behind, fast and automatic, like balance or breathing. The decision lived ahead of the math.

He threw a Stabilization Charm at the fracture line.

Twelve levels.

It tore out of him with a heat he felt in his hands and his sternum and the backs of his eyes — the Ledger adjusting, a permanent subtraction, twelve points of himself deleted in the space of a single breath. The energy hit the collapsing road and caught it. Dirt and stone and the remnants of old structural Shaping froze mid-failure, the cascade of collapse stopping along a ragged line, the surface hardening in the exact configuration of its own ruin. It looked like a wave caught mid-break — road surface locked at wrong angles, exposed drainage channel underneath, the farmer stranded on a tilted platform of stabilized earth.

Dren was already moving. He dropped his pack and crossed the stabilized section, testing each step, reaching the man in eight careful strides. "Give me your hand. Leave the — yeah, leave it, we'll get it." He hauled the farmer to solid ground and went back for the herb pack. The farmer came up shaking, his palms bleeding from the broken road surface, and he looked at Kem and looked away. In the Margins, you didn't stare at someone who'd just spent for you. It drew attention to a debt that couldn't be repaid.

Vel stood at the collapse's edge, reading the fracture patterns the way she read everything — as information, as cost. Her eyes tracked the stabilized section, the structural failure, the depth of the drainage channel. She was checking whether the Charm would hold. Whether the road ahead was viable. Whether the twelve levels had been spent wisely or just spent.

Kem sat down on the road's edge.

His hands were shaking. Not from effort — from subtraction. Twelve levels gone and the Ledger had adjusted with the flat permanence it always had, the background hum of his remaining abilities shifting to a thinner key. Things that had been there minutes ago were not there now. A Barrier Charm he'd been holding in reserve — deleted. A speed ability he'd used twice in the Margins — gone. Not diminished, not weakened. Removed, as cleanly as if they'd never existed. The place where they'd been was a gap he could feel, a silence in the chord.

He checked the number.

Thirty-one.

The number settled into him the way all Ledger losses settled — not gradually but all at once, a new baseline his body adjusted to between one breath and the next. He could feel the change in the background hum of his awareness. It was quieter now. Not silent — the Ledger's presence never went fully silent above zero — but thin, a sound at the edge of hearing where it had once been a steady note he'd never needed to listen for. At three hundred the hum had been a room full of noise, too constant to register. At forty-three it had been a conversation he could follow. At thirty-one it was a voice speaking just above a whisper, and he had to hold very still inside himself to hear what it was saying.

What it said was: you have this, and this, and this, and after that there's nothing.

Two hundred and sixty-nine levels gone since he left the Vault. A number so large it felt like someone else's biography. A hundred and thirty of those had gone into other people — healing, defense, infrastructure, the woman's wrist, the labor camp, the road. The rest on his own survival and his own early stupidity. Either way, deleted. Each one a capability removed from a kit that had once held everything. He was down to the last drawer now, and he could see the bottom.

He sat with it. The number was a fact, the same way all Ledger numbers were facts — precise, irreversible, indifferent to what you thought about them. But this one was also someone else's. Thirty-one was the number Vel Ashwick had carried at the start of this journey. Thirty-one was the number she'd been living with for forty years of field medicine and ditch-sleeping and healing people the Hoarded had abandoned. When she'd first told him her level, weeks ago in the forest, he'd thought: that's barely enough to survive on. That's the Ledger of someone who's given almost everything away.

Now it was his Ledger.

"Thirty-one," he said.

Vel had turned from the collapse to look at him. Something moved across her face — a tightening around the eyes, a shift he couldn't read and didn't trust himself to interpret. She looked at him the way you'd look at something you'd been watching approach from a long way off.

"Then you know," she said, and turned back to the road.

* * *

They stopped at an abandoned farmstead half a mile south of the collapse. One of the Margins' standard ruins — Shaping-raised walls crumbling in the heat, a roof that sagged but held, a courtyard with a dead well and a stone bench worn smooth by years of someone sitting on it. The farmer with the herbs had gone his own way without a word, heading east with his bleeding hands and his pack. In the Margins, help was weather. It happened and then it was over and you didn't write about it.

Kem sat against the courtyard wall. The stone was warm through his shirt. His Ledger hummed at thirty-one, a sound he could almost isolate now — each remaining ability a distinct frequency in a chord that had been getting thinner for weeks. He could feel the shape of what he had left. A Healing Touch. A Light ability. A few combat applications. A Stabilization Charm weakened from use. The list was short enough to hold in his mind without effort, and holding it felt like holding a handful of coins and counting them and knowing you'd counted right and wishing you'd counted wrong.

Vel sat across the courtyard with Maren's route journal open on her knees. She traced a line on a page with one finger, her other hand holding the battered cover flat. The handwriting on the visible page wasn't hers — he'd noticed this weeks ago without thinking much of it. Two different hands in the journal, one neater than the other, the margins dense with annotations. Vel held the journal with both hands, with a care she applied to nothing else she owned. Everything about how she handled it was different from how she handled supplies or weapons or her own body — gentler, more specific, as though the pages could be hurt.

"I want to know what you meant," Kem said.

Dren was refilling their water from a clay jug he'd found in the farmstead, testing each mouthful before pouring. He glanced up at Kem, then at Vel. His pouring slowed.

"What thirty-one feels like," Vel said. "Now you know."

"That's not what you meant."

She closed the journal. Didn't put it away — held it in both hands on her lap, thumbs on the cover.

"You've been holding something back about zero," he said. "You told me it's a threshold. A door. Something's on the other side. You were specific about it not being death." He could hear the formal shape of his own speech — the Hoarded cadences surfacing from wherever they lived when he wasn't paying attention, the long clauses and careful diction of a person who'd spent twenty-six years in rooms where language was managed as carefully as levels. He didn't correct it. "You didn't tell me how the door works."

"No."

"Tell me."

She looked at him. The assessment look — the one he recognized from the first day she'd found him, three days out of the Vault at two-eighty-six, burning through levels on problems a child could solve. He'd read it then as contempt. He was no longer sure what else had been in it.

"The threshold doesn't open because you hit a number," she said. "It's not a lock with a key. It requires a cascade. Sustained spending in proximity to the threshold site. One person hitting zero alone can cross, but the threshold barely opens. Barely holds." She paused. Picked a different track than the one she'd started on. "Two spenders approaching zero together — the combined energy output stabilizes the opening. The threshold holds long enough for a real crossing. And if one of the spenders is descending from a high starting level — burning a large range in a compressed span — the cascade amplifies. The amplitude of the descent matters. The bigger the drop, the more energy the threshold gets."

Kem heard the mechanics. He heard the logic — two sources of expenditure reinforcing each other, the way two fires built close together throw more heat than two fires apart. It made a kind of physical sense, the Ledger responding to concentrated output rather than a single number.

He also heard the years.

"How high," he said.

"The higher the starting point, the stronger the cascade. Someone descending from two hundred. Two-fifty. Three hundred." She said the number without inflection. "The energy output scales with the starting differential."

"And you knew this when you found me."

She answered a different question. She always did that — narrowed the frame, offered a smaller adjacent truth, and you might not notice the real one sliding past. "I saw a single crossing, years ago. One person, alone. The threshold opened for a few seconds and it was — wrong. Flickering. Unstable. The person made it through, but I knew it wasn't reliable. I knew I couldn't count on a solo crossing."

"So you needed a partner." He spoke carefully, laying each word down, because the path was leading somewhere and he needed to see it to its end before he reacted. "Someone at a high level. Someone willing to burn all the way down alongside you. Someone who'd start at three hundred and spend everything between there and zero."

"Yes."

"And you found me."

Vel's mouth opened. He saw the beginning of something — a qualifier, a correction, the impulse to redirect. Then she closed her mouth and just looked at him.

Thessa's voice surfaced in his head, clear and precise. It had been sitting there since the settlement — present every time he put weight on it, sharp in the way of things you choose not to examine because the next crisis is always closer. *Vel came through here years ago. Asked about high-level defectors. Whether any Hoarded lords had walked out. Whether anyone at two-fifty or above might be persuaded to burn down.*

He hadn't confronted Vel about it then. There'd been the Hoarded representative, the escape, the eighteen levels he'd burned getting them clear. Then the river. Then the Margins, and the labor camp, and ninety-eight levels poured into strangers who needed them. There had always been the next thing. And under every next thing, Thessa's words had been working deeper, the way a splinter works deeper when you leave it alone.

"Thessa told me," he said. "That you'd been through her settlement before. Years ago. Looking for someone at my level."

"Yes."

One word. No qualification.

"How many years."

"Several."

"How many people did you consider before me."

"I heard about two defectors. One had spent down too far by the time I found him. The other —" She stopped. "She wasn't willing."

"So I was the first one who was high enough and stupid enough."

"You were the first one who was high enough and actually wanted zero."

The distinction landed and sat there between them. It didn't help.

He thought about the forest. The first day. Vel watching him stumble through basic trail navigation with the particular attention of someone taking measurements. He'd thought she was deciding whether he was worth the effort of teaching. He'd thought the assessment in her eyes was: can this person be made competent? Now the assessment read differently. Can this person be guided to spend at a rate I can work with? Does he have enough starting material? Will he last the distance?

Every generous reading of every moment they'd shared had a second reading underneath it, and the second reading was worse. The campfire where she'd told him about the drowning girl — was that trust, or was it investment? Building the emotional architecture that would keep him committed? The restraint lessons — wisdom, or budget management? And the Margins, watching him burn ninety-eight levels on strangers — had she been running the subtraction with every healing he performed, every enforcer he fought, every irrigation ditch he couldn't walk past?

He didn't know. That was the thing that sat heaviest. He couldn't separate what had been real from what had been engineered, because the engineering was good enough that it might have been real all along. Or the real parts were real and the engineering ran beneath them like a drainage channel under a road, and both were true at once, and he was going to have to live inside both.

"So that's what I am," he said. "A battery."

Dren set down the water jug. Slowly, as if the sound of clay on stone might break something.

Vel didn't answer.

"When you found me in the forest," Kem said. "When you looked at me and decided to make the offer. You weren't disgusted by the waste. You were calculating. Whether I had enough left. Whether my rate of spending was something you could work with. Whether I'd last the journey." He was talking faster now, the Hoarded composure cracking along its usual fault lines — he talked more under pressure, always had, filled silence with words because silence left room for the thing he was trying to outrun. "The teaching. The fire-making, the field medicine, the restraint lessons. You were keeping your investment functional."

"I was keeping you alive."

"Which is the same thing."

"It isn't."

"It looks exactly the same from where I'm sitting, Vel."

She put the route journal in her pack. The motion was careful and deliberate — both hands, inner pocket, the way she always stowed it. He watched her do it and thought: that's the thing she protects. That journal. Whatever it means to her. I'm the thing she uses.

* * *

He stood up.

The courtyard was small and hot and the Margins pressed in through every gap — the dead fields visible through the empty doorway, the ruined structures lining what had once been a road, the posted labor notices still legible on the farmstead's outer wall. RENEWABLE EXPENDITURE UNIT. PRODUCTIVITY TIER THREE. PROJECTED ANNUAL OUTPUT: 4.2 LEVELS. He'd read a dozen of these in the labor camp two days ago. They classified human beings by yield.

"I want to understand something." His voice had gone strange — conversational, almost pleasant. He could hear the Hoarded polish in it, the register his father used in Council sessions when he was about to end someone's career without raising his voice. He hated the sound of it coming out of his own mouth. He used it anyway. "You spent forty years healing people. That girl drowning in the canal when you were seventeen. Every farmer and every settlement since. Forty years of jumping in when nobody else would. And then you found me." He turned to face her. "You looked at me and you saw levels. You calculated my output." He gestured — at the faded notices on the wall, at the Margins visible through the door, at the landscape of extraction around them. "You recruited me as a component. That's what they do here. They look at people and see what can be extracted." He paused. His hand was still raised, pointing at the posted notice that classified humans as Renewable Expenditure Units, and the parallel was so clean it made him sick. "You replicated the system you claim to oppose. You saw levels, not a person. You just had a nicer reason for it."

The words landed in the courtyard and stayed.

Vel stood against the far wall with her hands at her sides. Her face was doing the thing it did when she couldn't find the smaller adjacent answer — when the real question was too large for the redirect she usually offered. He watched her cycle through responses. A mouth movement of a thought started and abandoned. A shift in posture that meant she'd found a qualification and discarded it. She was searching for the distinction between what she'd done and what the Hoarded did. Between her purpose and their extraction. Between the way she'd used him and the way the posted notices on the wall behind her described a system for using everyone.

She didn't find it.

The silence went ten seconds. Fifteen. Long enough that Dren, who had been sitting very still by the water jug, stood up carefully and said, "I'll check whether the road south is clear," and walked out through the courtyard entrance. His boots ground on loose stone and then it was the two of them and the heat and the Margins.

"You're right," Vel said.

The words arrived without force. Flat. Quiet voice, the one that surfaced under real pressure — every syllable weighed and placed. They didn't surprise him. The fifteen seconds of silence had already been the answer. This was confirmation.

"Not about all of it," she said. "What grew between us — the river, the camp, the conversations, the labor camp, all of it since — that was real. I didn't plan it. I couldn't have."

"But the starting point."

"The starting point was me needing someone at your level and finding you and making a deal that served my purpose." She said it without inflection. Not apologetic. Not defiant. Just the shape of the thing. "Yes."

"And the spending guidance. The restraint training. That wasn't discipline — it was resource management. You were protecting your cascade budget."

She flinched. Small — a contraction in her expression that most people wouldn't have caught. He caught it because he'd been watching her for weeks and because he'd been trained by twenty-six years in the Vault to read faces that never said what they meant.

"Both," she said. "It was both. I was teaching you because you'd have died without it, and I was managing the cascade because I needed you to have enough left at the end. I can't separate them. I've tried."

"That's convenient."

"It's true."

"Those aren't the same thing."

She didn't answer. She looked at the wall behind him, at the Shaping-smooth surface going to dust, at the heat shimmering through the courtyard entrance where Dren had gone.

"I can't make the origin different," she said. Her voice had dropped to something barely audible — not trailing off, just conserving, rationing even the energy of speech. "I found you because I needed your levels. Everything after happened because you're a person, and I couldn't have predicted what kind. But I can't cut the beginning off and hand you the rest and pretend the rest stands on its own." A pause. "It doesn't."

Kem stood in the courtyard and felt the heat on his skin and the thirty-one humming at the edge of his mind and the specific weight of the word *component* sitting in his mouth like a stone. He looked at the labor classification notice still posted on the farmstead wall. The Hoarded had looked at the Margins and seen energy to extract. Vel had looked at him and seen energy to extract. The distinction was supposed to be that she cared about him and the Hoarded didn't care about anyone. But the structure was the same. The arithmetic was the same. And in the Margins — standing in the wreckage of a system that had ground generations down to nothing through precisely that arithmetic — the caring didn't undo the structure. It just made the structure harder to see.

"No," he said. "It doesn't."

He could leave. The thought surfaced on its own, automatic — the obvious response to discovering you've been used. Walk away. Go north. Find a settlement, offer his remaining levels for food and shelter, live out his days at thirty-one or whatever he burned down to.

Except he couldn't. Thirty-one levels in the deep Margins, with Sable's team somewhere behind them and the territory ahead getting worse, and no route knowledge, no map, no understanding of what lay between here and anywhere safe. Vel had the journal. Vel had the forty years of field experience. Vel had the knowledge of what was south and what was coming from the north. Without her, he was a man with thirty-one levels and no plan in country that would eat him alive.

And she couldn't survive without him either. She was at eighteen — he'd heard her tell Dren two days ago, adjusting a supply calculation. Eighteen levels and the body of a woman who'd been grinding herself down for four decades. She needed what he had left for the crossing and for whatever stood between here and the threshold.

They were stuck with each other. The betrayal and the dependency were the same structure, and pulling out one would collapse the other.

They stood there. The heat pressed on the courtyard walls and neither of them moved. The Margins offered no comfort, because the Margins never had comfort to give — not to the people who'd been spent down here, and not to two people burning through on their way somewhere else.

Kem became aware that he was touching his right hand. The ring finger, specifically. The House signet — heavy gold, the Aldraste crest of three locked towers, the Hoarded symbol of preservation through inaction. He'd worn it since the Vault. Through every mile of this journey. In the Spent Towns, a tavern keeper had seen it and her face had closed, and he'd covered it with his palm. In the Margins, nobody recognized the crest — they only knew the Hoarded's systems, not their symbols. He'd kept the ring on because taking it off would have meant thinking about who he was, and he hadn't been ready to think about that.

He pulled it off.

The finger underneath was pale and slightly grooved from weeks of wear, the skin soft where the gold had pressed. He held the signet in his palm. It caught the courtyard light — warm, heavy, ornate. Three locked towers. The family that chose stillness. The system that extracted from the Margins and preserved at the Vault and treated the distance between those two things as natural order. The ring his father had worn while his mind emptied out behind his eyes.

He thought about throwing it into the scrub past the farmstead wall. Letting it vanish into Margins dirt, one more thing the Margins swallowed.

He didn't.

He closed his hand around it, felt the edges of the towers press into his palm, and put it in his pocket.

Dren came back through the entrance. "Road's passable if we cut through the fields for a stretch. Another collapse a quarter mile on, but the drainage is dry — we can walk it." He looked at Kem. He looked at Vel. Whatever he read in the space between them, he filed without comment. "We should move. That stabilization won't hold past dark."

"Yeah," Kem said.

They gathered their packs. Vel shouldered hers with the automatic efficiency of a person who'd been carrying loads for forty years — her body knew how to distribute weight, settle straps, start moving without wasting the effort of starting. Kem picked up his pack. It was lighter than it had been two days ago. Everything was lighter. He couldn't tell anymore whether that was the pack or the Ledger or the particular weightlessness of knowing that the person he'd trusted most had built their relationship inside a structure designed to use him.

He walked ahead. Ten feet, then fifteen. The distance of a man who couldn't leave — thirty-one levels in the Margins, hostile territory in every direction, neither of them capable of surviving alone — and couldn't stay close. The distance of a man trying to outpace the inside of his own head and aware that it wasn't working.

Vel followed at the gap he'd set. She didn't close it. She didn't try.

Dren walked between them, which was a choice, and a kind one, because it meant neither of them had to look at the back of the other's head.

They walked for an hour without speaking. The road south was worse than what they'd come in on — stretches where the Shaping had failed entirely, leaving raw earth that the summer heat had baked into a surface like broken pottery. Twice they stepped off the road into the dead fields, picking through soil that crunched underfoot. Dren found the dry drainage channel he'd scouted and they walked the bottom of it for a quarter mile, crumbling walls of old earth on either side, the sky a strip of white above them.

Kem thought about his father. The Conservatory Rite — the weekly ritual of sitting perfectly still, demonstrating the discipline of never acting. He thought about how his father's eyes had gone empty behind the composure. The personality thinning year by year. The Stillness eating him from the inside while his Ledger held at three hundred and everyone in the room celebrated the number.

He'd left the Vault because he couldn't watch it anymore. He'd spent his first ability on opening a door because staying still had become impossible. And Vel had been positioned for exactly that kind of person — someone whose revulsion at the Hoarded philosophy would make him willing to burn everything down. She hadn't caused his departure. But she'd been ready for it, the way a channel cut in dry ground is ready for rain, and when he fell she caught him and pointed him south.

The signet pressed against his thigh through the pocket fabric. He didn't take it out.

The Margins stretched south. Depleted earth, crumbling infrastructure, the summer heat rising in waves that made the road shimmer and the distance uncertain. Behind them, the stabilized collapse sat in its frozen ruin — road surface locked at wrong angles, the drainage channel exposed, Kem's twelve levels hardened into the ground. The Charm would hold until dark, maybe longer. Then the Shaping would thin and the road would finish collapsing, and the Margins would swallow another piece of infrastructure the Hoarded had built too cheaply and walked away from, and nobody would come to fix it.

Ahead of them, more road. More Margins. More distance to cover with less of everything — fewer levels, fewer words, fewer of the small exchanges that had once made the walking bearable. Kem kept his eyes forward and his hand out of his pocket and his pace just fast enough that Vel's footsteps behind him stayed at the distance he'd set.

Thirty-one. Vel's number. His number now.

He kept walking.


# Chapter 14

They walked south through the Margins on a road that had stopped being a road sometime in the last decade. Cart ruts packed hard and then abandoned, cracking in the summer heat into brittle plates that broke under their boots. Vel led. Kem followed. Between them, Dren adjusted his pack strap against his bad arm and said nothing, which was the smartest thing anyone in their group had done in two days.

"Water stop. Twenty minutes." Vel's voice carried the flat precision she used for logistics — no wasted syllables, no inflection that might be mistaken for warmth. She'd always been compressed, but in the weeks before the fracture the compression had come with glances, small corrections, adjustments that acknowledged Kem as a person she was teaching. Now the words were instructions addressed to the air, and if he happened to be standing in it, fine.

"Right," Kem said.

That was the conversation. The previous day they'd managed three words total between dawn and mid-afternoon: "Left here" from Vel, and "Yeah" from Kem. Before that, an entire morning of walking in which the only sound between them had been the crunch of boots on dead soil and the creak of pack straps. They'd learned each other's rhythms over weeks — when to eat, when to stop, which side of the trail each preferred, how to divide camp tasks without discussion — and all of that mechanical knowledge kept running after the trust had been pulled out from underneath it. The partnership functioned. The people inside it didn't speak to each other.

Kem had been angry for two days and the anger had done what anger does when you can't afford to act on it: it had calcified into something load-bearing. Not the cold fury of the confrontation, which had burned through in the first hours. What replaced it was a structural resentment he'd organized his breathing around. He was aware of it the way he was aware of his Ledger: a number at the edge of consciousness, persistent, counting down.

Thirty-one. He'd checked that morning, and again after breakfast, and once more when they passed a posted notice nailed to what had once been a settlement gate — PRODUCTIVITY TIER REASSIGNMENT: ALL UNITS BELOW LEVEL 20 REPORT TO DISTRICT PROCESSING — the paper bleached near-illegible by sun and rain. The checking was compulsive now. At two hundred, the Ledger had been a weather system: always there, sensible in general terms, but you didn't count individual degrees. At fifty it had become arithmetic. At thirty-one it was a pocketful of coins he kept reaching in to count, and every time the count came back the same he felt the relief and the dread of it in equal measure.

Thirty-one. The same number Vel had carried when they met. The same level she'd lived at through forty years of spending while he'd burned through two hundred and sixty-nine in six weeks. He felt the irony like a bruise he couldn't stop pressing. Everything she'd taught him about economy, precision, the cost of carelessness — she'd taught him so he'd burn down efficiently for her. A maintenance schedule for a component she needed functional. The lessons weren't wrong. They were just also a tool.

Dren found the well at the water stop. He hauled the bucket one-armed, the rope dragging across his bad forearm — the scar from the silo shrapnel still pink and ridged where Kem's inexpert stitches had pulled the skin unevenly. Dren didn't wince. He shifted his grip and hauled again, and the small economy of that adjustment — accommodating old damage without pausing to acknowledge it — was a competence Kem had spent twenty-six years never developing.

They drank. Vel refilled the waterskins. Kem cupped water in his hands and pressed it against his face and for one moment the coolness was so immediate and animal that he forgot to be angry. Then Vel said "Losing light. Two more hours," and the architecture of his resentment resettled into place.

They walked.

The road deteriorated further. Whatever institutional maintenance had kept it passable had been withdrawn years ago — a posted notice on a cairn read INFRASTRUCTURE RENEWAL SUSPENDED PENDING PRODUCTIVITY TIER REVIEW, the paper bleached and curling. The settlements thinned out. Farmland that had been depleted by overuse of Fertility Charms spread flat on either side, baked to a pale exhaustion that looked malicious: soil that had given everything it had and been asked for more, and now gave nothing. Summer in the Margins was a weight, not a temperature. The heat pressed down and the landscape absorbed it and threw it back, and Kem's shirt stuck to his ribs and the dust coated the inside of his mouth and the Ledger hummed its thin note and all of it was just walking. Just south.

He watched Vel's back. She moved the way she always had — each step the minimum required, shoulders set against the pack, scarred hands loose at her sides. Reading the terrain automatically, the way forty years of doing this had trained her: threats, routes, water, cover. He knew how she walked when tired — a slight hitch in her right hip from an injury she'd never mentioned. He knew how she held the waterskin when she was rationing — higher on her body, closer to her face. He knew the angle of her head when she was consulting memory rather than landscape.

He knew all of it and none of it told him what she was thinking. He could read every muscle in her back and still had no idea what was behind it. He didn't know if she was sorry or angry or relieved or just tired, and the gap where that understanding used to live — where the teaching relationship and the grudging trust and whatever real thing had been growing between them had made her interior at least partially readable — was sealed shut. She'd answered his accusation with silence, and the silence had become the whole landscape between them, and he couldn't cross it because he wasn't sure he wanted to.

The road curved between two settlements built close together and then died separately — roofless stone walls on the left, three occupied houses on the right, a woman watching from a window with the stillness of someone deciding whether strangers meant danger or inconvenience. She looked at Dren first, read trader, and her shoulders dropped. Then she looked at Kem and the assessment changed. He was thinner now, darker from weeks of sun, the softness of the Vault pounded out of him, but something in his posture or his confusion still signaled wrong.

"Path forks," Vel said. "South-southeast."

"Fine."

Dren looked between them and then at the horizon and then at his own feet — a three-part evasion Kem had come to recognize as the man's version of diplomatic restraint. He'd been doing it since the fracture. Never commenting. Never mediating. Just adjusting his trajectory to account for two people who'd stopped being anything to each other except logistics.

The fork led into lower ground. A settlement appeared — larger than the ones they'd passed, maybe thirty structures around a central clearing. Smoke from cooking fires. Children's voices carrying in the hot still air. As they got closer, Kem saw the ground was wrong.

Not the road surface. The ground itself. A fissure ran diagonally across the settlement's eastern edge, a crack in the earth wide enough to stand in and deep enough that its bottom was shadow. Three structures on the east side were listing at angles that made the eye flinch. One had collapsed entirely, its stone walls folded inward like a hand closing. Subsidence — decades of Shaping-ability mining had hollowed the substrate, and the surface was dropping into the gaps in slow motion, taking whatever people had built on top of it.

Residents moved around the fissure's edge with the practiced worry of a community that had been watching the ground eat their buildings for months. An old man stacked salvaged timber. Two women argued about a retaining wall. Children played in the rubble as if collapsed buildings were a feature of the landscape, which here they were.

Then a sound cut through the afternoon. A high, sustained note — someone calling for help who'd been calling for a while and whose voice had passed through panic into a steady broadcast of need.

Vel stopped walking.

Kem stopped walking.

They both looked at the same structure: a two-story building on the settlement's eastern edge, tilted fifteen degrees toward the fissure, its second floor sagging where the ground beneath the foundation had given way. Dust rose from a fresh crack in the wall. The voice came from inside.

Nobody discussed it.

---

Vel moved first. She dropped her pack and crossed the clearing at a pace Kem recognized — controlled urgency, time mattering but running a waste of energy. He dropped his pack and followed. Dren started forward, looked at his bad arm, and stopped. He found a retaining wall to sit against and watched, his face carrying the expression of a man doing math he couldn't change.

A crowd had gathered — fifteen or so residents who'd heard the voice and come to stand at a distance that said they wanted to help and couldn't. A man with stone dust on his clothes was telling anyone who'd listen: "The whole east wall shifted. My sister's in there with her kids. The floor's holding but it won't — it's not gonna hold."

Vel didn't ask for details. She walked to the building's west side — the stable side — and put her hand flat on the wall. Kem watched her settle into the focused immobility she used when she was sensing rather than seeing. A Perception ability — level-bought structural awareness, the load-bearing points and stress fractures mapped through stone. One level, gone. The faintest tremor in her hand as the information landed.

"Northwest corner's holding," she said. Not the logistics register — the operational one, the voice that expected competence from whoever was listening. "Southeast's gone. Floor beam runs north-south, cracked midway. You reinforce the beam first, then clear the debris at the east doorway. Two inside. Woman, at least one child. Maybe two."

She was talking to him. Not looking at him, but talking to him, and the operational shorthand was so familiar that Kem's body responded before his anger could object.

"Reinforcement on the beam," he said. "How wide?"

"Two meters. Both sides of the crack."

He walked to the east wall. The building groaned — a low structural complaint that traveled through the stone and into his boots. The east doorway was blocked by fallen masonry: a lintel dropped, stones tumbled inward on both sides. Through the gap he could see darkness and dust and the shape of someone crouching.

"We're coming in," he said to the gap. Not reassurance. Just information.

He reached for the Reinforcement Charm. At two hundred levels, it had been a thought — intention flowing into stone, the structure responding like it was eager to cooperate. At thirty-one it was work. He had to push, had to feel the shape of what he was holding together and convince the ability that this specific timber was worth the cost. The beam was cracked diagonally where the foundation shift had torqued it. Kem pressed his hands flat against the wall near the crack and spent.

Two levels. The charm flowed into the wood and he felt it stiffen — the crack didn't close, but the grain around it hardened, locked into resistance against the forces pulling it apart. The building's groan changed pitch. Lower, more stable. Not silence — the structure was still dying — but slower.

Twenty-nine.

"Beam's holding," he said.

"Clear the doorway."

He reached for a lifting ability. Three weeks ago he'd have spent forty levels on this without thinking. Now he had to calculate how much stone, what minimum force, whether he could shave the output to the bare edge of what was needed. Two hundred pounds of masonry in the way. He found the ability's shape in his Ledger — thinner than it used to be, the options around it fewer, like a pantry going bare — and spent.

Two levels. The stones lifted. Not gracefully — they lurched upward and to the side, scraping against each other, one catching on a wall stud and having to be finessed through a gap he couldn't see clearly. But they moved. The doorway cleared. Dust billowed.

Twenty-seven.

He ducked inside. Dark. The building's interior was a single room partitioned by a half-collapsed storage wall. Dust so thick the air tasted solid. He could hear breathing — fast, shallow, the sound of someone holding still because holding still was all they had.

"Light," Vel said from outside. She'd moved to the northwest corner and was bracing the wall — not with an ability, with her hands flat against the stone, as if the pressure of a human body could supplement what the Reinforcement Charm was already doing. Through a crack in the wall Kem could see her jaw set, her eyes tracking something above him. She'd spent again — a second ability, something structural, to hold the corner while he worked inside. Her hands were shaking from the sustained effort.

The interior was too dark to work in. He spent.

One level. A soft luminescence filled the room — nothing dramatic, just enough to see by. The ability was barely there. A year ago he could've lit a ballroom. Now a single room cost him a level he'd never get back.

Twenty-six.

A woman was crouched against the far wall, her body curved over two children. She looked up at the light and her face was streaked with dust and the particular expression of someone who'd been prepared to die and was now being asked to recalibrate. The older child — maybe seven — was pressed against her chest. The younger — three, four — was tucked between her body and the wall, whimpering.

"Can you move?" Kem asked.

"I — the floor. It dropped. I can't —" She pointed. Between her and the doorway, a section of floorboard had caved in, leaving a gap that dropped into the undermined foundation. Maybe four feet across. Maybe a meter deep. Not lethal. But with a child on each arm and the building groaning around them, uncrossable.

"Stay there."

He went to his knees beside the gap. One joist snapped, the boards above it hanging like a half-open trapdoor. If he could stabilize the floor on either side of the break, she could cross with the children.

The building groaned. Something shifted overhead. Dust rained.

"Ceiling's moving," Vel called. "You've got maybe three minutes."

One level. The joist stiffened under the stabilization. The hanging boards settled. Not repaired — held.

Twenty-five.

"Come now," he said to the woman. "Bring them. Go fast."

She stood. She lifted the younger child onto her hip and took the older one's hand and walked across the stabilized floor with the deliberate care of someone crossing thin ice. The boards held. The building groaned. Kem held the stabilization with whatever dregs of duration the ability had left. They crossed. They reached the cleared doorway and went through. Kem followed. Behind them a section of the second-floor wall collapsed into the space they'd been standing in three seconds earlier.

Outside, the sun hit like a slap. Kem stood in the clearing with dust in his lungs and twenty-five levels on his Ledger and watched Vel step back from the wall she'd been bracing. She was breathing hard. Dust on her face and in her short gray hair. Her hands at her sides. They were six feet apart and neither made eye contact.

The woman set the younger child down. The child reached immediately for her mother's face with both hands — patting cheeks, tugging ears, the repetitive physical insistence of a small person making sure the world's most important fact hadn't changed. The mother closed her eyes and held still for it.

The settlement crowd pressed in. The brother — the stone-dust man — was already there, talking fast, touching his sister's arms, counting children with his eyes. A woman offered water. An older man started clearing debris from the building's perimeter, as if the crisis had reminded him the next one was already forming.

A man Kem didn't recognize approached while they stood in the aftermath. Middle-aged, wearing the stained work clothes of someone who quarried or laid stone. He looked at Vel. He looked at Kem. He took in the six feet of space between them and the quality of that space — two people who'd just performed a precision extraction in seamless operational concert and couldn't manage to face each other.

"Thank you," the man said. "I mean — hell, thank you." He looked between them again. "Are you two partners? Travelers?"

Neither Kem nor Vel answered.

The man waited. He read whatever was in the gap between them and his face did a small, complicated thing — the expression of someone who has stumbled into a room where the furniture is arranged for a fight he isn't part of.
