"Right," he said. "Never mind."

He walked away.

Kem sat on a pile of rubble. The stone was warm from the afternoon sun and rough against his legs and he could feel every scrape, every bruise, every place the last twenty minutes had knocked his body against a hard surface. His right palm was cut — a shallow gash from grabbing stone in the dark. He pulled a mundane bandage from his belt pouch, something Vel had taught him to carry weeks ago when she was still teaching him things, and started wrapping the cut with clumsy efficiency.

Vel watched him do it. She was standing nearby, breathing still uneven, and Kem could see the moment when the old instinct surfaced — the instructor's reflex to correct his grip, adjust the tension, show him the right way. Her mouth opened a fraction of an inch.

She closed it. Turned away and picked up her pack.

Kem finished the bandage. It was too loose. He left it.

Twenty-five. He sat in the rubble and the number pressed against the back of his skull like the Ledger's background hum — which at this level wasn't a hum anymore but a thin, high tone, the kind of sound you stop hearing until someone asks about it and then it's all you can hear. Twenty-five out of three hundred. Out of the absurd surplus he'd been born into, the levels his father had preserved to the point of mental dissolution, the levels his mother had never touched because touching them would admit the Hoarded philosophy was less than absolute.

The sun baked down on the rubble and the settlement that was still sinking and the dust that was still settling. Kem was so tired — not sleepy, not the tiredness of needing rest, but the deep systemic exhaustion of a body that had been spending itself for weeks and was running out of what it had to give — that for one moment, sitting on the warm stone with his cut hand and his twenty-five levels and the taste of dust coating the inside of his mouth, he understood.

He didn't agree. He didn't accept. But he understood.

Why someone would choose never to feel any of this.

The thought arrived whole and terrible, like a door opening onto a room he'd always known was there. The full-body cost of embodiment — the pain, the depletion, the constant arithmetic of what each action ate and what it left, the knowledge that every ability used was one fewer ability remaining, that the well had a bottom and he could see it now — pressed in at once. And for one breath he saw the Vault. The Conservatory hall. The silence. His father's empty eyes. The specific serenity of a man who'd never spent a single level, who'd never felt his body crack against stone or his lungs fill with dust or his hands bleed from pulling someone else's children out of the dark.

For one breath, preservation made a sick, perfect sense. The Hoarded didn't sit in the Conservatory because they were lazy or cowardly. They sat because they knew — somewhere in the institutional memory that shaped their philosophy — what spending felt like, and they'd decided the feeling wasn't worth the cost. Never hurt. Never lose. Never feel the arithmetic of what you've spent. Just sit. Just preserve. Let the world happen to other people.

He understood it. In his bones, in his cut hands, in the hum of his depleted Ledger, he understood the appeal of never feeling any of this. And the understanding was terrible because it was real — not abstract, not philosophical, but earned through the same physical suffering that made it comprehensible. The only people who could truly understand why someone would choose numbness were the ones who'd felt enough to want it.

Kem looked at the woman holding her children.

The older girl had stopped shaking. The younger one was still touching her mother's face — patting, pulling, the repetitive checking of a child who needed physical proof that the person she loved most hadn't gone away. The mother let her do it. Eyes closed, arms tight around both of them, dust still in her hair. Alive because six levels and two people who wouldn't look at each other had pulled her out.

The comprehension broke. Not gradually — all at once, the way a fever breaks, and what was underneath it was just Kem sitting on rubble with cut hands and twenty-five levels and the knowledge that he had, for one moment, understood his father. The understanding didn't go away. It filed itself somewhere permanent, a piece of knowledge he'd carry whether he wanted to or not. But it was a thing he knew, not a thing he chose. He'd seen the logic and the logic was sound and the logic was monstrous, and the child checking her mother's face was the answer to it, and the answer didn't require explanation.

He sat with it. Nobody came to talk to him. The settlement went back to the business of not falling apart.

---

Evening. Vel found a flat area on the settlement's west side, uphill from the subsidence, and made camp with the silent efficiency that characterized everything now. Fire built, water heating, bedrolls laid in the configuration that hadn't changed since the fracture: hers on one side, Kem's on the other, Dren's between them. The geometry of a détente mapped onto sleeping arrangements.

Dren was sitting against his pack, cleaning his knife one-handed. The scar on his bad arm showed below his rolled sleeve — Kem's own handiwork, the ugly stitches from the field medicine that had saved the arm and mortified Kem's sense of what saving should look like. The scar tissue had contracted as it healed, pulling the skin into a permanent ridge. Dren caught Kem looking.

"Still works," he said, and flexed the hand to demonstrate. Three fingers closed. Two lagged behind. "Mostly."

The flex was the kind of thing Dren said instead of saying the thing underneath it — that his arm was damaged and wouldn't fully recover and the daughter who needed him back was going to get a father who couldn't grip a loading hook the way he used to. But Dren didn't say things underneath. He said what was on top and let you do your own digging.

A Margins child appeared.

Not one of the rescued children — a different kid, maybe eight, with the particular scrawniness of someone who'd grown up where food was organized by scarcity. Dark hair cut short. Bare feet calloused enough that the rubble didn't bother them. She was examining Kem's pack with the unconcerned curiosity of a person who hadn't yet learned that other people's things were private, turning over his waterskin, his spare belt, the cloth he used for his knife.

Then she found it. The signet. It must have fallen from his pocket when he'd pulled the bandage out. The Aldraste House ring — gold, heavy, stamped with the family crest. She held it up and turned it in the firelight.

"What's this?"

The gold caught the light and threw a warm glow on the child's palm. The glow was absurdly disproportionate to the thing's actual significance, and Kem's awareness of the gold's weight — what that metal would buy in a settlement where families lived on stewed grain and salvage — sat in his chest like something he'd swallowed wrong.

"It's a signet," he said. "A family ring." He'd started to say *from a great House* and the words died before they reached his mouth, because describing House Aldraste as great to a Margins child whose entire settlement was sinking into holes the Hoarded had mined and abandoned felt like a cruelty he couldn't commit even by accident. "From a family in the north. It means the person who carries it belongs to people who never spend. Who preserve everything and do nothing."

The child turned the ring over. Examined whatever heraldic symbols the Aldraste ancestors had chosen to represent eternal inaction. Put it on her thumb, where it hung loose enough to spin.

"Sounds boring," she said.

Kem laughed. A small, dry, surprised sound — he'd been fairly certain he'd forgotten how. "Yeah," he said. "It was."

She lost interest. Set the ring on the ground beside his pack and wandered toward the cooking fire where someone was making a meal that smelled like stewed grain and not much else.

Kem picked up the signet. The gold was warm from the child's hand. He turned it the way she had, looked at the crest the way she had — from the outside, from a perspective that carried no assumptions about what the symbol should mean. A ring. Gold. The emblem of people who sat still while the world around them subsided into holes they'd dug and left. He put it back in his pocket.

Across the campsite, Vel was kneeling by the fire. She'd pulled out Maren's route journal — the battered book with the cracked spine and the pages that fell open to the places they'd been turned to a thousand times. Cross-referencing something, her finger tracing a line on one page while her eyes moved to the next. The firelight caught the annotations: two handwritings. One was smaller, neater, entries organized with careful headers and distances. The other was looser, faster — the writing of someone adding notes in the margins of someone else's careful system.

Vel's handwriting was the second one. Kem realized this with the quiet certainty of someone who'd been seeing a thing for weeks and only now understood what he was looking at. The careful writing — the headers, the organized entries, the primary hand that had built the journal's structure — wasn't hers.

She turned a page. The way she did it — lifting the corner, easing the paper over instead of flipping — was the way you handle something that belongs to a person you love. Not a tool. Not a reference. The care she showed those pages was a care she applied to nothing else in her life. Not her pack, not her weapons, not her own body. A specific tenderness directed at a specific object that someone else had made.

Kem didn't ask. He'd learned enough about Vel's silences to know that the things she'd talk about, she talked about, and the things she wouldn't, she guarded with an obliqueness so practiced it could make you forget you'd ever been curious. But he saw it. The journal that wasn't hers. The handwriting that wasn't hers. The care that was all hers, directed at whoever had written those careful headers and route distances in a hand he'd never seen before.

He filed it alongside the comprehension from the rubble, in the growing collection of things he understood without being able to use. Knowledge that changed nothing about his situation but changed something about how he read the person he was traveling with.

Dren had fallen asleep or was pretending to. His breathing was even. The fire crackled. Somewhere in the settlement, a child was singing — not the one who'd found his signet, a different child, a melody Kem didn't recognize, the kind of song that belongs to a place and never travels.

He could stop. He could tell Vel in the morning that twenty-five levels was enough, that he'd given more than he owed, that the remaining walk toward zero was her journey and not his. The math supported it. Twenty-five levels, spent carefully, could sustain him for years. He could walk north, find the Spent Towns, learn a trade, become someone who used a level or two a year on practical things and lived a quiet life. The signet was in his pocket and nobody north of the Margins would know who he'd been or what he'd done or how far he'd fallen.

He could just stop.

Except he kept seeing the labor camp. The sick child — eight levels. The enforcement mechanism — twenty. The combat, the infrastructure, the smaller interventions adding up like a bill he hadn't known he was running. Ninety-eight levels poured into people he'd never met, people who'd had no claim on him except that they were suffering and he could help. Nobody had asked him for those levels. Nobody had guilted him into spending them. He'd seen what was in front of him and he'd spent because spending was what you did when you could and other people couldn't, and somewhere in that process the person making decisions in his body had changed.

Not through revelation. Not through a lesson Vel had designed for him — though she'd designed plenty, and the lessons worked, and the working was part of what he couldn't forgive her for. Through doing it. Through the specific, physical experience of using what he had for someone else and feeling the cost and doing it again. The person who'd sat in the Conservatory hall waiting for his life to begin was gone. The person who'd replaced him — leaner, sunburned, dust-covered, twenty-five levels, cut hands, a comprehension of preservation he wished he didn't have — didn't stop. Not because stopping was wrong. Not because he owed Vel anything. Not because the cascade mechanism required his levels or the journey needed his body.

Because the person who spent for other people was who he was now. And that person went where spending took him, and if that was zero, and if zero was a threshold, then he'd cross it or he wouldn't, but he wouldn't walk away.

It wasn't exactly a decision. More like noticing that a decision had already happened — somewhere between the labor camp and this collapsed mine and a child's hands on her mother's face. He'd already chosen. He just hadn't caught up to it until now.

He lay back on his bedroll. The stars were out, thick and dense in the Margins sky where there wasn't enough light pollution from ability use to wash them away. The Ledger hummed its thin note. Twenty-five. The ground beneath him was solid, or as solid as Margins ground got. Somewhere to the south the land would drop into the Scour, and the threshold would be there or it wouldn't, and Vel would explain the rest or she wouldn't, and none of that changed what he was going to do in the morning.

The child's melody drifted through the camp. Dren shifted in his sleep. A page turned.

They would walk south.


# Chapter 15

The Margins had a smell Sable could not have described six weeks ago because she had not possessed the vocabulary. It was the smell of soil that had been asked for too much and given too little back — thin, mineral, faintly metallic, like the residue left in a cup that had been refilled past what the material could hold. She walked through it in mid-summer heat that pressed against her scalp and the backs of her hands and the strip of skin above her collar where the Vault's formal neckline had given way, three weeks ago, to an unlaced opening she no longer thought about.

She was at two hundred and thirty-eight. She checked the number the way she used to check her schedule — a background habit, institutional, automatic. Except the schedule had never changed and the number did, and each time it was lower than the last time, and each time the specific sensation of having less was accompanied by the less specific sensation of having become more, and she could not make those two accounting entries balance.

The settlement was eight structures around a collapsed well. She had not intended to stop. Her route — if the word still applied to a trajectory she'd been improvising since dismissing the team — passed a quarter-kilometer east, along a Margins track that had once been a Hoarded supply road and was now packed earth with the ghosts of cart ruts baked into it. She stopped because she saw a man sorting grain.

He was sitting at a rough wooden table outside the largest of the eight structures, which was the largest in the way that a slightly taller child is tallest in a family of short children. He was separating grain into two piles, one kernel at a time, and the rhythm of his hands was wrong.

Sable knew that rhythm.

She had watched it in Council chambers. She had watched it in the administrative offices where Senior Auditor Erenn's pen would stop mid-signature, hold still for three seconds, and resume as though nothing had happened. She had watched it in Lord Aldraste's hands at the last formal reception she'd attended — the way he reached for his glass and the hand paused in the air between intention and action, a gap no one in the room acknowledged.

The man's fingers moved, stopped, moved again. Three kernels sorted. A pause. Two more. A pause that lasted longer than the work.

Sable sat down on the stone wall across from him. "You're Hoarded," she said.

He looked up. The eyes took a moment to find her — a half-second lag between the sound and the attention, like a door opening after the knock. Then the person behind the eyes was present, sharp, assessing, the remnant gaze of someone who had once occupied a high office and a high number.

"Was," he said. "Fifteen years now."

"What level did you leave at?"

"Two hundred and ninety." He went back to the grain. His fingers worked a kernel, then two, then stopped. "I know what you're looking at."

His name was Rennic. He'd been a clerk in the House of Accounts — not aristocracy, not decision-making class, but administrative-adjacent for forty years. Close enough to attend sub-Council briefings. Close enough to know the filing protocols for classified expenditures. Close enough to the Hoarded families that he breathed the Vault's preserved air and followed its preserved customs and maintained his three hundred for fifty-two years without spending a single level on anything except the standard birth-registration Orientation that every citizen received automatically.

He told her this while sorting. The two piles were edible and not-edible. A distinction he used to make by sight in the time it took to pour a bowl. Now it required an hour, because his processing speed had narrowed the way a river narrows through sediment — not dramatically, not all at once, but enough that what had been easy became work and what had been work became impossible.

"I left because of this," he said, holding up his left hand. The fingers had a fine tremor — not a shake, not palsy, just a persistent imprecision, as though the signals between his brain and his fingertips were traveling through thicker wire. "One morning I reached for a pen and the hand did not move. Not paralysis. The hand forgot it was supposed to be doing something. Thirty seconds. Then it came back. My supervisor told me it was focus discipline."

His speech decelerated on the word "discipline." Not a stutter. A slowing — the syllables arriving at uneven intervals, as though whatever produced them kept losing power and finding it again, a mechanism running on intermittent supply.

"I believed that for two months," Rennic said. "Then it happened during a Council briefing. Pen in my fingers, the whole room waiting, and the hand just — stopped." He looked at the hand as though it belonged to someone else. "The person operating it had stepped out. Listen to me. I can't even describe my own body without filing a report on it."

Sable's chest constricted around something she did not have an administrative term for. She recognized that deflection — reframing the terrifying as the stylistic. She had been doing it for six weeks.

"The condition," she said. "How long?"

"Since before I left. That's why I left." Rennic placed a grain kernel carefully. His hand hovered over the pile, uncertain which it had chosen, then set it down on the left. "Everyone tells you it'll clear up outside. You'll spend, you'll use abilities, you'll wake the dead parts up. I've been spending. I'm at a hundred and twelve. Fifteen years of spending." He set both hands flat on the table, palms down, as though grounding them. "Whatever preservation does to the inside, it's done before you notice. Spending doesn't fix it. It's like — have you ever seen a wall erode? You can't see it happening. But one year the mortar's flush and the next year it's not and the year after that there's a gap and rain gets in and then the wall is a problem."

Sable sat with him. The minutes accumulated in the way they did in the Margins — slower than in the Vault, heavier, because here time was not scheduled and organized and filled with agendas but simply passed through, with all the weight of passing through visible.

Rennic talked about his settlement. The well had collapsed because the original Shaping work was cheap — a Hoarded contract, twenty years ago, where an ability-worker had been paid to dig and reinforce the well shaft using the minimum levels the contract specified, which turned out to be about half what proper reinforcement required. The same story as every other Margins structure: built for a balance sheet, not for the people inside it. Three families remained. The rest had drifted further south, following the thinning logic of a population that knew it was being used up and had nowhere to go but toward the place where using up ended.

He offered her water. She accepted. The cup had a crack running its length, patched with tree resin that had gone amber. Someone had repaired this cup rather than replacing it. In the Vault, damaged items were not repaired. They were stored.

"You're here for the Aldraste boy," Rennic said. Not a question. He'd been a clerk for four decades. He could read an assignment on a person the way he read a ledger entry.

"I was."

He studied her with the flickering attention that Stillness left him — present, then slightly less present, then present again, a candle in a draft. "You've spent. I can see it. Not the number — the way you hold yourself. You hold yourself like someone who has used her body."

Sable finished the water. She set the cup down carefully, respecting the crack and the resin and the person who had mended it. She looked at Rennic's hands, which had resumed their slow separation — edible, not-edible, edible, pause, not-edible — and she thought about Councilor Erenn's pen stopping mid-signature, and Lord Aldraste's hand hovering over a glass, and the quiet wing of the administrative building where four senior officials had been reassigned when their work became — the institutional word was "inconsistent."

She had filed those reassignments herself. She had written the transfer memos. She had used the phrase "reduced capacity due to distinguished service" and had not, at any point in twenty-seven years of filing, considered that the phrase was a euphemism for a degenerative condition the institution she served refused to name.

She stood. "Thank you for the water."

"You're not going back for him," Rennic said.

Sable didn't answer. She shouldered her pack — a field pack she had assembled herself from Margins materials after abandoning the Vault-issued equipment that none of them had known how to use — and picked up the walking staff she'd cut four days ago from a dead tree. The staff was imperfect, slightly curved, the bark stripped by her own hands with a knife she'd purchased in a Margins settlement for three marks and a Warming Charm she'd activated for a family whose fire had gone out in the night. Two levels on that Warming Charm. She'd filed the expenditure in her head the way she filed everything: date, purpose, cost, authorization. The authorization column had been blank. She'd spent it anyway.

She left the settlement heading south. Rennic's hands were still sorting when she looked back. Three kernels. Pause. One. Pause. The slow mechanical patience of a body running on whatever the Vault hadn't already ruined.

###

The farmhouse was missing its eastern wall.

Sable assessed the structure from fifty meters — a habit she had developed in the field that bore no resemblance to the risk assessments she'd conducted in the Vault, which had involved spreadsheets and probability matrices and had never once required her to determine whether a building might collapse on the people inside it. Three walls standing. Roof sagging where the center beam had cracked. The missing wall opened onto the Margins landscape like a wound that had stopped trying to heal. Hoarded construction: fast, cheap, built by contracted ability-workers who'd been paid to spend the minimum and told the people living inside would be fine.

She found them inside. Or rather, she found the evidence of them first. Two packs stacked against the intact north wall with the spacing of people who had stopped sharing space by choice. A fire ring in the center, coals still warm. A man she did not recognize sitting on an upturned crate near the gap, his left arm wrapped in bandaging that had been changed recently but not well.

The man looked at her without reaching for a weapon. He had the body of someone who worked for a living — stocky, road-worn, tanned — and the eyes of someone running calculations she was not part of. "She's here," he called toward the back of the structure.

Vel Ashwick came through the interior doorway first. Sable had studied this woman's file for weeks before the deployment — age fifty-eight, freelance healer, level approximately sixteen, forty years of continuous field work, wanted in three Hoarded jurisdictions for unauthorized ability use. The file had included a physical description: lean build, scarred hands, short graying hair. The file had not prepared her for the way Vel occupied space. She moved like a person who had been counting the cost of every action for four decades and had eliminated every motion that wasn't load-bearing. She looked at Sable and did not look surprised, and the absence of surprise told Sable more about Vel's awareness of her surroundings than any Perception ability could have.

Kem Aldraste came second.

Sable had to stop herself from cross-referencing the Council portrait. The portrait: pale, soft, well-fed, the untouched face of a twenty-six-year-old who had never been outside. The person in front of her: sunburned, leaner, bruised along one forearm, hands roughened with calluses that were clearly recent. He carried himself with the exhaustion of someone who had been using a body he didn't know he had, and his eyes, when they found hers, were not the eyes of a runaway aristocrat. They were the eyes of someone who had spent things he could not get back and knew it and had not stopped.

"Auditor Orm," Kem said. The Hoarded formality surfaced like a reflex — the automatic acknowledgment of institutional rank before personal identity. Then it collapsed and he was just a tired young man in a ruined farmhouse. "I'm not going back."

"I know," Sable said.

The five of them stood in the farmhouse — Sable, Vel, Kem, and the man on the crate, and the Margins' summer heat pressing through every gap in the remaining walls — and for a long moment nobody spoke. Dust moved in the light from the missing wall. A piece of the ceiling's plaster detached silently and fell to the dirt floor. Nobody flinched at it. They had all been in buildings like this long enough that small collapses were background noise.

Sable's training supplied the opening. "The current situation as I understand it involves a — " She stopped. She was not filing a verbal summary to the sub-Council review board. She was standing in a building that was collapsing in slow motion, looking at a young man she had been sent to retrieve, and the institutional scaffolding that had held her sentences upright for twenty-seven years was giving way the same way the farmhouse's east wall had given way: not suddenly, not dramatically, but with a settled finality that meant it was not coming back.

"I am not here to — " She caught herself again. The formal diction. She was using the formal diction. She had been using it for forty-seven years and for six weeks it had been failing her and it was failing her now, in this specific room, in front of people who did not speak in reports. "I'm not here to bring you back."

Vel said, "Then why are you here."

The question arrived without diplomatic padding, without preliminary context-setting, without any of the structures Sable used to wrap questions in before delivering them. Vel just asked.

"I don't know," Sable said. "I've been following you for three weeks since I sent the team back, and I don't — I have a provisional explanation but it doesn't hold up to internal review."

The man on the crate — Dren, her records said, a Spent Town trader — made a sound that was almost a laugh. Vel's expression remained exactly what it was.

"Can I sit down," Sable said.

"You're asking permission to sit on a dirt floor in a building nobody owns," Vel said.

Sable sat down. The motion was not graceful. Six weeks ago she would not have known what sitting on a dirt floor entailed because she had never been on a dirt floor. Now she sat with the practiced inelegance of someone who had learned that the ground was where you ended up when chairs weren't available, and chairs had not been available for most of a month.

As she settled, Vel reached toward one of the packs without looking and pulled out a waterskin. She held it out — not toward Sable but toward the space between them, an offer directed at no one in particular. Kem took it, drank, and passed it to Sable. The whole exchange was wordless, practiced, and absent of any warmth. Two people who had learned each other's needs and stopped caring that they had.

Kem sat across from her. He'd been sitting on dirt floors for weeks — the motion was automatic, his legs folding under him with the ease of someone whose body had stopped expecting furniture. He looked at Sable. "How many have you spent."

"Sixty-two."

"How does it feel."

Sable opened her mouth. "The initial expenditure produced significant proprioceptive feedback that I was not prepared for. The subsequent — " She heard herself. She stopped. Dren covered his mouth with his hand. Vel waited with the patience of someone who had listened to worse.

"It feels like being in my body for the first time," Sable said. "Which I realize is — it sounds — I'm forty-seven. That's a ridiculous thing to say at forty-seven."

"It's not ridiculous," Kem said.

A silence followed that was specific enough for Sable to read. Twenty-seven years of reading rooms — the geometry of who sat where, who looked at whom, whose silence was strategic and whose was structural — had given her an instinct for the architecture of non-speech. Vel and Kem were three meters apart and the quality of the distance was wrong. It was not hostile, not tactical — it was the distance of two people who knew each other's rhythms and had lost the reason for them. They hadn't spoken to each other since she'd arrived. They had spoken to her, and around each other, and in the other's presence, but not to each other.

"You're not speaking to each other," Sable said.

"We communicate as needed," Vel said.

"You're exchanging logistical sequences. That's not the same." Sable heard herself conducting a relational audit on two people she'd been sent to arrest, and the absurdity of it almost reached her face before institutional training caught it. "I'm sorry. That's — it's not my — "

"She can't help it," Dren said to the room. "Give her a minute."

Kem looked at Sable. The Hoarded formality was gone from his face entirely now. What replaced it was something Sable did not have a category for — the direct attention of someone who had stopped performing and started asking. "You knew my father."

"I worked with Lord Aldraste for twenty years."

"Have you seen what's happening to him."

The institutional response assembled itself behind her teeth: Lord Aldraste represents the highest standard of Hoarded discipline and his contributions to Council governance remain valued. She let it assemble and she let it sit there and she did not say it. Instead she thought about Lord Aldraste at the last reception she'd attended before the retrieval assignment. He had been standing near the west window of the Council chamber. His wife had been beside him, her hand near his elbow — not touching, not guiding, but ready, in the way you stand near something that might need to be caught. He had been looking out the window. When someone spoke to him, the response came four seconds late, and the words were correct, and the eyes were not.

"Yes," Sable said.

Kem waited. He'd learned to wait — that was new, that was Vel, who said things once. The Kem in the Council file would have filled the silence with words.

"The Stillness," Sable said. No qualifying clause. No "so-called." No "condition sometimes observed in long-term preservation subjects." The word, bare, in a room without institutional walls.

"You've seen it in others," Kem said.

"Four Council members. At minimum. Councilor Erenn's pen stops mid-signature. He pauses for three seconds. Resumes. Nobody in the room says anything." She was cataloging now, and the cataloging felt different here than it did in the Vault — not filing for the record but excavating, pulling classified observations out of the administrative archive where she'd stored them under neutral headings. "Councilor Dais asks the same question twice in meetings, not because the answer was insufficient but because she doesn't remember asking the first time. Lord Aldraste — " She stopped. "You know about your father."

"I sat next to him for twenty-six years," Kem said. "He left around year fifteen."

"And the man I met today," Sable said. "Three hours north. A clerk named Rennic who left the Vault fifteen years ago at two hundred and ninety. He's at a hundred and twelve. He's been spending since he left." She looked at her own hands. "The Stillness didn't improve. He sorts grain. A task that should take minutes takes him an hour because his hands stop. Not badly. Not dangerously. Just enough that he knows, every hour of every day, that the thing the Vault did to him is still there."

Vel, who had been standing against the wall with the absolute economy of a person who did not waste energy on posture, sat down. Not joining the circle — adjacent to it. Close enough to hear, separate enough to maintain whatever she was maintaining with the silence between her and Kem.

"It's portable," Sable said. "The damage. It follows you out of the Vault."

"The Hoarded know this," Kem said. He did not frame it as a question.

Sable thought about the quiet wing. The fourth floor of the administrative complex where certain senior officials had been reassigned when their work product became — "inconsistent." She had processed those reassignment memos. She had used the phrase "modified responsibilities commensurate with evolving capacity." She had filed them and moved to the next item on her agenda.

"They know," she said.

"And they celebrate it." Kem's voice went flat — the specific flatness of a Hoarded-raised voice encountering something too large for the emotional vocabulary it was given. "They give speeches about my father's discipline. His commitment. His sacrifice. He can't finish a sentence and they act like he's achieved something."

Dren shifted on his crate. "Shit," he said — not to anyone, just the word by itself, acknowledging the shape of the thing.

"I cannot — " Sable paused, restarted. "I can't bring you back." The contraction landed on her tongue like a new word. She had been contracting more for three weeks and it still felt like wearing clothes that did not quite fit — more comfortable than the old ones, but unfamiliar. "The Council authorized a retrieval under protective observation protocols. The actual terms: confinement, monitored access, restricted external contact. A room and a window and your father across the table."

She hadn't described the retrieval parameters out loud before. The words had existed in classified documentation and in her head and nowhere else, and saying them in a farmhouse with a missing wall felt like tearing a page out of a ledger — an act that could not be reversed by refiling.

"I watched Rennic sort grain for an hour," she said. "His hands shake. Not badly. Just enough. He told me the Vault called it focus discipline and he believed them for two months. If I bring you back, that's what I'm delivering you to. That room. That table. Your father's face. Twenty years of watching it happen from the inside."

"Yes," Kem said.

The farmhouse was quiet in the way the Margins were quiet — not the Vault's cultivated silence but the silence of exhaustion, of spaces where energy had been spent and not replaced. Wind pushed through the missing wall and moved dust across the floor. Somewhere beyond the settlement, the landscape stretched south in the particular flatness of depleted territory — soil too thin to hold, structures too cheap to stand, a geography of extraction that had taken everything and left the bill.

###

Vel said, "What happened to your team."

"Dismissed. Three weeks ago." Sable straightened her back — an automatic Vault posture adjustment that she caught halfway through and let happen, because some habits were structural and some were cosmetic and she was still sorting which was which. "After the engagement near the southern settlements. Three of my agents experienced severe psychological distress related to involuntary level expenditure. One of them fused himself to a boulder with a misdirected Binding ability. We had to chip him free. He spent nine levels on the extraction process. He cried for two hours. Not from pain — because nine levels was more than he had spent in his entire life and he could not process why it felt like relief."

She paused. The next part was harder. "The other two adapted. They were developing competence. They were beginning to spend on tasks beyond the mission parameters — utility tasks, convenience tasks, tasks that made them more effective but also more — more. More present. More capable. Happier." The word landed wrong in the air, the way words did when they described feelings Sable had only recently learned to identify. "I calculated that within approximately two more weeks I would have five field-capable agents who had independently discovered that action was superior to preservation, and the Council would have a problem significantly larger than one defected lord."

"So you sent them back," Vel said.

"I filed a mission-complete report indicating the target had been neutralized through natural depletion. That report is false. It has been on file for three weeks." Sable looked at the callus forming on her right palm, the one from the walking staff. Six weeks ago her hands had been the hands of a person who had never gripped anything harder than a pen. "I've been following you since."

Dren said, "You should eat something before anyone goes anywhere."

"There are two things you need to know first," Sable said. The briefing structure came back and she let it. Some frameworks were load-bearing even when the institution behind them was rotten. "The Council has authorized the Red Reserve."

Vel's face changed. A small contraction around the eyes — the arithmetic face, the one Sable had learned to recognize in people who calculated cost for a living.

"Trained combat spenders. Four operatives. Two freshly deployed in response to the retrieval's reported failure — they were authorized when the Council concluded that standard methods were insufficient." Sable watched Vel process this. "Approximate levels: two-fifty to two-seventy range. Full ability combat training. These are not the team I brought. They will not fuse themselves to geological formations."

Kem looked at Vel. It was the first time he'd looked at her directly since Sable arrived, and the look carried the weight of two weeks of silence. "How bad."

Vel's voice was quiet. "Untrained three-hundreds are a demolition site. Trained two-fifties know where to put the charges."

"The second thing," Sable said, "is that the deployment is not new."

Vel looked at her.

"During the pursuit, I was authorized to access all Council records related to the Aldraste retrieval. I used that access broadly." The administrative language was precise and she kept it precise because the precision mattered here — these were facts that depended on the specific institutional mechanisms that produced them. "The Red Reserve maintains a permanent classified garrison at the Scour. Two operatives have been stationed at the site for years. One commander has held the posting for five years. This is not a response to Kem's departure. This is a standing operation. It has its own budget allocation. Its own reporting cycle. Its own operational designation within the Reserve's classified structure."

The silence that followed was a different species from the fractured silence between Vel and Kem. This one was structural — the silence of information rearranging a room.

"Five years," Vel said.

"At minimum. The records I accessed were not comprehensive — I was operating under retrieval authorization, not intelligence clearance. But the deployment codes predate the Aldraste assignment by years. The garrison is there. It has been there. At the threshold." Sable could feel the institutional fury building — the specific rage of a bureaucrat who discovers that the system she maintained for decades contained a second system she was never meant to see, concealed not by encryption or restricted access but by the simple expedient of filing it in a classification tier that auditors of her rank were not trained to look for. "They have been guarding the Scour for years. Guarding it from people who reach zero."

"The threshold opens at zero," Vel said. Her voice was flat — she was confirming, not explaining.

"I'm aware." Sable looked at her. "You've known this. You watched someone cross."

"Eight years ago."

Sable processed this the way she processed any high-impact disclosure — ranking it against existing institutional knowledge, assessing the gap between what she had been told and what she was now learning. The gap was vast. The Hoarded had been guarding a threshold for at least five years. A threshold that opened when people hit zero. A threshold to a place beyond the Ledger. The entire architecture of preservation — the Stillness they celebrated, the levels they hoarded, the inaction they ritualized — was organized around preventing this.

Not because zero was death. Because zero was a door.

She looked at Kem. He was sitting very still, the kind of stillness that had nothing to do with the Hoarded's cultivated version. The stillness of someone fitting a new piece into a picture that had been wrong for a long time and was only now resolving into what it actually showed.

"They know," Kem said. "The Council. They know about zero. They know about the door. And they built the whole — all of it — the Rites, the preservation, the philosophy, the celebrating people for sitting still until their brains rot — they built it to keep people from walking through."

Sable didn't confirm this. She didn't need to. The confirmation was the garrison. The confirmation was the classified budget line and the five-year commander and the fact that the Hoarded had spent resources — had spent, the word itself a betrayal of their own philosophy — to station trained spenders at the one place where spending led somewhere the Hoarded did not control.

Dren stood up from the crate. "I'll put food together. You all need to eat and then decide things." He walked out through the gap in the wall, moving with the practical authority of a man who responded to existential revelation by making sure everyone had dinner. His arm, the one with the bad bandaging, he held close to his body. Not protecting it — accommodating it. The injury was old enough to be furniture.

Sable stood. Her knees registered the protest of joints that had spent six weeks learning that the ground was not always flat and surfaces were not always forgiving. She picked up the walking staff from where she'd leaned it.

"I'm going south," she said. "Not with you. I need to see the Scour. Whatever happens there — the threshold, the door, whatever you call it — I need to see it, because if it's real, then I spent twenty-seven years filing paperwork for a system that knew what it was suppressing and suppressed it anyway."

"It's real," Vel said.

"I need to see it myself." Sable gripped the staff. The callus on her palm pressed against the bark. "If zero is a door, then everything the Hoarded built — the preservation, the control, the Stillness they let happen to people, the Stillness that's happening to Rennic right now, to your father right now — it isn't protecting anyone from loss. It's protecting them from somewhere they could go." She heard her own words and recognized them as the farthest thing from a Council memo she had ever produced. "From something they could become."

Kem said, "Sable."

She looked at him.

"Thank you. For telling us."

She almost said "it's within the scope of my — " and stopped. She was not going to do that anymore. "Be careful of the garrison commander. Five years at the Scour means she knows the terrain. She knows the threshold. She's not an administrator playing soldier. She's been there."

Vel nodded once.

Sable walked out through the missing wall. The Margins stretched south, flat and depleted and baked under mid-summer heat that turned the horizon into a smear. The walking staff struck packed earth with each step. Behind her, the farmhouse held three people who were going to the same place she was going, for different reasons, through the same landscape, and she was not with them. She was not with anyone. She was a Senior Auditor of the Hoarded Council, forty-seven years old, level two hundred and thirty-eight, walking south alone with a question she could not file or forget.

She did not look back at the farmhouse. The road south was cracked earth and dead grass and the remnants of a drainage ditch that no one had maintained in years, its edges caved in, the stonework scavenged. The Margins spent everything, including its own infrastructure. She walked through it and it walked through her, and the number at the edge of her awareness — two hundred and thirty-eight — sat where it always sat, neither welcome nor unwelcome, a fact about her that was changing in a direction she could not predict and could not, she discovered, bring herself to regret.

Three hours south. Then the Scour.

Then the door, or nothing. She would take either. The not-knowing had become heavier than any answer could be.


# Chapter 16

Dren was packing before sunrise.

Vel heard him through the thin wall of the abandoned waystation — the sound of a bedroll being tied wrong, retied, tied wrong again. She lay still and counted the sounds. Buckle. Strap. A muffled curse when the leather caught on something. She knew what she was listening to. She'd been expecting it since the Margins farmhouse, since Sable's warning, since Dren had started looking south with the specific frown of a man calculating days against a child's illness.

She got up and walked outside. The Margins dawn was thin and colorless — summer heat already pressing down before the light had properly arrived, the depleted soil giving off a chalky smell that belonged to no season she'd ever learned. The waystation had been a Hoarded supply post once. You could tell by the stamped signage on the doorframe: PRODUCTIVITY TRANSFER STATION 7-SOUTH, the letters faded to ghosts. Someone had scratched *fuck off* into the wood beneath them in a careful hand, the letters neatly spaced, as if the vandalism were its own form of compliance.

Dren came out a few minutes later. He carried his pack low on his left shoulder because his right arm wouldn't take the weight. The wound from the silo shrapnel had healed into a thick, shiny seam that pulled when he lifted anything above his chest — Kem's stitching from weeks ago, amateur work on a real injury, the kind of healing that held the parts together without restoring the function. Dren had stopped mentioning it around the time they entered the deep Margins. He'd just shifted his grip, his carry, his sleep position, the way working people adjust around damage without discussing it.

"Morning," he said.

"Yeah."

He set his pack down by the crumbling foundation wall and looked at it. Then he looked at her. The look had weight in it — the weight of someone about to say something he'd been composing for days and still didn't have right.

"I'm heading back," Dren said.

Vel nodded. She'd known, but the words still changed the air between them. "When?"

"Now." He rubbed his bad arm with his good hand, the gesture automatic and unconscious, the way you touch a thing that hurts to check whether it still hurts. "Should've gone a week ago, if I'm honest. Two weeks ago. I kept — I don't know. I kept finding reasons."

"You don't owe us an explanation."

"I know I don't." He sat down on the foundation wall. The stone was warm already, the Margins holding yesterday's heat in its bones. "I'm going to give you one anyway because I'd rather say it than carry it home."

Vel leaned against the doorframe and waited. She could hear Kem stirring inside the waystation — the creak of a body that had gotten thinner over six weeks of hard travel, joints that protested the floor more than they used to. Kem at eighteen. She was at sixteen. Numbers that would have been a punchline in the Spent Towns.

"My daughter's sick," Dren said. He looked at his hands. "You know that. But I don't think you know what sick means when you're at a hundred and twenty and the healer in Roth charges forty for the work she needs. That's a third of me, Vel. A third of everything I've got left." He picked at a callus on his palm, the motion small and repetitive. "And I've been out here six weeks watching you two burn toward nothing, and I keep thinking — she doesn't care about thresholds. She doesn't know what a cascade is. She's nine years old and her lungs sound like paper and she's waiting for her father to come home with enough levels to fix it."

"I know."

"You know the number. You don't know what it sounds like when she breathes."

Vel didn't argue. He was right. She knew the arithmetic of Dren's life the way she knew any cost structure — precisely, functionally, without the sound of labored breathing at three in the morning in a room she'd never seen.

Dren was quiet for a moment. A thin wind came across the depleted flats, carrying dust that tasted like chalk and old Fertility residue. Three hundred meters south, the road continued through what had been farmland once — the rows still visible, ghost-lines in exhausted earth. Everything here had been used and left.

"I watched the kid spend ninety-eight levels on strangers," Dren said. "In the Margins. People he'd never met. And I watched you count every one of those levels and not stop him, because you needed them for your thing but you couldn't say that out loud, not in front of people the system already ate." He looked up at her. "I'm not judging. I'm telling you what it looks like from where I'm sitting. Two people burning everything they've got on a door that might not even open, while the world's full of doors that'd open fine if someone had the levels."

"That's a fair description."

"It's a shit description. Doesn't cover any of it." He scratched his jaw. His beard had come in patchy and sun-bleached over the weeks — a trader's face that had been outdoors in the wrong latitudes. "My girl's my zero, Vel. That's the place I'm trying to reach. Same thing you're doing. I just — my threshold's a healer's table in Roth and my cascade's forty levels I can barely spare."

Something in Vel's chest went tight in a way she didn't want to examine. Not her ribs — those had healed badly weeks ago and she'd stopped noticing — but something under them, behind the breastbone, where grief lived when you didn't let it surface. She looked at the ghost-lettered signage on the doorframe instead of at Dren's face.

"You've been good company," she said. The words were inadequate and she knew it.

"I've been paid company," Dren said. Then, quieter: "Some of it was paid. The last bit wasn't." He stood up and shouldered his pack, wincing when the strap settled on his bad arm. He didn't adjust it. He left it where it hurt. "Take care of the kid. He's worth more than his numbers."

"I know that."

"Do you?" The question was direct and without malice, which made it worse. Dren met her eyes and held them. "Because from where I've been standing, you know he's useful. That's not the same thing."

Vel didn't answer. The wind picked up the chalk-dust and threw it sideways.

Kem came out of the waystation, blinking in the raw light. His face was thinner than it had been at the start — the softness of Vault living stripped away by weeks of walking, the bones showing through. He'd gotten quieter since the fracture. Not Hoarded-quiet, the careful silence of people preserving themselves, but a different kind: the quiet of someone deciding, day by day, whether to keep going.

"Dren."

"Yeah." Dren looked at him. "Heading home."

Kem stood in the doorway and Vel watched him process it — the slight tension at the jaw, the way his hands hung at his sides without reaching for anything. He'd learned that from her, she realized. The economy of gesture. Not wasting movement. She didn't know how to feel about that.

"Your arm," Kem said.

"Still on. That's your doing." Dren flexed it, grimaced, and let it fall. "Worst field work I've ever received and I'd take it again."

"I could —" Kem stopped. They both knew he couldn't. A healing ability at his level would cost more than either of them could afford, and the old Kem who threw forty-one levels at a hillside was someone they'd left behind in the forested highlands, weeks and a lifetime ago.

"Don't." Dren said it gently. "You've got eighteen levels and a long walk ahead. Spend them on something that matters."

"Your arm matters."

"My arm works. It just hurts." Dren shrugged with his good shoulder. "I've had worse days on the trade routes. Pulled a cart with a broken wrist once because the mule died outside Garren and I had a delivery that'd feed Mira for a month." He paused, looking at Kem with an expression Vel couldn't quite read — something between assessment and affection, the look of a man who has watched a boy become something else and isn't sure the boy knows it yet. "You spend different now. You know that? In the Spent Towns you were throwing levels around like a drunk lord at a festival. Now you count. That's — that's something."

Kem didn't say anything. He put out his hand. Dren looked at it — the hand of a former Hoarded aristocrat, the soft skin long gone, the knuckles scraped, the calluses new and poorly placed, the hand of someone who'd learned to grip and carry and climb in six weeks of desperate education. Dren took it. They shook once, firm, and Dren let go.

"Tell Mira —" Kem started, and didn't finish.

"Yeah," Dren said. He understood what Kem couldn't articulate, or he chose not to make him try, which amounted to the same thing.

He shifted the pack once, nodded at Vel, and turned north. His boots crunched on the Margins road — that specific sound of feet on depleted ground, a thin gritty compression that was nothing like forest soil or Spent Town cobblestone. Vel watched his back until the dust haze took it. He didn't look back. People who were going home to sick children didn't look back.

The waystation was quieter after that. Two packs where there had been three. Two shadows on the road.

---

They walked for an hour without speaking. The road south was a Hoarded-built supply line, cracked and subsided where the earth had been hollowed by decades of Shaping-ability mining underneath. Vel mapped the fractures automatically — step left, avoid the depression, the subsidence pattern here suggested a gallery collapse thirty meters down. The old instincts, the body's knowledge, running underneath the silence like a river under stone.

Two packs felt wrong. The rhythm of three people walking — Dren's heavier tread, his occasional commentary on terrain quality, the way he'd hum tunelessly through boring stretches of road — had been background noise she'd stopped consciously hearing. Now its absence was loud. She kept catching herself looking right, where Dren had walked, and finding open air and depleted scrub. The landscape had the same problem: things removed leaving shapes you couldn't stop noticing.

They passed a Margins farmstead where someone was still trying. An old woman worked a hand plow through chalky soil, moving down rows that had been Fertility-Charmed to death decades ago and were now producing whatever chalk and subsoil and human stubbornness could manage. The woman didn't look up as they passed. She had the focused inwardness of someone doing something she knew wouldn't work and doing it anyway because the alternative was sitting still.

Kem stopped.

She didn't notice for three steps. Then the absence of his footfalls registered — a gap in the rhythm she'd been unconsciously tracking — and she turned.

He stood in the middle of the road. The sun was fully up now, pressing down on the landscape with the flat weight of deep summer. His shadow was small beneath him. He looked like what he was: a twenty-six-year-old at eighteen levels, burned down from three hundred, standing in the wreckage of a system he'd been born to benefit from.

"No," he said.

"No what?"

"No more steps." He didn't raise his voice. The old Kem would have. The old Kem filled silence with words when he was scared, talked fast, asked questions. This Kem stood still and said the thing once. "Not until you tell me why."

"I told you the mechanism. The cascade —"

"Not the mechanism. You." He took a breath that cost him something. "Why you, specifically, Vel. Why you've been walking toward zero for eight years. Why you carry that journal like it's the last thing in the world that matters. Why Thessa looked at you like you'd killed someone. What happened to make you — this."

The road was silent. No birdsong in the Margins — the trees here were scrub, half-dead things that had survived generations of Fertility depletion by learning to need nothing. Vel could feel the Ledger at the edge of her awareness, that low hum of remaining potential: sixteen. Sixteen things she could still do. Every one of them a door closing behind her.

She could deflect. She'd been deflecting for thirty years — answering the smaller question precisely enough that people didn't notice she'd skipped the real one. She could tell him about the cascade's energy requirements, the geographic specificity, the timing variables. She could bury the answer in technical detail until his urgency ran out.

She looked at his face. The sun was in his eyes and he didn't squint. He just waited.

"Okay," she said. "Sit down."

There was a stretch of collapsed wall along the roadside — the foundation of something that had been a storage facility, or a bunkhouse, or one of the Productivity Transfer Stations the Hoarded had littered across the Margins like bureaucratic droppings. They sat on it. The stone was hot through her clothes. Vel pulled Maren's route journal from the inside pocket of her pack and held it in her lap. The leather was soft from years of handling, the corners worn smooth, the binding repaired twice with different thread.

"Her name was Maren," Vel said. And then she stopped, because the name in her mouth did something to her breathing that she hadn't been prepared for, and she needed a moment before the next part.

Kem waited. He sat with his hands on his knees and he waited.

"We spent together," Vel said. "Eleven years. We ran the deep routes — Margins territory, places the Hoarded had used up and moved on from. Healing jobs, infrastructure, the kind of work nobody pays for because nobody with levels goes there voluntarily. She was —" Vel looked down at the journal. Maren's handwriting on the edges of the pages, the cramped annotations in the margins, the route numbers she'd invented for paths that had no official designation. "She was better at it than me. Faster. Braver. She'd walk into a settlement with nothing but her Ledger and a bag of tools and people would — they'd just let her in. I had to work for that. She just had it."

The wind moved the scrub brush along the roadside. Small, dry sounds. The landscape doing its minimum.

"We'd split healing jobs. She'd take the harder cases because she had the touch for it — steady, warm, she could talk someone through the pain while she was spending on them. I did the structural stuff, the field repair, the boring work." Vel turned a page of the journal without looking at it, the gesture habitual, a thing her hands did when she was thinking about Maren. "She kept this journal. All the routes. Where the settlements were, what they needed, which ones had clean water. She drew maps. They were terrible maps, but they worked." A pause. "We were good at it. At the work. At each other. I don't know how to separate those things."

"You don't have to," Kem said.

Vel looked at him. She hadn't expected that — a sentence that wasn't a question, that wasn't pushing for more, that just offered a flat surface for the next thing to rest on. She took a breath.

"She hit zero first," Vel said. "Eight years ago. It wasn't planned. We were in a fight — not combat, a building collapse, old Shaping-mining damage in a settlement south of here. She was at three. She used a Stabilization on a family's house. That took her to one. Then a piece of ceiling came down on a kid and she threw a Barrier. Half a level, maybe less. But it took the last of it."

Vel's hands were steady on the journal. She noticed that with the detached interest of someone watching their own body perform a competence they didn't feel. Steady hands. Interesting.

"The threshold opened. I saw it. I was six meters from her. She was there and then she was — somewhere else. Not gone. I could see it, for a second. Maybe two seconds. A place. Real place. Buildings. Light. People. She looked back at me." Vel stopped again. The stone was hot under her legs and the sun pressed on the back of her neck and the memory had a specific quality she could not render in words: the visual fact of Maren's face turning toward her from inside an impossible doorway, and the doorway closing, and the air where it had been being just air again.

"Then it closed," Vel said. "And she wasn't anywhere."

She realized her thumb was tracing the spine of the journal, back and forth, the leather worn to a groove where she'd done this before. Hundreds of times, probably. Thousands. In camp, on the road, in settlements, in the dark. The groove her thumb had made was its own kind of record — the physical evidence of eight years of touching the last thing Maren had touched.

Kem was quiet for a long time. Long enough that Vel could hear her own pulse in her ears, which she hated, because it meant the body was doing things she hadn't authorized.

"That was eight years ago," he said.

"Yes."

"And you've been —"

"Yes."

"The whole time. The journal. The routes. Looking for a way to —"

"Yes." She cut him off because hearing him assemble it was worse than saying it herself. "I was at sixty-two when she crossed. I spent thirty-one levels in eight years working out the mechanism, looking for a co-spender, mapping the route. I found the cascade theory in a Margins settlement two years after she crossed — an old woman who'd seen three people vanish over her lifetime and had figured out the proximity effect. I tested it with small spends near a secondary site. And I looked for someone with the levels to make it work."

"Someone with the levels," Kem repeated. His voice was flat. Not angry — or not only angry. He was hearing the thing he already knew but in a new register, the personal register, the one where Vel's manipulation wasn't just tactical but desperate.

"I found you because I needed you," Vel said. "That's true and I'm not going to dress it up. I heard about a Hoarded lord who walked out of the Vault and I went north and I looked at you and I saw two hundred and eighty-six levels and a willingness to burn. I saw a cascade partner. That's what you were to me, at the start."

"At the start."

"Don't make me qualify every sentence, Kem. You know what I mean."

"I don't, actually." He picked a piece of mortar from the collapsed wall and turned it in his fingers. A small, aimless motion. "I know what you've told me, which is always exactly enough to keep me moving. You told me about the threshold when I needed to know. You told me about the cascade when you couldn't hide it anymore. And now Dren's gone and you're telling me about —" He set the mortar fragment down. "About a woman you loved. Because there's nothing left to lose by telling me."

"That's not why."

"Then why now?"

"Because you asked." She looked at him. "Because you stopped walking and asked."

"I've been asking for weeks, Vel. After Thessa. After the mechanism. I've been asking with every silent fucking mile since the farmhouse."

"Asking isn't the same as stopping." Vel turned the journal in her hands. The leather was body-warm. "You were still moving. I could still not answer."

He absorbed that. She watched him do it — the slight shift in his posture, the way his jaw worked on something he chose not to say. He'd gotten better at that. At the start he'd said everything that occurred to him, a river of curiosity and anxiety and observation. Now he held things back. She didn't know if that was growth or damage.

"Tell me the rest," he said.

"The rest is the part that doesn't make me look good."

"None of this makes you look good, Vel."

Fair. She looked at the depleted landscape — the ghost-rows in the soil, the half-dead scrub, the remains of infrastructure that had been built to extract and never to sustain. A Productivity Tier classification sign was still bolted to a post fifty meters south, the text weather-stripped to fragments: RENEWABLE... UNIT... ANNUAL OUTPUT. The Margins' wallpaper. The bureaucratic language of a system that had used people as fuel and documented it with the dispassion of inventory management.

"I'm doing the same thing," Vel said. The words came out flatter than she'd intended, which meant they came out honest. "You said it in the Margins, after the mechanism. That I looked at you the way they look at everyone here. Like a resource." She touched the journal's cover. "You weren't wrong. The Margins make it worse because the pattern's the same — someone with power needing something from someone without it, wrapping the need in a reason that sounds good enough to justify the taking. The Hoarded call it Development Opportunities. I called it saving you the trouble of dying for nothing."

"And the difference?"

"I don't know if there is one." That cost her. She could feel it in her chest — not the old rib injury, which was a known quantity, but the pressure of saying a true thing that she'd been structuring her entire justification around not saying. "The intent's different. I care about you. That's real. But the shape of the thing — finding someone, using their levels, guiding them toward the outcome I need — that's the shape of what they did here." She gestured at the Margins around them. "It doesn't matter that I had a better reason. The people who made Fallow work thirty years in the fields had reasons too."

Kem was very still. Not Hoarded still — not the performed immobility of the Conservatory. A different kind. The stillness of someone hearing something they'd been needing to hear and finding it worse and better than they'd expected.

"Maren wouldn't —" Vel started, and stopped. She opened the journal to a page near the back. The handwriting was smaller here, more urgent, the annotations spilling off the ruled margins into the page's edges. She didn't show it to Kem, but she looked at it while she spoke, the way you hold something solid when the ground shifts. "Maren would've hated what I did. How I did it. She spent for people because she saw them. I spent you because I saw her. Those aren't the same thing, and pretending they are is another kind of lie I've been telling myself."

She closed the journal.

"I'm not sorry I found you. I'm not sorry I used your levels. I'm sorry that I couldn't figure out how to need you without it being the same thing everyone else in this system does to everyone else."

Kem said nothing. The quiet stretched. Vel could hear the creak of the old signpost in the wind, the dry tick of scrub branches rubbing against each other, the sound of two people breathing in depleted air. She had said it. All of it, or as close to all of it as she was capable of. Eight years of reasons and justifications and night-arguments with a woman who wasn't there to argue back, compressed into sentences that felt smaller than the thing they contained. Grief always felt smaller out loud. The interior version had rooms and corridors and years. The spoken version was a story about a woman and a door.

The sun was high now. Their shadows had shrunk to almost nothing beneath them — two dark shapes pressed tight against the base of the wall like animals hiding from the heat. The Margins road stretched south, empty, the subsidence cracks running through it like the landscape's own form of depletion, the ground spending itself downward into old mine galleries nobody maintained.

"What if she's not there?" Kem said.

Vel opened her mouth and closed it. She had rehearsed answers to this question. In the dark, in the weeks since the fracture, lying on hard ground listening to Kem not speaking to her, she had built careful arguments about probability, about what she'd seen, about the cascade theory's reliability. She discarded all of them.

"Then I'll be at zero in a world without the Ledger," she said. "And that's still better than this."

Kem looked at her. The sun was making his eyes water, or something else was, and he didn't wipe it away. She held his gaze because she owed him that — the willingness to be seen while she was most exposed.

"Okay," he said.

"Okay what."

"Okay I'll keep walking." He stood up from the wall. His knees cracked — the sound of a young body that had been used harder than it was built for. "Not because it's fine. It isn't fine. You used me and you'd do it again and we both know that."

"Yes."

"But you said it." He brushed stone dust from his legs. A practical gesture, performed while he found the rest of the sentence. "You didn't — you said the thing. About the shape of it. About the Margins. About what you did being the same as what they did. That's — I needed that more than I needed the story."

"The story's true."

"I know. But the story's information. What you said about using me — that's recognition. That's different." He looked south, down the road. "I'm not forgiving you. I want to be clear about that."

"I know."

"Good." He started walking.

---

They moved south through the afternoon without conversation, but the silence between them had changed. The old silence — the logistics-only quiet that had followed the fracture — had been a wall. Solid, maintained, load-bearing in the way that resentment is load-bearing: holding up a structure by holding two people apart. This silence was different. It was the quiet of two people who had said the hard things and were letting the words settle into the space between them like sediment in still water. Not comfortable. But habitable.

Vel walked and counted. Sixteen. She'd spent nothing today but the number was always there, the Ledger's quiet arithmetic at the edge of awareness. Sixteen things she could still do. Somewhere ahead — two days, maybe three — the Scour waited. A basin of exposed rock and permanent wind and Red Reserve agents who had been guarding a threshold for years.

She thought about Dren. Heading north with a bad arm and a hundred and twenty levels and a nine-year-old daughter whose lungs sounded like paper. He'd said *my daughter is my zero* and the phrase sat in Vel's chest like a stone, hard and precise and irreducible. His zero had a name and an address and a disease. His cascade was forty levels he could barely afford and a healer's table in a town he'd been away from for six weeks.

And he'd looked at her and seen the same thing. The same desperate arithmetic. *Same thing you're doing. Just different math.*

The road bent around a Margins settlement that had been abandoned years ago — roofless structures, the walls still standing because Shaping-built walls outlasted the people who lived inside them. A notice was nailed to what had been a community board: PRODUCTIVITY TIER ASSESSMENT — ALL UNITS REPORT TO SECTOR ADMINISTRATION FOR ANNUAL OUTPUT REVIEW. Below it, in different handwriting, someone had written: *They left. We're still here. The notice is still here too.*

Vel kept walking. Kem walked beside her. Not ten feet ahead, the way he'd been since the fracture. Beside her. Not close enough to touch, and not the careful distance of two people performing separation.

At a water stop — a Margins communal well, still functional, the rope frayed and the bucket dented but the water clean — Kem filled both their canteens without being asked. He handed hers back and their fingers touched on the metal and neither of them made anything of it. That was the repair: not a moment, not a gesture, not a word. Just the ordinary human fact of one person handing another person water.

The journal was in her pack. Maren's handwriting on the pages, the routes marked in a cramped careful hand, the annotations crowded into margins that weren't wide enough for what she'd needed to say. Vel had been carrying it for eight years. She would carry it for two more days. And then she would be at zero, in a place without the Ledger, and Maren would be there or she wouldn't, and either way the journal would have done its work.

The road dipped where the earth had subsided over an old mine gallery. Vel stepped around the depression without thinking. Kem stepped around it after her, matching her line, the unconscious mimicry of two people who had learned each other's gait. He didn't know he was doing it. She did. It was one of the things she'd taught him without teaching him — the body learning from proximity, absorbing another person's economy of movement the way soil absorbs rain, quietly and without permission.

She checked her Ledger. Sixteen.

The Scour was two days south. Red Reserve agents, trained and armed and guarding a threshold that had been there longer than anyone alive. Four of them against two people with thirty-four levels between them. The arithmetic was the worst she'd ever faced, and she'd faced bad arithmetic for forty years.

But she'd told Kem the truth. All of it. And he was still walking.

That would have to be enough.


# Chapter 17

The wind hit first. Not a gust but a condition — a constant, gritty pressure that came off the Scour like breath from a furnace, carrying the taste of powdered rock and the flat mineral smell of geology laid bare. Kem shielded his eyes with one hand and looked south, and the world dropped out from under itself.

The Scour was a wound. Not a valley, not a canyon — a wound, as if something had taken a bite from the earth's surface and exposed everything beneath it. The erosion basin stretched maybe two kilometers across, carved down through layered rock that showed its history in stripes: ochre and pale gray and a deep, oxidized red that looked like old blood in the mid-summer sun. The walls of the basin dropped away in terraces and shelves, carved by wind into shapes that suggested time more than violence — overhangs, columns, narrow fins of harder rock left standing when the softer material around them eroded away over centuries. No vegetation. No shade. No cover. The sun struck the exposed rock and reflected, so the heat came from everywhere at once, and the basin floor shimmered with it.

Vel stood next to him at the ridgeline's edge, one hand resting on her pack where the route journal sat. Her face did something Kem had learned to read over six weeks — not emotion, exactly, but the registration of a cost she hadn't budgeted for. She was looking at the basin floor the way she looked at everything: measuring.

"There it is," she said.

Somewhere down there, eight years ago, Maren had reached zero. The threshold had opened, shown a flash of somewhere else, and closed. Vel had watched. Vel had been watching ever since, from 62 levels, from 31, from 20, from 14, burning herself down across eight years to follow a woman through a door that might not open twice.

Fourteen levels. That's what Vel had left. Kem had eighteen. Thirty-two between them. He'd spent more than that on a single defensive perimeter during the agent fight south of the Spent Towns. A Hoarded lord would spend more than that on a formal dinner if the Hoarded ever ate formally, which they didn't, because eating formally might require effort and effort was the enemy.

"I count four," Vel said. She was looking through a gap in the ridgeline rock toward the basin's navigable descent — a switchback path cut into the wall, the only conventional way down. Two figures stood at the top of the path. Below, on the basin floor near a cluster of small stone structures, two more.

Not the untrained level-300 agents who'd accidentally detonated a grain silo and given themselves migraines. These were Red Reserve. The people who knew what abilities were for and had been permitted to use them.

"Two at the descent," Vel said. "Two on the floor. The descent pair are fresh — they're standing wrong. Too stiff, too visible. They've been posted recently. The floor pair are permanent. Look at the camp."

Kem looked. The structures near the basin floor weren't field shelters. They were buildings — low, stone, with canvas-and-leather roofing, built for years of use. A fire pit with a carbon-black ring that had been burning for a long time. A gear rack. Clothing on a line, bleached by sun and wind. Someone had been living here. The wind-polish on the stone path between the structures showed thousands of footsteps ground into the same route.

"Sable said permanent garrison," Kem said. "I was hoping she was being dramatic."

"Sable doesn't do dramatic."

"No. She does bureaucratic, which is worse."

Vel almost produced a sound that in another person would have been a laugh. "I need to map their positions properly. That's two levels."

Two levels. Out of fourteen. A seventh of everything she had left, spent on looking. But they couldn't plan a route past four trained combatants by squinting from a ridgeline.

"Do it."

Vel closed her eyes and activated a Perception ability. The air around her shifted — a subtle displacement, like the pressure change before a storm, as the Ledger drew from her. When she opened her eyes, they tracked with an inhuman precision, sweeping the basin in systematic arcs.

"The commander is on the floor, near the threshold point. Woman. Compact build. She's been patrolling that same area for years — I can see the wear paths in the rock. Loops back and forth, same route, different times of day. Five years of that." Vel's enhanced eyes moved. "The other permanent agent is running a circuit between the camp and the east side of the basin, covering the tunnel exit. The descent pair are young. One's fidgeting with her gear."

She blinked. The sharpness left her eyes and she sat down heavily on the ridgeline rock. "Twelve."

Kem let the number settle. Twelve and eighteen. Thirty.

"I should mention," he said, "that at my current level, I could perform the Conservatory Rite without anyone noticing. I'm finally discreet enough for a Hoarded ceremony. Took losing two hundred and eighty-two levels, but the form is improving."

Vel's mouth moved. The structural foundation of a smile without the architecture.

A voice rose from the basin floor — not shouted but carried. Projected by ability or by acoustics, or by five years of learning exactly how sound moved in this wound in the earth.

"I can see you on the ridgeline. I've been watching you for thirty minutes."
