Vel went still. Kem's hand moved toward nothing — a reflex from when reaching for something meant he had options. He had twelve options left. Thirteen if he counted not using any of them.

"I know what you're here for," the voice called. "I know what you're trying to do. I've seen three people try it. I'd like to talk before we do this the other way."

"She's alone down there," Vel said. The Perception was fading but traces remained. "The circuit agent is at the eastern wall. Descent pair haven't moved."

Kem stood. "I'll go."

"If she—"

"Then you'll have twelve levels and no cascade partner and this will have been a very long walk for nothing." He said it flatly. Six weeks of approaching zero had burned the drama out of his delivery. "But she announced herself. She asked to talk. She's following procedure."

"I hate procedure."

"No you don't. You hate that other people's procedures get in the way of your procedures."

He took the switchback descent. The path was carved into the basin wall in tight turns, the stone underfoot worn smooth — not from erosion but from boots. Thousands of days of patrols. His own boots were held together by friction and optimism, the soles worn so thin he could feel the heat of each step.

The commander met him where the descent path leveled onto the basin floor. She was shorter than the voice had suggested — compact, weathered, somewhere in her forties. Brown skin darkened further by five years of Scour sun. Calloused hands. She wore field clothing that had been patched so many times the patches had patches, and her hair was cut short in a style that had nothing to do with fashion and everything to do with wind. She stood the way Vel stood — feet settled, weight centered, the posture of someone who'd spent enough levels to know what her body could do and couldn't.

And at the edge of her camp, near the gear rack, Kem noticed a row of stones set on a flat shelf of rock. Seven of them, each a different type and color from the basin's geological layers. They were arranged with a care that had nothing to do with function — spaced evenly, selected for something. Shape, maybe. Pattern. The collection of a person who'd been alone with rocks for five years and had started paying attention to them.

"Commander Osen," she said. "Red Reserve. Scour garrison."

"Kem Aldraste."

She looked at him the way a mechanic looks at an engine — assessing condition, not character. "I know. Your retrieval order's been sitting in my dispatch pouch for six weeks." She nodded toward the descent path. "Those two up there are for you. The Council sent them three weeks ago. They're competent enough."

"Just competent enough?"

"Nobody sends their best people to sit in a hole in the ground. The best people are back at the Vault, not spending anything." She said it without bitterness. Just fact. "I've been here five years. I volunteered for the posting and I re-upped twice. People don't do that for the career advancement."

"Why do they do it?"

She looked at the stretch of featureless basin floor behind her. The threshold point — or what Kem assumed was the threshold point, because it looked exactly like every other square meter of rock.

"Because I wanted to know if it was real." She turned back. "It's real. I've watched three people hit zero and go somewhere else. Right there. One breath they're standing, the next breath they're not. Not dead. Just not here." She paused. "I have orders to prevent that from happening to anyone else. So I stand here, and I make sure nobody walks through a door that the people who sign my orders would rather pretend doesn't exist."

She was Sable. The recognition hit Kem with the specificity of a face seen twice in different light. Commander Osen was what Sable Orm would have become with different orders and five more years — competent, spent, loyal to a system she understood was built on a lie, guarding something she knew was real because the alternative was everyone knowing, and everyone knowing would collapse the architecture of inaction the Hoarded had been building for centuries. Sable had been deciding. Osen had decided.

"You came here to talk," Kem said. "Not about my retrieval."

"Your retrieval's a footnote. If you want to go home, the two agents at the descent will escort you north with minimal incident. That's the easy version." She crossed her arms. "But you're not here for that, and the woman on the ridgeline isn't either."

"No."

"Then I need to tell you something, because I follow protocol and because I'd rather you understood the situation before we have to do this the hard way." She uncrossed her arms. "The threshold has degraded."

The wind blew dust across the space between them. Kem felt the ground shift — not literally, but in the way the ground shifts when the map you've been following turns out to describe a different territory.

"Three years ago, the Council sent a specialist. I don't know what she did exactly — spent a lot of levels over two weeks doing something to the cascade site. Dampening, they called it. Weakening the energy signature. Afterward, the readings changed." Osen spoke the way Vel gave field assessments — compressed, specific, no decoration. "Natural erosion was already happening. The geological alignment the mechanism depends on has been shifting — rock layers, water tables, something about the substrate. The dampening accelerated the decay."

"How bad?"

"Bad enough." She looked at him directly. "Eight years ago, two spenders going to zero in proximity would have generated a reliable cascade. Stable threshold, sustained opening, clean crossing. That's what the woman with the journal experienced — I watched it happen. Clean. Now? My readings say a two-person cascade has maybe a thirty percent chance of producing a stable opening. Maybe less. You'll burn everything you have and the door might flicker for two seconds and close, or it might not open at all."

Kem's mouth was dry. Behind him — above him, on the ridgeline — Vel was listening. He was certain of that. Her Perception traces would be fading, but the basin's acoustics carried, and Vel had forty years of listening for the information that would kill her plans.

Eight years of calculations. The cascade mechanism, the proximity requirements, the descent rate, the co-spending ratio — all of it calibrated to a threshold that no longer existed as she'd understood it. Three words to undo nearly a decade: it's not enough.

"What would sustain it?" Kem asked.

"More energy. More contributors. The specialist's report — which I wasn't supposed to read but I've been here five years and reading is what I do between patrols — said a three-person cascade would likely compensate. Three people descending toward zero in proximity, generating overlapping energy." She paused. "The Council didn't share that assessment with me officially. They don't want me thinking about how to open the door. They want me thinking about how to keep it shut."

"But you thought about it."

"I've had five years." She looked at the row of stones on her gear shelf. "You think about everything, out here. Every possible version of the thing you're supposed to prevent."

Kem turned and climbed back up the switchback. The heat rose with him, the basin wall radiating stored sun. By the time he reached the ridgeline, sweat had soaked through his shirt and the dust had turned to paste on his neck.

Vel was where he'd left her, sitting on the hot rock, the route journal open on her knees. She'd heard everything. Her face was the face of someone who'd just been told the house they'd been building for eight years had no foundation.

"Three," she said.

"Yeah."

"We don't have three."

"No."

Vel looked at the journal. Maren's handwriting on the open page — annotations about the basin's wind patterns, the geological layering, the location of the cascade site marked with a small x and the word *here* in handwriting that was not Vel's own. Written eight years ago, when the threshold was intact and two people would have been enough.

"I spent eight years on math that doesn't work anymore." Her voice was flat. Not compressed — empty. The flatness of a vessel that had just been poured out.

Kem sat down beside her. The rock burned through his trousers.

"So we improvise."

Vel looked at him.

"You've been improvising for forty years. I've been improvising for six weeks. Between us that's forty years and six weeks of making it up as we go, which is still about forty years more experience than every Hoarded lord in the Vault combined."

"Improvisation isn't going to generate a third cascade contributor."

"No. But it might get us to the threshold point alive, and a thirty percent chance of the door opening is thirty percent more than we had yesterday."

Vel closed the journal. Her hand lingered on the cover — the specific care she showed nothing else, the tenderness she reserved for a dead woman's handwriting.

"The tunnel," she said. "If we reach the basin floor before they reposition, we'll have eight hundred meters of open ground to the threshold. If we can start the cascade—"

"With two."

"With two. It might work. It might flicker and close. It might hold long enough for one of us and not the other." She stopped. "But it's what we have."

"And if it needs three?"

"Then we're two people with twenty levels standing in front of four trained fighters who have a thousand, and I'll think of something very clever in the time it takes them to end us."

"That's a terrible plan."

"It's the plan we have." Vel looked at the position of the sun. "We go in two hours. The light will be behind us coming out of the tunnel exit — in their eyes, not ours. Buys us thirty seconds of confusion, maybe."

"Thirty seconds and twenty levels."

"I've worked with less."

"When?"

"I'll tell you on the other side."

---

They moved laterally along the ridgeline as the shadows stretched, staying below the lip where rock met sky. The wind was constant, pushing grit into every fold and seam, and Kem's eyes watered from it until the tears cut clean tracks through the dust on his face. The Scour's rim was a different world from the basin floor — up here, the rock was sharper, less wind-polished, with lichens and dried grasses clinging to cracks. Thirty meters below that rim, nothing lived.

The tunnel entrance was a horizontal scar in the rock face: two meters high, three wide, the edges showing the clean bite of Shaping abilities. Miners had cut into the basin wall decades ago, extracting ore or mineral deposits that the Hoarded had found profitable enough to allow spending on. The Hoarded hadn't done the cutting — they'd contracted Margins laborers, people already depleted enough that spending a few more levels on mining made no difference to the system's arithmetic. The tunnel was the kind of place the Hoarded produced without entering: someone else's sweat shaped into someone else's profit.

The ceiling sagged where reinforcement abilities had long since faded. Rubble narrowed the passage from three meters to barely one. Cool air moved out of the darkness, carrying the smell of old stone and water that had been finding its way through cracks for years.

Vel knelt at the entrance, journal open. "Maren mapped the interior. Main shaft runs about two hundred meters in, then angles down and east. Three partial collapses, one full blockage. That was eight years ago."

"So it's probably worse."

"Probably."

"Great."

They entered the tunnel. The darkness was immediate and total — not the darkness of night, which had gradients and stars, but the underground dark that had never seen light and took the concept as a personal affront. Kem activated a Light ability. Two levels. The glow bloomed in his palm, warm and steady, and illuminated rough-cut walls, a floor of broken stone, and a ceiling that looked like it had been asked to hold itself up and had agreed under protest.

Sixteen.

The tunnel smelled like mineral dust and age. The walls were cool against Kem's shoulder when he turned sideways to fit through a narrow section. Behind him, Vel moved the way she always moved in tight spaces — low, efficient, each shift of weight deliberate. She'd been crawling through bad places for four decades and her body knew them the way a river knows its bed.

The first collapse was a slump — the ceiling had sagged and shed a layer of fractured rock, narrowing the passage to a low crawl. Kem went through on his stomach, the Light held in front of him, stone pressing his back. The pressure was steady and indifferent. Rock didn't care whether you got through or didn't. Vel followed, moving faster, her smaller frame fitting where his shoulders caught. She didn't comment on the tight fit. She didn't comment on anything. Underground, she became even more compressed — as if the weight of rock above them matched the weight she always carried.

At the second collapse, the tunnel was blocked by a slab of layered rock resting at an angle, leaving only a triangular gap at the bottom. The gap was body-width, maybe. If the slab shifted while they were under it, the gap would close.

Kem studied it. The slab was fractured along its bedding planes — thin layers of different hardness, the geological record of whatever had deposited this rock a hundred million years ago. The lower layers showed water staining. The upper layers were dry, sun-bleached where they'd once been exposed before the ceiling above collapsed.

"That moves, we're under it," Kem said.

"I see that."

"Reinforcement?"

"Your call."

It was his call because it was his levels. Three of them. A structural Reinforcement would lock the slab in place long enough for them to pass — freeze its fracture planes, hold its weight, prevent the small shifts that would turn a gap into a grave. Three levels. The same cost as a Flame Charm for cooking, a Light for reading, a Strength boost for carrying a heavy pack. Things he'd spent without thinking six weeks ago.

He placed both hands on the slab. The stone was cool and faintly damp. He activated the Reinforcement and felt three levels leave — not a smooth withdrawal but three specific subtractions, each one a point of absence that registered in the Ledger's constant hum. The hum was quieter now. It had been getting quieter for weeks, the background presence of his remaining levels fading as the numbers dropped. At three hundred, the Ledger had been a wall of sound — every ability available, every potential action humming at once. At thirteen, it was a whisper. A few voices left in a room that used to be full.

Thirteen. He held the slab while Vel crawled through, her body fitting neatly into the gap, her movements unhurried. She trusted the Reinforcement. She trusted his ability to hold it. Six weeks of distrust and fracture and repair, and she crawled under a slab of rock held in place by his remaining levels without hesitation.

Kem followed, shoulders scraping stone, the Light bobbing ahead of him. He released the Reinforcement as he cleared the gap, and the slab groaned and settled, and behind him the gap narrowed by half. No going back that way.

The tunnel angled downward. The rock changed — darker, wetter, the mineral smell sharpening. Water had been working through these walls for years, widening joints and softening surfaces. A section of floor had subsided, opening a gap that dropped into blackness below. Kem held his Light over the edge. Four meters down, rubble. Not useful. Not immediately lethal. But the floor around the gap was laced with hairline cracks, radiating outward like a frozen impact.

He tested the floor with his weight. It held. He took three careful steps, feeling each surface before committing. The fourth step found a crack that widened under his foot — a slow, grinding give, the rock preparing to let go. He shifted back, found solid ground, and pressed himself against the wall where the stone was load-bearing.

Behind him, Vel activated a Navigation ability. The cost was two levels. The effect was nearly invisible — no light, no sound, no obvious alteration. But her movement changed. She crossed the unstable section in four steps, each foot landing precisely on solid ground, her body angling through gaps between bad stone as if the path had always been obvious and she was simply walking it. She moved the way water finds the cracks in a system — inevitably, without effort, following the route that was already there.

Ten.

The next section was stable but narrow — body-width, the walls pressing close enough that Kem could feel both at once, his chest on one side and his shoulder blades on the other. The claustrophobia was rational, which made it worse. There was no room to panic. Panicking would wedge him tighter. So he breathed through his mouth and moved inch by inch and thought about the fact that his father, at three hundred levels, had never been in a space smaller than a Vault hallway. Lord Aldraste's entire life had been large rooms and wide corridors and the absolute assurance that nothing would press against him from any direction. Level three hundred and not a scratch. Level three hundred and dying from the inside.

The tunnel opened slightly. Vel used an Evasion ability — two more levels — to pass through a final constriction where the rock had shifted and the ceiling met the floor at an angle that shouldn't have been navigable. The ability didn't make her smaller. It made her luckier — the constriction had a viable route, barely, and the ability found it the way her Navigation had found the solid ground.

Eight.

Vel Ashwick, forty years of spending, the most precise user of abilities Kem had ever seen, was at eight levels. Eight. A Hoarded serving attendant spent more than that in a year maintaining the Vault's temperature. The ambient ceremony at a Conservatory Rite consumed more than that — formal abilities that kept the hall's acoustics perfect and the air at exactly the right temperature, all spent by people whose levels were not tracked because their levels did not count.

Vel's hands were shaking. Not from fear — from the Ledger's diminishing signal. The hum that had been a background constant for fifty-eight years of life was barely audible at eight levels. She was running out of something more fundamental than abilities. She was running out of the sensation of having them.

The exit was ahead. A change in the air pressure, a taste of wind that carried heat instead of mineral cold, a lightening of the darkness from absolute to merely deep. Kem activated his last defensive ability — a Ward, the lightest one he had, barely enough to deflect a thrown punch. He set it at the tunnel exit, a thin shell of protection that would buy them three seconds if someone was waiting outside.

Twelve. He was at twelve levels. Everything he owned in the world.

Between them, twenty. What a Hoarded lord spent on a tantrum. What a Conservatory Rite burned on candles. What a Vault serving staff spent in a month on the maintenance of people who would never spend anything on anyone.

They came out of the tunnel into late-afternoon sun, and the basin floor hit them like a fist made of light and heat. Red-gray rock, wind-scoured to a polish, hot enough to feel through boot soles. The erosion channels carved shallow grooves across the floor — waist-high ridges of harder stone left standing between worn troughs, enough to crouch behind, not enough to hide behind. The air shimmered.

The threshold point was roughly eight hundred meters to the west. Kem couldn't see it. There was nothing to see — no marker, no altar, no monument. Just rock. The most important point in the geography of the Ledger world was an unmarked stretch of basin floor indistinguishable from every other stretch.

The garrison camp was visible to the south-southwest: stone buildings, the fire pit, the gear rack, the clothesline. The circuiting agent was between them and the camp, maybe three hundred meters away, walking his patrol. He hadn't spotted them yet.

The descent agents were out of sight behind the curve of the basin wall.

Commander Osen was at the threshold point. Standing. Waiting. Her row of stones would be behind her, on the gear shelf near the camp, seven rocks she'd collected from the geological layers of a place she'd guarded for five years. The small, irrelevant thing a person does to remain a person in the service of an institution that doesn't require her to be one.

"Eight hundred meters," Vel said, breathing hard. The tunnel's demands, the spending, the heat — all of it compounding in a fifty-eight-year-old body that had been counting for forty years.

"Open ground."

"She'll see us before we're halfway."

"She sees us now."

The circuiting agent spotted them. A shout — sharp, trained, the practiced alarm of someone who'd drilled for exactly this — bounced off the basin walls.

The commander's voice came back, unhurried: "I see them."

They moved.

Two hundred meters across the basin floor. The rock was uneven, each step a negotiation with exposed strata and erosion channels. Kem's boots slipped on smooth stone. The sun was behind them, as Vel had planned — the late-afternoon light in the eyes of anyone looking east toward the tunnel exit. It bought them nothing against the commander, who was west of them at the threshold point, but it might slow the descent agents when they came.

Three hundred meters. The circuiting agent had altered his patrol, moving to intercept. He was younger than Osen, fresher, the movements of someone who hadn't been ground down by five years of desert and doubt. Maybe level 258. Maybe higher. More levels in his warm-up routine than Kem and Vel had in total.

Four hundred meters. The descent agents appeared at the base of the switchback, moving fast. The fidgeting one had stopped fidgeting. They fanned out west, covering the approach to the threshold point, cutting off the direct route.

Five hundred meters. The basin floor was open and bright and offered nothing. No cover. No strategy. Just two people walking toward a point that might not do anything when they reached it, through a field of trained operatives who could end them without breaking a sweat.

Kem checked his Ledger. Twelve. Still twelve. The number sat where it always sat — at the edge of awareness, a presence so constant that noticing it was like noticing your own breathing. Except at three hundred, the Ledger had been a cathedral of sound, every potential action a note in a chord so full it was almost silence. At twelve, it was a stripped thing. A few notes left, each one distinct and separate, each one a specific ability he could name and feel. He knew exactly what he could still do. The list was short enough to recite.

Vel was at eight. She'd know her list too. She'd been memorizing lists like that for forty years — the shrinking catalogue of what she could still afford, measured against the growing catalogue of what she needed. She'd been doing this math since before Kem was born.

At six hundred meters, Commander Osen called out. Her voice carried across the basin with the flat clarity of a woman who had been projecting her voice across this exact distance for five years.

"You can't make it work." She didn't sound angry. She didn't sound triumphant. She sounded like someone stating a measured result. "It's not enough anymore."

Vel didn't stop.

Kem didn't stop either. He looked at the commander across two hundred meters of open ground — a woman who had spent five years guarding a door she knew was real, who had watched three people walk through it and done nothing, who collected rocks and followed procedure and went to sleep every night in a camp built for permanence at the edge of the world's most important secret.

She wasn't a villain. She was a monument. The most expensive monument the Hoarded had ever built — a trained, competent, spent person positioned at the end of everything to make sure nobody found out that the end of everything was actually a beginning. The Hoarded spent her the way they spent everything — other people's lives, exchanged for the preservation of a lie.

She sounded almost sorry.

---

Three hours behind them, on the ridgeline, Sable Orm sat on a rock that had been absorbing the day's heat and looked down.

The basin opened beneath her like an anatomical diagram of the earth — layered, exposed, each stratum visible in its own color and texture. She could see the garrison camp. She could see the four agents. She could see two small figures moving across the basin floor toward a point that looked like every other point, and she could see the commander standing at that unremarkable spot, the unchanging center of a pattern Sable had spent six weeks learning to read.

She'd been tracking for nine days. The first Perception spend — one level — had confirmed their direction from the Margins south. The second — another level, another subtraction from the account she'd maintained at three hundred for forty-seven years — had given her distance and terrain, the shape of two people's passage through depleted country.

Two-thirty-six. Her Ledger hummed at two-thirty-six. The sound was different from what she remembered at three hundred — not quieter, exactly, but thinner. As if the instrument that produced it had lost a few of its strings. She imagined describing this in a report. *The acoustic quality of the Ledger at reduced levels suggests a spectral narrowing consistent with—*

She stopped. She was writing reports in her head again. Filing observations under categories that had stopped applying somewhere around day four, when she'd realized she wasn't tracking Kem Aldraste anymore. She was following. The difference was administrative and also total.

The wind pushed at her hair, which had long since abandoned any formal Hoarded arrangement and now did whatever the weather demanded. Her field clothing was dirty. Her boots were scuffed. She looked, she suspected, like someone who had been outside for six weeks for the first time in her life, which was exactly what she was.

From the ridgeline, she could see the two figures stop at maybe six hundred meters from the threshold point. The commander's voice wouldn't carry this far — the basin swallowed sound below a certain distance — but Sable could see the body language. Osen standing her ground. The two small figures continuing to walk.

Sable had read the classified files. She'd accessed them through her Senior Auditor credentials during the third week of pursuit, because no one had thought to restrict an auditor's access to documents about a place no auditor was supposed to visit. The threshold assessment. The dampening operation. The specialist's report: *Two-contributor cascade insufficient at current degradation parameters. Three-contributor minimum recommended for stable threshold event.*

Three contributors. Vel and Kem were two. They might not know the threshold had degraded — or maybe they did, now, if the commander had followed protocol and disclosed. Either way, two people with twenty levels between them were walking toward a door that probably required three to open and was guarded by four people with a thousand.

Sable did the math. She was not good at field calculations — her expertise was quarterly audits, compliance reviews, budget reconciliation. But even she could see the gap. The gap was the size of a person. A third contributor.

She had two hundred and thirty-six levels. More than enough to stabilize a degraded cascade, if the specialist's report was accurate. More than enough, and she was sitting on a rock three hours away, watching.

She didn't know what she would do when she got down there. She had been not knowing for nine days. Each day she'd made one more decision that wasn't a decision — one more Perception spend on tracking instead of heading north, one more night camped in the direction of the Scour instead of the direction of the Vault, one more morning where she woke up and chose south without examining the choice. The question she couldn't stop asking — *if zero is a door, then what was I locking?* — had become less a question and more a direction, pulling her forward the way the Perception ability had pulled her attention south.

She'd told herself she was observing. Gathering intelligence. Following the retrieval target to confirm his final disposition and file a comprehensive report. The institutional language was comfortable, worn smooth by decades of use, and it fit the shape of what she was doing the way a key fits a lock it was made to open. Except she'd stopped believing it somewhere around day five, when she'd realized she couldn't write the report even in her head without the sentences breaking apart. *The target proceeded south toward the Scour in the company of—* in the company of a woman who had been spending for forty years and had eight levels left and was walking toward a door because someone she loved was on the other side. Sable couldn't file that. There was no form for it. No classification that held.

Below, the two figures kept walking. The descent agents were closing from the west. The circuiting agent was moving to intercept. The commander stood at the threshold point, unmoving, the center of a geometry that was about to collapse into something Sable couldn't predict and couldn't look away from.

She stood up. The rock below was three hours of descent — the switchback, or a secondary route she'd mapped from the ridgeline, a scree slope that was faster and less visible. Three hours. By then it might be over. By then the two people down there might have burned their remaining twenty levels and found out whether thirty percent was enough or whether the door stayed shut.

Or she might arrive and the math might change.

Sable adjusted her pack. She checked her footing. She looked down at the basin floor one more time — the layered rock, the shimmering heat, the four agents and two figures and one unmarked point that the entire architecture of the Hoarded had been built to hide.

She started walking down.


# Chapter 18

Eight hundred meters of open ground. Vel measured the distance the way she measured everything now — in what it would cost.

Four agents between her and the threshold point. The commander at the far end, standing exactly where Maren's route journal said the crossing happened, eight years ago. Two permanent garrison operatives spread across the basin floor to cover the eastern approach — their approach, because the tunnel had deposited them exactly where they'd planned, east of the Reserve's primary sight lines, and it had cost them everything they'd budgeted for stealth. The two freshly deployed agents, brought in for Kem, were cutting a wide arc from the garrison camp to close the flank.

Vel crouched behind a shelf of wind-cut rock that offered approximately nothing. The striated layers — ochre, gray, dark red — were beautiful in the way that geology was beautiful when you weren't trying to hide behind it. Eighteen inches of eroded sandstone between her and four people who had more levels in their morning warm-up routines than she had left in total.

Kem was three meters to her left, pressed flat against the basin floor. His breathing was controlled. His eyes were moving — scanning the agent positions, tracking their approach vectors, doing the tactical assessment she'd taught him to do six weeks and two hundred and eighty-eight levels ago. He was at twelve. She was at eight. Between them, twenty levels. The commander alone had more than that in her suppression reserve.

"You heard her," Vel said. The commander's words from the parley were still in the air, heavy as the heat: the threshold had degraded. Two might not be enough. "Two might not cut it."

"Yeah." Kem glanced at the agents. The fresh pair was a hundred meters out and closing. "Does that change the plan?"

"The plan was always get to the threshold point and spend everything. Plan hasn't changed. The math has."

"The math was already bad."

"Now it's worse."

Wind crossed the basin floor and dried the sweat on Vel's forearms. Summer at the Scour — the sun was directly overhead, the shadows small and tight beneath them. No cover past this shelf. No vegetation. Just open ground, layered rock, and four trained combatants who knew exactly what they were trying to do.

The commander hadn't moved from the threshold point. She stood eighty meters behind her forward agents with her arms at her sides — not relaxed, positioned. She wore a windscarf around her neck, the kind the Reserve issued for remote postings, and even from this distance Vel could see the mends: three patches, three different colors of thread. Nobody sent replacement gear to a classified posting. Five years at the edge of nothing, and the commander had darned her own scarf.

"Kem." Vel waited until his eyes were on hers. "When I say spend, spend. When I don't, use your body. Everything you've learned."

"I know."

"I know you know. I'm saying it because I need to hear it out loud."

She took a breath and felt the Ledger's hum — faint now, barely there, like a tuning fork someone had struck in another room. Eight. That was what she was. That was everything she had left of fifty-eight years of spending on strangers and people she loved and a woman who'd crossed this same ground and left her behind.

"Go," she said.

They ran.

---

The first agent hit Kem with a Combat Pulse before they'd covered fifty meters. It wasn't the wild, oversized blast of an untrained Hoarded aristocrat — it was a tight, focused beam that cracked the layered rock where Kem had been standing half a second before. He'd thrown himself sideways, and the move was ugly, all knees and elbows, but he'd read the telegraph. The agent's shoulder had dropped and his weight had shifted to his back foot, and Kem had recognized the pattern because Vel had spent three weeks drilling it into him: every ability-reliant fighter loads their body before they load the ability. Watch the body.

Three weeks ago he couldn't have read it. Six weeks ago he wouldn't have known what he was looking at.

Vel spent her first level.

A Perception burst — a flicker of enhanced awareness that lit up the basin floor like a flare behind her eyes. The agents' positions sharpened. She could see their stances, the micro-adjustments in their shoulders and hips that told her where the next attacks would come. The two permanent agents had settled into covering positions, one northeast, one due east, overlapping their fields of fire. They moved with the ease of people who had drilled this ground for years — every ridge of rock, every wind gully, every line of approach memorized through repetition. The fresh pair was closing from the south, faster, less patient, eager.

One level gone. Seven left. The cost registered in her body like a small door closing somewhere inside her chest — a room she'd never enter again.

The eastern garrison agent threw a suppression field. Not the commander's wide-area dampener from the parley — a focused corridor of thickened air that turned the ground in front of them into something like wading through cold sand. Vel's left leg hit it and her stride broke. The sensation was specific: not pain but resistance, as if the air had opinions about her forward motion.

"Left," she called, and Kem cut left without asking why. Trust or habit — it didn't matter which. He moved.

A force beam scorched the air above them. The rock shelf behind them cracked from the thermal differential — the surface so hot it popped, sending stone chips into Vel's back. One caught her shoulder blade and stuck. She'd dig it out later, if later existed.

"How many can they throw before they feel it?" Kem asked, sliding into a shallow wind gully that provided two feet of cover.

"More than us. Don't count theirs. Count ours."

A Combat Pulse blew a chunk of striated rock into fragments three meters to their left. The agent who'd thrown it was already moving, not pausing to assess. Trained. The agents in the first chase — the bumbling Hoarded aristocrats who'd fused themselves to boulders and given themselves migraines — had been comic. These agents were the opposite of comic. They threw abilities the way working people swung tools: efficiently, without flourish, without waste.

Vel used her second level — a burst of evasive speed that carried her past the suppression corridor's edge and into a position the circling agent hadn't anticipated. She'd planned this move in the long nights in the Margins, working through approach angles on the basin floor map she'd reconstructed from Maren's journal annotations. The speed bought her a line of advance that bypassed the garrison agents' overlapping fields. Two seconds. Maybe three.

Six levels. Another door closed. The Ledger's hum dropped into a register she could barely distinguish from silence.

Kem was up and moving again. He ran low, left arm pressed against his side — not injured yet, just protecting his torso the way she'd taught him. He wasn't burning abilities. He was advancing on his body, the body that had learned to carry packs and cross rivers and dig through collapsed tunnels and take hits. Six weeks of learning what a person could do without the Ledger's help.

Ahead: eight hundred meters of exposed ground, now closer to six hundred. The threshold point was somewhere in all that emptiness — no marker, no glow, no sign that anything had ever happened here except geological erosion and wind. Just a spot that Maren had annotated in the journal margins with a small cross and a note in her cramped handwriting: *here*.

The fresh-deployed pair had closed the flank. They moved together, covering each other's angles — one threw suppression while the other advanced, then they swapped. Standard coordinated pressure. These two had trained together, and their synchronization was the kind that only came from repetition.

The lead agent — the one who'd thrown the first Pulse — blinked forward. Not the blink-step ability Vel associated with high-level combat training, but something close: a spatial compression that moved him thirty meters in a heartbeat. He materialized to Vel's left, already swinging.

His strike was Strength-augmented, focused, economical. No wasted energy. The fist came at her ribs with the kind of force that would crack her sternum if it landed.

Vel got her forearm up. The impact ran through her skeleton. She heard her wrist pop — ligament, not bone, but the distinction was academic when the pain reached her teeth. The blow spun her sideways and she used the rotation, letting her body turn with the hit instead of fighting it. Forty years of fighting people who trusted abilities more than physics. A Strength-augmented strike pulled the fighter forward — the ability loaded the muscles but didn't change momentum, and a committed swing at two hundred and sixty levels of enhancement was like swinging a sledgehammer: powerful, directional, and slow to recover.

She was already past him. His follow-through carried him into the rock shelf, and she heard the stone crack under the impact of his shoulder.

No levels spent. Just a woman with fifty-eight years of knowing what bodies did under force.

But she was bleeding. The stone chip in her shoulder blade had cut deeper than she'd thought, and the wrist was already swelling. She could feel the joint stiffening, the ligament damage announcing itself in a low persistent ache that would get worse.

"Five hundred meters," she said. They'd been fighting for less than four minutes.

Kem was engaged. The other fresh agent had found him, and the two were fighting — Kem with his body, the agent with modulated Combat Pulses that Kem was dodging with the controlled recklessness of someone who understood that every dodge was a bet on his own reaction time. He wasn't elegant. He wasn't precise. He was alive, which was what mattered.

The garrison agent threw another suppression corridor, and this one caught Vel's left leg from knee to ankle. Her quad seized. She stumbled, caught herself on her good hand, kept moving. The basin floor was hot under her palm — midday sun on layered stone, the heat radiating through her skin.

The commander still hadn't engaged. She stood at the threshold point, eighty meters behind the fighting, and watched. Her windscarf moved in the constant Scour wind — the mended patches catching light differently from the original fabric. She was letting her agents do the work. Tire them. Drain them. Force them to spend their remaining levels on survival, so that when they reached the threshold point — if they reached it — they'd have nothing left for the cascade.

Except that was exactly what Vel wanted. Every level spent near the threshold point fed the cascade mechanism. The fight itself was the mechanism. The commander was creating the conditions for the crossing even as she tried to prevent it.

But the commander knew that too. That was why she'd stationed her agents four hundred meters out from the threshold — far enough that the cascade proximity effect was negligible. She was trying to end the fight before they got close enough for their spending to matter.

Vel had planned for this. She'd spent months in the Margins working through the arithmetic of fighting four people with levels she could count on her fingers. The key insight was the power differential itself: agents at 250-plus calibrated their abilities for opponents at similar levels. Their attacks were proportioned for high-level targets, which meant every strike they threw was wildly disproportionate for two people at eight and twelve. The excess energy had to go somewhere — into the rock, the air, the ground. It created debris, dust, noise, concussive scatter. It created chaos. And chaos was cover.

A Combat Pulse detonated the rock three meters to her right. Fragments flew. Dust billowed. Through the cloud, Vel saw the spatial-compression agent recovering from his overshoot, turning back toward her, loading another strike. Behind him, the garrison agent was repositioning.

Now.

She spent two levels.

The combination was one she'd designed specifically for this fight. A redirect field layered over a delayed resonance pulse. The first ability — one level — caught the energy signature of the incoming spatial compression and inverted its vector. The agent had been loading a blink-forward, and the redirect turned it into a blink-backward — hurling him twenty meters away from her, into the path of his own partner. The second ability — one level — detonated the residual energy from the collision as a concussive burst. Not powerful by any standard except her own; at four remaining levels, Vel couldn't generate the kind of force these agents produced accidentally. But the redirect used the agent's own output, and a level-270 spatial compression reflected back at the sender hit like a level-270 ability.

Both fresh agents went down. Not permanently — they had hundreds of levels of physical resilience buffering them, and they'd be up in seconds. But seconds were what Vel needed.

Four levels. The sensation of spending at these numbers was unlike anything at higher levels. At 200, losing a level was like losing a coin from a full purse. At four, it was like losing a finger. She could feel the absence in her body — specific, located, a reduction in what she was. The Ledger's hum was almost gone. A whisper at the far edge of perception, barely distinguishable from the wind.

Her hands were shaking. Not fear. Physiology. The body's response to spending at terminal levels — tremor, nausea, a cold-sweat feeling that had nothing to do with temperature. She'd seen Fallow's hands shake the same way at level four. This was what depletion felt like from the inside.

Three hundred meters. Kem had broken free of the fresh agent's engagement and was running. His shirt was torn across the chest where a near-miss Pulse had singed the fabric, and there was blood on his palms from where he'd caught himself on the rock. He was breathing hard, favoring his left side — he'd taken a hit she hadn't seen.

"You good?"

"No." He didn't elaborate. He kept moving.

Behind them, the fresh agents were getting up. Ahead, the two garrison agents repositioned to form a line between them and the threshold point. And the commander stepped forward for the first time.

Vel felt the ability charge before she saw it — a low-frequency vibration in the layered rock underfoot, the kind of thing that registered in her teeth and the soles of her feet rather than her ears. The commander was building something big. Not a Combat Pulse. Not a suppression field. Something designed for this specific scenario — something she'd spent five years developing for the day someone tried to reach the threshold point with nothing left to lose.

A focused killing strike. Compression beam. The ability gathered in the commander's hands with the specificity of someone who had spent five years learning exactly how abilities performed across this basin floor — wind speed, distance, rock reflection, the way heat warped energy trajectories at midday. She knew her killing ground the way a carpenter knew wood grain.

The beam came at Vel.

She couldn't dodge it. Her body was too slow, too depleted. Her wrist was swelling, her shoulder bled, and the burst of speed she'd used earlier had burned through the last of her physical reserves. She had four levels but the combination she'd planned for this distance required positioning she didn't have — the spatial-compression agent had forced them off their approach line, and now she was ten meters too far east for the angle she needed.

She was going to die four hundred meters from the place where Maren had vanished.

Kem stepped in front of her.

He didn't hesitate. He'd seen the build, read the targeting — not the way a trained fighter reads it, but the way someone who's been watching closely for six weeks reads the moment before something terrible. His hands came up and he spent five levels.

The defensive burst erupted between Vel and the beam — a wall of raw energy, dense and trembling. Not the absurd hemisphere he'd have thrown six weeks ago, not the untrained blast that had leveled a hillside in the forested highlands. A wall. Four meters wide, angled to catch the commander's beam at its optimal deflection angle. The precision was extraordinary. This was what six weeks of learning economy looked like at the moment it mattered.

The commander's beam hit the wall and detonated against it. Stone fragments erupted. The concussion wave knocked Vel backward, and she hit the basin floor on her bad shoulder and felt the stone chip shift deeper. The pain was specific and bright — she would remember exactly where this rock entered her body, would carry the scar for however long she lived after this.

Through the dust and noise, she felt Kem's levels drop. Twelve to seven. Five levels gone in a single defensive act. The cascade proximity — they were close enough now that it registered — made his spending a secondary vibration in her own awareness, a pulse that resonated against the fading whisper of her Ledger.

Kem's wall collapsed. He was on his knees. Blood from his split palms — he'd instinctively braced against the ground during the detonation — was soaking into the layered rock. His face was white under the dust.

"Thanks," Vel said. Her voice came out flat, clinical. The way it always got when things were worst.

"Sure." He spat grit. "Don't mention it."

---

Three hundred meters to the threshold point. Four agents, the two fresh ones hurt and regrouping, the two garrison agents holding position, the commander standing exactly where she'd stood for five years. Guarding a door she knew was real because the alternative — everyone knowing — would mean the end of everything the Hoarded had built.

Vel pulled Kem up. Her bad wrist protested, and she shifted her grip to her forearm. He was heavier than he'd been at the start of the journey — he'd lost the soft Hoarded weight and gained the denser mass of someone who'd been using his body. She got him vertical.

"Move."

They moved.

The garrison agents closed in. These two were the long-term operatives — the ones who'd been stationed here for years alongside the commander. They fought differently from the fresh pair. Less raw power, more positioning. They knew the basin floor's micro-terrain: every wind-carved channel, every shelf of harder stone that could serve as cover or obstacle. They used the ground the way Vel used her remaining levels — with the precision of scarcity.

The northeast agent threw a binding ability. Not the clumsy restraints she'd broken in the Margins — a professional binding that locked around Kem's right ankle like a hand closing. He went down hard, face-first into the layered rock. She heard the impact from ten meters away: the crack of his jaw on stone, a sound that made her stomach turn.

Kem rolled, spitting blood. His chin was split open. The binding held his ankle, and the agent was already throwing a second — this one aimed at Vel.

She spent a level. A misdirection pulse — perceptual interference that made her appear three meters to the left of her actual position. The binding hit the false image and dissipated into empty rock.

Three levels.

The other garrison agent came in from the east. He was fast, disciplined, and he moved with the patient confidence of someone who had plenty to spend and all day to spend it. His first ability — a concussive wave — wasn't aimed at either of them. It was aimed at the ground between them, cracking the layered rock into a crevice that separated Vel from Kem by three meters of broken terrain.

Separating them. The commander's strategy: keep them apart so their spending couldn't reinforce each other's cascade proximity. Smart. The kind of smart that came from watching three crossings and learning how the mechanism worked.

"Kem, stay close." Vel jumped the crevice. Her bad ankle — she hadn't even registered the ankle injury until now, hadn't noticed it happen — protested, and she landed badly on the far side. She was running out of body to damage.

Kem tore the binding from his ankle. It was weakening without its caster's sustained focus, and he ripped it free with his hands — fingernails breaking, skin tearing. The pain didn't show on his face. Or maybe everything hurt enough now that individual pains had stopped registering.

He got up. Blood ran from his split chin, dripping onto his torn shirt. His hands were raw. Something in his left side moved wrong when he stood — the rib from before, aggravated or newly cracked.

Behind them, the fresh agents had recovered and were closing in again. Four combatants. Three hundred meters of open ground. The commander waiting.

Vel looked at Kem. He looked at her. Neither of them said anything. There was nothing to say that their bodies weren't already saying: we are damaged, depleted, outmatched, and we are not stopping.

The northeast agent threw a Combat Pulse. Kem sidestepped it — not cleanly, his injured side made the motion lopsided — and kept advancing. The Pulse hit the rock behind him and blew a crater.

Two hundred meters now. Close enough that Vel could see the commander's face clearly. The woman wasn't young — mid-forties, maybe, with the deep weathering of someone who'd lived under the Scour's sun for years. The lines around her eyes were cut by wind and squinting. She looked tired in a way that had nothing to do with the fight — tired the way Fallow was tired, the way people got when they'd been doing something they understood was wrong for long enough that the understanding had stopped mattering.

"You can still stop," the commander called. Her voice carried across the flat basin, clear despite the wind. "Turn around. Walk out. Nobody has to die today."

Vel didn't answer. Answers cost breath, and she didn't have breath to spare.

Kem answered for her. Not with words — with his second critical spend. The spatial-compression agent had recovered and was blink-stepping toward them from the southeast, loading a killing strike. Kem waited. Let the agent close. Let the blink-step commit the agent to a vector — the same principle Vel had used against the first agent, but Kem did it differently. He'd learned economy, but economy looked different in him than it did in her. Her economy was surgical. His was architectural: he saw the structure of the moment and found the load-bearing point.

Two levels. A precision channeling that locked the agent's ability-control pathways for three seconds. The technical term was a disruptor, but the effect was simpler: the agent's Strength augmentation, his spatial compression, his reflexive shielding — all of it went dark. For three seconds, he was just a man. A fit, trained man, but a man at the end of his own muscles, not the end of a 270-level Ledger.

The agent's blink-step died mid-transit. He stumbled out of the spatial compression at normal speed, normal strength, and the killing strike he'd been loading flickered and collapsed. His fist — no longer backed by two hundred and seventy levels of enhanced force — connected with Kem's shoulder at the speed and weight of a normal human punch.

It still hurt. Kem grunted. But it didn't kill him.

And the agent, suddenly fighting without abilities for the first time since he'd been permitted to train, stumbled over the striated rock and went down. Kem hit him — twice, hard, one fist and then the other — not hard enough to keep him down permanently but enough to buy space and time. The agent's head cracked against the layered stone and he lay still for a moment, blinking.

Seven to five. Kem was at five.

The number sat in Vel's awareness like a stone in her shoe — not her own count but his, transmitted through the cascade proximity that was growing stronger with every meter they closed. She could feel his levels the way she could feel her own heartbeat: not visual, not numerical, but a pulse in the background of her body's awareness.

A hundred and fifty meters. The commander was right there. Her remaining garrison agent was falling back to her position, and behind them the three hurt agents were regrouping. The window was closing — in thirty seconds the fresh pair would be back in the fight and the numbers would be impossible again.

Vel spent her fourth and fifth levels.

Not on combat. On the advance itself. A resonance pulse — one level — that vibrated at the binding agent's frequency, shattering the restraints he was trying to throw. And a concussive burst — one level — channeled through the broken binding's residual energy, redirecting it at the garrison agent's center mass. She heard ribs crack. The agent sat down on the layered rock and stayed there, mouth open, hands flat on the ground, breathing in short shallow gasps that Vel recognized from her own cracked ribs months ago in the chase.

One more agent out of the fight. Three still standing, plus the commander. But the three were behind them now — hurt, regrouping, seconds from re-engaging.

Vel was at three levels. The tremor in her hands had spread to her arms. The nausea was worse. Spending at terminal levels made the body rebel — as if the organism itself understood that what it was losing could never be replaced and tried to stop the hemorrhage.

Kem was beside her. Five levels. Bleeding from his chin, his hands, his ribs. Moving because stopping meant dying.

"Now," Vel said.

Kem spent three levels.

Not on combat. Not on defense. A movement burst — raw kinetic energy that launched them both forward. His hand closed around her forearm and the world compressed. Not the uncontrolled, absurd speed of his early abilities, not the blast that had sent him into a tree in the forested highlands when he'd first discovered what his body could do. This was focused. Directional. Efficient in a way that would have made the Kem of six weeks ago impossible.

A hundred and fifty meters in three seconds. The rock blurred beneath them. The wind screamed. Vel's stomach dropped and her vision grayed at the edges, and then they were there.

The threshold point.

The commander was five meters away. Her remaining garrison agent was ten meters behind them, turning, realizing. The three hurt agents were a hundred meters back and sprinting.

Kem released her arm. He was at two. She was at three.

And now she spent them.

She'd planned this sequence for eight years. Since the night she'd watched Maren cross, since the flash of buildings and light and a world without the Ledger's hum. Since she'd started counting backward from thirty-one, measuring every level against this moment.

Level three: a resonance burst into the ground at the threshold point. Not combat, not defense — a raw injection of ability energy into the cascade site. The earth absorbed it, and Vel felt the response: a vibration deep under the layered rock, something ancient stirring, something that recognized what she was doing because it had been designed for this. The threshold mechanism, dormant since the Hoarded's dampening, began to warm.

Level two: a sustained pulse, keeping the resonance alive, feeding it, letting her own energy and Kem's proximity amplify the signal. The vibration strengthened. The air above the threshold point began to change — not visually, not yet, but she could feel it: a thinning, as if the space itself was becoming less certain of what it was.

The commander ran at her. Vel saw the woman's face — not rage, not triumph, something desperate and almost pleading — and the commander's ability built in her hands, a containment field meant to lock Vel in place, to freeze her body and her Ledger at three, at two, at one, at anything above zero —

Level one.

Vel spent it on nothing. On everything. On reaching. A single level of raw ability energy, the last she would ever have, released directly into the ground where Maren had stood eight years ago. The last of what she was — forty years of spending on drowning girls and strangers and collapsed mines and a woman she'd loved who'd crossed this same threshold and left her behind.

The Ledger emptied.

Zero.

The silence hit her like a wave. Not the absence of sound — the absence of the sound she'd carried for fifty-eight years, the background frequency of the Ledger, the constant peripheral awareness of her remaining levels that had been there since her first breath. Gone. Not faded, not diminished — gone, the way a color might go out of the world, the way you might wake up one morning unable to smell. For an instant the absence was so total she thought she was dying.

She wasn't dying.

The threshold opened.

---

It didn't open like a door. It tore. A ragged split in the air above the layered rock, flickering, struggling to hold its shape. The edges frayed and reknit and frayed again, the way a wound bleeds and clots and bleeds. The degradation the commander had warned about was real — the threshold couldn't sustain itself. Three years of deliberate dampening had ground the mechanism down, and the cascade energy from two spenders wasn't enough to overcome it.

But it was open. Barely. Momentarily. Open.

Through the tear, Vel saw the sub-Ledger.

Low buildings of hand-quarried stone, the tool marks visible on every surface. A sky that wasn't the wrong color — just different, a quality of light that had never been filtered through the Ledger's persistent hum. Streets between buildings, packed earth, and in the distance people moving, carrying things, working. The sound came through distorted — stone on stone, a voice calling, the specific rhythm of a civilization built entirely by people who had nothing left but their hands and their time.

And silence. Not her silence — the silence of the Ledger's absence in her own body — but the silence of a world that had never had the Ledger at all.

She stepped through.

The crossing was nothing. One footstep on layered Scour rock and the next on packed sub-Ledger earth. No sensation, no dramatic passage — just a step, and then the wind was different and the air smelled like stone dust and dried herbs and something she couldn't name. The light was warmer. The ground was cooler. She was somewhere else.

She turned around.

The threshold was behind her — the tear in the air, flickering, weakening. Through it she could see the basin floor. The layered rock. The commander, five meters from the threshold point, her containment field dying in her hands because the target had left. The agents behind her, closing. The Scour wind whipping dust across the exposed ground.

And Kem.

He was still on the other side. On his knees at the threshold point, two levels left, blood on his face and his hands and his ruined shirt. He was looking at the threshold. Looking at her. Through the flickering gap between worlds, their eyes met.

"Kem." She didn't know if he could hear her — the threshold distorted sound, turned words into pressure rather than language.

He was staring at the sub-Ledger behind her. His face held an expression she'd never seen on him — not awe, not fear. Something quieter, something that looked like the moment before a person realizes they've already decided.

The threshold flickered. Contracted. The edges were pulling inward, the tear shrinking. Without sustained cascade energy, without a third spender feeding it, the degraded site was collapsing. The commander's five years of dampening had done their work. The door was closing.

Kem had two levels. If he spent them now, the additional cascade energy might sustain the opening long enough for him to cross. Or it might not. The math was the same bad math it had been since the commander told them the site was degraded.

Two levels feeding a failing threshold. It wasn't enough.

The commander approached him. She moved slowly — no longer attacking, the fight drained out of her by the sight of the threshold actually opening. She'd watched three crossings from a distance over five years. She'd never been this close.

"Stay down," the commander said. Her voice carried through the threshold's distortion, thick and distant. "It's closing. You can't make it."

Kem looked at the commander. Then back at the threshold. At Vel. At the sub-Ledger.

"She's not wrong," he said. Not to Vel. Not to the commander. To the air, to the math, to the twenty levels he'd had an hour ago and the two he had left.

The threshold contracted another inch. From this side, Vel could feel it — the degraded mechanism grinding toward closure, the cascade energy dissipating. The opening was the size of a large door. Then a small door. The light from the basin floor dimmed as the edges closed.

Vel stood in the sub-Ledger and watched the door close on the person she'd brought here. The person she'd recruited as a battery and who'd turned into something she hadn't planned for, something that had spent ninety-eight levels on strangers in the Margins and saved her life with a wall of energy on an empty basin floor.

Then someone else's levels hit the cascade.

Seven of them. A burst of raw ability energy poured into the threshold from the basin floor — not from Kem, not from the agents. From someone who'd arrived from the direction of the ridgeline.

Sable Orm stood on the layered rock with her hands raised.

Through the flickering threshold, Vel saw her clearly. Disheveled, sun-damaged, six weeks away from the desk where she'd spent her career. Her formal Hoarded hair had come undone weeks ago and she hadn't bothered to fix it. She wore field clothes that didn't fit properly — borrowed or taken from someone along the route. She was shaking, and her face held an expression that was not bureaucratic composure or administrative certainty. It was the face of a woman who'd run out of reasons not to act.

Sable spent.

Vel felt each level enter the cascade — seven distinct pulses that hit the threshold like kindling on embers. The first two steadied the edges, stopping the contraction. The third reversed it. The fourth and fifth expanded the tear to something sustainable — a door-sized opening, stable, holding. The sixth locked the frame. The seventh flooded the mechanism with enough energy to sustain itself for seconds longer.

The threshold held.

The third contributor the degraded site required.

Sable at 229. She stood on the basin floor with the Scour wind pulling at her ruined hair, and she looked through the threshold at the world the Hoarded had been hiding. The answer to the question that had brought her from the Margins: *if zero is a door, then everything I served was protecting people from freedom.* She could see it now. The buildings. The people. The light without the Ledger's hum. She could see it, and it was real, and the lie she'd believed for forty-seven years was finished.

The commander turned to her. "Auditor —"

"Don't." Sable's voice was steady. "Don't say anything right now."

On the basin floor, three hurt agents were closing in. The threshold was stable but temporary — Sable's burst had bought time, not permanence. Seconds.

Kem was still on his knees. He looked at Sable. Then at the threshold, steady now, open, waiting. Then at Vel.

She could see him doing the math one last time. Two levels. The last two levels of a Ledger that had started at three hundred. The last of everything the Vault had given him.

"Go," Vel said.

He spent them.

The cascade swelled — the combined energy of Vel's zero, Sable's seven, and Kem's final two feeding the degraded threshold, holding it open. Kem stood. And Vel watched it happen through the gap between worlds: the moment his Ledger emptied. A subtle shift in the way he held himself, as if something he'd been carrying since birth had been cut from his back. His shoulders changed. His face changed. The constant background tension of a person aware of their own remaining power — the number always at the edge of consciousness, the perpetual arithmetic — went out of him like a light.

Zero.

He walked through the threshold.

He came through stumbling. Vel caught him — her bad wrist screaming, her shoulder bleeding, her hands closing on his arms because she wasn't going to let him fall now, not after everything. His weight settled against her, warm and heavy and real, and the cracked rib made him hiss through his teeth. His hands left bloody prints on her sleeves.

They stood together on the packed earth of the sub-Ledger.

Through the closing threshold, Sable Orm remained on the other side. The basin floor, the wind, the agents, the commander. Two hundred and twenty-nine levels and a decision that would define the rest of her life. She stood among all of it and looked through the gap at the world she'd just confirmed existed.

The commander took a step toward her. Behind the commander, the agents were regrouping, confused, trying to understand what had just happened.

The commander shouted something through the threshold — aimed at Vel, or at Kem, or at both of them. Through the distortion it was impossible to make out. A warning, maybe. Or a goodbye. The kind of thing someone says when they've been guarding a door for five years and watched two people walk through it.

The threshold collapsed.

The tear in the air folded inward. The light from the basin floor died. The image of Sable — standing on the rock with her hands at her sides and her face unreadable — shrank to a point and vanished. The wind stopped. The heat stopped. The Scour was gone.

The sub-Ledger sealed itself.

Vel let go of Kem. He sat down on the packed earth. He put his bleeding hands in his lap and breathed — carefully, around the cracked rib, the sound of someone relearning how to exist in a body without the Ledger's constant presence.

Vel stood beside him. The wind here — a different wind, from a different sky — moved through the sub-Ledger's low streets. Somewhere among those hand-quarried buildings, someone was working stone with a hand tool, and the rhythm carried: patient, deliberate, the sound of a world built by people who had nothing left but themselves. The light was warm and steady and did not hum.

Maren was somewhere in those buildings. Eight years older in a world that demanded everything from the body and offered nothing from the Ledger. Alive. Changed. A person Vel hadn't seen for eight years and had burned everything to reach. The reunion would not be simple. Eight years of absence didn't vanish. Maren had built a life here, and Vel had spent eight years trying to follow, and neither of them was the person who'd been separated by the flash of somewhere else on the Scour basin floor.

But she was here. And Maren was here. And that was the thing that mattered, the thing that had been worth thirty-one levels and six weeks and every fight and every cost and every door that had closed inside her as the Ledger emptied toward this moment.

Kem looked up at her. His face was battered — split chin, bruised jaw, dust in his hair, blood drying on his neck. He looked like someone who'd been dragged through the world backward. He also looked, for the first time since she'd met him, like someone who was exactly where he was supposed to be.

"Hey," she said.

"Hey."

"We made it."

He almost laughed. It came out as a huff of air around his cracked rib, and his face twisted. "Yeah." He looked at his hands — just hands now, no number at the edge of consciousness, no potential, no stats. Just skin and blood and bone. "Yeah. We did."

They sat together on the packed earth of the sub-Ledger, and the wind moved through the streets, and somewhere a stone tool struck stone, and the light was warm, and the Ledger was silent, and the door was closed behind them, and it was enough.


# Chapter 19

The first thing Kem noticed was the quiet.

Not silence — the sub-Ledger was not silent. Somewhere outside the window, a bird called, and wind moved through leaves, and a voice said something about water that another voice answered. Ordinary sounds, the background noise of any settlement on any slope anywhere.

The quiet was inside him.

He lay on stone. Cool stone with a grain his back could feel through the cloth someone had put under him. The ceiling above was low — blocks fitted together without the seamlessness of Shaping, every joint visible, every surface marked by tools. He counted the chisel marks in one block while his mind tried to understand what was missing.

The Ledger was gone.

He reached for it the way you reach for a word you've forgotten — involuntary, automatic, the reflex of twenty-six years of living with a number at the edge of consciousness. There was nothing. No hum, no presence, no tally running in the background of his thoughts. For as long as he could remember, his Ledger had been constant — first at 300, so high it was more like weather than information, and then the descent: every spend a subtraction, every ability a cost, the number always there. He had felt it at 245 after the outriders. At 148 before the river. At 50 leaving the Margins. At 31 — Vel's number — and at 12 in the tunnel and then the final spends in the Scour, one after another, the number shrinking under his hands until there was nothing left to count.

Now there was nothing left to count.

It was the opposite of a phantom limb. He kept reaching for the number and finding empty air where certainty used to live. The loudest quiet he'd ever heard — like the ringing after a bell stops, except the ringing didn't fade. It just became the silence. His mind had been listening to something for twenty-six years, and now there was nothing to listen to, and the listening itself was the sound.

He sat up. Small room. A single window letting in oblique afternoon light. A wooden bed frame underneath him, a straw mattress, a table with a clay cup. The walls were hand-quarried stone, each block a slightly different shade, the mortar irregular. No Shaping-smooth surfaces. No invisible seams. Everything showing the work that made it.

He drank the water. It tasted like water.

A woman appeared in the doorway — maybe sixty, short gray hair, arms marked from wrist to elbow with old burn scars. She wore an apron stained dark green and carried a basket of dried herbs that gave off a sharp, peppery smell.

"You're the second one today," she said. "The woman came through about four hours before you. She woke up faster."

"Vel."

"That her name? She didn't give one. Just stood up and walked out of here like she had somewhere to be." The woman set the basket down and looked at him with the practiced assessment of someone who had seen new arrivals before. "I'm Sera. This is the intake house. Eat that bread on the table — your body's going to feel wrong for a while. Some people get dizzy. Some people cry. One man threw up for six hours."

"I'm not going to cry."

"That's what the throwing-up man said, right before he cried."

The bread was dense and dark, the kind that took hands and time and heat. He ate it standing by the window, looking out at a slope of rooftops descending into a valley. Low buildings, stone and timber, gardens visible between them. The rooflines were uneven — not chaotic, but individual, each building shaped by whoever had built it and whatever stone they'd found. It was so far from the Vault's symmetry that the distance felt physical.

"How do I get to the council hall?"

"Down the main path, ten minutes. Can't miss it — biggest building, which isn't saying much." Sera paused at the door. "When you get there, try not to interrupt. They've been arguing about a cistern for three days and they're almost done. Or almost killing each other. Hard to tell."

He walked. His legs were unsteady at first — not from weakness but from a kind of recalibration, as if his body was relearning its own weight without the Ledger's presence. He'd spent so long feeling the number at the edge of himself that its absence changed his center of gravity.

The path was packed earth, worn smooth by feet. He passed gardens where people knelt in soil — real soil, not Fertility-Charmed earth, the kind that needed rotation and rest and manure and time. A man carrying a basket of root vegetables nodded at him and kept walking. Two children ran past without stopping. An old woman sat on a bench outside a building and watched him with the comfortable curiosity of someone who had been watching newcomers for a long time and had learned to wait before forming opinions.

Halfway down the path, he passed an open-sided building that stopped him.

Inside, three people were bent over a table. One was young — an apprentice, maybe sixteen — holding a thin blade of dark volcanic glass while an older woman guided her hand. The blade caught the light: obsidian, knapped to an edge that looked surgical. On the table, a man sat with his leg extended, a wound visible on his calf, and the apprentice was cleaning it with the obsidian edge while the older woman talked her through each motion.

"Shallower. You don't need to dig — let the edge do the work. Good. Now irrigate."

The apprentice poured a solution from a clay jug — something herbal, sharp-smelling — and the man on the table flinched and gripped the table's edge and the apprentice said, "Sorry, sorry," and the older woman said, "Don't apologize. You're helping him. He can flinch."

The man on the table looked at Kem looking at them. "First day?"

"Yeah."

"It hurts more than the abilities did. But you feel it." He shrugged — a complicated shrug, not dismissive, more like an acknowledgment of something he'd thought about and arrived at. "I didn't used to feel it."

Kem watched for another moment. The intimacy of it — the apprentice's face inches from the wound, the older woman's hands guiding, the patient gripping the table — was so far from anything in his experience that he didn't have a frame for it. In the Vault, injuries didn't happen. Above, injuries were healed with abilities that felt like warmth applied at a distance. This was flesh against flesh, blade against skin, each procedure requiring closeness and steadiness and time. Cruder and more human.

He could hear the settlement now — the way you hear a place when you stop moving and let it fill in. Hammers on stone somewhere uphill. A conversation about drainage carried through an open window. A child's voice reciting something — not a lesson exactly, more like a list, names or places, the kind of knowledge that existed here only because someone had decided it mattered enough to teach by mouth. No archive abilities, no recorded information maintained by Ledger power. Just people remembering things for other people.

He kept walking.

The council hall was stone, two stories, with a carved lintel showing hands — open, palm-up, empty. Tool marks deliberate, part of the design.

Inside, four people sat at a long table. Papers, hand-drawn maps, what looked like harvest schedules and water-rights disputes and all the mundane infrastructure of a civilization run without magic. Three of them were mid-argument. The fourth was an old man who appeared to be asleep.

" — don't have the labor for the east terrace this season, and we won't if we keep pretending the voluntary intake can replace actual farmhands — "

"That isn't what I said. I said the new arrivals have skills — "

"Skills from the Ledger world. You want someone who's been growing food with Fertility Charms trying to learn crop rotation in one season?"

The woman who'd spoken first — dark-skinned, close-cropped hair, ink on her fingers — noticed Kem. "New arrival?"

"Yes."

"Voluntary?"

"Yes."

"Welcome. I'm Oriel, this is Pol" — the man with the broken nose — "that's Ede" — a younger woman with the build of someone who worked stone — "and the one pretending to be asleep is Cassery, who's absolutely listening."

The sleeping man opened one eye. "I'm resting."

"Sit if you want," Oriel said. "We won't make you useful today."

Pol looked at him. "Another voluntary." His voice carried something — not hostility, but weight. "Third this year."

"Is that a lot?"

"Depends who you ask." Pol glanced at Oriel. "If you ask the voluntaries, each new arrival is proof the system above is rotten. If you ask the rest of us — the ones who got ground down to nothing by that system and didn't choose to be here — it's complicated."

"Pol."

"She asked me not to be a shit. I'm being honest, which is different." He looked at Kem. "How many levels did you start with?"

"Three hundred."

Something shifted in Pol's face — not surprise, but the shadow of an old anger, well-managed. "Three hundred. Right. I had ninety when the Hoarded labor program took fifty of them in eight years. I came through at zero with my hands wrecked and no say in any of it." He spread those hands on the table. The joints were thick, the fingers slightly crooked. "So when someone walks in who chose to burn down from three hundred — forgive me if I don't throw a party."

"I'm not asking for a party."

"Good. Because we don't have the labor to organize one." Pol said this without smiling and went back to his crop rotation, and Kem understood that the conversation was not over — it would never be entirely over, because the tension between voluntary and involuntary was structural, not personal, and no amount of good faith could erase the difference between choosing zero and having it chosen for you.

Oriel watched this exchange with the face of someone who had watched it many times. "Your friend Vel went to the lower settlement. She was looking for someone named Maren."

"I know."

"Maren's been here eight years. She's on the council — southern district committee, infrastructure and intake." Oriel's tone was careful. "She stopped asking about anyone coming through about three years ago. We all noticed."

"I'll go find them."
